Another Day of Life

我就像一本旧书，由于缺乏阅读而最终腐烂。没有什么可做的，只能旋转出记忆的线索，时不时地擦去积聚在那里的灰尘。

I am like one of those old books that ends up moldering for lack of having been read. There’s nothing to do but spin out the thread of memory and, from time to time, wipe away the dust building up there.

—塞内卡

—SENECA

所有记忆都存在。

All memory is present.

—诺瓦利斯

—NOVALIS

我们所有人都是朝圣者，沿着不同的道路朝着同一个目的地奋斗。

We are, all of us, pilgrims who struggle along different paths toward the same destination.

——安东尼·德·圣埃克苏佩里

—ANTOINE DE SAINT-EXUPÉRY

We Stand in Darkness, Surrounded by Light

跨越边境

CROSSING THE BORDER

乙在希罗多德开始他的旅行之前，他攀登岩石小路，驾船横渡大海，骑马穿越亚洲的荒野；在他遇到多疑的斯基泰人、发现巴比伦的奇观、探索尼罗河的奥秘之前；在他经历一百个不同的地方，看到一千个不可思议的事物之前，他会在一次关于古希腊的讲座中短暂出现，该讲座是比恩斯卡·马洛维斯特教授每周两次为华沙大学历史系一年级学生讲授的。他会出现，也会很快消失。他会彻底消失，以至于多年后的今天，当我翻阅那些课程的笔记时，我找不到他的名字。有埃斯库罗斯和伯里克利、萨福和苏格拉底、赫拉克利特和柏拉图；但没有希罗多德。但我们还是做了如此仔细的笔记。他们是我们唯一的信息来源。战争已于六年前结束，这座城市已成为废墟。图书馆被烧毁，我们没有教科书，根本没有书可言。

Before Herodotus sets out on his travels, ascending rocky paths, sailing a ship over the seas, riding on horseback through the wilds of Asia; before he happens upon the mistrustful Scythians, discovers the wonders of Babylon, and plumbs the mysteries of the Nile; before he experiences a hundred different places and sees a thousand inconceivable things, he will appear for a moment in a lecture on ancient Greece, which Professor Bieźuńska-Malowist delivers twice weekly to the first-year students in Warsaw University’s department of history. He will appear and just as quickly vanish. He will disappear so completely that now, years later, when I look through my notes from those classes, I do not find his name. There are Aeschylus and Pericles, Sappho and Socrates, Heraclitus and Plato; but no Herodotus. And yet we took such careful notes. They were our only source of information. The war had ended six years earlier, and the city lay in ruins. Libraries had gone up in flames, we had no textbooks, no books at all to speak of.

教授的声音平静、柔和、平稳。她那双黝黑、专注的眼睛透过厚厚的镜片，带着明显的好奇心注视着我们。她坐在高高的讲台上，面前有一百个年轻人，他们中的大多数人不知道梭伦是伟大的，不知道安提戈涅绝望的原因，也无法解释地米斯托克利如何引诱波斯人落入陷阱。

The professor has a calm, soft, even voice. Her dark, attentive eyes regard us through thick lenses with marked curiosity. Sitting at a high lectern, she has before her a hundred young people the majority of whom have no idea that Solon was great, do not know the cause of Antigone’s despair, and could not explain how Themistocles lured the Persians into a trap.

说实话，我们甚至不太知道希腊在哪里，或者就这个问题而言，一个以这个名字命名的当代国家有着如此非凡和非凡的过去，以至于值得在大学学习。我们是战争的孩子。战争年代，高中关闭，尽管在大城市里偶尔会召开秘密课程，但在这个演讲厅里，大部分坐着的是来自偏远村庄和小镇的女孩和男孩，他们读书少，受教育程度低。那是 1951 年，大学入学无需入学考试，家庭出身最重要——在共产主义国家，工人和农民的孩子最有机会进入大学。

If truth be told, we didn’t even quite know where Greece was or, for that matter, that a contemporary country by that name had a past so remarkable and extraordinary as to merit studying at university. We were children of war. High schools were closed during the war years, and although in the larger cities clandestine classes were occasionally convened, here, in this lecture hall, sat mostly girls and boys from remote villages and small towns, ill read, undereducated. It was 1951. University admissions were granted without entrance examinations, family provenance mattering most—in the communist state the children of workers and peasants had the best chances of getting in.

长凳很长，本来是可以坐几个学生的，但还是太少了，所以我们挤在一起。我左边是Z，一位来自拉多姆斯科附近村庄的沉默寡言的农民，正如他曾经告诉我的那样，在那种地方，每个家庭都会保留一块干的波兰熏肠作为药物：如果婴儿生病了，就会给它熏肠很烂。 “这有帮助吗？”我疑惑地问道。 “当然。”他坚定地回答道，然后又陷入了阴郁的沉默。在我右边坐着的是骨瘦如柴的W，他的脸瘦骨嶙峋，满脸麻子。每当天气变化时，他就会痛苦地呻吟。他说他在一次森林战斗中膝盖中弹。但到底是谁在和谁打架，到底是谁开枪打死了他，这些他都不会说。我们中间还有几个家庭条件较好的学生。他们穿着整齐，衣服比较漂亮，女孩子们都穿着高跟鞋。然而，这些都是引人注目的例外，罕见的现象——贫穷、粗野的乡村占主导地位：来自军队剩余的皱巴巴的外套、打补丁的毛衣、密布连衣裙。

The benches were long, meant for several students, but they were still too few and so we sat crowded together. To my left was Z.—a taciturn peasant from a village near Radomsko, the kind of place where, as he once told me, a household would keep a piece of dried kielbasa as medicine: if an infant fell ill, it would be given the kielbasa to suck. “Did that help?” I asked, skeptically. “Of course,” he replied with conviction and fell into gloomy silence again. To my right sat skinny W., with his emaciated, pockmarked face. He moaned with pain whenever the weather changed; he said he had taken a bullet in the knee during a forest battle. But who was fighting against whom, and exactly who shot him, this he would not say. There were also several students from better families among us. They were neatly attired, had nicer clothes, and the girls wore high heels. Yet they were striking exceptions, rare occurrences—the poor, uncouth countryside predominated: wrinkled coats from army surplus, patched sweaters, percale dresses.

教授向我们展示了古董雕塑和画在棕色花瓶上的希腊人物的照片——美丽、雕塑般的身体，高贵、修长的脸庞和精致的五官。他们属于某个未知的神话宇宙，一个充满阳光和银色的世界，温暖而充满光明，居住着苗条的英雄和跳舞的仙女。我们不知道该怎么办。看着照片，Z. 郁闷地一言不发，W. 扭曲着身体按摩他酸痛的膝盖。其他人则在一旁看着，目光专注却又漠不关心。在那些未来的先知宣布文明冲突之前，冲突很久以前就发生了，每周两次，在演讲厅里，我得知曾经住着一个名叫希罗多德的希腊人。

The professor showed us photographs of antique sculptures and of Greek figures painted on brown vases—beautiful, statuesque bodies, noble, elongated faces with fine features. They belonged to some unknown, mythic universe, a world of sun and silver, warm and full of light, populated by slender heroes and dancing nymphs. We didn’t know what to make of it. Looking at the photographs, Z. was morosely silent and W. contorted himself to massage his aching knee. Others looked on, attentive yet indifferent. Before those future prophets proclaiming the clash of civilizations, the collision was taking place long ago, twice a week, in the lecture hall where I learned that there once lived a Greek named Herodotus.

我对他的一生一无所知，也不知道他给我们留下了一本著名的书。无论如何，我们都无法阅读《历史》，因为当时它的波兰语译本被锁在壁橱里了。 20 世纪 40 年代中期，《历史》由 Seweryn Hammer 教授翻译，他将手稿存放在 Czytelnik 出版社。我无法确定细节，因为所有的文档都消失了，但碰巧汉默的文本是在 1951 年秋天由出版商发送给排字工的。如果不出意外的话，这本书应该在 1952 年出版，及时找到它的版本。当我们还在研究古代历史的时候，它就已经到了我们的手中。但事实并非如此，因为印刷突然停止了。谁下的命令？可能是审查员，但不可能确定。可以说，这本书直到三年后，即 1954 年底才终于付印，并于 1955 年到达书店。

I knew nothing as yet of his life, or about the fact that he left us a famous book. We would in any event have been unable to read The Histories, because at that moment its Polish translation was locked away in a closet. In the mid-1940s The Histories had been translated by Professor Seweryn Hammer, who deposited his manuscript in the Czytelnik publishing house. I was unable to ascertain the details because all the documentation disappeared, but it happens that Hammer’s text was sent by the publisher to the typesetter in the fall of 1951. Barring any complications, the book should have appeared in 1952, in time to find its way into our hands while we were still studying ancient history. But that’s not what happened, because the printing was suddenly halted. Who gave the order? Probably the censor, but it’s impossible to know for certain. Suffice it to say that the book finally did not go to press until three years later, at the end of 1954, arriving in the bookstores in 1955.

人们可以推测《历史》出版的延迟。它与斯大林去世之前和之后的时期相吻合。希罗多德手稿出版之际，西方广播电台开始谈论斯大林的重病。细节尚不清楚，但人们害怕新一轮的恐怖浪潮，宁愿保持低调，不冒任何风险，不给任何人任何借口，等待事情发生。出去。气氛很紧张。审查人员加倍警惕。

One can speculate about the delay in the publication of The Histories. It coincides with the period preceding the death of Stalin and the time immediately following it. The Herodotus manuscript arrived at the press just as Western radio stations began speaking of Stalin’s serious illness. The details were murky, but people were afraid of a new wave of terror and preferred to lie low, to risk nothing, to give no one any pretext, to wait things out. The atmosphere was tense. The censors redoubled their vigilance.

但希罗多德呢？一本两半千年前写的书？嗯，是的：因为那些年里我们所有的思考、观察和阅读都被对暗示的痴迷所控制。每个词都会让人想起另一个词；每一个都有双重含义，一个虚假的底部，一个隐藏的意义；每一个都包含一些秘密编码、巧妙隐藏的东西。没有什么是平淡的、字面的、明确的——从每一个手势和词语的背后，都可以看到一些指称的信号，凝视着有意义的眨眼的眼睛。写字的人和读字的人很难沟通，不仅因为审查员可以在途中没收文本，还因为当文本最终到达他手中时，后者读到的内容与清楚写下的内容完全不同，不断询问他自己：这位作者到底想告诉我什么？

But Herodotus? A book written two and a half thousand years ago? Well, yes: because all our thinking, our looking and reading, was governed during those years by an obsession with allusion. Each word brought another one to mind; each had a double meaning, a false bottom, a hidden significance; each contained something secretly encoded, cunningly concealed. Nothing was ever plain, literal, unambiguous—from behind every gesture and word peered some referential sign, gazed a meaningfully winking eye. The man who wrote had difficulty communicating with the man who read, not only because the censor could confiscate the text en route, but also because, when the text finally reached him, the latter read something utterly different from what was clearly written, constantly asking himself: What did this author really want to tell me?

因此，一个被暗示所折磨、消耗殆尽的人就会求助于希罗多德。他会在那里找到多少典故啊！《史》共九卷，每一卷都是典故。假设他很偶然地打开了第五卷。他打开信，读到，在科林斯，经过三十年的嗜血统治后，一个名叫赛普塞鲁斯的暴君去世了，他的儿子佩里安德继承了王位，后来事实证明，佩里安德比他的父亲更加嗜血。这位佩里安德，当他还是一名训练中的独裁者时，想学习如何保持权力，因此派了一位使者去见米利都的独裁者老色拉西布卢斯，向他请教如何最好地让人民处于奴役状态。恐惧和征服。

And so a person consumed, obsessively tormented by allusion reaches for Herodotus. How many allusions he will find there! The Histories consists of nine books, and each one is allusions heaped upon allusions. Let us say he opens, quite by accident, Book Five. He opens it, reads, and learns that in Corinth, after thirty years of bloodthirsty rule, the tyrant called Cypselus died and was succeeded by his son, Periander, who would in time turn out to be even more bloodthirsty than his father. This Periander, when he was still a dictator-in-training, wanted to learn how to stay in power, and so sent a messenger to the dictator of Miletus, old Thrasybulus, asking him for advice on how best to keep a people in slavish fear and subjugation.

希罗多德写道，色拉西布鲁斯将佩里安德派来的人带出了城，来到了一片长着庄稼的田地里。当他走过麦田时，他不断地询问使者并让他一遍又一遍地重复。 再说一遍他从科林斯来要问的事。与此同时，每当他看到一粒麦穗比其他麦穗高出时，他就把它折断并扔掉，他继续这样做，直到他毁掉了庄稼中最好、最高的茎。穿过田野后，色拉西布鲁斯将佩里安德的人送回家，但没有向他提供任何建议。当这个人回到科林斯时，佩里安德渴望听到特拉西布鲁斯的建议，但特工说他根本没有提出任何建议。事实上，他说，他很惊讶佩里安德把他派去见这样一个人——一个毁掉自己财产的疯子——他描述了他所看到的色拉西布鲁斯的所作所为。

Thrasybulus, writes Herodotus, took the man sent by Periander out of the city and into a field where there were crops growing. As he walked through the grain, he kept questioning the messenger and getting him to repeat over and over again what he had come from Corinth to ask. Meanwhile, every time he saw an ear of grain standing higher than the rest, he broke it off and threw it away, and he went on doing this until he had destroyed the choicest, tallest stems in the crop. After this walk across the field, Thrasybulus sent Periander’s man back home, without having offered him any advice. When the man got back to Corinth, Periander was eager to hear Thrasybulus’ recommendations, but the agent said that he had not made any at all. In fact, he said, he was surprised that Periander had sent him to a man of that kind—a lunatic who destroyed his own property—and he described what he had seen Thrasybulus doing.

然而，佩里安德理解色拉西布鲁斯的行为。他意识到自己一直在建议他杀害优秀的公民，从此他就对他的人民进行了不懈的残暴。如果说赛普塞卢斯在他的屠杀和迫害期间没有完成任何事情，那么佩里安德完成了这项工作。*

Periander, however, understood Thrasybulus’ actions. He realized that he had been advising him to kill outstanding citizens, and from then on he treated his people with unremitting brutality. If Cypselus had left anything undone during his spell of slaughter and persecution, Periander finished the job.*

还有阴郁、多疑的冈比西斯？这个图中有多少典故、类比、相似之处啊！冈比西斯是当代强国波斯的国王。他在公元前529 至 522 年间统治。

And gloomy, maniacally suspicious Cambyses? How many allusions, analogies, and parallels in this figure! Cambyses was the king of a great contemporary power, Persia. He ruled between 529 and 522 B.C.E.

一切都让我确信冈比西斯完全疯了……他的第一个暴行是杀死了他的兄弟斯梅尔迪斯……第二个暴行是杀死了他和他一起来到埃及的妹妹。她也是他的妻子，也是他的亲妹妹……还有一次，他发现十二名最高级别的波斯人犯有微不足道的轻罪，并将他们活埋到脖子以下……。这些是他对波斯人和他的盟友的疯狂行为的几个例子。在孟菲斯期间，他甚至打开了一些古墓并检查了尸体。

Everything goes to make me certain that Cambyses was completely mad … His first atrocity was to do away with his brother Smerdis… and the second was to do away with his sister, who had come with him to Egypt. She was also his wife, as well as being his full sister… [and] on another occasion he found twelve of the highest-ranking Persians guilty of a paltry misdemeanour and buried them alive up to their necks in the ground…. These are a few examples of the insanity of his behaviour towards the Persians and his allies. During his time in Memphis he even opened some ancient tombs and examined the corpses.

冈比西斯……出发攻击埃塞俄比亚人，没有征用物资，也没有考虑到他打算远征到天涯海角 ……如此愤怒和疯狂，他带着所有的陆军出发了……然而，他们他们还没走到五分之一的路程就完全耗尽了食物，然后他们也耗尽了轭动物，因为它们都被吃掉了。如果冈比西斯看到所发生的事情后改变主意并回头，他就会通过明智的行动来弥补他最初的错误；但事实上，他并没有在意这种情况，而是继续前进。只要有植物可供采集，他的手下就可以靠吃草来维持生命，但随后他们就到达了沙漠。就在那时，他们中的一些人做了一件可怕的事：他们抽签，每十个人中选出一个人，然后把他吃掉。当冈比西斯听说此事后，由于害怕同类相食，他放弃了对埃塞俄比亚的远征，并遣返了他的部下。

Cambyses… set out to attack the Ethiopians, without having requisitioned supplies or considered the fact that he was intending to make an expedition to the ends of the earth … so enraged and insane that he just set off with all his land forces… However, they completely ran out of food before they had got a fifth of the way there, and then they ran out of yoke-animals as well, because they were all eaten up. Had Cambyses changed his mind when he saw what was happening, and turned back, he would have redeemed his original mistake by acting wisely; in fact, however, he paid no attention to the situation and continued to press on. As long as there were plants to scavenge, his men could stay alive by eating grass, but then they reached the sandy desert. At that point some of them did something dreadful: they cast lots to choose one in every ten men among them—and ate him. When Cambyses heard about this, fear of cannibalism made him abandon his expedition to Ethiopia and turn his men back.

正如我提到的，希罗多德的作品于 1955 年出现在书店。斯大林去世已经过去两年了。气氛变得更加轻松，人们呼吸更加自由。伊利亚·爱伦堡的小说《解冻》刚刚问世，它的标题暗示了刚刚开始的新时代。那时文学似乎就是一切。人们向它寻求生活的力量、指导和启示。

As I mentioned, Herodotus’s opus appeared in the bookstores in 1955. Two years had passed since Stalin’s death. The atmosphere became more relaxed, people breathed more freely. Ilya Ehrenburg’s novel The Thaw had just appeared, its title lending itself to the new epoch just beginning. Literature seemed to be everything then. People looked to it for the strength to live, for guidance, for revelation.

我完成了学业并开始在一家报社工作。它被称为Sztandar Młodych（青年旗帜）。我是一名新手记者，我的任务就是追踪发给编辑的信件的踪迹，回到它们的起源。作家们抱怨不公正和贫困，抱怨国家夺走了他们的最后一头牛，或者他们的村庄仍然没有电力。审查制度减弱了，例如，人们可以写道，在乔多夫村有一家商店，但其货架总是空空如也，根本没有东西可买。进步在于这样一个事实：当斯大林在世时，我们不能写出一家商店是空的——所有的商店都必须库存充足，商品爆满。我从一个村庄到另一个村庄，从一个城镇到另一个城镇，在干草车或摇摇晃晃的公共汽车，因为私家车很少见，甚至自行车也不容易拥有。

I completed my studies and began working at a newspaper. It was called Sztandar Młodych (The Banner of Youth). I was a novice reporter and my beat was to follow the trail of letters sent to the editor back to their points of origin. The writers complained about injustice and poverty, about the fact that the state took their last cow or that their village was still without electricity. Censorship abated and one could write, for example, that in the village of Chodów there is a store but that its shelves are always bare and there is never anything to buy. Progress consisted of the fact that while Stalin was alive, one could not write that a store was empty—all of them had to be excellently stocked, bursting with wares. I rattled along from village to village, from town to town, in a hay cart or a rickety bus, for private cars were a rarity and even a bicycle wasn’t easily to be had.

我的路线有时会带我去边境沿线的村庄。但这种情况很少发生。因为越靠近边境，土地就越空旷，遇到的人也就越少。这种空虚增加了这些地区的神秘感。我也对边境地区的安静感到震惊。这种神秘和安静吸引了我，让我着迷。我很想看看另一边有什么。我想知道当一个人跨越边境时会经历什么。一个人有什么感觉？一个人怎么想？这一定是一个充满情感、激动和紧张的时刻。另一边是什么样子？它肯定是——不同的。但“不同”是什么意思呢？它是什么样子的？它像什么？也许它与我所知道的一切都不相似，因此是不可想象的、不可想象的？因此，我最大的愿望，让我不得安宁，折磨着我，诱惑着我，实际上是相当谦虚的：我只想要一件事——跨越边界的那一刻，这个行动，这个简单的事实。穿过它并立即回来——我想，这就完全足够了，可以满足我莫名其妙而又强烈的心理饥饿。

My route sometimes took me to villages along the border. But this happened infrequently. For the closer one got to a border, the emptier grew the land and the fewer people one encountered. This emptiness increased the mystery of these regions. I was struck, too, by how silent the border zone was. This mystery and quiet attracted and intrigued me. I was tempted to see what lay beyond, on the other side. I wondered what one experiences when one crosses the border. What does one feel? What does one think? It must be a moment of great emotion, agitation, tension. What is it like, on the other side? It must certainly be—different. But what does “different” mean? What does it look like? What does it resemble? Maybe it resembles nothing that I know, and thus is inconceivable, unimaginable? And so my greatest desire, which gave me no peace, which tormented and tantalized me, was actually quite modest: I wanted one thing only—the moment, the act, the simple fact of crossing the border. To cross it and come right back—that, I thought, would be entirely sufficient, would satisfy my quite inexplicable yet acute psychological hunger.

但如何做到这一点呢？我的学校或大学朋友都没有出过国。任何在其他国家/地区有联系人的人通常都不愿意发布广告。我什至因为这奇怪的日元而生自己的气。尽管如此，它却一刻也没有减弱。

But how to do this? None of my friends from school or university had ever been abroad. Anyone with a contact in another country generally preferred not to advertise it. I was even cross with myself for this bizarre yen; still, it didn’t abate for a moment.

有一天，我在走廊里遇到了我的主编。伊雷娜·塔尔沃夫斯卡 (Irena Tarłowska) 是一位身材魁梧、英俊的女人，一头浓密的金发偏向一侧。她说了一些我最近的故事，然后问我近期的计划。我说出了我要去的各个村庄的名字，以及在那里等待着我的问题，然后鼓起勇气说：“有一天，我非常想去国外。”

One day I encountered my editor in chief in the hallway. Irena Tarłowska was a strapping, handsome woman with thick blond hair parted to one side. She said something about my recent stories, and then asked me about my plans for the near future. I named various villages to which I would be going, the issues that awaited me there, and then summoned my courage and said: “One day, I would very much like to go abroad.”

“国外？”她惊讶又有点害怕地说，因为在那个年代出国可不是一件平常的事。 “在哪里？做什么的？”她问。

“Abroad?” she said, surprised and slightly frightened, because in those days going abroad was no ordinary matter. “Where? What for?” she asked.

“我在想捷克斯洛伐克，”我回答道。我不敢说巴黎或伦敦之类的东西，坦白说，我对它们并不真正感兴趣。我什至无法想象他们。这只是关于跨越某个地方的边界。哪一个并不重要，因为重要的不是目的地、目标、结局，而是近乎神秘和超然的行为。跨越边境。

“I was thinking about Czechoslovakia,” I answered. I wouldn’t have dared to say something like Paris or London, and frankly they didn’t really interest me; I couldn’t even imagine them. This was only about crossing the border—somewhere. It made no difference which one, because what was important was not the destination, the goal, the end, but the almost mystical and transcendent act. Crossing the border.

那次谈话之后一年过去了。我们新闻编辑室的电话响了。主编正在叫我去她的办公室。 “你知道，”当我站在她的办公桌前时，她说，“我们要派你来。你会去印度。”

A year passed following that conversation. The telephone rang in our newsroom. The editor in chief was summoning me to her office. “You know,” she said, as I stood before her desk, “we are sending you. You’ll go to India.”

我的第一反应是惊讶。紧接着，我惊慌失措：我对印度一无所知。我狂热地在自己的思绪中寻找一些联想、图像、名字。没有什么。零。 （印度之行的想法源于几个月前贾瓦哈拉尔·尼赫鲁访问了波兰，他是第一位访问波兰的非苏联国家总理。第一次接触正在建立。我的故事是为了把那个遥远的地方带到印度。着陆更近。）

My first reaction was astonishment. And right after that, panic: I knew nothing about India. I feverishly searched my thoughts for some associations, images, names. Nothing. Zero. (The idea of an Indian trip originated in the fact that several months earlier Jawaharlal Nehru had visited Poland, the first premier of a non-Soviet-bloc country to do so. The first contacts were being established. My stories were to bring that distant land closer.)

在我们的谈话结束时，我得知我确实要走向世界，塔尔沃夫斯卡把手伸进柜子，拿出一本书，递给我说：“这是一份礼物，路上用的。 ”这是一本厚厚的书，封面是硬邦邦的黄布。正面印有希罗多德的金色字母：《历史》。

At the end of our conversation, during which I learned that I would indeed be going forth into the world, Tarłowska reached into a cabinet, took out a book, and handing it to me said: “Here, a present, for the road.” It was a thick book with a stiff cover of yellow cloth. On the front, stamped in gold letters, was Herodotus, THE HISTORIES.

这是一架老式双引擎 DC -3飞机，因战时前线的突袭而磨损严重，机翼被废气熏黑，机身上的补丁。但它飞向罗马，只有少数乘客，几乎空无一人。我坐在窗边，兴奋地第一次鸟瞰外面的世界。在那之前我连山都没去过。我们的脚下慢慢地经过了五彩斑斓的棋盘、斑驳的拼布被子、灰绿色的挂毯，仿佛铺在地上晒干。但黄昏来得很快，然后就是黑暗。

It was an old twin-engine DC-3, well-worn from wartime forays along the front lines, its wings blackened by exhaust fumes and patches on its fuselage. But it flew, and headed, with only a few passengers, almost empty, to Rome. I sat by the window, excitedly looking out to see the world from a bird’s-eye view for the first time. Until then I hadn’t even been to the mountains. Beneath us slowly passed multicolored chessboards, motley patchwork quilts, gray-green tapestries, as if stretched out on the ground to dry in the sun. But dusk came quickly, then darkness.

“现在是晚上，”我的邻居用带有外国口音的波兰语说道。他是一位回国的意大利记者，我只记得他的名字叫马里奥。当我告诉他我要去哪里以及为什么，这是我第一次出国旅行，我真的什么都不知道时，他笑了，说了一些大意是“别担心！”并承诺提供帮助。我心中暗喜，信心也稍稍增加了一些。我需要这个，因为我正在向西飞行，并且被教导要像害怕火一样害怕西方。

“It’s evening,” my neighbor said in Polish, but with a foreign accent. He was an Italian journalist returning home, and I remember only that his name was Mario. When I told him where I was going and why, that this was my first trip abroad and that I really knew nothing, he laughed and said something to the effect of “Don’t worry!” and promised to help. I was secretly overjoyed and felt slightly more confident. I needed that, because I was flying west and had been taught to fear the West like fire.

我们在黑暗中飞行；即使在机舱内，灯光也几乎不亮。突然，发动机全速运转时飞机各部分所承受的紧张感开始减弱，发动机的声音变得更安静、不再那么急迫——我们已经接近旅程的终点​​了。马里奥抓住我的手臂，指着窗外：“看！”

We flew in darkness; even inside the cabin the lights were barely shining. Suddenly, the tension which afflicts all parts of the plane when the engines are at full throttle started to lessen, the sound of the engines grew quieter and less urgent—we were approaching the end of our journey. Mario grabbed me by the arm and pointed out the window: “Look!”

我当时就傻眼了。

I was dumbstruck.

在我下方，我们飞过的黑暗的整个长度和宽度现在都充满了光明。那是一道强烈的光芒，令人眼花缭乱、颤抖、闪烁。人们有一种液体物质的印象，就像熔岩一样，在下面闪闪发光，表面闪闪发光，随着亮度的脉动，上升和下降，膨胀和收缩。整个发光的幻影是有生命的，充满了运动、振动和能量。

Below me, the entire length and breadth of the blackness through which we were flying was now filled with light. It was an intense light, blinding, quivering, flickering. One had the impression of a liquid substance, like molten lava, glimmering down below, with a sparkling surface that pulsated with brightness, rising and falling, expanding and contracting. The entire luminous apparition was something alive, full of movement, vibration, energy.

这是我一生中第一次看到一座灯火通明的城市。在那之前我所知道的城镇都非常黑暗，令人沮丧。商店的橱窗从来不发光，没有色彩缤纷的广告，路灯的灯泡也微弱。到底谁需要灯呢？晚上，街道上空无一人：很少见到汽车。

It was the first time in my life I was seeing an illuminated city. What few cities and towns I had known until then were depressingly dark. Shop windows never shone in them, there were no colorful advertisements, the street lamps had weak lightbulbs. And who needed lights anyway? In the evenings the streets were deserted: one encountered few cars.

当我们下降时，这片光影越来越近，并且呈现出巨大的比例。最后，飞机重重地撞在停机坪上，发出嘎吱嘎吱的声音。我们已经到了。罗马机场——一个巨大的、用玻璃围起来的地方，挤满了人。我们在一个温暖的夜晚开车穿过繁忙拥挤的街道进入城市。喧嚣、交通、灯光和声音——就像麻醉剂一样。我有时会迷失方向——我在哪里？我看起来一定像森林中的一个生物：震惊，有点害怕，睁大眼睛，试图接受、理解、区分事物。

As we descended, this landscape of lights drew nearer and assumed enormous proportions. Finally the plane thumped against the tarmac, crunched and creaked. We had arrived. The Rome airport—a great, glassed-in lump full of people. We drove into the city on a warm evening through busy, crowded streets. Bustle, traffic, lights, and sounds—it worked like a narcotic. I became disoriented at moments—where was I? I must have looked like a creature of the forest: stunned, a little fearful, wide-eyed, trying to take in, understand, distinguish things.

早上，我无意中听到隔壁房间里的谈话，并认出了马里奥的声音。后来我发现，这是一场关于如何穿着的讨论，因为我是如何穿着 1956 年华沙条约组织的运动时尚的。我有一套切维厄特羊毛套装，带有锐利的灰蓝色条纹——一件双排扣夹克肩部突出、棱角分明，裤子过长、过宽，裤脚很大。我有一件浅黄色尼龙衬衫和一条绿色格子领带。最后是鞋子——鞋底又厚又硬的大号乐福鞋。

In the morning I overheard a conversation in the adjoining room and recognized Mario’s voice. I would find out later that it was a discussion about how to dress me, seeing as how I had arrived sporting fashions à la Warsaw Pact 1956. I had a suit of Cheviot wool in sharp, gray-blue stripes—a double-breasted jacket with protruding, angular shoulders and overly long, wide trousers with large cuffs. I had a pale-yellow nylon shirt with a green plaid tie. Finally, the shoes—massive loafers with thick, stiff soles.

东西方的对抗不仅发生在军事领域，也发生在生活的各个领域。如果说西方人衣着轻便，那么东方人则根据对立定律，衣着厚重；如果西方人穿紧身衣服，那么东方人则恰恰相反——一切都必须突出一英里。人们不必随身携带护照：——人们可以在远处看到谁来自铁幕的哪一侧。

The confrontation between East and West took place not only in the military realm but in all other spheres of life as well. If the West dressed lightly, then the East, according to the law of opposites, dressed heavily; if the West wore closely fitting clothes, then the East did the reverse—everything had to stick out by a mile. One did not have to carry one’s passport around:—one could see at a distance who was from which side of the Iron Curtain.

我们在马里奥的妻子的陪同下开始逛商店。对我来说，这些都是发现探险。最让我眼花缭乱的有三件事。首先，商店里摆满了商品，实际上是堆满了商品，商品压垮了货架和柜台，高高的、色彩缤纷的小溪流到了人行道、街道和广场上。其次，女售货员没有坐着，而是站着，看着大门。奇怪的是，他们静静地站着，而不是坐下来互相交谈。毕竟，女性有很多共同点。与丈夫的问题，与孩子的问题。穿什么，身体健康，昨天炉子上有没有烧焦的东西。在这里我的印象是他们根本不认识，也没有交谈的欲望。第三个震惊是售货员回答了向他们提出的问题。他们用完整的句子回答，然后在最后加上“谢谢！马里奥的妻子会问一些事情，他们会带着同情和关注听她说话，如此专注并向前倾斜，看起来就像是要参加一场比赛一样。然后人们听到了那经常重复的圣礼感恩！

We started making rounds of the shops, accompanied by Mario’s wife. For me, these were expeditions of discovery. Three things dazzled me the most. First, that the stores were full of merchandise, were actually brimming with it, the goods weighing down shelves and counters, spilling out in towering, colorful streams onto sidewalks, streets, and squares. Second, that the salesladies did not sit, but stood, looking at the entrance doors. It was strange that they stood in silence, rather than sitting and talking to one another. Women, after all, have so many subjects in common. Troubles with their husbands, problems with the children. What to wear, one’s health, whether something burned on the stove yesterday. And here I had the impression that they did not know each other at all and had no desire to converse. The third shock was that the salesclerks answered the questions posed to them. They responded in complete sentences and then at the end added “Grazie!” Mario’s wife would ask about something and they would listen to her with sympathy and attention, so focused and inclined forward that they looked as if they were about to start in a race. And then one heard that oft-repeated, sacramental grazie!

晚上我鼓起勇气独自出去了。我一定住在市中心的某个地方，因为火车站就在附近，从那里我沿着加富尔街一直走到威尼斯广场，然后穿过小街小巷回到火车站。我没有注意到建筑、雕像、纪念碑；我没有注意到。我只对咖啡馆和酒吧着迷。人行道上到处都是桌子，人们坐在桌子旁喝酒聊天，或者只是看看街道和路人。在又高又窄的柜台后面，酒保倒着饮料、混合鸡尾酒和煮好的咖啡。服务员忙忙碌碌，用魔术师的戏法送来玻璃杯和杯子，这种戏法我以前只在苏联马戏团见过一次，当表演者凭空迷住一个木盘、一个玻璃高脚杯和一只尖叫的公鸡时。

In the evening I summoned the courage to go out alone. I must have been living somewhere in the center, because Stazione Termini was nearby, and from there I walked along Via Cavour all the way to Piazza Venezia, and then through little streets and alleys back to Stazione Termini. I did not notice the architecture, the statues, the monuments; I was fascinated only by the cafés and bars. There were tables everywhere on the sidewalks, and people sat at them, drinking and talking, or just simply looking at the street and the passersby. Behind tall, narrow counters the barmen poured drinks, mixed cocktails, brewed coffee. Waiters bustled about, delivering glasses and cups with a magician’s legerdemain, the likes of which I had seen only once before, in a Soviet circus, when the performer charmed a wooden plate, a glass goblet, and a screeching rooster out of thin air.

有一天，我发现一张空桌子，坐下来点了一杯咖啡。过了一会儿，我意识到人们在看着我。我换了一套新西装，一件洁白如雪的意大利衬衫，一条最时髦的圆点领带，但我的外表和手势、我的坐姿和动作一定还是有什么东西暴露了我——被背叛了。我来自哪里，来自一个多么不同的世界。我感觉到他们把我当成了外星人，尽管坐在罗马神奇的天空下我应该感到高兴，但我开始感到不愉快和不舒服。我已经换了衣服，但显然我无法掩盖它下面的东西，这些东西塑造了我并将我标记为异物。

One day I spotted an empty table, sat down, and ordered a coffee. After a while I became conscious that people were looking at me. I had on a new suit, an Italian shirt white as snow, and a most fashionable polka-dotted tie, but there must still have been something in my appearance and gestures, in my way of sitting and moving, that gave me away—betrayed where I came from, from how different a world. I sensed that they took me for an alien, and although I should have been happy, sitting there beneath the miraculous skies of Rome, I began to feel unpleasant and uncomfortable. I had changed my suit, but I apparently could not conceal whatever lay beneath it that had shaped and marked me as a foreign particle.

*全文中所有斜体引用均来自希罗多德的《历史》，由 Robin Waterfield 翻译，并由 Carolyn Dewald 进行介绍和注释（牛津和纽约：牛津大学出版社，1998 年）。

* All quotations in italics throughout the text are from Herodotus, The Histories, translated by Robin Waterfield, with an introduction and notes by Carolyn Dewald (Oxford and New York: Oxford University Press, 1998).

被驱逐到印度

CONDEMNED TO INDIA

A穿着浅色柔和纱丽的空姐正在四引擎印度航空国际巨型飞机的舱门内迎接乘客。她服装的柔和色调表明，一场平静、愉快的飞行正在等待着我们。她的双手排列得像在祈祷一样。我很快就了解到，anjali 是印度教的一种问候手势。小屋里有一股浓烈而陌生的香气——我想，肯定是某种东方熏香、印度教草药、水果和树脂的气味。

Astewardess dressed in a light, pastel-colored sari was greeting passengers in the doors of the four-engine Air India International colossus. The subdued hues of her outfit suggested that a peaceful, pleasant flight awaited us. Her hands were arranged as if in prayer; the anjali, I would soon learn, was a Hindu gesture of greeting. In the cabin was a strong and unfamiliar aroma—surely, I thought, the scent of some eastern incense, Hindu herbs, fruits, and resins.

我们在夜间飞行，透过窗户只能看到机翼尖端闪烁的小绿光。这还是在人口爆炸之前，当时航空旅行很舒适，飞机通常只载有少数乘客。所以就是这一次。乘客们在几个座位上躺着睡觉。

We flew by night, only a small green light twinkling at the tip of the wing visible through the window. This was still before the population explosion, when air travel was comfortable, with planes often carrying but a few passengers. So it was this time. Passengers slept, stretched out across several seats.

我觉得自己无法闭上眼睛，于是我把手伸进包里，拿出塔尔沃夫斯卡给我的那本书。希罗多德的《历史》是一部长达数百页的巨著。我发现这么厚的书很吸引人，我从引言开始，译者塞维林·哈默在其中描述了希罗多德的一生，并向我们介绍了他的作品的意义。哈默写道，希罗多德出生于公元前485 年。位于小亚细亚港口城市哈利卡纳苏斯。大约 450 年，他搬到了雅典，几年后又从那里搬到了希腊殖民地图里伊 (Thurii)。意大利南部。他于公元前425 年左右去世。他一生广泛旅行。他给我们留下了一本书——我们可以认为这是他写的唯一一本书。

I felt that I wouldn’t be able to shut my eyes, so I reached into my bag and took out the book that Tarłowska had given me. The Histories of Herodotus is a lengthy tome of several hundred pages. I found such thick books alluring, and I began with the introduction, in which the translator, Seweryn Hammer, describes Herodotus’s life and introduces us to the meaning of his work. Herodotus, writes Hammer, was born in 485 B.C.E. in Halicarnassus, a port city in Asia Minor. Around 450 he moved to Athens, and from there, several years later, to the Greek colony of Thurii, in southern Italy. He died around 425 B.C.E. He traveled extensively during his life. And he left us a book—one can assume it is the only one he wrote.

哈默试图再现一个生活在两半千年前的人，我们对他知之甚少，他的外貌也难以想象。甚至他留下的一件事，在其原始版本中，也只有少数专家能够接触到，他们除了拥有古希腊语知识之外，还必须知道如何破译一种非常特殊的符号：文本看起来像一个无休无止、无差别的词，横跨数十卷纸莎草纸。哈默写道：“单个单词或句子没有被界定，就像章节和书籍是未知的一样；文字像挂毯一样编织得很密。”希罗多德将自己隐藏在这种语言结构后面，就像隐藏在屏幕后面一样，我们比他的同时代人更没有能力穿透它。

Hammer tried to bring to life a man who lived two and a half thousand years ago, about whom we know little, and whose appearance is difficult to imagine. Even the one thing he left behind was, in its original version, accessible to only a handful of specialists who, in addition to possessing a knowledge of ancient Greek, had to know how to decipher a very specific kind of notation: the text looked like one unending, undifferentiated word stretching across dozens of rolls of papyrus. “Individual words or sentences were not demarcated,” wrote Hammer, “just as chapters and books were unknown; the text was as densely woven as a tapestry.” Herodotus concealed himself behind this verbal fabric as behind a screen, which we are even less well equipped to penetrate than his contemporaries were.

夜晚结束了，白天来临了。透过小窗户，我第一次能够看到我们星球的广阔无垠。这一景象让我想起了无限的想法。在此之前我所知道的世界大约有五百公里长、四百公里宽。我们就在这里，似乎永远在飞翔，而我们下方很远的大地却不断变换着颜色——一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的；一会儿是烧焦的，棕色的。然后是绿色；然后，很长一段时间，深蓝色。

The night ended and day came. Looking through the little window, I was able to gaze for the first time on an enormous expanse of our planet. The sight brought thoughts of infinity to mind. The world I had known until then was perhaps five hundred kilometers in length and four hundred in width. And here we were, flying seemingly forever, while the earth, very far below us, kept changing colors—for a while it was burnt, brown; then green; and then, for a long while, dark blue.

抵达新德里时已是傍晚。我顿时浑身湿热，站在那儿汗流浃背。和我一起飞行的人突然消失了，被一直在等待他们的色彩缤纷、充满活力的朋友和亲戚所带走。

It was late evening when we landed in New Delhi. I was instantly awash in heat and humidity, and stood dripping with sweat. The people with whom I had been flying suddenly vanished, swept away by the colorful, animated crowd of friends and relatives that had been waiting for them.

我独自一人，不知道该怎么办。机场大楼那里又小又黑又荒凉，与罗马相差甚远。它独自矗立在夜色中，我不知道在黑暗的深处有什么。不一会儿，一个身穿白色宽松及膝长袍的老者出现了。他留着灰色的胡子，戴着橙色的头巾。他说了一些我听不懂的话，尽管我猜想他是在问我为什么独自站在空荡荡的机场中央。我不知道该回答什么，环顾四周，思考着——接下来该怎么办？我对这次旅行毫无准备。我的笔记本上既没有名字，也没有地址。我的英语很差。不过，这也不完全是我的错：我唯一的愿望就是实现无法实现的目标——跨越边界。我别无他求。但在表达这个愿望时，我开始了一系列的事件，这些事件现在把我一路送到了这里，在世界的另一边。

I was left alone and had no idea what to do. The airport building was small, dark, and deserted, a far cry from Rome’s. It stood all by itself cloaked in night, and I didn’t know what lay beyond, in the depths of the darkness. After a while an old man in a white, loose knee-length garment appeared. He had a gray beard and an orange turban. He said something I did not understand, although I assume he was asking why I was standing there alone, in the middle of the empty airport. I had no idea what to answer and looked about me, pondering—what next? I was quite unprepared for this journey. I had neither names nor addresses in my notebook. My English was poor. I was not entirely to blame, though: my sole desire had been to achieve the unachievable—to cross the border. I wanted nothing more. But in expressing that wish I’d started the chain of events that had now deposited me all the way here, on the far side of the world.

老人想了想，示意我跟他走。入口的一侧停着一辆破旧破旧的公共汽车。我们上了车，老人启动了发动机，我们就出发了。我们只走了几百米，司机就放慢了速度，并开始猛烈地按喇叭。在我们面前，在本应是道路的地方，我看到一条宽阔的白色河流消失在闷热闷热的夜晚的浓黑中。河边的人们在露天睡觉，有的睡在木板床上，有的睡在垫子上、毯子上，但大多数直接睡在光秃秃的沥青和两岸的沙岸上。

The old man thought for a while, then motioned with his hand for me to follow him. To one side of the entrance stood a scratched-up, dilapidated bus. We got in, the old man started the motor, and we set off. We had covered only several hundred meters when the driver slowed down and began honking violently. Before us, where the road should have been, I saw a broad, white river vanishing somewhere in the thick blackness of the sultry, sweltering night. The river was of people sleeping out in the open, some on wooden plank beds, others on mats, on blankets, but most directly on the bare asphalt and the sandy banks stretching on each side of it.

我以为，头顶上响起的号角声吵醒了人群，他们会愤怒地扑向我们，殴打我们，甚至可能用私刑处死我们。离得很远！当我们一寸寸地向前推进时，他们一个个站起来，移到一边，带着孩子，推着几乎无法行走的老妇人。在他们的热心顺从、顺从的谦卑中，有一些东西抱歉，就好像睡在路上是某种犯罪行为，他们很快就试图抹去其痕迹。就这样，我们慢慢地朝城市走去，号角吹响，人们骚动起来，让路——一直不停。当我们到达城镇时，它的街道变得同样难以行走：它似乎也只是一个巨大的营地，由穿着白衣、夜间梦游的幽灵组成。

I thought that the crowds, awakened by the roar of the horn resounding directly over their heads, would fall upon us in a rage, beat us, perhaps lynch us even. Far from it! As we inched forward, they rose one by one and moved aside, taking with them children and pushing along old women barely able to walk. In their ardent compliance, in their submissive humility, there was something apologetic, as if sleeping here on the road were some crime whose traces they were quickly trying to erase. And thus we inched our way toward the city, the horn blaring, people stirring and giving way—on and on and on. Once we reached town, its streets turned out to be equally difficult to navigate: it too seemed just one enormous camp of white-clad, somnambular phantoms of the night.

就这样我们到达了一个被红色灯泡照亮的地方：酒店。司机把我留在接待处，一言不发地消失了。办公桌旁的那个戴着蓝色头巾的男人带我上楼，来到一间小房间，里面只有一张床、一张桌子和一个盥洗台。他一言不发地掀开床单，床单上有惊慌失措的虫子，他把床单甩到地板上，低声说了句晚安，然后就离开了。

In this fashion we arrived at a place illuminated by a red light-bulb: HOTEL. The driver left me at reception and disappeared without a word. The man at the desk, this one sporting a blue turban, led me upstairs to a little room furnished with only a bed, a table, and a washstand. Without a word he pulled off the bedsheet, on which scurried panicked bugs, which he shook off onto the floor, muttered something by way of good night, and departed.

我独自一人坐在床上，开始考虑自己的处境。消极的一面是，我不知道自己在哪里。从积极的方面来说，我有一个栖身之所。一个机构（一家酒店）为我提供了庇护。我感到安全吗？是的。不舒服吗？不，奇怪吗？是的。我无法准确地定义这种奇怪的感觉在哪里，但是早上，当一个赤脚的男人拿着一壶茶和几块饼干走进房间时，这种感觉变得更加强烈。我以前从未发生过这样的事情。他将托盘放在桌上，鞠了一躬，没有再说什么，轻轻退了出去。他的举止里有一种自然的礼貌，一种深刻的机智，一种令人惊讶的精致和尊严，使我立刻对他产生了钦佩和尊重。

Left alone, I sat down on the bed and started to consider my situation. On the negative side, I didn’t know where I was. On the positive, I had a roof over my head; an institution (a hotel) had given me shelter. Did I feel safe? Yes. Uncomfortable? No. Strange? Yes. I could not define precisely wherein lay this strangeness, but the sensation grew stronger in the morning, when a barefoot man entered the room bearing a pot of tea and several biscuits. Nothing like this had ever happened to me before. He placed the tray on the table, bowed, and, having uttered not a word, softly withdrew. There was such a natural politeness in his manner, such profound tactfulness, something so astonishingly delicate and dignified, that I felt instant admiration and respect for him.

一小时后，当我走出酒店时，更令人不安的事情发生了。在街的对面，一个狭窄的小广场上，黄包车夫从黎明起就聚集在一起——他们瘦瘦的、弯腰驼背，双腿骨瘦如柴。他们肯定知道有一位老爷已经来到了酒店。根据定义，老爷子必须有钱，所以他们耐心等待，准备好服务。但一想到舒舒服服地躺在人力车里，拉着一个饥饿、虚弱、一只脚已入坟墓的流浪汉，我就感到极度的厌恶、愤怒和恐惧。成为剥削者？吸血者？以这种方式压迫另一个人？绝不！我是在一种完全相反的精神中长大的​​，我被教导说即使是像这样的活骷髅也是我的兄弟，同类的灵魂，亲近的人，我的肉中之肉。因此，当车夫们向我扑来，恳求鼓励，为我的生意吵闹、互相争斗时，我开始坚决地推开他们、斥责他们、抗议。他们很惊讶——我在说什么，我在做什么？毕竟他们一直指望着我。我是他们唯一的机会，他们唯一的希望——哪怕只是为了一碗米饭。我头也不回地往前走，冷漠、坚决，还有点沾沾自喜，因为我没有让自己被操纵去扮演水蛭的角色。

Something more disconcerting occurred an hour later, when I stepped out of the hotel. On the opposite side of the street, on a cramped little square, rickshaw drivers had been gathering since dawn—skinny, stooped men with bony, sinewy legs. They must have learned that a sahib had arrived in the hotel. A sahib, by definition, must have money, so they waited patiently, ready to serve. But the very idea of sprawling comfortably in a rickshaw pulled by a hungry, weak waif of a man with one foot already in the grave filled me with the utmost revulsion, outrage, horror. To be an exploiter? A bloodsucker? To oppress another human being in this way? Never! I had been brought up in a precisely opposite spirit, taught that even living skeletons such as these were my brothers, kindred souls, near ones, flesh of my flesh. So when the rickshaw drivers threw themselves upon me with pleading encouragement, clamoring and fighting amongst themselves for my business, I began to firmly push them away, rebuke them, protest. They were astounded—what was I saying, what was I doing? They had been counting on me, after all. I was their only chance, their only hope—if only for a bowl of rice. I walked on without turning my head, impassive, resolute, a little smugly proud of not having allowed myself to be manipulated into assuming the role of a leech.

老德里！狭窄、尘土飞扬、酷热的街道，散发着热带发酵的令人窒息的气味。而这群默默移动的人，出现又消失，他们的脸阴暗、潮湿、匿名、封闭。孩子们安静，不发出任何声音。一名男子呆呆地盯着他的自行车残骸，它已经散落在街中央。一个女人在卖用绿叶包裹的东西——这是什么？这些叶子包裹着什么？一名乞丐展示了他的胃部皮肤是如何贴在脊髓上的——但这可能吗？人们必须小心行走，注意，因为许多摊贩直接将商品摊在地上、人行道上、路边。一名男子将两排人类牙齿和一些旧钳子放在一张报纸上，以此为他的牙科服务做广告。他的邻居——一个干瘪、萎缩的家伙——是叫卖书籍。我在漫不经心地堆放、落满灰尘的书堆中翻找，找到了两本：海明威的《丧钟为谁而鸣》（对于学习英语很有用）和牧师 JA 杜波依斯的《印度教礼仪、风俗和仪式》。杜波依斯神父于1792年以传教士身份抵达印度，一待就是三十一年，他研究印度教生活方式的成果就是我刚刚购买的这本书，这本书在英国人的帮助下于1816年在英国出版。东印度公司。

Old Delhi! Its narrow, dusty, fiendishly hot streets, with their stifling odor of tropical fermentation. And this crowd of silently moving people, appearing and disappearing, their faces dark, humid, anonymous, closed. Quiet children, making no sound. A man stares dully at the remains of his bicycle, which has fallen apart in the middle of the street. A woman sells something wrapped in green leaves—what is it? What do those leaves enfold? A beggar demonstrates how the skin of his stomach is plastered to his spinal cord—but is this even possible? One has to walk carefully, to pay attention, because many vendors spread their wares directly on the ground, on the sidewalks, right on the edge of the road. Here is a man who has laid out two rows of human teeth and some old pliers on a piece of newspaper, thereby advertising his dental services. His neighbor—a wizened, shrunken fellow—is hawking books. I rummage through the carelessly arranged, dusty piles and settle on two: Hemingway’s For Whom the Bell Tolls (useful for learning English) and the priest J. A. Dubois’s Hindu Manners, Customs, and Ceremonies. Father Dubois arrived in India as a missionary in 1792 and stayed for thirty-one years, and the fruit of his studies of Hindu ways of life was the book I had just purchased, which was published in England in 1816 with the assistance of the British East India Company.

我回到旅馆，翻开海明威的第一句话：“他平躺在森林里布满松针的棕色地板上，双臂交叉托着下巴，高高的头顶上，风吹过松树的树顶。 ”。我什么都不懂。我有一本小型英语-波兰语袖珍词典，这是华沙唯一可以买到的词典。我设法找到了“棕色”这个词，但没有找到其他词。我继续说下一句话：“山坡缓缓倾斜……”又——一言不发。 “旁边有一条小溪……”我越想理解这段文字，就越灰心、绝望。我感觉自己被困住了。被语言围困。在那一刻，语言给我的印象是某种物质的东西，某种具有物理维度的东西，一堵在路中间升起的墙，阻止我走得更远，封闭了世界，使它变得遥不可及。这是一种不愉快和羞辱的感觉。这也许可以解释为什么在第一次接触外国的某人或某物时，有些人会感到恐惧和不确定，充满不信任。这次会议将带来什么？结局会如何呢？最好不要冒险，留在熟悉的茧里！最好不要把脖子伸出自家后院！

I returned to the hotel, opened the Hemingway to the first sentence: “He lay flat on the brown, pine-needled floor of the forest, his chin on his folded arms, and high overhead the wind blew in the tops of the pine trees.” I understood nothing. I had a small English-Polish pocket dictionary, the only one that had been available in Warsaw. I managed to find the word “brown,” but none of the others. I proceeded to the next sentence: “The mountainside sloped gently…” Again—not a word. “There was a stream alongside …” The more I tried to understand this text, the more discouraged and despairing I became. I felt trapped. Besieged by language. Language struck me at that moment as something material, something with a physical dimension, a wall rising up in the middle of the road and preventing my going further, closing off the world, making it unattainable. It was an unpleasant and humiliating sensation. It might explain why, in a first encounter with someone or something foreign, there are those who will feel fear and uncertainty, bristle with mistrust. What will this meeting bring? How will it end? Better not to risk it and to remain in the cocoon of the familiar! Better not to stick one’s neck out of one’s own backyard!

如果不是我买了当时往返于格但斯克和孟买之间的巴托里号客轮的回程票，我可能一时冲动就逃离印度回国了。埃及总统贾迈勒·阿卜杜勒·纳赛尔刚刚将苏伊士运河国有化，促使英国和法国以武装干预作为回应；战争爆发后，运河被堵塞，巴托里号被困在地中海的某个地方。我与家人隔绝，被迫前往印度。

On first impulse, I might have fled India and returned home, if not for my having bought a return ticket on the passenger ship Batory, which in those days sailed between Gdańsk and Bombay. The Egyptian president Gamal Abdel Nasser had just nationalized the Suez Canal, prompting England and France to respond with armed intervention; as war broke out, the canal was blocked, and the Batory was stuck somewhere on the Mediterranean Sea. Cut off from home, I was condemned to India.

被扔进深水里，我不想被淹死。我意识到只有语言才能拯救我。我开始思考希罗多德在世界各地流浪时是如何处理外语的。哈默写道，希罗多德只懂希腊语，但由于当时的希腊人分散在整个地球上，他们的殖民地、港口和工厂无处不在，《历史》的作者可以利用他遇到的同胞提供的帮助，这些同胞曾为希腊人服务过。作为他的翻译和指导。此外，希腊语是当时的通用语言，欧洲、亚洲和非洲的许多人都说这种语言，后来被拉丁语取代，然后是法语和英语。

Cast into deep water, I didn’t want to drown. I realized that only language could save me. I started to think about how Herodotus, wandering the world, had dealt with foreign languages. Hammer writes that Herodotus knew only Greek, but because Greeks at the time were scattered over the entire planet, had their colonies, ports, and factories everywhere, the author of The Histories could avail himself of help offered by the countrymen he encountered, who served as his translators and guides. Moreover, Greek was the lingua franca of those days, and many people in Europe, Asia, and Africa spoke the language, which was later replaced by Latin, and then French and English.

我开始日日夜夜地死记硬背单词。我用冷毛巾敷在太阳穴上，感觉头要炸裂了。我从来没有离开过海明威，但现在我跳过了我无法理解的描述性段落并阅读了对话，这更容易：

I began cramming words, night and day. I placed a cold towel on my temples, feeling my head was bursting. I was never without the Hemingway, but now I skipped the descriptive passages I couldn’t understand and read the dialogues, which were easier:

“你有多少？”罗伯特·乔丹问道。

“How many are you?” Robert Jordan asked.

“我们有七个人，有两个女人。”

“We are seven and there are two women.”

“二？”

“Two?”

“是的。”

“Yes.”

我全都明白了！还有这个：

I understood all of that! And this, too:

“奥古斯丁是一个非常好的人，”安塞尔莫说……

“Augustín is a very good man,” Anselmo said….

“你很了解他？”

“You know him well?”

“是的。许久。”

“Yes. For a long time.”

我在城市里走来走去，抄下招牌、商店里的商品名称、在公交车站听到的话语。在电影院里，我在黑暗中盲目地写下屏幕上的文字，并注意到街上示威者举着的横幅上的口号。我不是通过图像、声音和气味来了解印度，而是通过文字；此外，这些词不是本土印地语的，而是外来的、强加的语言，那时这种语言已经在这里完全扎根，对我来说，这是进入这个国家不可或缺的钥匙，几乎与这个国家一模一样。我明白，每一个不同的地理宇宙都有它自己的神秘面纱，只有通过学习当地的语言才能解开它的神秘面纱。没有它，即使人们在其中度过一整年，这个宇宙仍将是不可穿透和不可知的。我也注意到命名和存在之间的关系，因为我回到旅馆后意识到，在城里我只看到了我能够命名的东西：例如，我记得那棵金合欢树，但不记得那棵站立的树旁边，我不知道他的名字。简而言之，我明白，我知道的单词越多，展现在我面前、我能捕捉到的世界就越丰富、越充实、越丰富多彩。

I walked around the city, copying down signboards, the names of goods in stores, words overheard at bus stops. In movie theaters I scribbled blindly, in darkness, the words on the screen, and noted the slogans on banners carried by demonstrators in the streets. I approached India not through images, sounds, and smells, but through words; furthermore, words not of the indigenous Hindi, but of a foreign, imposed tongue, which by then had so fully taken root here that it was for me an indispensable key to this country, almost identical with it. I understood that every distinct geographic universe has its own mystery and that one can decipher it only by learning the local language. Without it, this universe will remain impenetrable and unknowable, even if one were to spend entire years in it. I noticed, too, the relationship between naming and being, because I realized upon my return to the hotel that in town I had seen only that which I was able to name: for example, I remembered the acacia tree, but not the tree standing next to it, whose name I did not know. I understood, in short, that the more words I knew, the richer, fuller, and more variegated would be the world that opened before me, and which I could capture.

在我到达德里后的那些日子里，我一直被这样的想法折磨着：我不是在做记者，我不是在为我以后要写的故事收集材料。毕竟我不是作为游客来的。我是一名特使，负责交代、传递、联系。但我发现自己两手空空，感觉无能为力，甚至不知从何开始。毕竟我对印度一无所知，也没有主动要求了解。跨越边界——就是这样。而已。但现在，苏伊士战争让我无法返回，我只能继续前行。我决定去旅行。

During all those days after my arrival in Delhi I was tormented by the thought that I was not working as a reporter, that I was not gathering material for the stories that I would later have to write. I hadn’t come as a tourist, after all. I was an envoy, engaged to render an account, to transmit, relate. But I found myself empty-handed, and feeling incapable of doing anything, at a loss even to know where to begin. I knew nothing about India, after all, and hadn’t asked for it. Crossing the border—that was it. Nothing more. But now, since the Suez war made returning impossible, I could only move forward. I decided to travel.

•••

•   •   •

我住的酒店的接待员建议我去贝拿勒斯：“神圣的小镇！”他们解释道。 （我已经注意到印度有多少东西是神圣的：神圣的城镇、神圣的河流、数百万头神圣的牛。令人震惊的是，神秘主义渗透到生活中的程度，印度有多少寺庙、教堂和各种小祭坛。每一步，有多少火、多少香在燃烧，有多少人的额头上有仪式标记，有多少人一动不动地坐着，凝视着某个超然的点。）

The receptionists in my hotel advised me to go to Benares: “Sacred town!” they explained. (I had noticed already how many things in India are sacred: the sacred town, the sacred river, millions of sacred cows. It is striking, the degree to which mysticism permeates life, how many temples there are, chapels and various little altars at every step, how many fires and how much incense is burning, how many people have ritual markings on their foreheads, how many are sitting motionless, staring at some transcendent point.)

我听取了接待员的意见，乘公共汽车前往贝拿勒斯。人们开车穿过贾木纳河和恒河的山谷，穿过平坦、绿色的乡村，那里点缀着农民在稻田里涉水、用锄头挖地、或头上顶着包袱、篮子或麻袋的白色剪影。但窗外的景色是变化无常的，常常是一片广阔的水域充满了景观。正是秋水泛滥的季节，河流变成了宽阔的湖泊，名副其实的海洋。在他们的海岸上，赤脚的洪水灾民扎营。他们在上涨的洪水面前逃跑，但仍与洪水保持接触，只逃到必要的距离，并在洪水开始退去时立即返回。在即将结束的日子里，在可怕的炎热中，水蒸发了，乳白色的、静止的雾气笼罩在一切之上。

I heeded the receptionists and took a bus to Benares. One drives there through the valley of the Jamuna and the Ganges, through flat, green countryside dotted with the white silhouettes of peasants wading in rice paddies, digging in the ground with hoes, or carrying bundles, baskets, or sacks on their heads. But this view outside the window was mutable, and frequently an immense expanse of water filled the landscape. It was the season of the autumn floods, and rivers metamorphosed into broad lakes, veritable seas. On their shores camped barefoot flood victims. They fled before the rising water but maintained their contact with it, escaping only as far as was necessary and returning immediately when the floodwaters started to recede. In the ghastly heat of the dying day, the water vaporized and a milky, still fog hovered over everything.

我们在傍晚到达贝拿勒斯，实际上是在晚上。这座城市似乎没有郊区，通常是为与市中心的相遇而准备的。在这里，人们突然从黑暗、寂静、空旷的夜色中出现，进入灯火通明、拥挤而喧闹的市中心。为什么这些人如此蜂拥而至，互相攀爬，四面八方，就在外面，有这么多的自由空间，有这么多的空间供每个人使用？下了公共汽车后，我去散步。我到达了贝拿勒斯的郊区。一侧是黑暗中寂静、无人居住的田野，另一侧是城市的建筑，人口稠密，熙熙攘攘，灯火辉煌，震耳欲聋的音乐。我无法理解这种拥挤、相互摩擦、无休止推搡的生活的必要性——当那边有这么多自由空间时更是如此。

We reached Benares in late evening, at night really. The city seemed to have no suburbs, which normally prepare one gradually for the encounter with downtown; here one emerges all of a sudden out of the dark, silent, and empty night into the brightly lit, crowded, and noisy city center. Why do these people flock and swarm together so, clamber all over one another while all around, just beyond, there is so much free space, so much room for everyone? After getting off the bus I went for a walk. I reached the outskirts of Benares. To one side, in the darkness, lay the still, uninhabited fields, and to the other rose the buildings of the city, densely peopled, bustling, brilliantly illuminated, throbbing with loud music. I cannot fathom this need for a life of congestion, of rubbing against one another, of endlessly pushing and shoving—all the more so when right over there is so much free space.

当地人建议我晚上不要睡觉，这样我就能趁天黑到达恒河岸边，在沿河延伸的石阶上等待黎明。 “日出很重要！”他们说，他们的声音充满了真正宏伟的承诺。

The locals advised me not to go to sleep at night, so that I would get to the banks of the Ganges while it was still dark and there, on the stone steps that stretch along the river, await the dawn. “The sunrise is very important!” they said, their voices resounding with the promise of something truly magnificent.

当人们开始聚集在河边时，天确实还黑着。单独和成群。整个氏族。朝圣者的队伍。拄着拐杖的跛子。年迈的虚拟骷髅，有的背在年轻人的背上，有的则扭曲、疲惫不堪，艰难地沿着柏油路爬行。牛和山羊跟在人们身边，还有一群瘦骨嶙峋、患有疟疾的狗。我也加入了这场奇怪的神秘剧。

It was indeed still dark when people began converging on the river. Singly and in groups. Entire clans. Columns of pilgrims. The lame on crutches. Aged virtual skeletons, some carried on the backs of the young, others—twisted, exhausted—crawling with great difficulty on their own along the asphalt. Cows and goats trailed alongside the people, as did packs of bony, malarial dogs. I too joined this strange mystery play.

到达河边的台阶并不容易，因为前面是一片狭窄、空气不流通、肮脏的小街道，街道上挤满了乞丐，他们不断地推着朝圣者，同时发出令人难以忍受的可怕和刺耳的哀叹。最后，经过各种通道和拱廊，人们出现在楼梯的顶部，楼梯直下到河边。尽管黎明刚刚触及天空，但成千上万的信徒已经在那里。有些人精力充沛，奋力前行，谁知道在哪里，为什么。其他人则盘腿而坐，双臂伸向天空。楼梯的最底层是进行净化仪式的人——他们涉水而行。在河里，时不时将自己完全淹没在水中。我看到一个家庭正在对一位肥胖的祖母进行净化仪式。奶奶不会游泳，一下子就沉到了水底。家人赶紧赶来，将她带回水面。祖母尽了最大的力气，但他们一放开她，她就又沉下去了。我可以看到她凸出的眼睛，她惊恐的脸。她再次沉入水中，他们再次在浑浊的水中寻找她，并再次将她拉了上来，她已经奄奄一息。整个仪式看起来像是酷刑，但她毫无抗议地忍受着，甚至可能是在狂喜中。

Reaching the riverside steps is not easy, because they are preceded by a thicket of narrow, airless, and dirty little streets tightly packed with beggars, who nudge the pilgrims importunately all the while raising a lament unbearably terrible and piercing. Finally, passing various passages and arcades, one emerges at the top of the stairs that descend straight down to the river. Although dawn has barely touched the sky, thousands of the faithful are already there. Some are animated, pushing their way who knows where and why. Others sit in the lotus position, stretching their arms up toward the heavens. The bottom rungs of the stairs are occupied by those performing the purification ritual—they wade in the river and now and then submerge themselves completely in the water. I see a family subjecting a stout grandmother to the purification rite. The grandmother doesn’t know how to swim and sinks at once to the bottom. The family rush in and bring her back up to the surface. The grandmother gulps as much air as she can, but the instant they let her go, she goes under again. I can see her bulging eyes, her terrified face. She sinks once more, they search for her again in the murky waters and again pull her out, barely alive. The whole ritual looks like torture, but she endures it without protest, perhaps even in ecstasy.

此时的恒河宽阔、辽阔、慵懒，河边伸展着一排排木柴堆，上面燃烧着数十、数百具尸体。好奇的人可以花几卢比乘船前往这个巨大的露天火葬场。半裸、满身烟灰的男人在这里忙碌，许多小男孩也是如此。他们用长杆调整柴堆以引导更好的气流，以便火葬可以更快地进行；尸体的队伍没有尽头，等待的时间很长。掘墓人将仍在发光的骨灰耙起，然后将它们推入河中。灰色的灰尘在波浪上漂浮了一段时间，但很快，它就被水浸透了，下沉并消失。

Beside the Ganges, which at this point is wide, expansive, and lazy, stretch rows of wooden pyres, on which are burning dozens, hundreds of corpses. The curious can for several rupees take a boat over to this gigantic open-air crematorium. Half-naked, soot-covered men bustle about here, as do many young boys. With long poles they adjust the pyres to direct a better draft so the cremation can proceed faster; the line of corpses has no end, the wait is long. The gravediggers rake the still-glowing ashes and push them into the river. The gray dust floats atop the waves for a while but very soon, saturated with water, it sinks and vanishes.

火车站

和宫殿

THE TRAIN STATION

AND THE PALACE

我如果在贝拿勒斯，人们找到了希望的理由——圣河的净化，随之而来的是一个人精神状况的改善，有望接近无限——加尔各答的西尔达站却产生了相反的效果。我乘火车从贝拿勒斯到达加尔各答，正如我所发现的，这是从相对的天堂到绝对的地狱的进步。贝拿勒斯车站的售票员打量了我一眼，问道：“你的床在哪里？”

If in Benares one finds cause for hope—a cleansing in the holy river, and with it the improvement of one’s spiritual condition, the promise of drawing closer to the infinite—Sealdah Station in Calcutta has the opposite effect. I arrived in Calcutta from Benares by train, a progress, as I was to discover, from a relative heaven to an absolute hell. The conductor at the Benares station looked me over and asked: “Where is your bed?”

我明白他在说什么，但显然看起来好像我不明白，过了一会儿，他重复了他的问题，这次更加坚持：

I understood what he was saying but apparently looked as if I didn’t, for a moment later he repeated his question, this time more insistently:

“你的床在哪儿？”

“Where is your bed?”

事实证明，即使是中等富裕的人，更不用说像欧洲人这样的特定种族的成员，也用自己的床在铁路上旅行。他们在仆人的陪同下到达车站，仆人头上顶着卷起的床垫，还有毯子、床单、枕头，当然还有其他行李。上了车（火车车厢里没有座位），仆人整理好主人的床铺，然后一言不发地消失了，仿佛消失在稀薄的空气中。尽管我是在兄弟情谊和个人平等的精神中长大的​​，但这种空手而行的情况而另一个人走在后面，手里拎着床垫、手提箱和一篮子食物，看起来极其无礼，引起了抗议和反对。但进入火车车厢后，我很快重新评估了自己的位置，因为人们明显惊讶的声音从四面八方响起。

It turns out that even the moderately wealthy, not to mention members of a chosen race like the European, travel the rails with their own beds. They arrive at the station accompanied by servants carrying rolled-up mattresses on their heads, as well as blankets, sheets, pillows, as well as, of course, other luggage. Once aboard (there are no seats in the train cars) the servant arranges his master’s bed, then vanishes without a word, as if dissolving into thin air. Raised as I was in the spirit of brotherhood and individual equality, this situation, in which one walks empty-handed while another walks behind, laden with a mattress, suitcases, and a basket of food, seemed offensive in the extreme, a cause for protest and objection. But upon entering the train car I quickly reevaluated my position, as voices of clearly astonished people resounded from every direction.

“你的床在哪儿？”

“Where is your bed?”

除了我的手提行李之外，我觉得自己很愚蠢。但我怎么知道除了门票之外我还需要一张床垫呢？即使我知道并买了床垫，我也无法自己搬动它，而必须雇用仆人。但以后我该怎么处置这个仆人呢？还是与床垫一起？

I felt idiotic to have nothing with me except my hand luggage. But how could I have known that I would need a mattress in addition to a ticket? And even if I had known, and had bought a mattress, I couldn’t have carried it by myself and would have had to engage a servant. But what would I have done with the servant later? Or with the mattress, for that matter?

我已经注意到，不同的人被分配在这里从事各种类型的活动和家务，并且这个人警惕地守护着他的角色和他的位置——这个社会的平衡似乎依赖于此。早上一个人端茶，另一个人擦鞋，另一个人仍然洗衬衫，另一个人打扫房间——如此循环往复。但愿我不会要求熨烫我衬衫的人在上面缝一颗纽扣。当然，对我来说，按照上述方式长大，自己缝上纽扣是很简单的事，但那样我就会犯一个可怕的错误，因为我会剥夺一个背负大家庭负担的人的权利。被迫靠在衬衫上缝纽扣谋生。这个社会是一个迂腐、精心编织的角色和任务、分类和目的的结构，需要大量的经验、渊博的知识和敏锐的直觉来洞察和破译其结构的微妙之处。

I had noticed already that a different person is assigned here to every type of activity and chore, and that this person vigilantly guards his role and his place—this society’s equilibrium seems to depend upon it. One person brings tea in the morning, another shines shoes, another still launders shirts, an altogether different one cleans the room—and so on ad infinitum. Heaven forbid that I ask the person who irons my shirt to sew a button on it. For me, of course, raised as I was in the manner foregoingly described, it would be simple just to sew on the button myself, but then I would be committing a terrible error, for I would be depriving someone burdened with a large family and obliged to make his living by sewing buttons on shirts of his livelihood. This society was a pedantically, meticulously woven fabric of roles and assignments, classifications and purposes, and a great deal of experience, a profound knowledge and a keen intuition were required to penetrate and decipher the delicacies of its structure.

我在火车上度过了一个不眠之夜，那些殖民时代的旧车摇晃着你，把你扔来扔去，隆隆作响，而你甚至还下着大雨，雨从关不上的窗户漏进来。当我们驶入西尔达车站时，天色阴沉。在巨大的航站楼的每一平方英寸上，在长长的站台上，在死胡同里，在附近的沼泽地里，坐着或躺着数以万计的憔悴的人——在倾盆大雨中、水里和泥里；正值雨季，热带倾盆大雨一刻也没有减弱。我立刻就被这些湿透的骷髅的简陋、它们的数量不计其数、也许最重要的是它们一动不动所震惊。它们似乎是这片凄凉景观中毫无生气的组成部分，其唯一的动力元素是从天空倾泻而下的水花。这些不幸者的完全被动当然有一定的逻辑和理性，尽管是绝望的：他们没有寻求庇护以躲避倾盆大雨，因为他们无处可去——这是他们的路的尽头——他们也没有努力遮盖。因为他们没有什么可以遮盖自己。

I passed a sleepless night on the train, for those old cars, dating back to colonial times, shook, hurled you about, rumbled, and you were even pelted with rain, which came in through windows that could not be shut. It was a gray, overcast day by the time we pulled into Sealdah Station. On every square inch of the enormous terminal, on its long platforms, its dead-end tracks, the swampy fields nearby, sat or lay tens of thousands of emaciated people—under streams of rain, in the water and the mud; it was the rainy season, and the heavy tropical downpour did not abate for a moment. I was struck at once by the poverty of these soaked skeletons, their untold numbers, and, perhaps most of all, their immobility. They seemed a lifeless component of this dismal landscape, whose sole kinetic element was the sheets of water pouring from the sky. There was of course a certain, albeit desperate, logic and rationality in the utter passivity of these unfortunates: they sought no shelter from the downpour because they had nowhere to go—this was the end of their road—and they made no exertion to cover themselves because they had nothing to cover themselves with.

他们是几年前结束的印度教徒和穆斯林之间内战的难民，这场战争见证了独立的印度和巴基斯坦的诞生，并导致数十万人，或许数百万人死亡，并造成数百万难民。后者徘徊了很长一段时间，找不到救援，只能听天由命，在西尔达站等地方过了一段时间的植物生活，最终死于饥饿或疾病。但还有更多的事情要做。这些战后流浪者一路上遇到了其他人——被印度强大而不受约束的河流从村庄和小镇上驱逐出来的洪水受害者军团。于是，数以百万计的无家可归、冷漠的人在路上拖着脚步，疲惫不堪地倒下，往往再也站不起来。其他人则试图到达城市，希望能在那里喝一口水，或许还能得到一把大米。

They were refugees from a civil war, which ended but a few years earlier, between Hindus and Muslims, a war which saw the birth of independent India and Pakistan and which resulted in hundreds of thousands, perhaps millions of dead and many millions of refugees. The latter wandered about for a long time, unable to find succor, left to their own fate, vegetating for a while in places like Sealdah Station before eventually dying there of hunger or disease. But there was more to this. These columns of postwar vagabonds encountered throngs of others along the way—the legions of flood victims evicted from villages and small towns by the waters of India’s powerful and unbridled rivers. And so millions of homeless, indifferent people shuffled along the roads, dropping from exhaustion, often never to rise. Others tried to reach the cities hoping to get a sip of water there, and perhaps a handful of rice.

•••

•   •   •

刚走出火车车厢就很困难——站台上没有足够的空间让我把脚放在上面。通常，不同颜色的皮肤在这里会引起注意；但没有什么可以让西尔达车站的居民分心，因为他们似乎已经融入了生活另一边的国度。我旁边的一位老妇人正在从纱丽的褶皱里挖出一点米。她把它倒进一个小碗里，开始四处寻找，也许是为了水，也许是为了火，这样她就可以煮米饭了。我注意到她附近有几个孩子正在盯着碗。凝视着——一动不动，一言不发。这持续了一会儿，而且这一刻还在拖延。孩子们不会把自己埋在稻米上；大米是老妇人的财产，这些孩子们被灌输了比饥饿更强大的东西。

Just getting out of the train car was difficult—there was no room for me to place my foot on the platform. Usually, a different color skin attracts attention here; but nothing distracts the denizens of Sealdah Station, as they seem already to settle into a realm on the other side of life. An old woman next to me was digging a bit of rice out of the folds of her sari. She poured it into a little bowl and started to look around, perhaps for water, perhaps for fire, so that she could boil the rice. I noticed several children near her, eyeing the bowl. Staring—motionless, wordless. This lasts a moment, and the moment drags on. The children do not throw themselves on the rice; the rice is the property of the old woman, and these children have been inculcated with something more powerful than hunger.

一名男子正从拥挤的人群中挤过。他推搡了老妇人，碗从她手里掉了下来，米饭散落在平台上，落在泥里，落在垃圾堆里。在那一瞬间，孩子们扑倒在地上，潜入那些还站着的人的腿之间，在淤泥中挖来挖去，试图找到米粒。老妇人空手站在那里，另一个男人推了她。老妇人、孩子们、火车站，所有的一切——都被无休无止的热带倾盆大雨浸透了。我也浑身湿淋淋地站着，不敢迈出一步；无论如何，我不知道该去哪里。

A man is pushing his way through the huddled multitudes. He jostles the old woman, the bowl drops from her hands, and the rice scatters onto the platform, into the mud, amidst the garbage. In that split second, the children throw themselves down, dive between the legs of those still standing, dig around in the muck trying to find the grains of rice. The old woman stands there empty-handed, another man shoves her. The old woman, the children, the train station, everything—soaked through by the unending torrents of a tropical downpour. And I too stand dripping wet, afraid to take a step; and anyway, I don’t know where to go.

我从加尔各答向南前往海得拉巴。南方与北方及其所有的痛苦有很大不同。南方似乎开朗、平静、昏昏欲睡，还有点乡土气息。当地王公的仆人肯定把我和其他人搞混了，因为他们在车站隆重地迎接我，然后直接开车送我到一座宫殿。一位彬彬有礼的老人欢迎了我，让我坐在一张宽大的皮扶手椅上，他肯定指望着比我原始的英语所能允许的更长、更深入的谈话。我结结巴巴地说了什么，感觉自己脸红了，汗水顺着额头流下来。老人慈祥的微笑，给了我一些勇气。一切都如梦似幻。超现实主义。仆人们把我带到了宫殿一侧的一间房间。作为王公的客人，我要住在这里。我想取消整件事，但不知道该怎么做——我无法用言语来解释其中存在一些误会。也许仅仅因为我来自欧洲就给这座宫殿带来了一些威望？我不知道。

From Calcutta I traveled south, to Hyderabad. The south was very different from the north and all its pains. The south seemed cheerful, calm, sleepy, and a little provincial. The servants of a local rajah must have confused me with someone else, because they greeted me ceremoniously at the station and drove me straight to a palace. A polite, elderly man welcomed me, sat me down in a wide leather armchair, and was surely counting on a longer and deeper conversation than my primitive English could allow. I stuttered something or other, felt myself turning red, sweat was pouring down my forehead. The elderly man smiled kindly, which gave me some courage. It was all rather dreamlike. Surrealistic. The servants led me to a room in one of the palace wings. As the guest of the rajah I was to live here. I wanted to call the whole thing off, but didn’t know how—I lacked the words with which to explain that there had been some misunderstanding. Perhaps just the fact of my being from Europe conferred some prestige on the palace? I don’t know.

我每天都坚持不懈地、狂热地死记硬背单词。天空中闪烁着什么？太阳。什么东西掉到了地球上？雨。是什么让树木摇晃？风。等等等等，每天二十到四十字。我读过海明威，在杜波依斯神父的书中，我试图理解有关种姓的章节。一开始其实并不困难：有四个种姓。第一个和最高的是婆罗门——祭司、精神之人、思想家、指明道路的人。第二个，较低的，是刹帝利——战士和统治者，刀剑和政治的人民。第三种，更低，是吠舍——商人、工匠和农民。第四个也是最后一个种姓是首陀罗——劳工、农民、仆人、雇佣工人。这就是问题开始的地方，因为事实证明，这些种姓分为数百个子种姓，而这些子种姓又分为数十个子种姓，依此类推，无穷无尽。印度充满了无限——神和神话、信仰和语言、种族和文化的无限；一切事物、所见之处，都充满着令人眼花缭乱的无穷无尽。

I crammed vocabulary words daily, doggedly, feverishly. What shone in the sky? The sun. What fell on the earth? The rain. What swayed the trees? The wind. Etc., etc., twenty to forty words daily. I read Hemingway, and in the book by Father Dubois I tried to make sense of the chapter on castes. The beginning actually wasn’t difficult: There are four castes. The first and highest are the Brahmans—priests, people of the spirit, thinkers, those who show the way. The second, lower down, are the Kshatriyas—warriors and rulers, people of the sword and of politics. The third, lower still, are the Vaisyas—merchants, craftsmen, and farmers. The fourth and final caste are the Sudras—laborers, peasants, servants, workers for hire. Here’s where the problems started, because it turns out that these castes are divided into hundreds of sub-castes, and these in turn into dozens of sub-subcastes, and so on into infinity. India is all about infinity—an infinity of gods and myths, beliefs and languages, races and cultures; in everything, and everywhere one looks, there is this dizzying endlessness.

与此同时，我本能地感觉到，我所感知到的周围一切都只是外在的迹象、图像、符号，代表着一个巨大而多样的世界，其中隐藏着我一无所知的信仰和想法。我也想知道这个领域是否是无法进入的因为我缺乏这方面的理论和书本知识，或者是否有更深刻的原因，即我的思想过于充满理性主义和唯物主义，无法识别和掌握像印度教这样充满灵性和形而上学的文化。

At the same time I felt instinctively that that which I perceived all around me were merely external signs, images, symbols, of a vast and varied world of hidden beliefs, ideas about which I knew nothing. I wondered, too, whether this realm was inaccessible to me because I lacked theoretical, book knowledge about it, or whether there was a more profound reason, namely that my mind was too fully imbued with rationalism and materialism to be able to identify with and grasp a culture as saturated with spirituality and metaphysics as Hinduism.

在这种状态下，我在法国传教士的著作中发现的丰富细节进一步让我不知所措，我会放下书本进城。

In such a state, and further overwhelmed by the richness of the details I found in the work of the French missionary, I would put down the book and go into town.

拉贾的宫殿——所有的阳台都装着玻璃，大约有一百个，当窗玻璃打开时，微风徐徐地吹进房间——周围是郁郁葱葱、精心照料的花园，花园里的园丁们不断地忙碌着，修剪、割草和耙地。再往前走，越过高墙，城市就开始了。人们沿着狭窄而拥挤的小街小巷走到那里，经过无数色彩缤纷的商店、摊位和出售食品、衣服、鞋子、清洁产品的摊位。即使不下雨，街道也总是泥泞不堪，因为所有的垃圾都被倒在了这里的街道中央——这条街不属于任何人。

The rajah’s palace—all glassed-in verandahs, maybe a hundred of them, which when the panes were opened allowed a light and bracing breeze to waft through the rooms—was surrounded by lush, well-tended gardens, in which gardeners constantly bustled, pruning, mowing, and raking. Further on, beyond a high wall, the city began. One walked there along little streets and alleyways, narrow and always crowded, passing countless colorful shops, stalls, and stands selling food, clothing, shoes, cleaning products. Even when it wasn’t raining, the streets were always muddy, because all waste gets poured into the middle of the street here—the street belongs to no one.

到处都有扬声器，从扬声器中发出刺耳、响亮、连续的歌声。它来自当地的寺庙。这些都是小型建筑，通常不比周围的一层或两层房屋大，但数量众多。他们看起来很相似：漆成白色，穿着花环和闪闪发光的装饰品，明亮而喜庆，就像参加婚礼的新娘一样。这些小寺庙里的气氛不知何故既宁静又欢乐。他们挤满了人，窃窃私语，烧香，翻白眼，伸出双手。有些人（圣器管理员？祭坛男孩？）向信徒分发食物——一块蛋糕、杏仁糖或糖果。如果一个把手伸得长一点，可以收到两份，甚至三份。人们必须吃掉得到的东西或将其放在祭坛上。每座寺庙的入场都是免费的：没有人问你是谁，或者信仰什么信仰。每个人单独进行礼拜，没有集体仪式，因此气氛轻松、自由，甚至有点混乱。

There are speakers everywhere, and emanating from them a piercing, loud, continuous singing. It’s coming from the local temples. These are small structures, often no larger than the one-or two-story houses surrounding them, but they are numerous. They look alike: painted white, dressed in garlands of flowers and glittering decorations, bright and festive like brides going to their wedding. The atmosphere in these little temples is somehow at once serene and joyful. They are full of people, whispering amongst themselves, burning incense, rolling their eyes, stretching out their hands. Some men (sacristans? altar boys?) distribute food to the faithful—a piece of cake, marzipan, or candy. If one holds out one’s hand a little longer, one can receive two, maybe even three portions. One must eat what one gets or place it on the altar. Admission to each temple is free: no one asks who you are, or of what faith. Everyone worships individually, on his own, without a collective rite, and as a result there is an atmosphere of ease, freedom, even a bit of disorder.

这些礼拜场所之所以如此之多，是因为印度教的神灵数量无穷无尽。没有人能够做一份完整的清单。此外，诸神之间并不相互竞争，而是和谐共处。人们可以同时相信一种或几种，甚至可以根据地点、时间、心情或需要将一种信仰换成另一种信仰。任何特定神的追随者的最终世俗野心都是建立一个专门的圣所，建造一座寺庙。考虑到这种自由多神论已经持续了数千年，人们可以想象这一点的物质后果。多年来建立了多少寺庙、教堂、祭坛和雕像，又有多少被洪水、火灾、台风、与穆斯林的战争摧毁。如果曾经建造的所有这些都仍然存在，那么它们肯定会同时覆盖地球的一半表面。

There are so many of these places of worship because the deities in Hinduism are infinite in number; no one has been able to make a complete inventory. Furthermore, the deities do not compete with one another, but rather coexist harmoniously and peacefully. One can believe in one or in several at once, even exchange one for another depending on place, time, mood, or need. The ultimate worldly ambition of any given deity’s followers is to erect a dedicated sanctuary, to build a temple. One can imagine the material consequences of this, bearing in mind that this liberal polytheism has lasted thousands of years already. How many temples, chapels, altars, and statues have been raised over the years, and how many have been destroyed by floods, fires, typhoons, wars with Muslims. If all the ones ever constructed were still to exist, simultaneously, they would surely cover half the surface of the globe.

在我的漫游中，我偶然发现了卡利神庙。她是毁灭女神，代表着时间的毁灭性运作。我不知道她能否得到安抚，因为毕竟时间无法停止。卡莉身材高大，肤色黝黑，伸出舌头，戴着一条骷髅项链，张开双臂站着。她是个女人，但在她怀里最好不要摔倒。

In my wanderings I happened upon the temple of Kali. She is the goddess of destruction and represents the ruinous workings of time. I do not know if she can be propitiated, because after all one cannot stop time. Kali is tall, black, sticks out her tongue, wears a necklace of skulls, and stands with her arms outstretched. She is a woman, but into her embrace it is better not to fall.

通往寺庙的路中间有两排摊位，出售刺鼻的气味、色彩缤纷的粉末、图画、吊坠和各种俗气的小玩意。一群满头大汗、兴奋的人们缓缓地排成一队，蜿蜒走向女神雕像。在 - 的里面圣所，空气中弥漫着浓烈的焚香、炎热和黑暗的气味。在雕像前进行象征性的交换——你给牧师一块之前购买的鹅卵石，他又递回另一块。我想你会离开未奉献的那一位，而接受那一位被祝福的。但我不确定。

The way to the temple leads between two rows of stalls selling pungent scents, colorful powders, pictures, pendants, all manner of kitschy bric-a-brac. A dense, slowly moving line of perspiring, excited people snakes its way to the goddess’s statue. Inside the sanctuary, an overpowering airless odor of incense, heat, darkness. A symbolic exchange takes place before the statue—you give the priest a previously purchased pebble, and he hands back another one. I suppose you leave the unconsecrated one and receive one that’s been blessed. But I’m not certain.

王公的宫殿里挤满了仆人。你真的看不到其他人，就好像整个庄园都被他们绝对统治了。无数的管家、男仆、侍者、女仆和男仆、泡茶和糖霜蛋糕的专家、熨烫员和送信员、灭蚊子和蜘蛛的人，以及许多其他难以理解的职责和角色，不断地在房间和沙龙中穿梭。沿着走廊和楼梯经过，掸去地毯和家具的灰尘，敲打枕头，摆好扶手椅，剪花、浇花。

The palace of the rajah is full of servants. You see no one else, really, and it’s as if the entire estate had been given over to their absolute rule. Countless butlers, footmen, waiters, maids and valets, specialists in brewing tea and frosting cakes, clothes pressers and messengers, exterminators of mosquitoes and spiders, and many more whose duties and roles it is impossible to fathom, course continually through the rooms and salons, pass by along the corridors and on the stairs, dusting rugs and furniture, beating pillows, arranging armchairs, cutting and watering the flowers.

他们的动作都沉默、流畅、小心翼翼，给人一种略带恐惧的印象。但没有明显的紧张，没有跑动或打手势。就好像一只孟加拉虎在这附近的某个地方盘旋；唯一的机会就是不要做出突然的动作。即使是白天，在耀眼的阳光下，仆人们也像无名的影子，一言不发地走来走去，总是保持在边缘，小心翼翼地不引起任何人的目光，更不与任何人交叉。 。

All of them move about in silence, fluidly, cautiously, giving a slightly fearful impression. But there is no visible nervousness, no running about or gesticulating. It’s as if a Bengal tiger were circling around here somewhere; one’s only chance is to make no sudden movements. Even during the day, in the glare of the shining sun, the servants resemble anonymous shadows, moving about without uttering a word, always in such a way as to remain on the periphery, careful not to catch anyone’s gaze, let alone cross anyone’s path.

根据职能和等级的不同，他们的着装也各不相同：从镶有宝石的金色头巾到简单的缠腰布（等级制度最底层的人在臀部佩戴的带子）。有些人穿着丝绸、绣花腰带和闪闪发光的肩章，而另一些人则穿着普通衬衫和白色长袍。他们有一个共同点：都是赤脚。即使他们装饰着刺绣、流苏、锦缎和羊绒，脚上什么也没穿。

They are variously dressed, according to function and rank: from golden turbans pinned with precious stones to simple dhotis—bands worn around the hips by those at the bottom of the hierarchy. Some are attired in silks, embroidered belts, and glittering epaulettes, while others wear ordinary shirts and white caftans. They have one thing in common: all are barefoot. Even if they are adorned with embroideries and tassels, brocades and cashmeres, they have nothing on their feet.

由于个人经验，我立即注意到了这个细节。它始于战争期间，在德国占领下。我记得1942年的冬天快到了，我没有鞋子。我的旧鞋已经坏了，我母亲没有钱买一双新鞋。提供给波兰人的鞋子售价为 400 兹罗提，鞋面采用厚牛仔布制成，涂有黑色防水膏，鞋底采用浅色椴木制成。哪里可以拿到 400 兹罗提？

I noticed this detail right away, owing to personal experience. It started during the war, under German occupation. I remember that the winter of 1942 was approaching and I had no shoes. My old ones had fallen apart, and my mother had no money for a new pair. The shoes available to Poles cost 400 złoty, had tops of a thick denim coated with a black, water-repellent paste and soles made of a pale linden wood. Where could one get 400 złoty?

当时我们住在华沙的克罗赫马尔纳街，靠近贫民窟大门的斯库皮夫斯基一家的公寓里。斯库皮耶夫斯基先生经营着一家小型家庭手工业，生产绿色浴室肥皂。 “我会给你一些寄售金条，”他说。 “等你卖掉四百块，就足够你买鞋了，战后你就可以还我了。”当时人们仍然相信战争很快就会结束。他建议我沿着华沙-奥特沃茨克铁路线工作，那里是度假旅客经常光顾的地方。他建议，度假者会想通过购买一块肥皂来犒劳一下自己。我听了他的话。我当时十岁，当时我哭了一生中一半的眼泪，因为事实上没有人愿意买这些小肥皂。在步行一整天的时间里，我什么也卖不掉——或者可能一根也卖不掉。有一次我卖掉了三个，满脸通红满脸幸福地回到家。

We were living in Warsaw then, on Krochmalna Street, near the gate to the ghetto, in the apartment of the Skupiewskis. Mr. Skupiewski had a little cottage industry making bars of green bathroom soap. “I will give you some bars on consignment,” he said. “When you sell four hundred, you will have enough for your shoes, and you can pay me back after the war.” People then still believed that the war would end soon. He advised me to work along the route of the Warsaw–Otwock railway line, frequented by holiday travelers; vacationers will want to pamper themselves a little, he counseled, by buying a bar of soap. I listened to him. I was ten years old, and I cried half the tears of a lifetime then, because in fact no one wanted to buy the little soaps. In a whole day of walking I would sell none—or maybe a single bar. Once I sold three and returned home bright red with happiness.

按下门铃后我就开始热切地祷告：神啊，求你让他们买点东西吧，至少让他们买一个！我实际上是在进行一种乞讨，试图引起怜悯。我会走进一间公寓说：女士，请从我这里买一块肥皂。它只花费一兹罗提，冬天快到了，我没有鞋子。这有时有效，但并不总是有效，因为还有许多其他孩子也在试图以某种方式克服——偷东西、骗人、贩卖这个或那个。

After pressing the buzzer I would start to pray fervently: God, please have them buy something, have them buy at least one! I was actually engaged in a form of begging, trying to arouse pity. I would enter an apartment and say: Please, madam, buy a soap from me. It costs only one złoty, winter is coming and I have no shoes. This worked sometimes, but not always, because there were many other children also trying to get over somehow—by stealing something, swindling someone, trafficking in this or that.

秋天到了，寒意袭来，脚底寒气逼人，痛得我不得不停止卖东西。我有 300 兹罗提，但 Skupiewski 先生慷慨地又投入了 100 兹罗提。我和妈妈一起去买鞋。如果用一块法兰绒包住腿，并在上面绑上报纸，即使在严冬的严寒中也可以穿着它们。

Cold autumn weather arrived, the cold nipped at the soles of my feet, and because of the pain I had to stop selling. I had 300 złoty, but Mr. Skupiewski generously threw in another hundred. I went with my mother to buy the shoes. If one wrapped one’s leg with a piece of flannel and tied newspaper on top of that, one could wear them even in the worst frosts of winter.

我回到了德里，我的返程机票随时都会到达。我找到了以前住的酒店，甚至还住了和以前一样的房间。我探索了这座城市，参观了博物馆，尝试阅读《印度时报》，研究了希罗多德。我不知道希罗多德是否到达了印度；考虑到当时通过这样一段话的难度，这似乎极不可能，尽管我们不能明确排除这种可能性。毕竟，他认识的地方离希腊很远！他确实描述了当时地球上最强大的国家波斯的二十个省份，称为总督省，而印度是其中人口最多的省份。他断言，印度人……是迄今为止已知世界上人数最多的民族，然后谈到了印度、它的地理位置、社会和习俗。印度人生活在亚洲，比其他任何人都更靠东，比任何其他有可靠信息的已知民族都更靠东，因为在他们之外，印度东部是沙地，因此不适合居住。印第安部落数量众多，而且他们并不都讲同一种语言。有些（但不是全部）是游牧民族。有些人住在 河边形成的沼泽里，吃从甘蔗船捕获的生鱼……。这些 沼泽印第安人穿着灯心草制成的衣服；首先，他们砍下这种植物，从河里收集起来，然后像编织篮子一样编织它，并像胸甲一样佩戴它。

I returned to Delhi, where my return ticket home was due to arrive any day. I found my old hotel, and even got the same room as before. I explored the city, went to museums, tried to read the Times of India, studied Herodotus. I do not know whether Herodotus reached India; given the difficulties of such a passage at that time, it seems highly unlikely, although one cannot rule it out definitively. After all, he came to know places so very far from Greece! He did describe twenty provinces, called satrapies, of what was then the greatest power on earth, Persia, and India was the most populous of those. Indians … are by far the most numerous people in the known world, he asserts, and then talks of India, its location, society, and customs. The Indians live further east in Asia than anyone else—further east than any other known people about whom there is reliable information—because beyond them the eastern part of India is sandy and therefore uninhabitable. There is a large number of Indian tribes, and they do not all speak the same language. Some, but not all, are nomadic; some live in marshes formed by the river and eat raw fish which they catch from cane boats…. These marsh Indians wear clothes made out of rushes; first they cut the plant down and gather it from the river, and then they weave it as one would a basket and wear it like a breastplate.

另一个印第安部落，称为 Padaei，居住在这些沼泽印第安人的东边……据说有以下习俗。如果他们的任何同胞——无论是男人还是女人——生病了，他最亲密的男性朋友（假设是一个生病的男人）就会杀死他，理由是如果他因病而消瘦，他的肉体就会变得虚弱。 被宠坏了。他否认自己生病了，但他们没有注意到，杀了他，并举行了盛宴。当女性生病时，女性最亲密的女性朋友也会遵循完全相同的程序。他们祭祀并吃掉任何年老的人，但这样做的情况很少见，因为他们会杀死所有年老之前生病的人。

Another tribe of Indians, called the Padaei, who live to the east of these marsh Indians, … are said to have the following customs. If any of their compatriots—a man or a woman—is ill, his closest male friends (assuming that it is a man who is ill) kill him, on the grounds that if he wasted away in illness his flesh would become spoiled. He denies that he is ill, but they take no notice, kill him, and have a feast. Exactly the same procedure is followed by a woman’s closest female friends when it is a woman who is ill. They sacrifice and eat anyone who reaches old age, but it is unusual for anyone to do so, because they kill everyone who falls ill before reaching old age.

然而，还有一个印第安部落，却有不同的习惯：他们不杀生，不种植庄稼，也不建造房屋。他们吃蔬菜，有种子……他们收集、烹饪……然后吃。如果他们中的任何一个生病了，他都会去某个偏僻的地方躺下，没有人关心他是死是病。

There is another Indian tribe, however, with different habits: they do not kill any living thing or grow crops, nor is it their practice to have houses. They eat vegetables, and there is a seed … which they collect, cook… and eat. If any of them falls ill, he goes and lies down in some remote spot, and no one cares whether he is dead or ill.

我所描述的所有印第安部落都像群居动物一样在公共场合发生性行为。此外，他们的肤色几乎和埃塞俄比亚人一样黑。他们射入女性体内的精液与她们的皮肤一样黑，而不是像其他男性那样白；埃塞俄比亚人射精的精液也是如此。

All the Indian tribes I have described have sexual intercourse in public, as herd animals do. Also, they are almost as black in colour as Ethiopians. The semen they ejaculate into their women is as black as their skin, not white like that of other men; the same goes for the semen Ethiopians ejaculate too.

后来我去了马德拉斯、班加罗尔、孟买和昌迪加尔。随着时间的推移，我越来越确信我所做的事情是令人沮丧和绝望的，我不可能了解和理解我所处的国家。印度是如此巨大。一个人如何才能描述一种在我看来是没有边界或终结的东西呢？

Later I traveled to Madras and Bangalore, to Bombay and Chandigarh. In time I grew convinced of the depressing hopelessness of what I had undertaken, of the impossibility of knowing and understanding the country in which I found myself. India was so immense. How can one describe something that is—and so it seemed to me—without boundaries or end?

我收到了一张从德里经喀布尔和莫斯科飞往华沙的往返机票。我在太阳落山时降落在喀布尔。深粉色、近乎紫色的天空将最后的阳光投射到山谷周围深蓝色的山脉上。天色渐渐暗下来，陷入了彻底的、深沉的寂静之中——那是一片风景、一片地区、一片世界的寂静，驴脖子上的铃铛和羊群路过的美妙的拍打声都无法打扰。机场军营。

I received a return ticket from Delhi to Warsaw via Kabul and Moscow. I landed in Kabul just as the sun was setting.
