Practically speaking, I should have left Peking the day after I arrived. But my newspaper was mum—fearful and fighting for its survival, it had forgotten about me. Or perhaps the editors had my interests in mind—perhaps they reckoned that away in China I would somehow be safe? In any event, I now think that the editors of Chungkuo were being informed by the Chinese embassy in Warsaw that the correspondent of Sztandar Młodych is the envoy of a newspaper hanging by a thread and it is only a matter of time before it goes under the ax. I think, too, that it was traditional Chinese principles of hospitality, the importance the Chinese ascribe to saving face, as well as their highly cultivated politeness, that kept me from being summarily expelled. Instead, they created conditions which they assumed would lead me to guess that the models of cooperation that had been agreed to earlier no longer obtained. And that I would say of my own accord: I am leaving.



世界道路上的记忆

MEMORY ALONG THE ROADWAYS

OF THE WORLD

我回国后我立即离开了报纸并在波兰通讯社找到了一份工作。因为我是从中国来的，所以我的新老板米哈乌·霍夫曼（Michał Hofman）得出结论，我的专长一定在于远东事务，并决定这将是我现在的工作重点——具体来说，是印度以东的亚洲部分，并延伸到印度东部。太平洋上无数的岛屿。

Immediately upon returning home I left the newspaper and got a job at the Polish Press Agency. Because I had arrived from China, my new boss, Michał Hofman, concluded my expertise must lie in matters of the Far East and decided that this would now be my beat—specifically, the part of Asia to the east of India and extending to the innumerable islands of the Pacific.

我们对一切都了解一点，但我对我被指派的国家一无所知，所以我熬夜研究缅甸和马来西亚丛林中的游击战，苏门答腊岛和苏拉威西岛的叛乱，菲律宾的摩洛部落。这个世界再次以一种无法理解的方式呈现在我面前，更不用说掌握了。更重要的是，考虑到我的工作，我投入的时间很少。一整天，来自不同国家的急件到达我的办公室，我必须阅读、翻译、压缩、编辑，然后发送到报纸和广播电台。

We all know a little about everything, but I knew nothing about the countries I had been assigned, and so I burned the midnight oil studying up on guerrilla warfare in the jungles of Burma and Malaysia, the revolts in Sumatra and Sulawesi, the rebellions of the Moro tribe in the Philippines. The world once again presented itself to me as something impossible to even begin to comprehend, let alone master. And all the more so because, given my work, I had so little time to devote to it. All day long, dispatches arrived in my office from various countries, which I had to read, translate, condense, edit, and send on to newspapers and radio stations.

就这样，因为我每天都会从仰光、新加坡、河内、马尼拉或万隆等地收到消息，所以我在亚洲各国的旅行——从印度和阿富汗开始，到日本和中国——不间断地进行着。在我桌子的玻璃下，有一张战前的亚洲地图我经常用手指在这片大陆上徘徊，寻找金边或泗水、所罗门群岛或难以定位的拉瓦格，这些地方刚刚发生了针对重要人物的政变企图，或者橡胶厂的工人在那里种植园刚刚举行罢工。我时而思绪万千，时而思绪万千，试图想象那些场景和事件。

In this manner, because news reached me daily from places like Rangoon or Singapore, Hanoi, Manila, or Bandung, my travels through the countries of Asia—commenced in India and Afghanistan, continued in Japan and China—went on uninterrupted. On my desk, under glass, I had a prewar map of the Asian continent, over which I often wandered with my finger, searching for Phnom Penh or Surabaya, the Solomon Islands or the difficult-to-locate Laoag, places where there had just been a coup attempt against Someone Important, or where the workers at a rubber plantation had just gone on strike. I transported myself in my thoughts now here, now there, trying to imagine those locales and events.

有时，当晚上办公室空无一人，走廊变得安静时，我想暂时摆脱有关罢工和武装冲突、政变和爆炸震撼我不知道的国家的电报，我就会伸手去翻阅《希罗多德的历史》 ，我的抽屉。

Sometimes, when the offices emptied in the evening and the hallways grew quiet, and I wanted a respite from telegrams about the strikes and armed conflicts, the coups and explosions convulsing countries I did not know, I reached for The Histories of Herodotus, lying in my drawer.

希罗多德在他的书一开始就解释了他为什么要写这本书：

Herodotus begins his book with a statement explaining why he set out to write it in the first place:

以下是哈利卡纳苏斯的希罗多德的调查结果。目的是防止人类事件的痕迹被时间抹去，并保存希腊人和非希腊人所取得的重要和卓越成就的声誉；其中所涉及的问题尤其包括希腊人和非希腊人之间敌对行动的原因。

Here are presented the results of the enquiry carried out by Herodotus of Halicarnassus. The purpose is to prevent the traces of human events from being erased by time, and to preserve the fame of the important and remarkable achievements produced by both Greeks and non-Greeks; among the matters covered is, in particular, the cause of the hostilities between Greeks and non-Greeks.

这段话是全书的关键。

This passage is the key to the entire book.

首先，希罗多德在其中告诉我们，他进行了某种“调查”（我更喜欢使用“调查”一词）。今天我们知道他为此奉献了一生——在当时来说，这确实是一个漫长的一生。他为什么这么做？为何年纪轻轻的他就做出这样的决定？有人鼓励他进行这些调查吗？委托他来承担这些任务？或者希罗多德是否为某个君主、长老会或神谕服务？谁需要这种情报？又是为了什么？

First of all, Herodotus informs us therein that he carried out some sort of “enquiry” (I would prefer to use the term “investigation”). Today we know that he devoted his entire life to this—and it was, for its time, a long life indeed. Why did he do it? Why, still in his youth, did he make such a decision? Did someone encourage him to conduct these investigations? Commission him to undertake them? Or did Herodotus enter the service of some potentate, or of a council of elders, or of an oracle? Who needed this intelligence? And what for?

或者也许他所做的一切都是出于自己的主动性，出于对知识的热情，受到一种焦躁不安、注意力不集中的冲动的驱使？也许他有一颗天生好奇的心，一颗不断产生一千个问题的心，让他不得安宁，让他彻夜难眠？如果他被这种绝对的私人狂热所困扰——毕竟这是众所周知的事情——他是如何找到时间来满足它的，年复一年？

Or maybe he did everything on his own initiative, possessed by a passion for knowledge, driven by a restless and unfocused compulsion? Perhaps he had a naturally inquiring mind, a mind that continuously generated a thousand questions giving him no peace, keeping him up at nights? And if he was gripped by such an absolute private mania—which after all has been known to happen—how did he find the time to satisfy it, year after year after year?

希罗多德承认他沉迷于记忆，并对其感到恐惧。他觉得记忆是有缺陷的、脆弱的、无常的——甚至是虚幻的。无论它包含什么，无论它存储什么，都可以蒸发，消失得无影无踪。他这一代人，当时生活在地球上的每个人，都被同样的恐惧所困扰。没有记忆，人就无法生存，因为正是记忆使人超越了野兽，决定了人类灵魂的轮廓。但它同时又是那么的不可靠、难以捉摸、阴险。这正是人对自己如此缺乏自信的原因。等等，那不是……？拜托，你还记得，那是什么时候……？难道不是那个……？试着回忆一下，怎么样……？我们不知道，超越“我们不知道”的是广阔的无知境界；换句话说——不存在。

Herodotus admits that he was obsessed with memory, fearful on its behalf. He felt that memory is something defective, fragile, impermanent—illusory, even. That whatever it contains, whatever it is storing, can evaporate, simply vanish without a trace. His whole generation, everyone living on earth at that time, was possessed by that same fear. Without memory one cannot live, for it is what elevates man above beasts, determines the contours of the human soul; and yet it is at the same time so unreliable, elusive, treacherous. It is precisely what makes man so unsure of himself. Wait, wasn’t that …? Come on, you can remember, when was that…? Wasn’t it the one that…? Try to remember, how was it …? We do not know, and stretching beyond that “we do not know” is the vast realm of ignorance; in other words—of nonexistence.

如今，人类不再像以前那样痴迷于记忆，因为他生活在大量记忆的包围之中。一切都在他的指尖——百科全书、教科书、词典、纲要、搜索引擎。图书馆和博物馆、古书店和档案馆。音频和视频记录。在公寓、仓库、地下室、阁楼里，保存着无限量的文字、声音和图像。如果他是个孩子，他的老师会告诉他需要知道的一切；如果他是大学生，他的教授会通知他。

Man does not obsess about memory today as he once did because he lives surrounded by stockpiles of it. Everything is at his fingertips—encyclopedias, textbooks, dictionaries, compendia, search engines. Libraries and museums, antiquarian bookshops and archives. Audio and video recordings. Infinite supplies of preserved words, sounds, images—in apartments, in warehouses, in basements, in attics. If he is a child, his teacher will tell him everything he needs to know; if he is a university student, he will be informed by his professors.

当然，这些机构、设备或设备都没有，或者几乎没有。技术在希罗多德时代就已存在。人所知道的一切，也只能是他头脑所能保存的一切。一些特权人士开始学习在纸莎草卷和泥板上书写。但剩下的呢？文化始终是一项贵族事业。只要它偏离了这一原则，它就会灭亡。

Of course none, or almost none, of these institutions, devices, or techniques existed in Herodotus’s time. Man knew as much, and only as much, as his mind managed to preserve. A few privileged individuals started to learn to write on rolls of papyrus and on clay tablets. But the rest? Culture was always an aristocratic enterprise. And wherever it departs from this principle, it perishes as such.

在希罗多德的世界中，唯一真正的记忆库是个人。为了找出所记忆的事物，必须接触到这个人。如果他住得很远，就必须去找他，出发去旅行。在最终遇到他之后，我们必须坐下来听听他要说的话——倾听、记住，也许写下来。这就是报告文学的开始；它就是在这样的情况下诞生的。

In the world of Herodotus, the only real repository of memory is the individual. In order to find out that which has been remembered, one must reach this person. If he lives far away, one has to go to him, to set out on a journey. And after finally encountering him, one must sit down and listen to what he has to say—to listen, remember, perhaps write it down. That is how reportage begins; of such circumstances it is born.

因此希罗多德环游世界，结识人们，聆听他们告诉他的事情。他们讲述自己是谁，讲述自己的历史。但他们怎么知道自己是谁、来自哪里呢？啊，他们回答说，他们是从别人那里得到的——首先也是最重要的，是从他们的祖先那里得到的。正是他们将自己的知识传授给了这一代人，就像这一代人现在正在将其传授给其他人一样。这些知识以各种故事的形式出现。人们围坐在火边讲故事。后来，这些将被称为传说和神话，但在它们第一次被讲述和听到的那一刻，讲述者和听众都相信它们是最神圣的真理，绝对的现实。

So Herodotus wanders the world, meets people, listens to what they tell him. They speak of who they are, they recount their history. But how do they know who they are, and where they came from? Ah, they answer, they have it on the word of others—first and foremost, from their ancestors. It is they who transmitted their knowledge to this generation, just as this one is now transmitting it to others. The knowledge takes the form of various tales. People sit around the fire and tell stories. Later, these will be called legends and myths, but in the instant when they are first being related and heard, the tellers and the listeners believe in them as the holiest of truths, absolute reality.

他们听着，火燃烧起来，有人添加了更多的木头，火焰重新温暖起来，加速了思考，唤醒了想象力。如果附近没有噼啪作响的火光，或者没有油灯或蜡烛照亮的房子的黑暗，故事的编排几乎是不可想象的。火光吸引、团结、激发注意力。火焰和社区。火焰和历史。火焰和记忆。赫拉克利特，生活在在希罗多德之前，认为火是一切物质的起源，是原始物质。他说，就像火一样，一切都在永恒的运动中，一切都会熄灭，只会再次燃烧。一切都在流动，但在流动中，它经历了转变。记忆也是如此。它的一些图像消失了，但新的图像出现了。新的产品与之前的产品并不相同——它们是不同的。正如一个人不能两次踏入同一条河流一样，一幅新图像也不可能与前一幅图像完全相同。

They listen, the fire burns, someone adds more wood, the flames’ renewed warmth quickens thought, awakens the imagination. The spinning of tales is almost unimaginable without a fire crackling somewhere nearby, or without the darkness of a house illuminated by an oil lamp or a candle. The fire’s light attracts, unites, galvanizes attentions. The flame and community. The flame and history. The flame and memory. Heraclitus, who lived before Herodotus, considered fire to be the origin of all matter, the primordial substance. Like fire, he said, everything is in eternal motion, everything is extinguished only to flare up again. Everything flows, but in flowing, it undergoes transformation. So it is with memory. Some of its images die out, but new ones appear in their place. The new ones are not identical to those that came before—they are different. Just as one cannot step twice into the same river, so it is impossible for a new image to be exactly like an earlier one.

希罗多德完全理解这种不可逆转的消逝原理，他想让自己对抗它的破坏力：防止人类事件的痕迹被时间抹去。

It is this principle of an irreversible passing away that Herodotus understands perfectly, and he wants to set himself in opposition to its destructive power: to prevent the traces of human events from being erased by time.

尽管如此，多么大胆，多么自负和使命感：不让人类事件的痕迹被时间抹去。人类事件！但他怎么知道“人类事件”这样的事情存在呢？他的前任荷马描述了一场单一的、具体的战争的历史，即特洛伊战争，以及孤独的流浪者奥德修斯的冒险经历。但人类事件呢？这个词本身就代表了一种新的思维方式、新的概念、新的视野。通过这句话，希罗多德向我们揭示了他自己绝不是一个外省的抄写员，一个对自己的小城邦心胸狭隘的情人，仅仅是当时希腊组成的数十个城邦之一的爱国者。不！从一开始，《历史》的作者就以一位世界范围的远见卓识者、一位能够涵盖全球范围的想象力——简而言之，作为第一位全球主义者的身份登上舞台。

Still, what boldness, what a sense of self-importance and mission: presuming to prevent the traces of human events from being erased by time. Human events! But how did he know that any such thing as “human events” even exists? His predecessor, Homer, described the history of a single, specific war, the one with Troy, and then the adventures of a solitary wanderer, Odysseus. But human events? That term in itself represents a new way of thinking, a new concept, a new horizon. With that sentence, Herodotus reveals himself to us as anything but a provincial scribe, a narrow-minded lover of his own little polis, mere patriot of one of the dozens of city-states of which Greece was then composed. No! From the very outset, the author of The Histories enters the stage as a visionary on a world scale, an imagination capable of encompassing planetary dimensions—in short, as the first globalist.

当然，希罗多德面前的世界地图，或者他想象的世界地图，与我们今天所面临的世界地图不同。他的世界比我们的小得多。它的中心由周围的山区和（当时）森林地区组成。爱琴海。位于西海岸的地区是希腊；那些在东部的波斯。因此，我们立即触及了问题的核心——希罗多德出生、长大，当他开始弄清楚周围的一切时，他的第一个观察结果就是世界被分裂，分裂为东方和西方，并且这些一半处于分歧、冲突和战争状态。

Of course, the map of the world which Herodotus has before him, or which he imagines, differs from the one confronting us today. His world is much smaller than ours. Its center consists of the mountainous and (at the time) forested lands around the Aegean Sea. Those lying on the western shore constitute Greece; those on the eastern, Persia. And so right away we hit upon the heart of the matter—Herodotus is born, grows up, and just as he starts to figure out everything around him, one of his very first observations is that the world is sundered, split into East and West, and that these halves are in a state of dissension, conflict, war.

他以及任何有思想的人立即想到的问题是“为什么会这样？”正是这个问题为希罗多德的杰作的前言提供了信息：以下是希罗多德对哈利卡纳苏斯进行的调查的结果……。特别是敌对行动的原因……

The question that immediately suggests itself to him, as well as to any thinking human being, is “Why should this be so?” And it is this very question that informs the foreword of Herodotus’s masterpiece: Here are presented the results of the enquiry carried out by Herodotus of Halicarnassus…. in particular, the cause of the hostilities …

恰恰。我们可以看到，这个自有历史以来就经常被重复出现的问题已经困扰了人类数千年：人们为什么要互相发动战争？战争的根源是什么？人们发动战争时希望达到什么目的？是什么驱使他们？他们怎么想？他们的目标是什么？一连串无休无止的问题！希罗多德毕生孜孜不倦地致力于寻找答案。但他从众多问题中，有些是相当笼统和抽象的，选择了最具体的来研究，那些发生在他眼前的事件，或者那些记忆仍然鲜活的，或者即使稍微褪色，仍然非常可用的事件。 。换句话说，他的注意力和探究集中在以下主题上：为什么希腊（即欧洲）要与波斯（即亚洲）发动战争？为什么西方（欧洲）和东方（亚洲）这两个世界会互相争斗，并且殊死一战？一直都是这样吗？会一直这样吗？

Precisely. We can see that this question, oft repeated since the dawn of history, has vexed humankind for thousands of years now: Why do peoples wage war against one another? What are the origins of wars? What do people hope to accomplish when they start a war? What drives them? What do they think? What is their goal? An unending litany of questions! Herodotus dedicates his life, diligently, tirelessly, to finding the answers. But from among the many issues, some quite general and abstract, he selects the most concrete to investigate, the events that took place before his very eyes or of which memories were still fresh and alive or, even if slightly faded, still very much available. In other words, he concentrates his attention and his inquiries on the following subject: Why does Greece (that is, Europe) wage war with Persia (that is, with Asia)? Why do those two worlds—the West (Europe) and the East (Asia)—fight against each other, and do so to the death? Was it always thus? Will it always be thus?

他对这个话题非常感兴趣。事实上，他全神贯注、全神贯注、贪得无厌。我们可以想象像他这样的人被一种无法让他平静的想法所占据。被激活，无法安静地坐着，不断地从一个地方移动到另一个地方。无论他在哪里似乎有一种焦躁和不安的气氛。那些不喜欢走出家门或走出自家后院的人——他们中的大多数人随时随地都在——把希罗多德这种从根本上与任何人或任何事无关的人视为怪胎、狂热分子，甚至疯子。

He is profoundly intrigued by this subject; indeed he is preoccupied, absorbed, insatiable. We can imagine a man like him possessed by an idea that gives him no peace. Activated, unable to sit still, moving constantly from one place to another. Wherever he appears there is an atmosphere of agitation and anxiety. People who dislike budging from their homes or walking beyond their own backyards—and they are always and everywhere in the majority—treat Herodotus’s sort, fundamentally unconnected to anyone or anything, as freaks, fanatics, lunatics even.

难道希罗多德的同时代人就是这样看待他的吗？他本人对此只字不提。他连这种事都在意吗？他忙于旅行、准备旅行，然后选择和整理带回家的材料。毕竟，旅程既不是在出发的那一刻开始，也不是在再次到达家门口时结束。它开始得更早，而且永远不会结束，因为在我们的身体处于静止状态很久之后，记忆的电影仍在我们体内运行。事实上，确实存在类似旅行传染的现象，而且这种疾病本质上是无法治愈的。

Could it be that Herodotus is regarded in just this way by his contemporaries? He says nothing about this himself. Did he even pay attention to such things? He was occupied with his travels, with the preparations for them and then with the selection and organization of the materials he brought home. A journey, after all, neither begins in the instant we set out, nor ends when we have reached our doorstep once again. It starts much earlier and is really never over, because the film of memory continues running on inside of us long after we have come to a physical standstill. Indeed, there exists something like a contagion of travel, and the disease is essentially incurable.

我们不知道希罗多德以什么身份旅行。作为一名商人（黎凡特人众所周知的职业）？可能不会，因为他对价格、商品、市场不感兴趣。作为外交官？当时这个职业还不存在。当间谍？但对于哪个州呢？作为游客？不，游客旅行是为了休息，而希罗多德则是在路上努力工作——他是一名记者、人类学家、民族志学家、历史学家。同时，他也是一个典型的流浪者，或者像他这样的人后来在中世纪的欧洲被称为朝圣者。但他的这种漫游并不是流浪汉、无忧无虑地从一处到另一处的旅程。希罗多德的旅程是有目的的——他希望通过这些旅程了解世界及其居民，收集他后来感到有必要描述的知识。最重要的是，他希望描述的是希腊人和非希腊人所取得的重要而卓越的成就。

We do not know in what guise Herodotus traveled. As a merchant (the proverbial occupation of people of the Levant)? Probably not, since he had no interest in prices, goods, markets. As a diplomat? That profession did not exist yet. As a spy? But for which state? As a tourist? No, tourists travel to rest, whereas Herodotus works hard on the road—he is a reporter, an anthropologist, an ethnographer, a historian. And he is at the same time a typical wanderer, or, as others like him will later be called in medieval Europe, a pilgrim. But this wandering of his is no picaresque, carefree passage from one place to another. Herodotus’s journeys are purposeful—they are the means by which he hopes to learn about the world and its inhabitants, to gather the knowledge he will feel compelled, later, to describe. Above all, what he hopes to describe are the important and remarkable achievements produced by both Greeks and non-Greeks.

这就是他的初衷。但随着每一次新的探险，世界在他的身上不断扩大、成倍增加、占据巨大比例。原来，埃及之外还有利比亚，利比亚之外还有埃塞俄比亚人的土地，换句话说，就是非洲；向东，穿越广阔的波斯（需要三个多月的快速步行），到达高耸入云、难以到达的巴比伦，再往前到达印第安人的故乡，其外部边界在谁知道呢？在哪里;地中海确实向西延伸很远，一直延伸到阿比拉和赫拉克勒斯之柱，他们说，除此之外，还有另一片海；北部还有海洋和草原，以及居住着无数斯基泰人的森林。

That is his original intent. But with each new expedition the world expands on him, multiplies, assumes enormous proportions. It turns out that beyond Egypt there is still Libya, and beyond that the land of the Ethiopians, in other words, Africa; that to the East, after traversing the expanses of Persia (which requires more than three months of rapid walking), one arrives at the towering and inaccessible Babylon, and beyond that at the homeland of the Indians, the outer boundaries of which lie who knows where; that to the West the Mediterranean Sea stretches far indeed, to Abyla and the Pillars of Herakles, and beyond that, they say, there is still another sea; and there are also seas and steppes to the north, and forests inhabited by countless Scythian peoples.

米利都（小亚细亚一座美丽的城市）的阿那克西曼德早于希罗多德绘制了第一张世界地图。根据他的说法，地球的形状像一个圆柱体。人们居住在它的上表面。它被诸天包围，悬浮在空中，与所有天体的距离相等。那个时代还出现了各种其他地图。最常见的情况是，地球被描绘成一个扁平的椭圆形盾牌，四面被大河俄刻阿诺斯的水域包围。俄刻阿诺斯不仅环绕着整个世界，也滋养着地球上所有的其他河流。

Anaximander of Miletus (a beautiful city in Asia Minor), who predated Herodotus, created the first map of the world. According to him, the earth is shaped like a cylinder. People live on its upper surface. It is surrounded by the heavens and floats suspended in the air, at an equal distance from all the heavenly bodies. Various other maps come into being in that epoch. Most frequently, the earth is represented as a flat, oval shield surrounded on all sides by the waters of the great river Oceanus. Oceanus not only bounds all the world, but also feeds all the earth’s other rivers.

这个世界的中心是爱琴海及其海岸和岛屿。希罗多德从那里组织他的探险活动。他越接近天涯海角，就越频繁地遇到新的事物。他是第一个发现世界多元文化本质的人。第一个主张每种文化都需要接受和理解，要理解它，必须首先了解它。文化之间有何不同？最重要的是他们的习俗。告诉我你的穿着、你的行为方式、你的习惯是什么、你崇拜哪些神——我就会告诉你你是谁。人不仅创造文化、居住文化，而且将文化随身携带——人就是文化。

The center of this world was the Aegean Sea, its shores and islands. Herodotus organizes his expeditions from there. The further he moves toward the ends of the earth, the more frequently he encounters something new. He is the first to discover the world’s multicultural nature. The first to argue that each culture requires acceptance and understanding, and that to understand it, one must first come to know it. How do cultures differ from one another? Above all in their customs. Tell me how you dress, how you act, what are your habits, which gods you honor—and I will tell you who you are. Man not only creates culture, inhabits it, he carries it around within him—man is culture.

•••

•   •   •

希罗多德对世界了解甚多，但他并不了解世界的一切。他从未听说过中国或日本，不知道澳大利亚或大洋洲，对美洲的存在一无所知，更不用说美洲的繁荣。说实话，他对西欧和北欧知之甚少。希罗多德的世界是地中海-近东地区；这是一个阳光明媚的世界，有海洋和湖泊、高山和绿色山谷、橄榄和葡萄酒、羔羊和谷物田——一个每隔几年就会充满鲜血的明亮的阿卡迪亚。

Herodotus, who knows a lot about the world, nevertheless does not know everything about it. He never heard of China or Japan, did not know of Australia or Oceania, had no inkling of the existence, much less of the great flowering, of the Americas. If truth be told, he knew little of note about western and northern Europe. Herodotus’s world is Mediterranean-Near Eastern; it is a sunny world of seas and lakes, tall mountains and green valleys, olives and wine, lambs and fields of grain—a bright Arcadia which every few years overflows with blood.



克罗索斯的幸福与不幸

THE HAPPINESS AND

UNHAPPINESS OF CROESUS

S为了寻找对他来说最重要的问题的答案，即东西方之间的冲突起源于何处，以及为什么存在这种敌意，希罗多德非常谨慎地行事。他并不声称自己理解。相反，他总是躲在阴影里，让别人来说话。在这种情况下，其他人是博学的波斯人。希罗多德说，这些博学的波斯人认为，世界范围的东西方冲突的煽动者既不是希腊人，也不是波斯人，而是第三种民族，即漂泊的商人腓尼基人。正是他们首先开始了绑架妇女的行径，进而引发了这场全球性的风暴。

Seeking an answer to the question most important to him, namely, where did the conflict between East and West originate, and why does this hostility exist, Herodotus proceeds with great caution. He does not lay claim to understanding. On the contrary, he keeps to the shadows and has others do the talking. The others are, in this case, the learned Persians. These learned Persians, Herodotus says, maintain that the instigators of the worldwide East-West conflict are neither the Greeks nor the Persians, but a third people, the Phoenicians, peripatetic merchants. It was they who first began the business of kidnapping women, which in turn triggered this global storm.

事实上，腓尼基人在希腊阿尔戈斯港绑架了一位名叫艾欧的国王的女儿，并用船将她带到了埃及。后来，几个希腊人登陆腓尼基城市提尔并绑架了该城国王的女儿欧罗巴。还有其他希腊人从科尔基斯国王那里绑架了他的女儿美狄亚。特洛伊的帕里斯抓住了希腊国王墨涅拉俄斯的妻子海伦，并将她带到了特洛伊。为了复仇，希腊人入侵特洛伊。一场伟大的战争爆发了，这场战争的历史因荷马的史诗而永垂不朽。

Indeed, the Phoenicians kidnap in the Greek port of Argos a king’s daughter called Io and take her by ship to Egypt. Later, several Greeks land in the Phoenician city of Tyre and abduct Europa, the daughter of its king. Still other Greeks kidnap from the king of Colchis his daughter, Medea. Paris of Troy, in turn, seizes Helen, wife of the Greek king Menelaus, and carries her off to Troy. In revenge, the Greeks invade Troy. A great war breaks out, whose history is immortalized by Homer.

希罗多德转述波斯智者的评论：虽然波斯人认为绑架妇女是一种犯罪行为，但他们也声称为此而激动并为妇女报仇是愚蠢的 一旦他们被绑架；他们说，明智的做法是不去关注它，因为很明显，这些妇女一定是自愿参与了她们自己的绑架，否则这件事永远不会发生。作为证据，他引用了腓尼基人介绍的希腊公主艾欧的例子：腓尼基人说他们不必通过绑架将她带到埃及。据他们说，她在阿尔戈斯与船长睡了，当她发现自己怀孕时，她无法面对父母，因此自愿与腓尼基人一起航行，以免被发现。

Herodotus paraphrases the commentary of Persian wise men: Although the Persians regard the abduction of women as a criminal act, they also claim that it is stupid to get worked up about it and to seek revenge for the women once they have been abducted; the sensible course, they say, is to pay no attention to it, because it is obvious that the women must have been willing participants in their own abduction, or else it could never have happened. And as proof he cites the case of the Greek princess Io, as the Phoenicians present it: The Phoenicians say that they did not have to resort to kidnapping to take her to Egypt. According to them, she slept with the ship’s captain in Argos, and when she discovered that she was pregnant, she could not face her parents, and therefore sailed away willingly with the Phoenicians, to avoid being found out.

为什么希罗多德要以波斯圣人所说的针锋相对的绑架年轻女性这样的小事来开始他对世界的伟大描述呢？因为他尊重叙事市场的法则：为了卖得好，故事必须有趣，必须包含一点香料，一些耸人听闻的东西，一些让人不寒而栗的东西。对绑架妇女事件的描述满足了这些要求。

Why does Herodotus begin his great description of the world with what is, according to the Persian sages, a trivial matter of tit-for-tat kidnappings of young women? Because he respects the laws of the narrative marketplace: to sell well, a story must be interesting, must contain of bit of spice, something sensational, something to send a shiver up one’s spine. Accounts of the abductions of women satisfy these requirements.

希罗多德生活在两个时代的交汇处：虽然书面历史时代已经开始，但口头传统仍然占主导地位。因此，希罗多德的生活和工作节奏很可能是这样的：他长途跋涉，回来后游历了希腊各个城市，组织了类似文学晚会的活动，在过程中他讲述了这些经历。他在旅行期间收集的印象和观察。他完全有可能靠这种聚会谋生，并且他也以这种方式资助他随后的旅行，因此对他来说拥有尽可能大的礼堂以吸引人群非常重要。因此，从一些能吸引注意力、激发好奇心的事情开始，对他来说是有利的——一些有点耸人听闻的事情。他的整部作品中都充满了令人感动、惊奇的故事情节。如果没有这样的刺激，他的观众就会早早就散去，感到无聊，留下他一个空钱包。

Herodotus lived at the juncture of two epochs: although the era of written history was beginning, the oral tradition still predominated. It is possible, therefore, that the rhythm of Herodotus’s life and work was as follows: he made a long journey, and upon his return traveled to various Greek cities and organized something akin to literary evenings, in the course of which he recounted the experiences, impressions, and observations he had gathered during his peregrinations. It is entirely likely that he made his living from such gatherings, and that he also financed his subsequent trips in this way, and so it was important to him to have the largest auditorium possible, to draw a crowd. It would be to his advantage, therefore, to begin with something that would rivet attention, arouse curiosity—something a tad sensational. Story plots meant to move, amaze, astonish pop up throughout his entire opus; without such stimuli, his audience would have dispersed early, bored, leaving him with an empty purse.

但关于绑架妇女的报道不仅仅是廉价的煽情、挑衅和辛辣的故事情节。在这里，希罗多德在他的研究一开始就试图制定他的第一条历史定律。他在这里的野心源于他在旅途中收集了来自不同时代和地点的大量材料，并希望确定和定义某种秩序原则，以强加于这些看似混乱且未经分类的事实集合。是否有可能得出这样的原则？是的，希罗多德回答道。这一原则是对“谁……首先实施了侵略犯罪行为”这一问题的答案。牢记这个关于优先顺序的问题，可以更容易地应对历史错综复杂的曲折，向我们自己解释是什么力量和事件推动了历史。

But the accounts of the abductions of women weren’t merely cheap sensationalism, provocative and piquant story lines. Here already, at the very start of his investigations, Herodotus tries to formulate his first law of history. His ambition here stems from his having gathered on his journeys an abundance of material from various epochs and places and wanting to determine and define some principle of order to impose upon this seemingly chaotic and unsorted collection of facts. Is it even possible to arrive at such a principle? Yes, Herodotus replied. That principle is the answer to the question “who … first undertook criminal acts of aggression.” Having this question as to precedence in mind makes it easier to negotiate the tangled and intricate twists and turns of history, to explain to ourselves what forces and events set it in motion.

对这一原则的定义，对它的认识，是非常重要的，因为在希罗多德的世界（以及当今的各个社会），永恒的复仇法则，以眼还眼的报复法则，曾经是（并且在今天的各个社会中）。仍然）活得很好。复仇不仅是一项权利，更是一项最神圣的义务。谁不履行这个职责，谁就会受到他的家庭、他的氏族、他的社会的咒骂。寻求报复的必要性不仅压在我这个受冤屈的部落成员身上。众神也必须服从它的命令，甚至非个人和永恒的命运也必须服从。

The defining of this principle, the awareness of it, is hugely significant, because in Herodotus’s world (as well as in various societies today), the eternal law of revenge, the law of reprisal, of an eye for an eye, was (and remains) alive and well. Revenge is not only a right—it is a most sacred obligation. Whoever does not fulfill this charge will be cursed by his family, his clan, his society. The necessity of seeking retribution weighs not only on me, the member of the wronged tribe. The gods, too, must submit to its imperatives, and so too must even impersonal and timeless Fate.

复仇有什么作用？对它的恐惧，对它不可避免的恐惧，应该足以阻止任何人做出损害他人的不光彩行为。它应该发挥刹车的作用，抑制理性的声音。然而，如果事实证明这是一种无效的威慑，而有人实施了犯罪行为，那么犯罪者就会被视为启动了一系列的报应，这种报应可能会延续几代人，甚至几个世纪。

What function does vengeance serve? Fear of it, dread in the face of its inescapability, should be enough to stop anyone from committing a dishonorable act that is damaging to another. It should function as a brake, a restraining voice of reason. If, however, it turns out to be an ineffectual deterrent, and someone commits an offense, the perpetrator will be seen to have set into motion a chain of retribution that can stretch for generations, for centuries even.

复仇机制中有一种沉闷的宿命论。一些不可避免且不可逆转的事情。不幸突然降临你跌倒了，你无法理解为什么。发生了什么？简单来说就是：你因十代人前所犯下的罪行而遭到了一位你甚至不知道其存在的祖先的报复。

There is a kind of dreary fatalism in the mechanism of revenge. Something inevitable and irreversible. Misfortune suddenly be falls you and you cannot fathom why. What happened? Simply this: that you have been revenged upon for crimes perpetrated ten generations ago by a forefather whose existence you weren’t even aware of.

希罗多德的第二条定律不仅适用于历史，也适用于人类生活，那就是人类的幸福永远不会长期停留在同一个地方。我们的希腊人通过描述吕底亚国王克罗伊索斯戏剧性的、影响深远的命运来证明这个定理，他的故事类似于圣经中的约伯的故事，克罗伊索斯也许是约伯的原型。

The second law of Herodotus, pertaining not only to history but also to human life, is that human happiness never remains long in the same place. And our Greek proves this theorem by describing the dramatic, affecting fortunes of the king of the Lydians, Croesus, whose story resembles that of the biblical Job, for whom Croesus was perhaps the prototype.

他的王国吕底亚是位于希腊和波斯之间的一个强大的亚洲国家。克洛伊索斯在他的宫殿中积累了巨大的财富，堆积如山的金银，使他闻名于世，并愿意向游客展示。这场演出发生在公元前六世纪中叶，比希罗多德出生几十年。

Lydia, his kingdom, was a powerful Asiatic state situated between Greece and Persia. Croesus accumulated great riches in his palaces, entire mountains of gold and silver for which he was renowned in the world and which he willingly displayed to visitors. This show took place in the middle of the sixth century B.C.E., several decades before the birth of Herodotus.

当时在世的每一位博学的希腊人偶尔都会访问吕底亚的首府萨迪斯，其中包括雅典的梭伦（他是一位诗人，雅典民主的创造者，并以其智慧而闻名）。克洛伊索斯亲自接待了梭伦，并命令他的仆人向他展示他的财宝，并且确信这些财宝使他的客人感到惊讶，他问他：“所以我真的想问你是否遇到过比所有人都幸福的人别的？ ”

The capital of Lydia, Sardis, was visited on occasion by every learned Greek who was alive at the time, including Solon of Athens (he was a poet, a creator of Athenian democracy, and famed for his wisdom). Croesus personally received Solon and ordered his servants to show him his treasures, and, certain that the sight of them astonished his guest, he queried him: “So I really want to ask you whether you have ever come across anyone who is happier than everyone else?”

在问这个问题时，他期待着被称为最幸福的男人。

In asking this question, he was expecting to be named as the happiest of all men.

但梭伦并没有丝毫奉承他，而是将几位英勇牺牲的雅典人称为最幸福的人，并补充道：“克罗伊索斯，当你向我询问人类及其事务时，你是在向一个非常了解如何做事的人提出你的问题。神是多么的嫉妒，以及它如何可能使我们感到困惑。任何一个人活得长久，必然会看到和忍受许多他宁愿避免的事情。我把一个人的生命的极限设在 七十年了。七十年就是 25,200 天……没有哪两天会发生完全相同的事件。由此可见，克洛伊索斯，人的生命完全是一个偶然的问题……。

But Solon did not flatter him in the least and instead cited as the happiest of men several heroically fallen Athenians, adding: “Croesus, when you asked me about men and their affairs, you were putting your question to someone who is well aware of how utterly jealous the divine is, and how it is likely to confound us. Anyone who lives for a long time is bound to see and endure many things he would rather avoid. I place the limit of a man’s life at seventy years. Seventy years makes 25,200 days … No two days bring events which are exactly the same. It follows, Croesus, that human life is entirely a matter of chance….

“现在，我可以看到你非常富有，你统治着很多人，但在我发现你死得很好之前，我无法说出你要求我对你说的话……在[一个人]死之前，你最好不要称他为幸福，而称他为幸运。

“Now, I can see that you are extremely rich and that you rule over large numbers of people, but I won’t be in a position to say what you’re asking me to say about you until I find out that you died well…. Until [a man] is dead, you had better refrain from calling him happy, and just call him fortunate.

“......有必要考虑任何事情的结局......并看看结果如何，因为神经常为人类提供繁荣，但随后彻底摧毁他们。”

“… It is necessary to consider the end of anything … and to see how it will turn out, because the god often offers prosperity to men, but then destroys them utterly and completely.”

事实上，索伦离开后，众神的惩罚残酷地降临在克洛伊索斯身上，很可能正是因为他认为自己是地球上最幸福的人。克洛伊索斯有两个儿子——身材魁梧的阿提斯和另一个又聋又哑的儿子。阿蒂斯是他父亲的掌上明珠，受到保护和注视。然而，尽管如此，克罗伊索斯的一位客人，一位阿德拉斯托斯，在一次狩猎中杀死了他，这并非故意，而纯粹是偶然。当阿德拉图斯意识到自己做了什么时，他崩溃了。在阿提斯的葬礼上，他等到所有人都离开了，坟墓周围变得安静下来后，意识到在他的经历中没有人比自己承受的不幸更重，他在墓地结束了自己的生命。

And in fact, after Solon’s departure, the punishment of the gods descended brutally upon Croesus, in all likelihood precisely because he thought himself the happiest man on earth. Croesus had two sons—the strapping Atys and another, who was deaf and dumb. Atys was the apple of his father’s eye, protected and watched over. And yet, despite this, not on purpose but purely by accident, a guest of Croesus, one Adrastus, killed him during a hunt. When Adrastus realized what he had done, he broke down. During Atys’s funeral, he waited until everyone had left and it grew quiet around the tomb, and then, realizing that there was no one in his experience who bore a heavier burden of misfortune than himself, he took his own life at the graveside.

儿子死后，克洛伊索斯在悲痛中度过了两年。在此期间，伟大的居鲁士在邻国波斯掌权，在他的领导下，波斯人的实力迅速增强。克洛伊索斯担心，如果居鲁士的国家继续积聚力量，有一天可能会威胁到莉迪亚，因此他制定了一项先发制人的计划。

After his son’s death, Croesus lives for two years in profound grief. During this time, the great Cyrus comes to power in neighboring Persia, and under him the might of the Persians increases rapidly. Croesus is worried that if Cyrus’s nation continues to gather strength it could one day threaten Lydia, and so he hatches a plan for a preemptive strike.

当时的习俗是，有钱有势的人在做出重要决定之前先咨询神谕。希腊有很多这样的神谕，但最重要的是位于德尔斐高耸山腰上的一座寺庙。为了得到根据神谕的有利预言，人们必须用礼物来安抚德尔斐神灵。因此，克洛伊索斯订购了大量的祭品。三千头牛将被宰杀，沉重的金条将被熔化，无数的银器将被锻造出来。他命令点燃大火，烧毁金银沙发、紫色斗篷和外衣。他还告诉所有吕底亚人，他们每个人都要尽其所能地做出牺牲。我们可以想象，数量众多、谦卑顺从的吕底亚人，沿着道路走向燃烧的大柴堆，把迄今为止对他们来说最珍贵的东西——金首饰、各种祭祀器皿和家用器皿——扔进火里，节日服装，甚至是最喜欢的日常服装。

It is the custom at the time for the wealthy and powerful to consult an oracle before making important decisions. Greece abounds in these oracles, but the most important resides in a temple on a towering mountainside—in Delphi. In order to obtain a favorable prophecy from the oracle, one must propitiate the Delphic deity with gifts. Croesus, therefore, orders a gigantic collection of offerings. Three thousand cattle are to be killed, heavy bars of gold melted, countless objects forged out of silver. He commands that a huge fire be lit, on which he burns in sacrifice gold and silver couches, purple cloaks and tunics. He also told all the Lydians that every one of them was to sacrifice whatever he could. We can imagine the numerous and humbly obedient Lydian people as they make their way along the roads to where the great pyre is burning and throw into the flames what until now was most precious to them—gold jewelry, all manner of sacral and domestic vessels, holiday vestments, even favorite everyday attire.

神谕所传达的意见通常是谨慎含糊和故意模糊的声明。这些文本的编写方式是为了让神谕能够在事情结果相反的情况下（这种情况经常发生）巧妙地从整个事件中撤退并保全面子。然而，揭开明天的面纱的愿望的力量却如此不减、坚不可摧，以至于人们带着数千年的顽固，仍然贪婪地、面红耳赤地聆听着占卜者的话语。正如人们所看到的，克洛伊索斯也受到了这种欲望的束缚。他不耐烦地等待着他派往各个希腊神谕处的使者归来。德尔斐神谕的答案是：如果你出击波斯人，你将摧毁一个伟大的国家。克洛伊索斯渴望这场战争，但被侵略欲望蒙蔽了双眼，他将这个预言解释为：如果你出击波斯，你就会摧毁它。毕竟，波斯确实是一个伟大的国家，克洛伊索斯在这一点上是正确的。

The opinions which the oracle delivers are typically pronouncements of cautious ambiguity and intentional murkiness. They are texts so composed as to allow the oracle, in the event that things turned out otherwise (which occurred often), to retreat adroitly from the whole affair and save face. And yet so undiminished and indestructible is the force of the desire to have the veil lifted on tomorrow that people, with a stubbornness lasting thousands of years already, still listen greedily and with flushed cheeks to the utterances of soothsayers. Croesus, as one can see, was also in that desire’s thrall. Impatiently he awaited the return of the envoys he had sent to the various Greek oracles. The answer of the Delphic oracle was: If you set out against the Persians, you will destroy a great state. And Croesus, who desired this war, blinded by the lust of aggression, interpreted the prediction to mean: If you set out against Persia, you will destroy it. Persia, after all—and in this Croesus was correct—was truly a great state.

于是他发动了进攻，但他输掉了战争，结果——按照预言——消灭了他自己的伟大国家，自己也沦为奴隶。波斯人将他们的俘虏带到了居鲁士那里，居鲁士建造了一个巨大的火葬柴堆，并制造了克洛伊索斯（被绑起来）和十四名吕底亚人。 男孩们爬到了山顶。也许他打算将它们作为对某个神或其他神的胜利祭品，或者也许他想履行他所发的誓言，或者也许他听说克洛伊索斯是一个敬畏神的人，所以他让他登上了神殿。因为他想看看是否有仙人能救他免于被活活烧死……。尽管克洛伊索斯在柴堆上的处境十分绝望，但他的思绪却转向梭伦所说的“活着的人都不会幸福”，他意识到梭伦说出这句话是多么神圣的启发。这个念头让他叹息一声，打破了长久的沉默，重复了三遍“索伦”这个名字。

So he attacked, but he lost the war, and as a result—and in accordance with the prophecy—annihilated his own great state and was himself enslaved. The Persians took their prisoner to Cyrus, who built a huge funeral pyre and made Croesus (who was tied up) and fourteen Lydian boys climb up to the top. Perhaps he intended them to be a victory-offering for some god or other, or perhaps he wanted to fulfil a vow he had made, or perhaps he had heard that Croesus was a god-fearing man and he made him get up on to the pyre because he wanted to see if any immortal would rescue him from being burnt alive …. Although Croesus’s situation up on top of the pyre was desperate, his mind turned to Solon’s saying that no one who is still alive is happy, and it occurred to him how divinely inspired Solon had been to say that. This thought made him sigh and groan, and he broke a long silence by repeating the name “Solon” three times.

现在，应站在柴堆附近的居鲁士的要求，翻译人员询问克洛伊索斯他在呼唤谁，这是什么意思。克洛伊索斯回答了，但当他讲述这个故事时，已经点燃的柴堆开始在其最远的边缘认真燃烧。居鲁士被怜悯所感动，但也担心遭到报复，他改变了决定，下令尽快扑灭大火，并杀死了克洛伊索斯和随行的男孩们。但所有控制火势的尝试都失败了。

Now, at the request of Cyrus, who is standing near the pyre, the interpreters ask Croesus whom he is calling and what does it mean. Croesus answers, but as he is telling the story, the pyre, which has already been lit, starts to burn in earnest at its farthest edges. Cyrus, moved by pity but also fearing retribution, reverses his decision and orders the fire extinguished as quickly as possible and Croesus and the boys accompanying him brought down. But all attempts to control the blaze fail.

克洛伊索斯意识到居鲁士改变了主意。当他看到尽管大家都努力扑灭火势但已经来不及控制火势时，他向阿波罗求助……。他哭泣着呼唤神明，突然间，晴朗的天气被乌云密布所取代。暴风雨袭来，大雨倾盆而下，柴堆被扑灭了。

Croesus realized that Cyrus had changed his mind. When he saw that it was too late for them to control the fire, despite everyone’s efforts to quench it, he called on Apollo …. Weeping, he called on the god, and suddenly the clear, calm weather was replaced by gathering clouds; a storm broke, rain lashed down, and the pyre was extinguished.

……一旦[居鲁士]把克洛伊索斯从柴堆上救下来，他就问他是谁说服他入侵他的国家并成为他的敌人而不是他的朋友。 “大人，”克洛伊索斯回答道，“这是我干的。你从中得到了，我也从中失去了。但责任在于鼓励我向你们发动战争的希腊神。毕竟，没有人会愚蠢到宁愿选择战争也不愿选择和平。在和平时期，儿子埋葬他们的父亲；在战争时期，父亲埋葬他们的儿子。不过，我想上帝一定希望这一切发生。”……

… Once [Cyrus] had got Croesus down from the pyre he asked him who had persuaded him to invade his country and be his enemy rather than his friend. “My lord,” Croesus replied, “it was my doing. You have gained and I have lost from it. But responsibility lies with the god of the Greeks who encouraged me to make war on you. After all, no one is stupid enough to prefer war to peace; in peace sons bury their fathers and in war fathers bury their sons. However, I suppose the god must have wanted this to happen.”…

赛勒斯解开了他，让他坐在附近。他对他印象非常深刻，他和他的随行人员都很钦佩这个人的风度。但克洛伊索斯却沉默不语，陷入了沉思。

Cyrus untied him and had him seated near by. He was very impressed with him, and he and his whole entourage admired the man’s demeanour. But Croesus was silent, deep in thought.

于是，当时亚洲最强大的两位统治者——战败的克洛伊索斯和胜利的居鲁士——并排坐着，看着刚刚他们中的一个人正要在柴堆上焚烧另一个人的残骸。我们可以想象，一小时前还在可怕的折磨中等待死亡的克洛伊索斯仍然处于震惊之中，当居鲁士问他能为他做什么时，他开始抱怨众神：“主人，”克洛伊索斯回答说， “没有什么比被允许将我的这些脚镣送给希腊之神更让我高兴的了，我比任何其他神都更尊敬他，并询问他欺骗他的恩人是否是他的正常做法。”

And so two of Asia’s then mightiest rulers—the defeated Croesus and the victorious Cyrus—sit side by side, looking at the remnants of the pyre upon which just a while ago one of them was going to immolate the other. We can imagine that Croesus, who only one hour earlier was awaiting death in dreadful torment, is still in shock, and when Cyrus asks him what he could do for him, he starts to rail against the gods: “Master,” Croesus replies, “nothing would give me more pleasure than to be allowed to send these shackles of mine to the god of the Greeks, whom I revered more than any other god, and to ask him if it is his normal practice to trick his benefactors.”

多么亵渎啊！更重要的是，克洛伊索斯在得到居鲁士的许可后，派了一支吕底亚人代表团前往德尔斐。他让他们把脚镣放在神庙的门槛上，问这位神明用他的神谕鼓励克洛伊索斯向波斯人进军是否感到羞耻……他们还要问希腊诸神通常是否如此忘恩负义。

What sacrilege! What is more, Croesus, having received Cyrus’s permission, sent a delegation of Lydians to Delphi. He told them to lay the shackles on the threshold of the temple and ask the god if he was not ashamed to have used his oracles to encourage Croesus to march against the Persians … And they were also to ask whether Greek gods were normally so ungrateful.

据说德尔斐的皮提亚对此作出了回答，这句话构成了希罗多德的第三条定律：

To which the Delphic Pythia was said to reply with the sentence that will constitute the third law of Herodotus:

“即使是神也无法逃脱他注定的命运。克洛伊索斯已经为他的祖先四代前所犯下的罪行付出了代价，他的祖先虽然是赫拉克勒斯家族的私人卫队成员，但却屈服于一个女人的诡计，杀死了他的主人，并占据了一个根本不属于他的地位。事实上，阿波罗希望萨迪斯的陷落发生在克洛伊索斯的儿子们的时代，而不是克洛伊索斯本人的时代，但不可能改变命运……”

“Not even a god can escape his ordained fate. Croesus has paid for the crime of his ancestor four generations ago, who, though a member of the personal guard of the Heraclidae, gave in to a woman’s guile, killed his master, and assumed a station which was not rightfully his at all. In fact, Apollo wanted the fall of Sardis to happen in the time of Croesus’ sons rather than of Croesus himself, but it was not possible to divert the Fates ….”

这是皮提亚人对吕底亚人的回应。他们……将声明转达给了克洛伊索斯。当他听到这句话时，他意识到错在他，而不是神。

This was the Pythia’s response to the Lydians. They … relayed the statement to Croesus. When he heard it he realized that the fault was his and not the god’s.

战斗结束

THE BATTLE’S END

我我以为我已经听到了关于克洛伊索斯的一切，在我看来，他实际上是一个很有同情心的人——一开始他对全世界都钦佩的宝藏（所有那些成吨的金银）表现出天真而毫不掩饰的自豪感。填满他的金库），然后，他对德尔菲神谕的预言也坚定不移的信念；他对儿子的死深感绝望，而儿子的死是他间接造成的；国家遭受惨痛损失后，他精神崩溃；他对烈士死在柴堆上的冷漠默许；他亵渎地否认神圣的裁决；最后，他需要为一个他甚至不认识的祖先的罪孽付出昂贵的赎罪代价。是的，我以为我已经彻底告别了这个受到惩罚、羞辱的人，突然他又出现在希罗多德的书页中，再次与居鲁士国王在一起，居鲁士国王率领波斯军队，开始征服马萨格泰人，这是一个生活在中亚深处、一直在阿姆河沿岸的野蛮好战的民族。

I thought that I had heard all I was going to hear regarding Croesus, who had actually come to seem quite sympathetically human to me—at first in his naïve and unconcealed pride in the treasures that the whole world admired (all those tons of gold and silver filling his vaults), and then, as well, in his unwavering faith in the prophecies of the Delphic oracle; in his bottomless despair over the death of his son, to which he had indirectly contributed; in his breakdown after the bitter loss of his nation; in his apathetic acquiescence to a martyr’s death upon the pyre; in his sacrilegious repudiation of divine verdicts; and then, finally, in the necessity of his costly atonement for the sin of an ancestor he did not even know. Yes, I thought that I had once and for all said goodbye to this punished, humiliated man, when suddenly he appeared again in the pages of Herodotus, once more in the company of King Cyrus, who, at the head of the Persian army, has set out to conquer the Massagetae, a wild and warlike people living deep in central Asia, all the way on the banks of the Amu Darya.

这是公元前六世纪。波斯人正在积极进取——他们正在征服世界。数年、数个世纪后，一个又一个的超级大国都会做出同样的尝试，但波斯人在那个昏暗而遥远的时代所进行的雄心勃勃的努力，在其大胆性和范围上仍然可以说是无与伦比的。他们已经征服了爱奥尼亚人和伊奥利亚人。占领了米利都、哈利卡纳苏斯和许多其他希腊在西亚的殖民地；夺取了米底人和巴比伦——简而言之，凡是远近所能夺取的，都归波斯人统治。现在，赛勒斯出发去征服位于当时已知和想象的世界尽头的一个部落。也许他相信，如果他击垮了马萨格泰人，占领了他们的土地和牲畜，他就离他可以胜利地宣称：“世界是我的！”的那一刻又近了一步。

It is the sixth century B.C.E. and the Persians are aggressively on the move—they are conquering the world. Years, centuries later, one superpower after another will make the same attempt, but the ambitious striving of the Persians, back in that dim and distant epoch, remains arguably unrivaled in its boldness and scope They had already conquered the Ionians and the Aeolians; captured Miletus, Halicarnassus, and many other Greek colonies in western Asia; grabbed the Medes and Babylon—in short, everything that could be seized in the near and distant vicinity came under Persian rule. And now Cyrus sets off to subjugate a tribe somewhere at the very ends of what was then the known and imagined world. Perhaps he believes that if he crushes the Massagetae, takes over their lands and herds, he will come yet another inch closer to the moment when he can triumphantly proclaim: “The world is mine!”

但这种对拥有一切的需求，早些时候导致了克洛伊索斯的垮台，现在又将导致居鲁士的失败。对人类无节制的贪婪的惩罚总是在他似乎距离实现他的梦想只有一步之遥的那一刻降临到他身上——这就是特别残酷和具有破坏性的讽刺。因此，报应伴随着对世界的强烈失望、对复仇命运的深深怨恨，以及令人沮丧的屈辱感和无力感。

But this need to possess everything, which earlier had led to Croesus’s downfall, will now in turn bring about Cyrus’s defeat. The punishment for man’s unrestrained rapaciousness befalls him always at the very moment—and here lies the particularly cruel and destructive irony—when he appears to be but a step away from attaining his dreams. The comeuppance is therefore accompanied by a savage disappointment in the world, a profound resentment toward a vengeful fate, and a depressing sense of humiliation and powerlessness.

然而现在，居鲁士出发前往亚洲深处，前往北方——征服马萨格泰人。这次远征并没有让他的同时代人感到惊讶，因为每个人都注意到他是如何不分青红皂白地攻击各个种族的……。有许多重要因素诱惑和诱使他开展这项活动。主要的两个原因是他出生的明显奇迹般的性质，以及他在战争中的好运气，从某种意义上说，居鲁士派出军队追击的任何种族都发现不可能逃脱。

For now, however, Cyrus sets off for the depths of Asia, for the north—to conquer the Massagetae. The expedition did not surprise his contemporaries, because everyone noticed how he attacked every race indiscriminately …. There were a number of significant factors tempting and inducing him to undertake this campaign. The main two were the apparently miraculous nature of his birth, and the good fortune that attended him in war, in the sense that any race which Cyrus sent his troops after found it impossible to escape.

人们对马萨格塔人的了解是，他们生活在中亚平坦的大草原上，以及阿姆河的岛屿上，这些岛屿被称为阿拉克斯，夏天他们会在那里挖出各种树根来吃，并储存成熟的果实。在树上找到以供随后冬季食用。我们了解到马萨格泰人使用类似于麻醉剂的东西，因此是当今瘾君子和瘾君子的先驱：他们还发现了一种植物，当他们成群结队地相遇时，他们会使用其果实。他们点燃篝火，坐在篝火周围，将这些水果扔到火上，然后嗅着从他们扔到火上的燃烧水果中升起的烟雾。水果就相当于希腊的酒：他们被烟雾熏得陶醉，然后他们把更多的水果扔进火里，喝得更醉，直到他们最终站起来跳舞，放声歌唱。

What is known about the Massagetae is that they live on the great flat steppes of central Asia, as well as on islands in the Amu Darya, which call Araxes, where in the summer they dig up various roots to eat, storing the ripened fruit they find in the trees for subsequent winter consumption. We learn that the Massagetae used something akin to narcotics, and were therefore the forerunners of today’s addicts and junkies: They have also discovered a kind of plant whose fruit they use when they meet in groups. They light a bonfire, sit around it, throw this fruit on the fire, and sniff the smoke rising from the burning fruit they have thrown on to the fire. The fruit is the equivalent there to wine in Greece: they get intoxicated from the smoke, and then they throw more fruit on to the fire and get even more intoxicated, until they eventually stand up and dance, and burst into song.

那时，马萨格泰人的女王是一位名叫托米丽丝的女人。她和赛勒斯之间将上演一场致命的血腥戏剧，克洛伊索斯也将在其中扮演角色。赛勒斯以一个诡计开始：他假装他要追寻的是托米里斯的手。但马萨格泰女王很快意识到波斯国王的意图不是针对她，而是针对她的王国。居鲁士看到自己无法按照自己希望的方式实现自己的目标，决定向阿姆河对岸的马萨格泰人发动公开战争，而他的军队刚刚到达了阿姆河的岸边。

In those days the queen of the Massagetae is a woman called Tomyris. A deadly, bloody drama will be enacted between her and Cyrus, one in which Croesus will also play a part. Cyrus starts with a subterfuge: he pretends that it is Tomyris’s hand that he is after. But the queen of the Massagetae quickly senses that the Persian king’s designs are not on her but on her kingdom. Cyrus, seeing that he will not attain his goal in the way he had hoped, decides to wage open war against the Massagetae on the other side of the Amu Darya, the river whose shores his forces have just reached.

从波斯首都苏萨到阿姆河沿岸，道路很长，或者更准确地说，没有道路。人们必须翻过山口，穿越喀拉库姆燃烧的沙漠，然后漫步在无尽的草原上。

From the Persian capital of Susa to the shores of the Amu Darya the road is long—or, more accurately, there is no road. One must cross mountain passes, traverse the burning desert of Kara-Kum, and then wander the endless steppes.

这让人想起拿破仑对莫斯科的疯狂征战。波斯人和法国人都怀有同样的激情：夺取、征服、占有。两者都会因为违反了希腊的一项基本原则——适度法则而遭受失败：永远不要想要太多，也不要渴望一切。但当他们开始创业时，他们太盲目了，看不到这一点；征服欲削弱了他们的判断力，剥夺了他们的理性。另一方面，如果理性统治世界，历史还会存在吗？

One is reminded of Napoleon’s mad campaign for Moscow. The Persian and the Frenchman are in the grips of an identical passion: to seize, conquer, possess. Both will suffer defeat on account of having transgressed a fundamental Greek principle, the law of moderation: never to want too much, not to desire everything. But as they are launching their ventures, they are too blind to see this; the lust for conquest has dimmed their judgment, has deprived them of reason. On the other hand, if reason ruled the world, would history even exist?

但目前，居鲁士的远征军仍在行军中。它一定是一支由人、马和物资组成的无休无止的纵队。疲倦的士兵不断从山崖上摔下来，后来许多人在沙漠中渴死，后来还有一些部队迷失在无路可走的草原上。那时候没有地图，毕竟没有指南针，没有双筒望远镜，没有路标。他们必须在沿途遇到的部落的帮助下进行侦察，四处询问，寻找向导，甚至可能咨询算命先生。无论情况如何，这支伟大的军队都在奋力前进，不知疲倦，有时确实如此，就像波斯人在鞭打下经常发生的那样。

For now, though, Cyrus’s expeditionary force is still on the march. It must be an interminable column of men, horses, and matériel. Tired soldiers keep falling off mountain cliffs, later many perish of thirst in the desert, later still some units are lost in the roadless expanses of the steppes. There were no maps in those days, after all, no compasses, no binoculars, no road signs. They must reconnoiter with the help of tribes they encounter along the way, ask around, find guides, perhaps even consult fortune-tellers. Whatever the case may be, the great army advances—laboriously, indefatigably, and at times surely, as was wont to happen with the Persians, under the lash.

只有居鲁士在这条苦难的道路上享受着一切可能的安慰。现在，伟大的国王带着来自家乡的充足食物和牲畜进行军事远征，他还从乔斯皮斯河（苏萨城位于其河岸）带来水，因为除了乔斯皮斯河以外，没有其他河流的水是允许通过国王的嘴唇。 Choäspes 的水是煮沸的，无论国王在任何特定场合出征，他都会有大量由骡子拉动的四轮马车陪伴，这些马车用银容器运水。

Only Cyrus enjoys all possible comforts along this road of suffering. Now, the Great King goes on his military expeditions well equipped with food and livestock from home, and he also brings water from the River Choäspes (on whose banks the city of Susa is situated), because water from no other river except the Choäspes is allowed to pass the king’s lips. This Choäspes water is boiled, and wherever the king might be campaigning on any given occasion, he is accompanied by a large number of four-wheeled wagons, drawn by mules, which carry the water in silver containers.

我对这水很着迷。水是提前煮沸的水。储存在银器皿中以保持凉爽。人们必须载着这些船只穿越沙漠。

I am fascinated by this water. Water that has been boiled ahead of time. Stored in silver vessels to keep it cool. One has to cross the desert freighted with those vessels.

我们知道，水是通过许多骡子拉的四轮马车来运输的。水车和沿途饮水的士兵有什么关系？没有：士兵死了，马车继续行驶。他们没有停下来，因为他们运的水不是给士兵的；这是专门为居鲁士煮的水。国王毕竟不喝别的水，所以如果喝完了，他就会渴死。人们怎么可能想到这样的可能性呢？另一件事情也让我感兴趣。事实上有两个国王在这个游行队伍中——伟大的居鲁士和被废黜的克洛伊索斯，克洛伊索斯昨天才刚刚逃脱死在燃烧的柴堆上的命运，这是第一位国王为他准备的命运。他们现在的关系怎么样？希罗多德认为他们是真诚的。但他并没有参加这次探险——他还没有出生。赛勒斯和克洛伊索斯是否乘坐相同的装备，毫无疑问都装饰着镀金车轮、镀金支柱和镀金轴？克洛伊索斯看到这一幕是否感叹不已？两位先生有交谈吗？如果他们这样做，也必须通过口译员，因为他们没有共同的语言。无论如何，有什么可谈的呢？他们就这样骑了几天，然后是几周。迟早，他们会用尽所有可能的话题。此外，如果他们中的一个（或两个）都是安静的人，具有神秘而内向的性格呢？

We know that the water is transported on numerous four-wheeled wagons drawn by mules. What connection between the water wagons and the soldiers dropping of thirst along the way? There is none: the soldiers die, and the wagons keep rolling. They do not stop, because the water they carry is not for the soldiers; it is water that has been boiled expressly for Cyrus. The king, after all, drinks no other, so if it ran out, he would die of thirst. How could one even contemplate such an eventuality? Another thing interests me as well. There are de facto two kings in this procession—the great, reigning Cyrus and the dethroned Croesus, who only yesterday just barely escaped death on a burning pyre, a fate that the first king had been preparing for him. What are relations between them like now? Herodotus maintains that they are cordial. But he did not take part in this expedition—he wasn’t even born yet. Do Cyrus and Croesus ride in the same equipage, no doubt adorned with gold-plated wheels, gold-plated stanchions, and a gold-plated shaft? Does Croesus sigh wistfully at the sight? Do the two gentlemen converse? If they do, it must be through an interpreter, because they share no language. And what is there to talk about, anyway? They ride thus for days, then weeks; sooner or later, they will have exhausted all possible subjects of conversation. And what if, moreover, one of them—or both—is the quiet sort, with a secretive and introverted personality?

我想知道当赛勒斯想要喝水时会发生什么。他呼叫仆人。这些挑水者必须是非常值得信赖的人，他们已经发过不可侵犯的誓言；不然怎么能阻止他们偷偷喝下这无价液体呢？于是，按照命令，他们拿来了一个银水罐。居鲁士现在独自喝酒，还是说：“喝点酒吧，克洛伊索斯？”希罗多德对这个问题保持沉默，但这是一个值得考虑的重要时刻——没有水，人就无法生活在沙漠中；没有水，人就无法生活在沙漠中。如果失去它，人很快就会脱水。

I wonder what happens when Cyrus wants a drink of water. He calls to the servants. These water bearers must be retainers of exceptional trustworthiness, who have taken an inviolable oath; otherwise, what would prevent their taking sips of the priceless liquid on the sly? And so, at the command, they fetch a silver pitcher. Does Cyrus now drink alone, or does he say, “Care for some, Croesus?” Herodotus is silent on this subject, but it is an important moment to consider—one cannot live in the desert without water; deprived of it, a human being succumbs quickly to dehydration.

但也许两位国王并不一起骑行——在这种情况下就不会出现问题。或者也许克罗伊索斯有他自己的一桶水，普通的水，不一定来自那条特殊的河流，Choäspes。但这一切都只是猜测，因为直到探险队到达广阔而平静的阿姆河之前，希罗多德才进一步提及克洛伊索斯。

But perhaps the two kings do not ride together—in which case the problem does not arise. Or maybe Croesus has his own barrel of water, ordinary water, not necessarily from that special river, Choäspes. But all this is mere speculation, because Herodotus makes no further mention of Croesus until the expedition reaches the broad and calm Amu Darya.

居鲁士未能占有托米丽丝，向她宣战。他的第一步是下令在岛上建造浮桥。河，为他的军队提供通向对岸的通道。当这项工作正在进行时，女王派来了一位信使，他向赛勒斯发出了充满智慧警告的常识性话语：“放弃你对这项事业的热情……。停下来统治你们自己的人民，并忍受我统治我的人民的景象。但不：你很难接受这个建议，因为和平是你最不想要的。如果你真的想与马萨格泰人较量一下，你就不必费心去费力去架桥；我们将从河边撤退三天的路程，然后你就可以过河进入我们的土地了。或者，如果你更愿意在你自己的土地上与我们见面，你也可以保持同样的距离。”

Cyrus, who failed at possessing Tomyris, declared war on her. His first step was to order the construction of pontoon bridges on the river, to give his army passage to the other side. While this work is in progress, a messenger arrives from the queen, who sends Cyrus commonsensical words full of wise caution: “Abandon your zeal for this enterprise …. Stop and rule your own people, and put up with the sight of my ruling mine. But no: you are hardly going to take this advice, since peace is the last thing you desire. If you really are committed to a trial of strength with the Massagetae, you need not bother with all the hard work of bridging the river; we will pull back three days’ journey away from the river and then you can cross over into our land. Or if you would rather meet us in your own land, you withdraw the same distance.”

居鲁士闻言，召集长老们开会，征求他们的意见。他们一致建议撤退，并提议在他们自己的波斯河一侧与托米里斯的军队交战。但有一种反对的声音——克洛伊索斯。他从哲学角度开始说：“你应该明白的第一件事是，”他告诉赛勒斯，“人类的事务都在一个轮子上，而当轮子转动时，并不会让同一个人总是繁荣昌盛。”

Upon hearing this, Cyrus convenes a meeting of elders and asks for their views. All of them, unanimously, advise a retreat, proposing that the engagement with Tomyris’s forces take place on their own, Persian side of the river. But there is one dissenting voice—that of Croesus. He begins philosophically: “The first thing you should appreciate,” he tells Cyrus, “is that human affairs are on a wheel, and that as the wheel turns around it does not permit the same people always to prosper.”

简而言之，克洛伊索斯直截了当地警告居鲁士，好运可能会抛弃他，事情可能会变得非常糟糕。他建议渡河到河对岸，在那里——因为他听说马萨格泰人不习惯波斯人那样的富裕，也没有经历过多少快乐——宰杀羊群，摆上美酒和诱人的菜肴，为他们举办一场盛大的宴会。马萨格泰人会吃喝，醉酒入睡，然后他们就会被俘虏。居鲁士接受克洛伊索斯的计划，托米里斯从河中撤退，波斯军队进入马萨格泰人的土地。

In short, Croesus warns Cyrus point-blank that good fortune might desert him and that things could then go very badly indeed. He counsels crossing to the other side of the river and there—because he has heard that the Massagetae are unaccustomed to riches such as the Persians have and have experienced few pleasures—to slaughter herds of sheep, set out fine wine and tempting dishes, and organize a great feast for them. The Massagetae will eat and drink, fall into a drunken sleep, whereupon they can be taken prisoner. Cyrus accepts Croesus’s plan, Tomyris retreats from the river, and the Persian troops cross into the lands of the Massagetae.

紧张局势很快就会出现，就像一场大对抗之前的典型情况一样。当克洛伊索斯早先所说的命运如轮子转动的话语被人们所接受后，居鲁士这位经验丰富的统治者，统治波斯二十九年，开始领悟到了命运的规律。即将发生的事情的严重性。他不再对自己有信心，不再像以前那样傲慢自满。他做了一个噩梦，当天亮时，出于对儿子冈比西斯生命的担忧，他在克洛伊索斯的陪同下送回了波斯。此外，针对他的阴谋和阴谋也不断增多。

Tensions soon arise, as is typical before a great confrontation. After Croesus’s earlier words sink in, those about fortune turning like a wheel, Cyrus, who is an experienced ruler, having now reigned over Persia for twenty-nine years, starts to grasp the seriousness of what is about to transpire. He is no longer sure of himself, no longer, as before, arrogant and self-satisfied. He has a nightmare, and when daylight comes, concerned for the life of his son, Cambyses, sends him back to Persia accompanied by Croesus. In addition, plots and conspiracies against him proliferate.

但他是军队的统帅，必须发号施令；每个人都在等着听他会说什么，他会带领他们去往何方。居鲁士所做的就是一点一点地执行克洛伊索斯的建议，却没有意识到他正在一步步走向毁灭。 （克洛伊索斯是否有意识地误导了居鲁士？他是否为他设置了陷阱，为他所遭受的失败和屈辱报仇？我们不知道——对此希罗多德保持沉默。）

But he is the commander of an army and must issue orders; everyone is waiting to hear what he will say, where he will lead them. And what Cyrus does is execute, point by point, Croesus’s advice, unaware that he is thereby proceeding step by step toward his own destruction. (Did Croesus consciously mislead Cyrus? Did he set a trap for him in order to avenge the defeat he endured and the humiliation he suffered? We do not know—about this Herodotus is silent.)

居鲁士首先派出他的军队中最不适合战斗的部分——各种营地的随从、流浪汉、弱者和病人，正如人们在古拉格集中营常说的那样，各种各样的人，dokhodiagi（goners）。他实际上是在判处这些人死刑，而事实正是如此，因为在与马萨格泰军队的精英相遇时，他们被砍成了一个人。现在，马萨格泰人杀掉了波斯后卫，注意到了摆好的筵席，他们就坐下来吃掉它。当他们吃饱喝足后，他们就睡着了——然后波斯人就向他们发起了进攻。许多马萨格泰人被杀，但更多人被俘，其中包括托米里斯王后的儿子，他是军队的指挥官，名叫斯帕加皮塞斯。

Cyrus sends in first the part of his army most unfit for battle—various camp hangers-on, vagabonds, the weak and the sick, all sorts of, as one used to say in the gulags, dokhodiagi (goners). He is in effect condemning these people to death, which is precisely what happens, because in the encounter with the elite of the Massagetae forces, they are cut down to a man. Now the Massagetae, having slaughtered the Persian rear guard, noticed the feast, which had been laid out, and they reclined and ate it. When they had eaten and drunk their fill, they fell asleep—and then the Persians fell on them. Many of the Massagetae were killed, but even more were taken prisoner, including Queen Tomyris’ son, who was the commander of the army and whose name was Spargapises.

在得知她儿子和她的军队的命运的消息后，托米丽丝向居鲁士派遣了一名信使，并说道：“把我的儿子还给我，然后你就可以离开这个国家，而不必为你对待三分之一的马萨诸塔军队的残暴付出代价。” 。但如果你不这样做，我以马萨格泰之主太阳的名义起誓，尽管你如此贪得无厌，我仍将满足你对鲜血的渴求。”

At the news of her son’s and her army’s fate, Tomyris sends Cyrus a messenger with the following words: “Give me back my son, and then you can leave this country without paying for the brutality with which you treated a third of the Massagetan army. But if you do not, I swear by the sun who is the lord of the Massagetae that for all your insatiability I will quench your thirst for blood.”

这些都是强烈的、险恶的言词，但赛勒斯仍然如此丝毫不予理睬。他为自己的胜利而陶醉，很高兴自己带领托米里斯走上了花园小路，并成功地向拒绝他求爱的人进行了报复。此时女王还没有意识到自己不幸的深重，那就是：当托米里斯王后的儿子斯帕加皮塞斯从酒中醒来，看到自己陷入困境时，他恳求居鲁士把他从锁链中释放出来。居鲁士答应了他的请求，但当斯帕加皮塞斯重获自由并重新控制自己的双手时，他就自杀了。一场死亡与鲜血的狂欢开始了。

These are strong, sinister words, of which Cyrus nevertheless takes not the slightest notice. He is intoxicated by his victory, pleased that he has led Tomyris up the garden path and succeeded in revenging himself upon one who rejected his advances. At this moment the queen is still unaware of the depth of her own misfortune, namely: When Spargapises, the son of Queen Tomyris, recovered from the wine and saw the trouble he was in, he begged Cyrus to release him from his chains. Cyrus granted his request, but as soon as Spargapises was free and had regained control of his hands, he killed himself. An orgy of death and blood begins.

托米丽丝发现居鲁士没有听从她的劝告，于是集结军队与他交战。希罗多德：我认为这是非希腊人之间有史以来最激烈的战斗……最初，两支军队互相射箭。当没有箭的时候，他们就用长矛和匕首战斗。最后，他们诉诸徒手摔跤。虽然一开始双方势均力敌，但渐渐地，马萨格泰人占据了上风。波斯军队大部分被消灭。赛勒斯也在死者之列。

Tomyris, seeing that Cyrus had not heeded her counsel, gathered her forces and engaged him in battle. Herodotus: I consider this to be the fiercest battle between non-Greeks there has ever been … Initially, both armies rain arrows down upon each other. When there are no arrows left, they fight with lances and daggers. And finally, they resort to barehanded wrestling. Although they are equally matched at the start, gradually the Massagetae gain the upper hand. Most of the Persian army perishes. Cyrus, too, is among the dead.

接下来发生的就是希腊悲剧的一幕。平原上布满了两军士兵的尸体。托米里斯带着一个空酒袋走进了这个战场。她从一名被杀的士兵走到另一名士兵，从新鲜的伤口中收集血液，足以装满酒袋。女王一定沾满了人类的鲜血，她一定正滴着人类的鲜血。天很热，她肯定用沾满血的手擦脸。她的脸上沾满了血。她环顾四周，寻找赛勒斯的尸体。当她找到它时，她把他的头塞进酒袋里，在愤怒中对他的尸体说道：“虽然我活着并取得了胜利，但你却用诡计俘获了我的儿子，从而毁掉了我。但我警告过你，我会满足你对鲜血的渴望，所以我会这么做。”

What ensues now is a scene from a Greek tragedy. The plain is strewn with the corpses of soldiers from both armies. Onto this battlefield steps Tomyris, carrying an empty wineskin. She walks from one slaughtered soldier to the next and collects blood from the still fresh wounds, enough to fill the wineskin. The queen must be drenched with human blood, she must be positively dripping with it. It is hot, so she surely wipes her face with her bloodied hands. Her face is smeared with blood. She looks around, searching for Cyrus’s corpse. When she found it, she shoved his head into the wineskin, and in her rage addressed his body as follows: “Although I have come through the battle alive and victorious, you have destroyed me by capturing my son with a trick. But I warned you that I would quench your thirst for blood, and so I shall.”

战斗就这样结束了。

That is how the battle ends.

赛勒斯就是这样死的。

That is how Cyrus dies.

舞台空荡荡，只剩下绝望而充满仇恨的托米莉丝。

The stage empties, and the only one left standing is the despairing, hate-filled Tomyris.

希罗多德没有做出任何评论，只是以记者的责任感补充了几条有关马萨古塔风俗的信息，毕竟这些信息对希腊人来说是陌生的：如果马萨古塔人想要一个女人，他就会把箭袋挂在她的马车外面，然后与她发生性关系，不用担心遭到报复。那里对生命的唯一限制如下。当一个人年老时，他的所有亲戚都会聚集在一起，将他和一些牲畜一起宰杀；然后他们把肉炖了吃。他们认为没有比这更幸运的死法了，而病死的人不是被吃掉而是被埋在地下，没有被牺牲就是一种灾难。

Herodotus offers no commentary, adding only, with a reporter’s sense of duty, several pieces of information about Massagetan customs, which were, after all, unfamiliar to the Greeks: If a Massagetan desires a woman, he hangs his quiver outside her wagon and has sex with her, with no fear of reprisal. The only imposed limit on life there is as follows. When a person becomes very old, all his relatives come together and sacrificially kill him and some livestock along with him; then they stew the meat and eat it. They believe that there is no more fortunate way to die, whereas anyone who dies after an illness is buried in the ground rather than eaten, and they regard it as a calamity that he did not get to be sacrificed.

论诸神的起源

ON THE ORIGIN OF THE GODS

我把希罗多德收进我办公桌的抽屉里，留下托米里斯在尸横遍野的战场上，在惨败的胜利中，绝望但又胜利——亚洲草原上不屈不挠、炽热的安提戈涅——我开始翻阅最新一批电报由路透社和法新社驻中国、印度尼西亚、新加坡和越南的记者发送。他们报告说，宾隆附近的越南游击队与吴庭艳军队再次发生小规模冲突（冲突结果和伤亡人数未知）。毛泽东又宣布了另一场运动：百花政治已死；现在的任务是对知识分子的再教育——谁会读书写字（这些技能突然变成了累赘），就会被强行驱逐到农村，在那里，拉犁或挖渠，认识真正的无产阶级农民生活中，他或她将摆脱自由主义、百花齐放般的幻想。印度尼西亚总统苏加诺是潘查西拉权力新政治的理论家之一，他命令荷兰人离开他的国家，他们的前殖民地。人们无法从这些简短的报道中学到什么。它们缺乏背景和所谓的当地色彩。我也许最容易想象北大的教授们，看到他们坐在一辆卡车上，由于寒冷而弯腰驼背，甚至不知道要去哪里，因为他们的眼镜在寒冷中起雾。

I put Herodotus away into the drawer of my office desk, leaving Tomyris on the corpse-strewn battlefield, in defeated victory, despairing but also triumphant—the indomitable and incandescent Antigone of the Asiatic steppes—and I start to leaf through the latest batch of telegrams sent by the correspondents for Reuters and Agence France-Presse in China, Indonesia, Singapore, and Vietnam. They report that Vietnamese guerrillas near Bing Long have engaged in yet another skirmish with the troops of Ngo Dinh Diem (the result of the clash and the number of casualties—unknown). That Mao Tse-tung has proclaimed another campaign: Dead is the politics of One Hundred Flowers; now the task is the reeducation of the intelligentsia—whoever knows how to read and write (these skills have suddenly metamorphosed into liabilities) will be forcibly deported to the countryside, where, pulling a plow or digging irrigation canals, coming to know real proletarian peasant life, he or she will be rid of liberal, One Hundred Flower-like chimera. That the president of Indonesia, Sukarno, one of the ideologues of the new politics of Pancasila power, has ordered the Dutch to leave his country, their former colony. One can learn little from these brief dispatches; they lack context and what one might call local color. I can perhaps imagine most easily the professors of Peking University, see them riding in a truck, hunched over from the chill, not even knowing where they’re headed because their eyeglasses are fogging over in the cold.

是的，亚洲正在发生很多事情，在各个办公室分发急件的女士不断地将新成堆的急件放在我的办公桌上。但随着时间的推移，我注意到另一个大陆开始引起我的注意：非洲。与亚洲一样，非洲也存在动荡：暴风雨和叛乱、政变和骚乱。但由于非洲距离欧洲较近（只有一水体地中海将它们分开），人们可以更直接地听到这片大陆的隆隆声，就好像它们来自隔壁一样。

Yes, much is happening in Asia, and the lady who distributes the dispatches around the various offices keeps placing fresh piles of them on my desk. But with time I notice that another continent starts to draw my attention: Africa. As in Asia, there is turmoil in Africa: tempests and revolts, coups and riots. But because Africa lies closer to Europe (only a single body of water, the Mediterranean Sea, separates them) one hears the rumblings of this continent with more immediacy, as though they were coming from just next door.

非洲对世界历史的贡献是巨大的——这不亚于对数百年历史的全球等级制度的转变。通过向新世界提供劳动力，它使其能够积累足够的财富和力量来超越旧世界。后来，在世代相传了最优秀、最强壮、最有韧性的人民之后，这片人口减少、疲惫不堪的大陆很容易成为欧洲殖民者的牺牲品。然而现在，它正在从昏睡中苏醒，为独立积聚力量。

Africa’s contribution to world history has been immense—nothing less than a transformation of a centuries-old global hierarchy. By furnishing the New World its labor force, it enabled it to amass enough wealth and power to surpass the Old World. Later, having given over many generations of its best, strongest, and most resilient people, the depopulated and exhausted continent fell easy prey to European colonizers. Now, however, it was awakening from its lethargy, gathering its strength for independence.

我开始倾向于非洲，也是因为从一开始，亚洲就让我感到非常害怕。印度、中国和大草原的文明对我来说都是巨大的，甚至想象接近其中任何一个都需要一生的研究——一个人几乎不可能指望完全了解它们。另一方面，非洲给我的印象是更加碎片化、差异化、因其多样性而小型化，因此更容易掌握、平易近人。

I began to incline toward Africa also because, from the very outset, Asia had greatly intimidated me. The civilizations of India, China, and the great steppes were for me colossi, and even to imagine drawing near to any one of them required a lifetime of study—one could scarcely hope to know them all well. Africa, on the other hand, struck me as more fragmentary, differentiated, miniaturized by its multiplicity, and thus more graspable, approachable.

几个世纪以来，人们一直被这片大陆周围的某种神秘气氛所吸引——一种感觉，一定有非洲独特的东西，隐藏的东西，黑暗中某个闪闪发光的氧化点，很难或几乎不可能到达。许多人都雄心勃勃，想要在这里考验自己的勇气，发现并揭示其令人困惑、令人困惑的核心。

For centuries people have been attracted by a certain aura of mystery surrounding this continent—a sense that there must be something unique in Africa, something hidden, some glistening oxidizing point in the darkness which it is difficult or well nigh impossible to reach. And many had the ambition to test their mettle here, to discover and uncover its bewildering, confounding core.

希罗多德对此非常感兴趣。他写道，曾拜访过阿蒙神谕的昔兰尼人向他讲述了他们与阿蒙人国王埃特阿库斯（阿蒙人住在利比亚沙漠西瓦绿洲）的一次对话。埃特阿库斯向他们讲述了他曾经遇到过一些纳萨莫内斯人的事，纳萨莫内斯人是一个利比亚部落，居住在锡尔蒂斯湾周围和锡尔蒂斯以东不远的地方。在访问期间，纳萨莫内斯夫妇被问及是否可以增加对利比亚无人居住的沙漠地区的了解。作为回应，他们讲述了部落中一些意气风发的酋长的儿子在成年后如何策划了一系列非凡的计划，包括抽签选出五个人去探索利比亚沙漠，以找出答案。如果他们能看到比以前看到的更多的东西就好了。利比亚人——他们的许多部落——沿着利比亚北海的整个海岸线散布开来……然后是利比亚的一部分，它是内陆地区，远离海洋和占领沿海的人民：这是利比亚的一部分那里是野生动物出没的地方。利比亚远离动物栖息地的内陆地区是沙漠，完全没有水，完全无人居住。据说，当年轻人离开他们的朋友时，他们已经配备了充足的食物和水。他们首先穿过居住区，然后到达野生动物出没的地方。接下来他们开始向西穿过沙漠。他们穿过了大片沙地，四周都是沙漠，过了很多天，他们终于看到平原上长出了树木。他们走近树木，试图采摘长在树上的果实，但正当他们这样做时，他们遭到了身材低于正常人的小个子男子的袭击，他们抓住了他们并把他们带走了。这两个群体——纳萨莫内人和他们的向导——根本无法理解彼此的语言。他们穿过广阔的沼泽，在沼泽的另一边，他们来到了一个小镇 每个人都和他们的向导一样高大，皮肤黝黑。小镇坐落在一条大河上，河水自西向东流淌，河里可以看到鳄鱼。

Herodotus was so intrigued. He writes that people from Cyrene, who had visited the oracle of Ammon, told him of a conversation they’d had with the king of the Ammonians, Etearchus (the Ammonians lived in the oasis of Siwa, in the Libyan desert). Etearchus told them about a visit he had once had from some Nasamones, a Libyan tribe who live around the Gulf of Syrtis and the land a little way east of the Syrtis. During the course of their visit, the Nasamones were asked whether they could add to what was known about the uninhabited desert parts of Libya. In response, they told how some high-spirited chiefs’ sons of their tribe, once they had reached adulthood, concocted a number of extraordinary schemes, including casting lots to choose five of their number to go and explore the Libyan desert, to find out if they could see more than had ever been seen before. Libyans—many tribes of them—have spread out along the whole of the Libyan coastline of the northern sea … Then there is the part of Libya which is inland from the sea and from the people who occupy the seaboard: this is the part of Libya which is infested by wild animals. Further inland from the part full of animals Libya is sandy desert, totally waterless, and completely uninhabited by anyone or anything. So when the young men left their friends, the story goes, they were well equipped with food and water; they first passed through the inhabited region and then reached the part which is infested by wild animals. Next they started to travel in a westerly direction through the desert. After they had crossed a great deal of sandy country, surrounded by nothing but desert, they at last, after many days, saw trees growing on a plain. They approached the trees and tried to pick the fruit that was growing on them, but as they were doing so they were set upon by small men of less than normal human stature, who captured them and took them away. The two groups—the Nasamones and their guides—could not understand each other’s language at all. They were taken through vast swamps and on the other side of these swamps they came to a town where everyone was the same size as their guides and had black skin. The town was on a sizeable river, which was flowing from west to east, and in it they could see crocodiles.

这是希罗多德第二卷中的一个片段——记录他的埃及之行。在这段长达数十页的文字中，我们可以清楚地观察到希腊人的技巧。

This is a fragment from Book Two of Herodotus—the account of his trip into Egypt. In this passage, running several dozen pages, we can clearly observe the Greek’s technique.

希罗多德如何工作？

How does Herodotus work?

他是一位完美的记者：他漫步、观察、交谈、倾听，以便日后能够记下他所学到和看到的东西，或者只是为了更好地记住。

He is a consummate reporter: he wanders, looks, talks, listens, in order that he can later note down what he learned and saw, or simply to remember better.

他如何旅行？如果走陆路，他会骑马、骑驴、骡子，或者最常见的是步行。

How does he travel? If by land, then he goes on horseback, donkey, mule, or, most frequently, on foot.

他是独自一人，还是有奴隶陪伴？我们不确定，但在那个时代，只要有能力，就会带着奴隶去旅行。奴隶带着行李、装水的葫芦、食物、书写工具——一卷纸莎草、泥板、刷子、刻刀、墨水。由于道路的艰辛拉平了阶级差异，奴隶更像是一个旅伴：他支撑着一个人的精神，提供保护，问路，侦察。我们可以想象希罗多德——一个好奇而浪漫的人，为了知识而求知，一个勤奋的学生，研究不切实际和基本无用的事物——和他的奴隶之间的关系，他的奴隶在路上必须处理平凡的、行人的、日常的事情，类似于堂吉诃德和桑丘·潘萨之间的那些。他们是后来卡斯蒂利亚人的古希腊人的祖先。

Is he alone, or accompanied by a slave? We do not know for certain, but in those days, whoever had the means took a slave along on journeys. The slave carried the luggage, the gourd with water, the food, the writing implements—a roll of papyrus, clay tablets, brushes, gravers, ink. Because the hardships of the road leveled class differences, the slave was more of a traveling companion: he sustained one’s spirits, afforded protection, asked for directions, reconnoitered. We can imagine how the relations between Herodotus—an inquisitive romantic desirous of knowledge for knowledge’s sake, a diligent student of the impractical and the largely useless—and his slave, who on the road had to take care of things mundane, pedestrian, everyday, resembled those between Don Quixote and Sancho Panza. They were the ancient Greek progenitors of that later Castilian pair.

旅行者除了雇佣奴隶外，还雇佣了一名向导和一名翻译。因此，希罗多德的团队可能至少统计了三个人（不包括他自己）。但往往朝着同一个方向的流浪者最终会聚在一起。

In addition to one’s slave, a traveler also hired a guide and an interpreter. Herodotus’s team might therefore have numbered—not counting himself—at least three people. But often wanderers headed in the same direction eventually joined up.

在埃及炎热的气候下，人们在早晨。因此，旅行者在黎明起床，吃早餐（小麦饼、无花果、羊奶酪、稀释的酒——仍然可以饮用，伊斯兰教在未来一千年内不会控制这里），然后出发。

In the hot Egyptian climate, one makes best progress in the morning. Travelers therefore rise at dawn, eat breakfast (wheat cakes, figs, sheep cheese, diluted wine—it’s still permissible to drink, Islam will not seize control here for another thousand years), and then set out.

希罗多德旅程的目标是什么？收集有关一个国家、其人民及其习俗的新信息，或测试已收集数据的可靠性。希罗多德并不满足于别人告诉他的事情——他试图验证每件事，比较和对比他听到的各种版本，然后制定自己的版本。

The goal of Herodotus’s journeys? To collect new information about a country, its people, and their customs, or to test the reliability of data already gathered. Herodotus is not content with what someone else has told him—he tries to verify each thing, to compare and contrast the various versions he has heard, and then to formulate his own.

当他到达埃及时，埃及国王普萨美提库斯已经去世一百五十年了。希罗多德发现（也许还在希腊时就被告知了这一点）普萨梅提库斯特别关注哪个是地球上最古老的种族的问题。埃及人相信他们是这样的，但普萨美提库斯虽然是他们的国王，却心存疑虑。他命令一个牧羊人在无人居住的山里抚养两个婴儿。他们说的第一个词所用的语言将证明说这种语言的人是地球上最古老的人。当孩子们两岁时，饥饿时，他们会喊“Bekos！”，在弗里吉亚语中意思是“面包”。因此，普萨梅提库斯宣称地球上最早的民族是弗里吉亚人，埃及人是后来才出现的，凭借这一澄清，他在历史上赢得了自己的地位。希罗多德对普萨梅蒂库斯的询问很感兴趣，因为它们证明了埃及统治者明白不可改变的历史法则，根据这一法则，任何自高的人都会被贬低：不要贪婪，不要争先恐后，保持节制和谦逊；否则，命运的惩罚之手将降临在你身上，它会斩首自吹自擂者和所有自以为是的人。 Psammetichus 想要饶恕埃及人遭遇了这种不幸，因此将他们从历史的前排移到了第二排：弗里吉亚人是第一排，而你只是紧随其后。

When he arrives in Egypt, its king, Psammetichus, has already been dead for one hundred and fifty years. Herodotus discovers (perhaps having been told this while still in Greece) that Psammetichus had been especially preoccupied with the question of which was the oldest race on earth. The Egyptians believed that they were, but Psammetichus, although their king, nevertheless had his doubts. He ordered a shepherd to raise two infants in uninhabited mountains. The language in which they spoke their first word would be proof that the people who speak that language are the oldest on earth. When the children were two years old and were hungry, they cried, “Bekos!,” which meant “bread” in the Phrygian language. Psammetichus therefore proclaimed that the first people on earth were the Phrygians and that the Egyptians came only later, and with this clarification he earned his place in history. Psammetichus’s inquiries interest Herodotus because they prove that the Egyptian ruler understood that unalterable law of history according to which whoever elevates himself will be humbled: Be not voracious, do not jostle your way to the fore, maintain moderation and humility; otherwise the chastising hand of Fate, which beheads braggarts and all who presume to lord it over others, will descend upon you. Psammetichus wanted to spare Egyptians this misfortune and so moved them from history’s front row to the second: the Phrygians were first, and you only came after.

这是我在孟菲斯与赫菲斯托斯的祭司交谈时听到的。我在那里获得的信息促使我前往底比斯和赫利奥波利斯，试图查明他们的说法是否与我在孟菲斯听到的相符。因此，希罗多德旅行是为了检查、比较、澄清。他聆听祭司们对埃及、其规模和地理的描述，并评论道：我的观点是，他们对这个国家的描述是正确的。他对任何事情都有自己的看法，并在别人的故事中寻求证实。

This is what I heard … in Memphis during my conversations with the priests of Hephaestus. The information I gained there led me to travel to Thebes and to Heliopolis, to try to find out whether their accounts would agree with what I had heard in Memphis. Herodotus travels, therefore, to check, to compare, to clarify. He listens to the priests’ descriptions of Egypt, its dimensions and geography, and comments: My view is that they are right in saying this about the country. He has his own opinion about everything and searches for confirmation in the stories of others.

希罗多德对尼罗河这条强大而神秘的河流尤其着迷。它的来源在哪里？它的水从哪里来？它携带的淤泥从何而来，为这个幅员辽阔的国家的富饶三角洲提供了肥沃的土壤？至于尼罗河发源于何处的问题，我所采访过的埃及人、利比亚人或希腊人都没有声称有明确的答案……所以他决定亲自寻找尼罗河的源头，尽可能冒险进入上埃及。我自己远行至象岛，亲眼所见，遂向他人打听……

Herodotus is especially fascinated by the Nile, this powerful and mysterious river. Where are its sources? Where do its waters come from? Whence comes the silt it carries, with which it fertilizes the rich delta of this immense nation? As for the question where the Nile rises, no Egyptian or Libyan or Greek I have spoken to claimed to have a definite answer … So he decides to search for the river’s sources himself, venturing as far as he can into Upper Egypt. I myself travelled as far as Elephantine and saw things with my very own eyes, and subsequently made enquiries of others …

象岛之后，地势陡峭，从此以后，必须有一根绳子，从船上拉到两岸，就像套牛一样，这样行进。如果绳子断了，船就会被水流冲向下游。这样的地形需要四天的路程，尼罗河在这里的蜿蜒曲折不亚于曲流河。再沿着尼​​罗河航行两个月，您将到达一座名为麦罗埃的大城市……。。

After Elephantine the land rises steeply, so that from then on one has to have a rope running from the boat to both banks, as one harnesses an ox, and to proceed like that. If the rope were to break, the boat would be carried downstream by the force of the current. This kind of terrain lasts for four days’ travelling, and the Nile here twists and turns as much as the Meander. Another two months of traveling and sailing up the Nile will bring you to a big city called Meroë …..

但从那时起，就没有关于它的可靠信息：这片土地因炎热而无人居住。

But from then there is no reliable information to be had about it: the land is uninhabited because of the heat.

他抛弃了尼罗河，抛弃了尼罗河的源头之谜，抛弃了河水季节性涨落的谜团，开始密切观察埃及人，他们的生活方式、习惯和风俗。他指出，几乎所有埃及的风俗习惯都与其他地方相反。他仔细地、一丝不苟地记录着：

He abandons the Nile, the mystery of its sources, the enigma of the seasonal rising and falling of its waters, and begins to observe closely the Egyptians, their way of being, their habits, their customs. He states that almost all Egyptian customs and practices are the opposite of those of everywhere else. And he carefully, scrupulously records:

例如，女性去城镇广场零售商品，而男性则留在家里编织……或者，男性头上顶着重物，而女性则肩上扛着。女性是站着小便，而男性是蹲着小便。他们在室内大小便，但在外面的街上吃饭；他们说，这样做的原因是，那些令人尴尬但又不可避免的事情应该私下做，而不令人尴尬的事情应该公开做。任何神或女神都没有女祭司；他们所有的神，甚至女神，都由男人以这种身份侍奉。儿子不愿意照顾父母可以不照顾，女儿不愿意照顾就必须照顾。

For instance, women go out to the town square and retail goods, while men stay at home and do the weaving … Or again, men carry loads on their heads, while women do so on their shoulders. Women urinate standing up, while men do so squatting. They relieve themselves indoors, but eat outside on the streets; the reason for this, they say, is that things that are embarrassing but unavoidable should be done in private, while things which are not embarrassing should be done out in the open. There are no female priestesses of any god or goddess; all their gods, and goddesses too, are served in this capacity by men. Sons do not have to look after their parents if they do not want to, but daughters must even if they are reluctant.

在世界其他地方，祭司都留着长发，但在埃及，他们剃光头……。在世界其他地方，人们与动物分开生活，但在埃及，动物和人类生活在一起……。他们用脚揉面团，用手揉粘土……其他人，除非受到埃及人的影响，否则将生殖器保留在自然状态，但埃及人实行割礼。

Everywhere else in the world, priests have long hair, but in Egypt they shave their heads …. Everywhere else in the world people live separately from their animals, but animals and humans live together in Egypt …. They knead dough with their feet and clay with their hands … Other people, unless they have been influenced by the Egyptians, leave their genitals in their natural state, but the Egyptians practise circumcision.

埃及的习俗和行为就这样不断地延伸出来，它们的与众不同、独特性和独特性让新来者感到惊讶和惊讶。希罗多德在说：看，这些埃及人与我们希腊人如此不同，但我们却和平共处（此时埃及有许多希腊殖民地，其居民与当地居民相处融洽）。是的，希罗多德从不因差异而感到震惊，也从不谴责它。相反，他试图了解它、理解它并描述它。不同之处？它通过某种悖论只是为了强调更大的统一性，说明它的活力和丰富性。

And on and on like this stretches the long list of Egyptian customs and behaviors, which surprise and astonish the newcomer with their otherness, distinctness, uniqueness. Herodotus is saying: Look, these Egyptians are so different from us Greeks, and yet we coexist so well together (there are many Greek colonies in Egypt at this time, whose inhabitants live on friendly terms with the local population). Yes, Herodotus is never shocked at difference, never condemns it; rather, he tries to learn about it, to understand and describe it. Difference? It serves by some paradox only to emphasize a greater oneness, speaking to its vitality and richness.

与此同时，他又回到了他的伟大激情，几乎是他的痴迷：责备他的亲戚们的骄傲、自负、对自己优越性的信仰（来自希腊语，“野蛮人”一词源自“barbaros”一词，指的是不会说希腊语，而是胡言乱语、难以理解的人，同样，他们是低等、低等的存在。正是希腊人后来向其他欧洲人灌输了这种嗤之以鼻的倾向，希罗多德每一步都在与这种冲动作斗争。当他将希腊人和埃及人并列时，他就是这样做的——就好像特意前往埃及，为他的温和、谦虚和常识哲学收集材料和证据。
