All the while he returns to his great passion, his obsession almost: reproaching his kinsmen for their pride, their conceitedness, their belief in their own superiority (it is from the Greek that the word “barbarian” comes, from the word “barbaros,” signifying someone who does not speak Greek but rather something garbled, incomprehensible, and who by the same token is a lower, inferior being). It was the Greeks who later instilled in other Europeans this tendency to turn up one’s nose, and Herodotus fights the impulse every step of the way. And he does so when juxtaposing Greeks and Egyptians—as if purposely traveling to Egypt to gather there material and proof for his philosophy of moderation, modesty, and common sense.

他从一个基本的、超然的问题开始：希腊人从哪里得到他们的神？他们来自哪里？你是什​​么意思，它们从哪里来？希腊人回应。他们是我们的神！哦，不，亵渎希罗多德，我们的神是从埃及人那里得到的！

He begins with a fundamental, transcendent matter: Where did the Greeks get their gods? Where do they come from? What do you mean, where do they come from? the Greeks respond. They are our gods! Oh, no, blasphemes Herodotus, we got our gods from the Egyptians!

他是多么幸运，在一个大众传播还不存在、只有少数人听到或读过他的世界里，他宣布了这一点。如果他的观点被广泛传播，我们的希腊人就会立即被石头砸死，或者被烧死在柴堆上！但由于希罗多德生活在前媒体时代，他可以有把握地说，埃及人是世界上第一个举行普遍节日集会、宗教游行和游行的民族，而希腊人则向埃及人学习。那么伟大的希腊英雄赫拉克勒斯呢？ ......希腊人从埃及得到了赫拉克勒斯的名字，而不是相反......我有大量证据指向这个方向。这里仅列出一项：希腊赫拉克勒斯的父母安菲特律翁和阿尔克墨涅的血统可以追溯到埃及……。事实上，赫拉克勒斯是一位非常古老的埃及神。正如他们自己所说，在阿玛西斯国王统治之前一万七千年，十二神从八神中降下，他们将赫拉克勒斯视为十二神之一。

How fortunate for him that he proclaimed this in a world in which mass communication did not yet exist and only a handful of people heard or read him. If his views had been disseminated widely, our Greek would have been instantly stoned, or burned on the pyre! But because Herodotus lived in a pre-media epoch, he could safely say that the Egyptians were the first people in the world to hold general festive assemblies, and religious processions and parades, and the Greeks learnt from the Egyptians. And what of the great Greek hero Heracles? … The Greeks got the name of Heracles from Egypt, rather than the other way round … I have a great deal of evidence pointing in this direction. Here is just one item: both parents of the Greek Heracles, Amphitryon and Alcmene, trace their lineage back to Egypt …. In fact Heracles is a very ancient Egyptian god; as they themselves say, it was seventeen thousand years before the reign of King Amasis when the Twelve Gods descended from the Eight Gods, and they regard Heracles as one of the Twelve.

我想尽可能清楚地了解这些事情，所以我也航行到腓尼基的提尔，因为我听说那里有一座供奉赫拉克勒斯的圣所，我发现那里的圣所布置得非常奢华，有大量的奉献祭品……。我和那里的祭司交谈，问他们圣所建立多久了，我发现他们也不同意希腊的说法……

I wanted to understand these matters as clearly as I could, so I also sailed to Tyre in Phoenicia, since I had heard that there was a sanctuary sacred to Heracles there, and I found that the sanctuary there was very lavishly appointed with a large number of dedicatory offerings …. I talked to the priests of the god there and asked them how long ago the sanctuary was founded, and I discovered that they too disagreed with the Greek account …

这些调查中引人注目的是它们的世俗主义。事实上，他们完全没有神圣和崇高，也没有通常伴随这种谈话的庄严语言。在这段历史中，诸神并不是高不可攀的、绝对的、超世俗的东西；而是某种东西。讨论是实事求是的，围绕着一个简单的问题：谁发明了它们——希腊人还是埃及人？

What is striking in these investigations is their secularism; in fact, the total absence in them of the sacred and the sublime, of the solemn language that typically attends such discourse. In this history the gods are not something unattainable, absolute, super-worldly; the discussion is matter-of-fact and revolves around the simple question of who invented them—the Greeks or the Egyptians?

尖塔的景色

THE VIEW FROM THE MINARET

H埃罗多德与他的同胞的争论不是关于神的存在（我们的希腊人不会想象没有更高的存在的世界），而是关于谁借用了谁的名字和概念。

Herodotus’s dispute with his countrymen is not over the existence of gods (our Greek would not conceive of the world without Higher Beings), but over who borrowed from whom their names and concepts.

为了支持他的立场，即整个希腊万神殿，或者至少是其中的一个重要部分，都源自埃及万神殿，他提出了一个对他来说无可辩驳的论据——时间、优先权和年龄。他问道，哪种文化更古老，希腊文化还是埃及文化？他立即回答如下：作家赫卡泰俄斯不久前在底比斯。他研究过自己的血统，将家族的历史追溯到十六代神祖。因此，宙斯的祭司们对他做了他们对我所做的事情（我并没有研究过我的家族历史）：他们带我进入神庙，向我展示那里的木制人物，并为我数数，直到我提到过的数字 [341]。 （澄清：赫卡泰俄斯是希腊人，巨像是埃及人，他们每一个都象征着一代人。）请注意，你们希腊人，希罗多德似乎在说，我们的家谱只能追溯到十五代，而埃及人的家谱是341.那么究竟是谁借用了比我们古老得多的埃及人的神呢？如果我们不是从埃及人借来的神呢？为了更加强烈地让他的同胞们记住分隔两国的历史时期的鸿沟，他阐述：现在，人类三百代就是一万年，因为一百年有三代。他引用了埃及祭司的观点，即在这一时期没有神以人的形式出现。因此，希罗多德似乎得出结论，我们认为是我们自己的诸神，希腊诸神，已经在埃及存在了一万多年了！

To shore up his position that the entire Greek pantheon, or at least a significant portion of it, was derived from that of the Egyptians, he reached for what to him was an irrefutable argument—that of time, of precedence, of age. Which culture, he asks, is older, the Greek or the Egyptian? And he immediately answers as follows: Some time ago the writer Hecataeus was in Thebes. He had studied his own lineage and had traced his family history back to a divine ancestor in the sixteenth generation. So the priests of Zeus there did to him what they did to me too (not that I had looked into my family history): they took me into the temple, showed me the wooden figures there, and counted them for me, up to the number I have mentioned [341]. (Clarification: Hecataeus is a Greek, and the colossi are Egyptian, and each one of them symbolizes one generation.) Observe, you Greeks, Herodotus seems to be saying, our genealogy goes back barely fifteen generations, whereas that of the Egyptians runs as much as 341. So exactly who borrowed gods from whom, if not we from the Egyptians, who are so much older than we are? And in order all the more emphatically to impress upon his compatriots the chasm of historic time separating the two nations, he elaborates: Now, three hundred human generations make 10,000 years, because there are three generations in a hundred years. And he quotes the opinion of Egyptian priests, that during this period no god ever appeared in human form. And so, Herodotus seems to be concluding, the gods that we deem to be our very own, Greek gods, already existed in Egypt for more than ten thousand years!

如果人们承认希罗多德是正确的，并且不仅众神，而且整个文化都是从埃及（即非洲）传入希腊（即欧洲），那么人们就可以论证欧洲文化的非欧洲起源。关于这个问题的争论充满了意识形态和情感，已经持续了大约两半千年了，与其现在踏入如此危险的雷区，不如让我们注意一件事：在希罗多德的世界里，一个有着多种文化和文化的世界。不同文明并存，它们之间的关系十分多样且不稳定；我们知道一种文明与另一种文明发生冲突的例子，但也有其他文明保持着交换关系和相互债务关系，在政治上使彼此致富。此外，有些文明曾经打过仗，后来又合作了，但后来又发现自己再次陷入战争。简而言之，对于希罗多德来说，世界多元文化主义是一个活生生的、脉动的组织，其中没有任何东西是永久固定或定义的，但它不断地自我改造、变异，产生新的关系和背景。

If one accepts that Herodotus is correct and that not only the gods, but culture in its entirety arrived in Greece (i.e., in Europe) from Egypt (i.e., from Africa), then one could argue for the non-European origins of European culture. A debate about this, brimming with ideology and emotion, has been raging for some two and a half thousand years already, and instead of stepping now onto such a dangerous minefield, let’s note one thing: In Herodotus’s world, one in which many cultures and civilizations existed side by side, relations between them were quite varied and fluid; we know of instances in which one civilization was in conflict with another, but there were others which maintained relations of exchange and mutual indebtedness, enriching one another politically. Moreover, there were civilizations that had once fought but subsequently cooperated, only later to find themselves once again at war. In short, for Herodotus, the world’s multiculturalism was a living, pulsating tissue in which nothing was permanently set or defined, but which continually transformed itself, mutated, gave rise to new relationships and contexts.

我第一次看到尼罗河是在 1960 年。我第一次看到尼罗河是在晚上，当时我的飞机正在接近开罗。从高处看去，一天中这个时候的河流就像一条黑色的、闪闪发光的树干，分叉和分叉，周围环绕着路灯花环和明亮的玫瑰花结，界定了这座巨大而繁华的城市的广场。开罗在历史上的这个时刻是第三世界的中心解放运动。许多住在这里的人明天将成为新国家的总统，各种反殖民主义的非洲和亚洲政党都在这座城市拥有席位。

I first see the Nile in 1960. My initial glimpse is in the evening, as my airplane approaches Cairo. From up high, the river at this time of day resembles a black, glistening trunk, forking and branching, surrounded by garlands of streetlights and bright rosettes defining the squares of this immense and bustling city. Cairo at this moment in history is the hub of Third World liberation movements. Many who live here will tomorrow become the presidents of new states, and various anticolonial African and Asian political parties have their seats in the city.

开罗也是阿拉伯联合共和国的首都，该共和国于两年前随着埃及和叙利亚的联盟而成立，其总统是四十二岁的埃及人贾迈勒·阿卜杜勒·纳赛尔，他身材高大、魁梧、威严、有魅力的人物。 1952年，时年三十四岁的纳赛尔领导了军事政变，推翻了法鲁克国王；四年后他成为总统。长期以来，他面临着强烈的内部反对：一方面是共产党人与他作斗争，另一方面是穆斯林兄弟会这个由原教旨主义者和伊斯兰恐怖分子组成的阴谋组织。为了打击他们，纳赛尔两人都维持了数量众多的各种警察部队。

Cairo is also the capital of the United Arab Republic, which came into being two years earlier with the union of Egypt and Syria and whose president is the forty-two-year-old Egyptian Gamal Abdel Nasser—a tall, massive, commanding, and charismatic figure. In 1952, Nasser, then thirty-four, led the military coup that overthrew King Farouk; he became president four years later. For a long time he faced strong internal opposition: on the one hand Communists fought him, and on the other the Muslim Brotherhood, a conspiratorial organization of fundamentalists and Islamic terrorists. To combat them both Nasser maintained numerous police units of all sorts.

我起得很早，要去市中心，那儿有一段路。我住在扎马雷克的一家酒店，扎马雷克是尼罗河上一座岛上的一个相当富裕的住宅区，曾经主要是外国人的居住区，但现在也已经住满了富裕的埃及人。知道离开酒店后我的行李箱就会被搜查，我认为明智的做法是取出藏在那里的空瓶捷克比尔森啤酒并沿途处理掉（当时纳赛尔是一位热心的穆斯林，开展反酒精运动）。我把瓶子藏在一个灰色纸袋里，然后带着它走到街上。虽然还是早上，但已经很闷热了。

I rose early to go into the center of the city, which was a ways off. I was staying in a hotel in Zamalek, a rather wealthy residential neighborhood on an island in the Nile once largely the precinct of foreigners but by now also already inhabited by well-to-do Egyptians. Knowing that my suitcase would be searched as soon as I left the hotel, I thought it wise to remove an empty bottle of Czech pilsner beer I had stashed there and dispose of it along the way (in those days Nasser, a zealous Muslim, was conducting an anti-alcohol campaign). I concealed the bottle in a gray paper bag and walked out with it into the street. It was morning still, but already sultry and hot.

我环顾四周寻找垃圾桶。但正当我这么做的时候，我刚从入口处出来，就遇到了坐在凳子上的警卫的目光。他在观察我。呃,我想,我不会把瓶子当着他的面扔掉,因为等会儿他会在垃圾桶里翻找,找到它,然后向酒店警察。我走了一会儿，发现了一个空箱子。当我正准备把瓶子扔进去时，我注意到有两个人穿着长长的白色长袍。他们站着说话，同时看着我。不，我不能把瓶子扔在这里：他们肯定会看到它，而且，这个箱子不是用来扔垃圾的。我继续走，直到注意到另一个垃圾桶——坐在附近一栋大楼的入口处，一个阿拉伯人聚精会神地看着我。不，不，我对自己说，你不能冒险，他正用怀疑的眼神看着你。于是，我拎着袋子和瓶子，若无其事地走着。

I looked around for a garbage can. But as I was doing so, I encountered the gaze of a guard sitting on a stool in the entryway from which I had just emerged. He was observing me. Eh, I thought, I won’t throw out the bottle in front of him, because later he will rifle through the garbage can, find it, and report me to the hotel police. I walked on a bit and spotted an empty chest. I was on the verge of throwing in my bottle when I noticed two people in long white djellabas. They were standing and conversing, but at the same time watching me. No, I couldn’t dispose of the bottle here: they would surely see it, and, moreover, the chest was not meant for refuse. I kept walking until I noticed another garbage can—and sitting nearby, at the entrance of a building, an Arab gazing at me attentively. No, no, I said to myself, you cannot risk it, he is looking at you very suspiciously. So, bag and bottle firmly in my hand, I nonchalantly strolled on.

再往前走，有一个十字路口，十字路口中间站着一名拿着棍棒和哨子的警察——在一个角落里，一个人坐在凳子上，看着我。我注意到他只有一只眼睛，但这只眼睛如此执着、如此迫切地盯着我，我开始感到不舒服，甚至害怕他会命令我向他展示我所携带的到底是什么。我加快了脚步，想要离开他的视野，而且更加敏捷，因为我看到，在我前方的远处，有一个垃圾桶，像海市蜃楼一样闪烁。不幸的是，在离它不远的地方，在一棵瘦弱的小树的树荫下，坐着一位老人，盯着我看。

Further on lay an intersection, in the middle of which stood a policeman with a club and a whistle—and on one of the corners sat a man on a stool, watching me. I noticed that he had only one eye, but this eye stared at me so insistently, so importunately, that I started to feel uncomfortable, even afraid that he would order me to show him what it was exactly that I was carrying. I quickened my pace to remove myself from his field of vision, and did so with all the more alacrity because I saw, flickering like a mirage in the distance ahead of me, a garbage can. Unfortunately, not far from it, in the shade of a small, scrawny tree, was an elderly man—seated and staring at me.

街道现在转弯了，但转弯后一切都和以前一模一样。我无法把瓶子扔到任何地方，因为无论我在哪里尝试，我都会遇到有人的目光转向我的方向。汽车在街道上行驶，驴子拉着满载货物的大车，一小群骆驼僵硬地走过，就像踩着高跷一样，但这一切似乎都发生在背景中，发生在与我所在的飞机不同的飞机上。走路时，我会被陌生人的视线所吸引，他们站着、散步、说话，最常见的是坐着，同时一直盯着我在做什么。我越来越紧张，当我开始大汗淋漓时，报纸我手里的包已经湿透了。我担心瓶子会从里面滑出来，摔碎在人行道上，进一步引起街上的兴趣。我真的不知道下一步该做什么，于是我回到酒店，把瓶子塞回行李箱。

The street now turned, but beyond the turn everything was exactly as before. I couldn’t throw the bottle out anywhere, because no matter where I tried, I encountered someone’s gaze turned in my direction. Cars drove along the streets, donkeys pulled carts loaded with goods, a small herd of camels passed by stiffly, as if on stilts, but all this seemed to be taking place in the background, on some plane other than the one on which I was walking, caught in the sightlines of perfect strangers, who stood, strolled, talked, most frequently sat, and all the while stared at what I was doing. I grew increasingly nervous, and as I started to sweat profusely, the paper bag in my hand was getting soggy. I was afraid that the bottle would slip out of it and shatter on the sidewalk, further arousing the street’s interest. I was truly at a loss as to what my next move should be, so I returned to the hotel and stuffed the bottle back into my suitcase.

直到晚上，我才再次带着它出去。晚上比较容易。我把瓶子扔进垃圾桶，转身，舒舒服服地躺下睡觉。

It wasn’t until nighttime that I walked out with it again. It was easier at night. I dropped the bottle into a garbage can, turned back, and with relief lay down to sleep.

现在，在城市里散步时，我开始更仔细地观察街道。他们都有眼睛和耳朵。这边是大楼看门人，那边是警卫，那边是坐在沙滩椅上一动不动的人，再远一点的地方有一个人无所事事地站着，只是看着。这些人中的许多人并没有做什么特别的事情，但他们的多条视线组合在一起，形成了一个纵横交错、连贯的全景观察网，覆盖了整个街道的空间，在这个网络上，任何事情都不会被注意到。已注意到并报告。

Now, walking around the city, I began surveying the streets more closely. They all had eyes and ears. Here a building janitor, there a guard, over there a motionless figure in a beach chair, a bit farther on someone standing idly, just looking. Many of these people were not doing anything in particular, yet taken together their multiple lines of vision created a crisscrossing, coherent, panoptic observation network, covering the entire space of the street, on which nothing could occur without it being noticed. Noticed and reported.

这是一个有趣的主题：多余的人为蛮力服务。一个发达、稳定、有组织的社会是一个有着明确界定和界定角色的社会，而这对于大多数第三世界城市来说是不可能的。他们的社区大部分由未成形的、流动的元素组成，缺乏精确的分类，没有位置、场所或目的。在任何时候，无论出于什么原因，这些没人关注、没人需要的人，可以形成一群人、一群人、一群乌合之众，他们对一切都有自己的看法，有时间做一切事情，并且愿意参与某事，意味着某事。

It is an interesting subject: superfluous people in the service of brute power. A developed, stable, organized society is a community of clearly delineated and defined roles, something that cannot be said of the majority of Third World cities. Their neighborhoods are populated in large part by an unformed, fluid element, lacking precise classification, without position, place, or purpose. At any moment and for whatever reason, these people, to whom no one pays attention, whom no one needs, can form into a crowd, a throng, a mob, which has an opinion about everything, has time for everything, and would like to participate in something, mean something.

所有独裁政权都利用了这种闲置的岩浆。他们甚至不需要维持一支昂贵的专职警察队伍。接触这些寻求生命意义的人就足够了。让他们感觉自己是有用的，有人指望他们做某事，他们被注意到了，他们有目标。

All dictatorships take advantage of this idle magma. They don’t even need to maintain an expensive army of full-time policemen. It suffices to reach out to these people searching for some significance in life. Give them the sense that they can be of use, that someone is counting on them for something, that they have been noticed, that they have a purpose.

这种关系的好处是相互的。为独裁政权服务的街头百姓，开始感到与当局合一，感到自己重要、有意义，而且，由于他平时有一些小偷小摸、打架斗殴、诈骗等良心不安的事情，现在他获得了一种安慰的感觉。的免疫力。与此同时，独裁权力在他身上有一根廉价的——实际上是免费的——但热心且无所不在的代理人触角。有时甚至很难称这个人为特工；他只是一个想要被认可的人，一个努力引人注目的人，一个试图提醒当局他的存在的人，一个总是渴望提供服务的人。

The benefits of this relationship are mutual. The man of the street, serving the dictatorship, starts to feel at one with the authorities, to feel important and meaningful, and furthermore, because he usually has some petty thefts, fights, and swindles on his conscience, he now acquires the comforting sense of immunity. The dictatorial powers, meantime, have in him an inexpensive—free, actually—yet zealous and omnipresent agent-tentacle. Sometimes it is difficult even to call this man an agent; he is merely someone who wants to be recognized, who strives to be visible, seeking to remind the authorities of his existence, who remains always eager to render a service.

有一次，当我离开酒店时，其中一个人拦住了我，要求我跟着他——他会带我参观一座古老的清真寺（我猜测那个人就是其中之一，因为他总是站在同一个地方，观察着）他的节拍可能是什么）。我生性很容易轻信，甚至不认为怀疑是理性的表现，而是性格缺陷。现在，一名便衣特工提议参观一座清真寺，而不是命令我去警察局报到，这一事实让我松了口气，甚至是快乐，我毫不犹豫地同意了。他很有礼貌，穿着整洁的西装，英语说得还过得去。他告诉我他的名字叫艾哈迈德。 “我的名字叫里萨德，”我回答道，“不过叫我理查德吧，这样对你来说会更容易一些。”首先，我们步行。然后我们坐了很长时间的公共汽车。我们在一个古老的街区下车——狭窄的小街，蜿蜒的小巷，狭窄的过道，小宫殿，死胡同，弯曲的灰褐色土墙，波纹状的墙壁。铁皮屋顶。没有导游的情况下走进这里的人将不会走出去。偶尔会有一扇门，但全部都关着，插得紧紧的。这里荒无人烟。有时一个女人像影子一样匆匆而过，有时一群孩子出现，但小孩子们很快又消失了，因为艾哈迈德的叫声吓坏了。

Once, as I was leaving the hotel, one of these people stopped me and asked that I follow him—he would show me an old mosque (I surmised that the man was one of them, as he always stood in the same spot, surveying what was probably his beat). I am by nature quite credulous, to the point even of regarding suspicion not as a manifestation of reason but as a character flaw; now the fact that an undercover agent proposed a visit to a mosque instead of ordering me to report to a police station brought me such relief—joy, even—that I agreed without a moment’s hesitation. He was polite, wore a tidy suit, and spoke passable English. He told me that his name was Ahmed. “And mine is Ryszard,” I replied, “but call me Richard, that will be easier for you.” First, we walk. Then we ride for a long time on a bus. We get off in an old neighborhood—narrow little streets, winding alleyways, cramped passages, small palaces, dead ends, crooked, grayish-brown clay walls, corrugated tin roofs. Whoever walks in here without a guide will not walk out. An occasional door here and there, but all of them shut, bolted fast. It’s deserted. Sometimes a woman hurries past like a shadow, sometimes a group of children appear, but the little ones quickly vanish again, frightened by Ahmed’s shouts.

我们到达两扇巨大的金属门前，艾哈迈德在门上敲出了密码。里面传来凉鞋拖拉的声音，然后是钥匙插入锁孔的大声刮擦声。一名年龄和外貌不明的警卫打开门，与艾哈迈德交谈了几句。他带领我们穿过一个封闭的小庭院，到达尖塔的大门，尖塔的门槛稍微陷入地面。门开着，两个人都示意我进去。里面弥漫着浓浓的暮色，但我只能辨认出沿着尖塔内墙延伸的蜿蜒楼梯的轮廓，其形状类似于一个大型工厂烟囱。在高处的某个地方，有一个光点在闪烁，从我们站的地方看去，它就像一颗苍白而遥远的星星——那就是天空。

We arrive at a pair of massive metal doors, on which Ahmed taps out a code. There’s a shuffling of sandals within, then the loud scraping of a key in the lock. A guard of indeterminate age and appearance opens the door and exchanges a few words with Ahmed. He leads us across a small enclosed courtyard to the doors of the minaret, its threshold slightly sunken into the ground. The doors are open, and both men gesture to me that I should enter. A dense twilight prevails within, but I can just make out the outlines of a winding staircase running along the minaret’s interior wall, which in its shape resembles a large factory chimney. Somewhere way up high gleams a point of light, which from where we are standing looks like a pale and distant star—it is the sky.

“我们去！”艾哈迈德用一种半是哄骗、半是命令的声音宣称，他早些时候告诉我，从尖塔的顶端可以看到开罗的整个景色。 “壮观的景色！”他向我保证。我们出发了。从一开始，事情看起来就不太好。楼梯不仅极其狭窄，而且很滑，上面覆盖着沙子和松散的灰泥。但最糟糕的是，它们没有扶手、没有把手、没有绳索——没有东西可以抓住。

“We go!” declares Ahmed, in a half-cajoling, half-commanding voice, having earlier told me that from the minaret’s summit one can see all of Cairo. “Great view!” he assured me. We set off. From the start things don’t look good. The stairs are not only extremely narrow, but slippery, covered in sand and loose plaster. But the worst thing is that they have no handrail, no handles, no ropes—nothing to grab on to.

哦，好吧——我们出发了。我们爬啊爬啊。

Oh, well—we’re off. We climb and we climb.

最重要的是不要往下看。既不向下也不向上，但仅在正前方最近的点处，台阶正前方与视线齐平。关闭自己的想象力，因为想象力只会让人恐惧。某种瑜伽会很有用，某种涅槃和坦陀罗、卡门或莫科什，能让人不思考、不感觉、不存在。

The most important thing is to not look down. Neither down nor up, but only at the closest point straight ahead, the steps directly in front at eye level. To turn off one’s imagination, because the imagination only frightens. Some sort of yoga would be useful, some sort of nirvana and tantra, karman or mokosh, something that would allow one not to think, not to feel, or be.

那好吧。又关了。

Oh, well. Off again.

这里又黑又窄。陡峭而扭曲。如果清真寺正在使用，宣礼员会从宣礼塔的顶部召唤信徒每天祈祷五次。这些叫声拖得很长，用单曲的形式发出，有时非常优美——庄严、感人、浪漫。但这座尖塔没有任何迹象表明它已经被任何人使用过很多年了。这是一个废弃的地方，散发着潮湿和陈旧灰尘的味道。

It is dark and cramped. Steep and twisting. From up there, from the top of the minaret, if the mosque is in use, a muezzin calls the faithful to prayer five times a day. These are drawn-out calls, uttered in singsong, sometimes very beautiful—solemn, moving, romantic. But nothing about this minaret suggested that it had been used by anyone in years. It was an abandoned place, smelling of dankness and old dust.

我不知道是因为努力，还是因为一种仍然模糊但日益增长的焦虑，但我开始感到疲劳，而且一定明显放慢了速度，因为艾哈迈德开始催促我前进。

I don’t know if it was from the effort, or from a still vague yet growing anxiety, but I started to feel fatigued and must have slowed down noticeably, because Ahmed began urging me on.

“向上！向上！”而因为他走在我的身后，他封锁了我一切转身、后退、逃跑的可能性。我无法折返并超越他——深渊就在那里，就在旁边。好吧，就是这样，我对自己说，除了继续攀登，别无他法。

“Up! Up!” And because he walked behind me, he blocked all possibility of my turning around, retreating, escaping. I could not double back and pass him—the abyss was right there, to the side. Well, that’s that, I said to myself, there’s nothing to do but keep climbing.

我们爬啊爬啊。

We climb and we climb.

我们已经沿着这条危险的楼梯爬了这么远，没有栏杆或扶手，任何一个突然的动作都可能导致我们俩从几层楼上摔下来。我们因一种矛盾的对贱民的拥抱而团结在一起——无论谁敢触碰对方，都肯定会追随他。

We had already ascended so far along this dangerous stairway with no railing or handholds that any sudden motion either of us might make would cause us both to tumble down several stories. We were united by a paradoxical embrace of untouchability—whoever would so much as touch the other would surely fall after him.

这种对称性很快就会变得对我不利。楼梯的尽头，也就是最顶层，有一个又小又窄的外部露台，环绕着宣礼塔——宣礼员的栖息处。通常，它会被砖块或金属屏障包围。这里的屏障似乎是金属的，但几个世纪后它已经生锈并脱落了。露出地面的墙没有任何保护。艾哈迈德轻轻地将我推到外面，他自己仍然站在楼梯上，安全地靠在墙上的开口上，说道：

That symmetry would soon change to my disadvantage. At the end of the stairs, at the very top, was a small, narrow exterior terrace that encircled the minaret—the muezzin’s perch. Normally, it would be surrounded by a brick or metal barrier. Here it appeared that the barrier had been metal, but after many centuries it had rusted and fallen away; the outcropping in the wall had no protection. Ahmed gently pushed me outside and, still standing on the stairs himself, leaning safely against the opening in the wall, said:

“给我你的钱。”

“Give me your money.”

我把钱放在裤子口袋里，我担心即使是伸手进去这样一个微不足道的动作也会让我摔倒在地。艾哈迈德注意到我的犹豫，这次用更尖锐的语气重复道：

I had my money in my pants pocket, and was afraid that even as insignificant a gesture as reaching into it would cause me to plunge to the ground. Ahmed noticed my hesitation and repeated, this time in a sharper tone of voice:

“给我你的钱！”

“Give me your money!”

抬头仰望天空，一切都只是为了不低头，小心翼翼地，小心翼翼地，我把手伸进口袋，然后慢慢地、非常慢地掏出钱包。他二话不说接过，转身就往下爬。

Looking up into the sky, anything just to not look down, carefully, cautiously, I slid my hand inside my pocket, and then just as slowly, very slowly, pulled out my wallet. He took it without a word, turned around, and started climbing down.

现在最困难的是从裸露的露台回到楼梯第一级的路线——不到一米的距离，我一厘米一厘米地跨过，痛苦不堪。然后是下降的痛苦，我似乎不是靠自己的腿，而是靠奇怪沉重、几乎瘫痪的四肢，感觉就像被钉在墙上一样。

Now the most difficult thing was the route back from the exposed terrace to the first step of the stairs—a distance of less than one meter, which I crossed centimeter by painful centimeter. And then the torment of the descent, which I managed seemingly not on my own legs, but on oddly heavy, almost paralytic limbs that felt as though they had been nailed to the wall.

警卫为我打开了大门，一些孩子——这些小巷里最好的向导——带我上了一辆出租车。

The guard opened the gates for me, and some children—the best guides in such back alleys—led me to a taxi.

我在扎马雷克又住了几天。我沿着和以前一样的街道走到开罗市中心。我每天都会遇到艾哈迈德。他总是站在同一个地方，掩盖他的节奏。

I lived for several more days in Zamalek. I walked to downtown Cairo along the same street as before. I encountered Ahmed every day. He always stood in the same spot, covering his beat.

他面无表情地看着我，就好像我们从未见过一样。

He looked at me with no expression on his face, as if we had never met.

我相信，我也面无表情地看着他，就好像我们从未见过面一样。

And I looked at him, I believe, also without expression, as if we had never met.

阿姆斯特朗音乐会

ARMSTRONG’S CONCERT

阿坝州喀土穆，1960 年

Khartoum, Aba, 1960

乙从喀土穆机场汇合时，我告诉出租车司机：维多利亚酒店。但他一言不发，没有任何解释或理由，就带我去了格兰德酒店。

Emerging from the airport in Khartoum, I told the taxi driver: Hotel Victoria. But without a word, with no explanation or justification, he took me to the Grand.

“总是这样，”我后来在这里遇到的一位利比亚人解释道。 “如果一个白人到达苏丹，他们认为他一定是一个英国人，如果他是一个英国人，那么他自然一定会住在格兰德酒店。这是一个很好的聚会场所。晚上大家都来这里。”

“It’s always like this,” explained a Libyan I met here later. “If a white man arrives in the Sudan, they think he must be an Englishman, and if he is an Englishman, then naturally he must be staying at the Grand. It’s a good meeting place. Everybody comes here in the evening.”

司机一手把我的行李箱从后备箱里拎出来，另一只手在空中画了一个半圆，示意我能看到的景色，然后自豪地说：“青尼罗河！”我望着脚下流淌的河水，呈灰翠色，很宽阔，水流湍急。酒店的露台又长又阴凉，面向河流，与河流之间隔着一条宽阔的林荫大道，林荫大道两旁种满了古老的枝条无花果树。

The driver, lifting my suitcase out of the trunk with one hand, inscribed a semicircle in the air with the other, indicating the kind of view I would have, and said with pride: “Blue Nile!” I looked at the river flowing below us—it had a grayish-emerald hue, was very wide, and flowed swiftly. The terrace of the hotel, long and shady, gave onto the river and was separated from it by a wide boulevard lined with old, branching fig trees.

门房带我进去的房间里，吊扇呼呼作响，但它的叶片并没有冷却，只是搅动着热空气。我想，这里是一座熔炉，于是决定进城。我不知道自己在做什么：刚走了几百米，我就意识到自己掉进了陷阱。地面上的天空散发出的热量使我的身体紧贴在沥青路面上。我的头是我呼吸急促。我感觉无法再走下去，但我也发现自己已经没有回头的力气了。我开始惊慌：如果我不尽快找到阴凉处，我确信太阳会杀死我。我开始疯狂地四处张望，却发现整个街区里只有我在动。我周围的一切都是死气沉沉的，紧闭的，静止的。没有人，没有任何动物。天哪，我该怎么办？太阳像铁匠的锤子一样敲打在我的头上；我能感觉到它的打击。离酒店太远了，附近没有建筑物，没有入口，没有遮阳篷，没有庇护所。终于，我发现了一棵芒果树。这是我能看到的最近的东西，我拖着身子向它走去。

A ceiling fan whirred in the room to which the porter led me, but its blades did not cool, merely churned the hot air about. It’s a furnace in here, I thought, and decided to go into town. I had no idea what I was doing: I had walked barely several hundred meters when I realized that I had fallen into a trap. The heat emanating from the sky above ground me against the asphalt. My head was pounding and I was short of breath. I felt unable to walk further, yet I also realized that I lacked the strength to turn back. I started to panic: if I didn’t find some shade soon, the sun, I was certain, would kill me. I began to look about frantically, but saw that in the entire neighborhood I was the only moving thing. Everything around me was lifeless, shuttered, still. No people, not a single animal. My god, what was I to do? The sun was beating like a blacksmith’s hammer on my head; I could feel its blows. It was too far to the hotel, and there was no building nearby, no entryway, no awning—no shelter. Finally, I spotted a mango tree. It was the closest thing in sight, and I dragged myself to it.

我走到树干旁，滑到地上，进入阴凉处。在这样的时刻，阴影是一种完全有形的东西，身体贪婪地接受它，就像干裂的嘴唇吸取水一样。

I reached the trunk and slid onto the ground, into the shade. Shade in such moments is something utterly tangible, and the body receives it as greedily as parched lips imbibe water.

下午，影子拉长，开始重叠，然后变暗，最后变成黑色——傍晚了。人就活过来了，求生的欲望也回来了；他们互相打招呼，交谈，显然很高兴他们能够以某种方式忍受日常的灾难，在地狱中又度过了一天。城市开始热闹起来，汽车出现在街道上，商店和酒吧里挤满了人。

In the afternoon the shadows lengthen, start to overlap, then darken and finally turn to black—it is evening. People come alive then, their will to live returns; they greet one another, converse, clearly happy that they have somehow managed to endure the quotidian cataclysm, to survive yet another day from hell. The city starts to bustle, cars appear in the streets, shops and bars fill with people.

我在喀土穆等待两名捷克记者，我将与他们一起前往刚果。整个国家正被内战的大火所吞噬。我越来越焦躁，因为我的同伴们仍然没有出现，他们本来应该从开罗飞来的。白天不可能在城里走走，而且房间里也太热了。我在露台上再也受不了了，因为每隔一会儿就会有人走到我面前问，你是谁？你从哪来？你叫什么名字？你为什么来这里？你想创业吗？买种植园？如果不，那么接下来你要去哪里？你一个人吗？你有家人吗？你有几个孩子？你做什么工作？您以前来过苏丹吗？您喜欢喀土穆吗？尼罗河呢？你的酒店呢？你的房间呢？

I am in Khartoum awaiting two Czech journalists, with whom I am to travel to the Congo. The country is ablaze, consumed by the fires of civil war. I am growing agitated, because there is still no sign of my companions, who were supposed to fly in from Cairo. It is impossible to walk around the city by day, but it’s also too hot in the room. And I can’t stand it any longer on the terrace, because every few moments someone walks up to me and asks, Who are you? Where are you from? What is your name? Why did you come here? Do you want to start a business? Buy a plantation? If not, then where will you go next? Are you alone? Do you have a family? How many children do you have? What do you do? Have you been in the Sudan before? How do you like Khartoum? And the Nile? And your hotel? And your room?

问题没有尽头。最初几天我都会礼貌地回答他们。如果按照当地习俗，出于友好的好奇心而询问他们怎么办？另一方面，提出问题的人也可能是警察——最好不要激怒他们。

The questions have no end. For the first few days I politely answer them. For what if they’re being asked out of friendly curiosity, in accordance with local custom? On the other hand, it could be that those who are asking are from the police—better not to irritate them.

提问者通常只出现一次，第二天就会被新的队伍取代。看来我就像接力赛中的接力棒一样被传递着。

The questioners usually appear just once, replaced the following day by a fresh contingent; I am being passed along like a baton in a relay race, it seems.

然后他们中的两个——总是在一起——开始更频繁地出现。他们非常友好。我想，自从执政军政府首领阿布德将军关闭了这所大学，将其作为不满和叛乱滋生的温床以来，学生们现在有很多空闲时间。

Then two of them—always together—started to appear more repeatedly. They were extremely friendly. Students, I guess, with a lot of time on their hands these days, since the chief of the ruling junta, General Abboud, had shut down the university, as a breeding ground of discontent and rebellion.

有一天，他们警惕地环顾四周，低声问我是否愿意给他们几英镑——他们会买一些大麻，我们会离开城市，进入沙漠。面对这样的offer该怎么办？我从未吸过大麻，很好奇它的感觉如何。另一方面，如果这两个人来自警察，并试图陷害我，也许是向我勒索钱财，或者将我驱逐出境怎么办？而这正是一段令人着迷的旅程的开始。我很紧张，但还是选择了大麻并给了他们钱。

One day, looking warily around them, they ask sotto voce if I would give them several pounds—they will buy some hashish, we will go out of the city, into the desert. What to do in the face of such an offer? I have never smoked hashish and am curious about how it makes one feel. On the other hand, what if these two are from the police, and trying to set me up, perhaps to extort money from me or else have me deported? And this at the very start of a journey that is promising to be so fascinating. I’m nervous, but choose the hashish and give them the money.

傍晚时分，他们开着一辆破旧的敞篷路虎停在了这里。它只有一个头灯，但却像防空反射镜一样坚固。它的光束将热带的黑暗分开，就像一堵看似无法穿透的黑墙，它暂时打开，让汽车通过，然后又立即关闭。黑暗如此浓密如果不是因为坑坑洼洼的路面，人们会产生这样的印象：车辆实际上是静止不动的。

They pull up in the early evening in a beat-up, open Land Rover. It has only one headlight, but it is as strong as an antiaircraft reflector. Its beam parts the tropical darkness, a seemingly impenetrable black wall, opening it for a moment to allow the car through before it immediately closes in again. So dense is the dark that one would have the impression—were it not for the brutal potholes—that the vehicle is actually standing still.

我们开了大约一个小时，一开始是在柏油路上，路边又薄又摇摇欲坠，很快就变成了一条沙漠公路，沿途偶尔有巨大的巨石，就像青铜铸造的一样。在其中一个路口，我们急转弯，继续行驶了一会儿，然后突然停下来。我们位于悬崖顶端，下面的尼罗河在月光的照射下闪烁着银色的光芒。景观被简化为极简主义的理想——沙漠、河流、月亮——此时此刻就足够了。

We drive for maybe an hour, at first on blacktop, thin and crumbling along the edges, which soon peters out into a desert road along which lie occasional immense boulders, as if cast in bronze. At one of them we make a sharp turn and drive on for a moment longer, then come to a sudden halt. We are at the top of an escarpment, and the Nile glimmers silver below, illuminated by the moon. The landscape is reduced to a minimalist ideal—desert, river, moon—which at this moment is world enough.

一名苏丹人从包里拿出一小瓶已经打开的白马酒，足够我们每人喝几口。然后他小心翼翼地将两根粗关节扭在一起，一根递给他的朋友，一根递给我。在火柴的光亮中，我突然看到他从夜色中显现出来，黝黑的脸和闪亮的眼睛，他看着我，似乎在考虑什么。我想，也许他给了我毒药，但我不知道我是否真的这么想，或者我是否想过任何事情，因为我已经在另一个世界了，在这个世界里，我已经失去了重量，一切都在其中无形体，一切都在运动。这个动作轻柔、柔和、呈波浪状。那是一种温柔的摇曳。前面没有任何东西，没有任何东西爆炸。一切都很平静。令人愉快的触感。一个梦。

One of the Sudanese removes from his bag a small and already opened bottle of White Horse, enough for a couple of swallows for each of us. Then he carefully twists together two thick joints, handing one to his friend and one to me. In the light of the match I suddenly see, emerging from the night, his dark face and shining eyes, with which he looks at me as if he is considering something. Perhaps he has given me poison, I think, but I don’t know if I actually thought that, or if I thought about anything at all because I am already in another world, one in which I have become weightless, in which everything is incorporeal and everything is in motion. This movement is gentle, soft, wavy. It is a tender swaying. Nothing barrels ahead, nothing explodes. All is calm and quiet. A pleasant touch. A dream.

但最非同寻常的是失重状态。这并不是我们在宇航员身上看到的那种笨拙、笨拙的失重状态，而是一种灵活、熟练、可控的状态。

But the most extraordinary thing is the state of weightlessness. Not that awkward, ungainly weightlessness we have seen with astronauts, but a nimble, adroit, controlled one.

我不记得我是如何准确地从地面升起的，但我清楚地记得漂浮在天空中，天空是黑暗的，但黑暗中却是明亮的，甚至是发光的，在五彩缤纷的圆圈中翱翔，这些圆圈分开，旋转，充满了整个空间。周围，​​就像呼拉圈旋转的光芒。

I do not remember how precisely I rose up off the ground, but I do distinctly remember floating through the skies, which were dark but of a darkness that was bright, even luminous, soaring amidst multicolored circles which parted, revolved, filled the space all around, and which resembled the light twirling of hula hoops.

沿着这条路航行，我感到无比的喜悦，因为我从自己身体的负担中解放出来，从它每一步所呈现的阻力中解放出来，从它顽固的、无情的反对中解放出来。谁会想到，事实证明，你的身体不一定是你的敌人，相反，哪怕只是片刻，哪怕只是在这种特殊的情况下，它也可以成为你的朋友。

Sailing along this way, I feel immense joy at being liberated from the burden of my own body, from the resistance it presents at every step, from its stubborn, relentless opposition. Who would have thought, but it turns out that your body need not be your enemy but rather can, if only for a moment, if only under such extraordinary circumstances, be your friend.

我可以看到路虎的引擎盖就在我面前，眼角的余光还可以看到破碎的后视镜。地平线呈深粉色，沙漠的沙呈石墨灰色。黎明前的尼罗河是浅海军蓝。我坐在敞篷车里，冻得瑟瑟发抖。一天中的这个时候，沙漠像西伯利亚一样寒冷；寒冷刺骨。

I can see in front of me the hood of the Land Rover, and out of the corner of my eye the shattered side-view mirror. The horizon is intensely pink, and the sand of the desert a graphite gray. The Nile in this predawn moment is a light navy. I am sitting in the open car and trembling from the cold. At this time of day the desert is as cold as Siberia; the chill pierces you to the bone.

但当我们再次进城时，太阳已经升起，瞬间又炎热起来。头痛欲裂。我唯一想要的就是睡觉。只是为了睡觉。不动，不存在，不生活。

But by the time we are once again entering the city, the sun has risen and it is instantly hot again. A terrible headache. The only thing I want is to sleep. Just to sleep. To not move, to not be, to not live.

两天后，苏丹人来到酒店问我感觉如何。我感觉怎么样？哦，我的朋友们，你们想知道我的感受吗？是的，你感觉怎么样，因为路易斯·阿姆斯特朗要来，明天体育场有一场音乐会。

Two days later, the Sudanese came to the hotel to ask how I was feeling. How am I feeling? Oh, my friends, you want to know how I feel? Yes, how do you feel, because Louis Armstrong is coming, there’s a concert tomorrow in the stadium.

我立刻就好了。

I am instantly better.

体育场距离城外有一段距离，又小又浅，最多只能容纳五千名观众。即便如此，座位也只有一半。场地中央矗立着一个讲台，灯光微弱，但我们坐在靠近前面的位置，可以清楚地看到阿姆斯特朗和他的小型管弦乐队。晚上天气炎热，空气不流通，当阿姆斯特朗穿着夹克、打着领结走出去时，他已经湿透了。伴随着汗水。他向大家打招呼，举起金色小号的手，对着廉价的噼啪作响的麦克风说，他很高兴能在喀土穆演奏，不仅高兴，而且非常高兴，之后他开始了他的全部，轻松而富有感染力的笑声。笑声引来了其他人的笑声，观众们却保持着冷漠的沉默，不知该如何是好。鼓声和贝斯声响起，阿姆斯特朗开始唱起一首适合当时和地点的歌曲——“南方的睡眠时间”。实际上很难说人们第一次听到阿姆斯特朗的声音是什么时候；其中有一种东西让人觉得人们已经永远知道它了，当他开始唱歌时，每个人都怀着对自己鉴赏力最真诚的信念宣称：为什么是的，那就是他，那就是萨奇莫！

The stadium was quite a distance outside the city, small, shallow, with a capacity of at most five thousand spectators. Even so, only half the seats were occupied. In the center of the field stood a podium, weakly illuminated, but we were sitting near the front and could see Armstrong and his small orchestra well. The evening was hot and airless, and when Armstrong walked out, attired moreover in a jacket and bow tie, he was already soaked with perspiration. He greeted everyone, raising into the air the hand holding his golden trumpet, and said into the cheap, crackling microphone that he was pleased to be playing in Khartoum, and not only pleased, but downright delighted, after which he broke into his full, loose, infectious laugh. It was laughter that invited others to laugh along, but the audience remained aloofly silent, not quite certain how to behave. The drums and the bass resounded and Armstrong launched into a song appropriate enough to the time and place—“Sleepy Time Down South.” It is actually difficult to say when one first heard Armstrong’s voice; there is something in it that makes one feel one has known it forever, and when he starts to sing, everyone, with the most sincere conviction of his or her connoisseurship, proclaims: Why yes, that’s him, that’s Satchmo!

是的，那就是他——萨奇莫。他唱了“你好多莉，这是路易斯，多莉”，他唱了“多么美好的世界”和“月亮河”，他唱了“我触摸你的嘴唇，火花四溅，那些魔鬼的嘴唇。”但观众们仍然静静地坐着。没有掌声。他们不明白这些话吗？这一切是否有太多公开表达的色情内容，不符合穆斯林的口味？

Yes, that was him—Satchmo. He sang “Hello Dolly, this is Louis, Dolly,” he sang “What a Wonderful World” and “Moon River,” he sang “I touch your lips and all at once the sparks go flying, those devil lips.” But the spectators continued to sit silently. There was no applause. Did they not understand the words? Was there too much openly expressed eroticism in all this for Muslim tastes?

每一首曲子结束后，甚至在演奏和演唱过程中，阿姆斯特朗都会用一块大白手帕擦脸。这些手帕是由一个人不断地为他更换的，他陪伴阿姆斯特朗环游非洲的唯一目的似乎就是这个。后来我看到他有一整袋，可能有几十个。

After each number, and even during the playing and singing, Armstrong wiped his face with a large white handkerchief. These handkerchiefs were constantly changed for him by a man whose sole purpose in accompanying Armstrong around Africa seemed to have been this. I saw later that he had an entire bag of them, dozens and dozens probably.

音乐会结束后，人们迅速散去，消失在夜色中。我很震惊。我听说阿姆斯特朗的音乐会引起了极大的热情、狂热和狂喜。尽管萨奇莫演奏了许多来自美国南部的歌曲，但在喀土穆的体育场里却看不到这些狂喜的痕迹。阿拉巴马州和路易斯安那州，他本人来自那里——这些歌曲起源于非洲奴隶。但那时他们的非洲和这里的非洲属于不同的世界，缺乏共同语言，无法交流，更不用说情感上的统一了。

After the concert people dispersed quickly, vanishing into the night. I was shocked. I had heard that Armstrong’s concerts elicited great enthusiasm, frenzy, ecstasy. There was no trace of these raptures in the stadium in Khartoum, despite the fact that Satchmo played many songs from the American South, from Alabama and Louisiana, where he himself came from—songs that had originated with African slaves. But by then their Africa and this one here belonged to different worlds, lacking a common language, unable to communicate much less partake of an emotional oneness.

苏丹人开车送我回酒店。我们坐在露台上喝了一些柠檬水。过了一会儿，一辆车送来了阿姆斯特朗。他如释重负地在一张桌子旁坐下，或者更准确地说，他瘫倒在椅子上。他是个粗壮的男人，肩膀又宽又下垂。服务员给他端来了一杯橙汁。他一口气喝了下去，然后又喝了一杯，又喝了一杯。他疲惫不堪，低着头坐着，一言不发。当时他六十岁了，而且——我当时并不知道——已经患有心脏病。音乐会期间的阿姆斯特朗和音乐会结束后的阿姆斯特朗是两个完全不同的人：第一个是快乐的、开朗的、充满活力的，声音洪亮，能够从他的小号中发出令人惊叹的音调；第二个是一个充满活力的人。第二个则沉重、疲惫、虚弱，脸上布满皱纹，毫无生气。

The Sudanese drove me back to the hotel. We sat down on the terrace for some lemonade. Moments later a car brought Armstrong. He sat down with relief at a table, or, more precisely, he collapsed into the chair. He was a stout, thickset man with wide, drooping shoulders. The waiter brought him an orange juice. He downed it in a single gulp, and then another glass, and another. He was depleted, sitting with his head bowed, silent. He was sixty years old at the time and—something I didn’t then know—already suffering from heart disease. Armstrong during the concert and Armstrong immediately after it were two entirely different people: the first was merry, cheerful, animated, with a powerful voice, able to coax an astonishing range of tones from his trumpet; the second was heavy, exhausted, weak, his face covered in wrinkles, extinguished.

无论谁离开喀土穆的安全城墙进入沙漠，都必须记住危险和陷阱正在等待着。沙尘暴不断地改变景观的结构，移动方向点，如果由于这些无情的自然反复无常，旅行者迷失了方向，他肯定会灭亡。沙漠是神秘的，会让人产生恐惧。没有人会独自进入其中，主要是因为没有人能够携带足够的水来克服一口井与另一口井之间的距离。

Whoever leaves the safe walls of Khartoum and sets off into the desert must remember that danger and traps lie in wait. Sandstorms constantly change the configuration of the landscape, moving the orientation points about, and if as a result of these relentless natural caprices the traveler should lose his way, he will surely perish. The desert is mysterious and can arouse fear. No one goes off into it alone, largely because no one can carry enough water to conquer the distance separating one well from the next.

在埃及之旅中，希罗多德知道撒哈拉沙漠无处不在，因此明智地留在河边，始终靠近到尼罗河。沙漠是阳光明媚的火，火是野兽，可以吞噬一切：埃及人将火视为有生命的生物（它会吞噬它所掌握的一切，直到最后，当它满足时，它会与它一起死亡）他举了一个例子，当时波斯国王冈比西斯出发征服埃及，然后是埃塞俄比亚，派遣部分军队对抗居住在绿洲上的阿蒙人。撒哈拉。这些军队从底比斯出发，经过七天的行军穿过沙漠到达一座名为绿洲的城市，此时他们的所有踪迹都消失了：……此后，唯一可用的信息直接或间接来自阿蒙人本身；没有人能说出他们身上发生了什么，因为他们没有到达阿蒙人那里，也没有回来。然而，阿蒙人对他们的失踪作出了解释。他们说，在军队离开绿洲并穿过沙漠向他们进发时——换句话说，在绿洲和他们的土地之间的某个地方——一股异常强劲的南风，带着成堆的沙子，在他们的时候袭击了他们。正在吃午饭并埋葬他们。

During his trip through Egypt, Herodotus, knowing that the Sahara was all around, wisely kept to the river, staying always close to the Nile. The desert is a sunny fire, and fire is a wild beast, which can devour everything: the Egyptians regard fire as a living creature (one which consumes everything it takes hold of until at last, when it is sated, it dies along with the object it has been devouring) … And he offers as an example what happened when the king of the Persians, Cambyses, having set off to conquer Egypt and then Ethiopia, dispatched part of his army against the Ammonians, a people inhabiting the oases of the Sahara. These troops, departing from Thebes, arrived after seven days of marching through the desert in a city called Oäsis, at which point all trace of them disappeared: … after that the only information available comes directly or indirectly from the Ammonians themselves; no one else can say what happened to them, because they did not reach the Ammonians and they did not come back either. The Ammonians, however, add an explanation for their disappearance. They say that after the army had left Oäsis and was making its way across the desert towards them—in other words, somewhere between Oäsis and their lands—an extraordinarily strong south wind, carrying along with it heaps of sand, fell on them while they were taking their midday meal and buried them.

捷克人杜尚和贾达抵达，我们立即出发前往刚果。刚果一侧的第一个定居点是一个路边小村庄——阿巴。它坐落在巨大的绿色墙壁的阴影下，丛林从这里突然开始，像一座陡峭的山峰从平原上拔地而起。

The Czechs arrived, Dushan and Jarda, and we set off immediately for the Congo. The first settlement on the Congolese side was a roadside hamlet—Aba. It nestled in the shade of an enormous green wall, the jungle, which began here abruptly, rearing up like a steep mountain from the plain.

阿坝有一个加油站，还有几家商店。这些地方被腐烂的木制拱廊遮蔽，拱廊下面躺着几个无所事事、一动不动的人。当我们停下来询问这个国家的更深处有什么在等待着我们，以及我们可以在哪里将一些英镑兑换成当地法郎时，他们就活跃起来了。

There was a gas station in Aba, as well as several shops. These were shaded by decaying wooden arcades, beneath which lounged several idle, motionless men. They sprang to life when we stopped to ask about what awaited us deeper inside the country, and where we could change some pounds into local francs.

他们是希腊人，形成了一个类似于希罗多德时代已经分散在世界各地的数百个殖民地的殖民地。这种类型的定居点显然在他们中间至今仍然存在。

They were Greeks, and formed a colony similar to the hundreds that were already scattered around the world in Herodotus’s time. That type of settlement had clearly survived among them to this day.

我的包里有一本希罗多德的书，当我们离开时，我把它拿给一位正在告别的希腊人看。他看到封面上的名字，笑了，但笑得我不知道他是在表达自豪，还是因为不知道这是谁而感到尴尬。

I had my copy of Herodotus in my bag, and when we were leaving I showed it to one of the Greeks as he was saying goodbye. He saw the name on the cover and smiled, but in such a way that I couldn’t tell if he was expressing pride, or else embarrassment at having no idea who this was.

佐庇鲁斯的脸

THE FACE OF ZOPYRUS

瓦在保利斯（刚果东部省）小镇的郊区，我们已经陷入瘫痪。我们的汽油已经用完了，希望有一天有人会经过这条路，并同意给我们一些，如果只有一罐罐头就好了。在那之前，我们只能在唯一可能的地方等待——一所由比利时传教士开办的学校，院长是虚弱、瘦弱、病重的皮埃尔神父。由于该国正处于内战之中，传教士们指导他们的部队进行军事演习。孩子们四人行进，肩上扛着又长又粗的棍子，边唱边喊。他们的表情是多么的严肃，动作是多么的矫健，这场军人的游戏是多么的庄严而又激动人心！

We have come to a standstill on the outskirts of the little town of Paulis (Congo, Eastern Province). We have run out of gas, and live in hope that someone will pass this way one day and agree to give us some, if only a jerry can full. Until then, we wait in the only place possible—a school run by Belgian missionaries, whose prior is the delicate, emaciated, and seriously ailing Abbé Pierre. Because the country is in the grips of civil war, the missionaries instruct their charges in military drills. The children march around in fours, holding long thick sticks against their shoulders, singing and shouting out. How stern their facial expressions are, how vigorous their gestures, how at once solemn and exciting is this game of soldier!

我在学校营房尽头的一间空教室里有一张婴儿床。这里很安静，我几乎听不到战斗演习的声音。前面是一个开满鲜花的花坛——郁郁葱葱的热带大丽花、唐菖蒲、百人花，还有其他美丽的植物，我还是第一次见到，也不知道它们的名字。

I have a cot in an empty classroom at the end of the school barracks. It is quiet here, and the sounds of the battle drills barely reach me. Out front is a flower bed full of blooms—lush, tropically overgrown dahlias and gladioli, centauries, and still other beauties, which I am seeing for the first time and whose names I do not know.

我也感染了战争的蔓延——不是当地的战争，而是另一场远在时间和地点的战争，波斯国王大流士正在对叛逆的巴比伦发动战争，希罗多德也描述过这一点。我坐在阳台的阴凉处，一边打苍蝇和蚊子，一边读他的书。

I too am infected with the contagion of war—not the local one, but another, distant in place and time, which the king of the Persians, Darius, is waging against rebellious Babylon, and which Herodotus describes. I am sitting in the shade on the verandah, swiping at flies and mosquitoes and reading his book.

大流士是一个二十多岁的年轻人，刚刚成为当时世界上最强大帝国的国王。在这个多民族的领域，一个或另一个民族不断地抬起头，反抗，为自由而战。所有这些起义和叛乱都被波斯人毫不留情地镇压下去。但这一次，一个巨大的威胁出现了，一个真正的危险，可能会严重影响波斯的命运：十九年前，即公元前538年，被居鲁士国王并入波斯帝国的巴比伦帝国的首都巴比伦正在发生叛变。

Darius is a young, twenty-something-year-old man, who has just become the king of what was then the world’s most powerful empire. In this multinational realm, one people or another are constantly lifting up their heads, rebelling, and battling for liberty. All such uprisings and revolts the Persians quash with ruthless ease. But this time a great threat has arisen, a genuine danger that could severely affect Persia’s destiny: Babylon, the capital of the Babylonian empire that had been incorporated by King Cyrus into the Persian empire nineteen years earlier, in 538 b.c.e., is in mutiny.

巴比伦渴望独立并不奇怪。它位于连接东西方、南北的贸易路线的交汇处，是地球上最大、最具活力的城市。它是世界文化和学习的中心，尤其以数学和天文学、几何学和建筑学而闻名。一个世纪之后，希腊雅典才能与它匹敌。

That Babylon desires independence is hardly surprising. Situated at the intersection of trade routes connecting the East with the West and the North with the South, it is the largest and most dynamic city on earth. It is the center of world culture and learning, renowned especially for mathematics and astronomy, geometry and architecture. A century will pass before Greek Athens is able to rival it.

目前，巴比伦人正在准备反波斯起义和宣布主权。他们的时机很好。他们知道波斯宫廷刚刚经历了长期的无政府状态，在此期间权力一直由贤士祭司阶层掌握。他们最近在一群波斯精英发动的宫廷政变中被推翻，他们刚刚从他们之中选出了一位新国王——大流士。希罗多德指出，巴比伦人做好了充分的准备。显然，他写道，他们度过了法师统治的整个混乱时期……为围攻做准备，但不知怎的，没有人注意到他们正在这样做。

For the time being, the Babylonians are preparing an anti-Persian uprising and a declaration of sovereignty. Their timing is good. They know that the Persian court has just come through a long period of anarchy, during which power had been held by the priestly caste of the Magi. They were recently overthrown in a palace coup staged by a group of Persian elites, who had only just selected from among themselves a new king—Darius. Herodotus notes that the Babylonians were very well prepared. Clearly, he writes, they had spent the whole troubled period of the Magus’ rule … getting ready for a siege, and somehow nobody had noticed that they were doing so.

下面的段落现在出现在希罗多德的文本中：一旦他们的叛乱公开化，这就是他们所做的。巴比伦男人聚集了城里所有的女人——除了她们的母亲和每个男人从自己家里选出的一名女人——并将她们勒死。这名单身女子继续担任厨师，而其他人则被勒死以节省物资。

The following passage now appears in Herodotus’s text: Once their rebellion was out in the open, this is what they did. The Babylonian men gathered together all the women of the city—with the exception of their mothers and of a single woman chosen by each man from his own household—and strangled them. The single woman was kept on as a cook, while all the others were strangled to conserve supplies.

我不知道希罗多德是否意识到他在写什么。他有想过这句话吗？因为当时，在六世纪，巴比伦至少有二三十万居民。因此，数以万计的妇女——妻子、女儿、姐妹、祖母、表兄弟姐妹、情人——被判处绞杀。

I do not know if Herodotus realized what he was writing. Did he think about those words? Because at that time, in the sixth century, Babylon had at least two to three hundred thousand inhabitants. It follows, then, that tens of thousands of women were condemned to strangulation—wives, daughters, sisters, grandmothers, cousins, lovers.

对于这次大规模处决，我们的希腊人没有再多说什么。谁的决定？人民议会的那个？市政府的？保卫巴比伦委员会的？是否对此事进行了一些讨论？有人抗议吗？谁决定了处决的方式——这些妇女将被勒死？还有其他建议吗？例如，他们会被长矛刺穿吗？还是用刀砍倒？还是在柴堆上烧掉？或者扔进流经城市的幼发拉底河？

Our Greek says nothing more about this mass execution. Whose decision was it? That of the Popular Assembly? Of the Municipal Government? Of the Committee for the Defense of Babylon? Was there some discussion of the matter? Did anyone protest? Who decided on the method of execution—that these women would be strangled? Were there other suggestions? That they be pierced by spears, for example? Or cut down with swords? Or burned on pyres? Or thrown into the Euphrates, which coursed through the city?

还有更多问题。那些一直在家里等待男人们从对她们宣判的会议回来的妇女们，能从她们男人的脸上看出什么吗？优柔寡断？耻辱？疼痛？疯狂？小女孩们当然没有怀疑。但年纪大的呢？直觉不会告诉他们一些事情吗？所有的人都遵守约定的沉默吗？他们的良心没有受到打击吗？难道他们都没有经历过歇斯底里的发作吗？尖叫着满街跑？

There are more questions still. Could the women, who had been waiting in their homes for the men to return from the meeting during which sentence was pronounced upon them, discern something in their men’s faces? Indecision? Shame? Pain? Madness? The little girls of course suspected nothing. But the older ones? Wouldn’t instinct tell them something? Did all the men observe the agreed silence? Didn’t conscience strike any of them? Did none of them experience an attack of hysteria? Run screaming through the streets?

然后？随后，他们将他们全部聚集起来并勒死。一定有一个集合地点，每个人都必须在那里报告并进行选拔。那些要住在一侧的人。其他人呢？是否有某种市政警卫将带到他们面前的女孩和妇女一一勒死？或者，丈夫和父亲必须在被任命监督的法官在场的情况下亲自将她们勒死吗？处决？周围一片寂静吗？或者是否有呻吟声，并恳求饶恕婴儿、女儿、姐妹的生命？数以万计的尸体是如何处理的？因为体面地安葬死者是生者持续安宁的条件；如果没有它，死者的灵魂就会在夜间回来并折磨幸存者。从那一刻起，巴比伦的夜晚就让人们感到恐惧了吗？他们惊慌地醒来了吗？噩梦困扰着他们吗？他们无法入睡吗？他们是否感觉到恶魔掐住了他们的喉咙？

And later? Later, they gathered them all together and strangled them. There must have been a meeting place, where everyone had to report and where the selection took place. Those who were to live to one side. And the others? Were there municipal guards of some sort who strangled one by one the girls and women brought to them? Or did the husbands and fathers have to strangle them themselves, in the presence of judges appointed to supervise the executions? Was there silence? Or were there moans, and pleading for the lives of infants, daughters, sisters? How were the bodies disposed of, the tens of thousands of them? Because a decent burial of the dead is a condition for the continued peace of the living; without it, the spirit of the departed returns by night and torments the survivors. Did Babylon’s nights terrify its men from that moment onward? Did they wake in panic? Did nightmares haunt them? Were they unable to fall asleep? Did they feel demons seizing them by the throat?

以节约物资。是的，因为巴比伦人正在准备长期围困。他们了解巴比伦的价值，这是一座富饶繁荣的大都市，一座拥有空中花园和镀金神庙的城市，并且知道大流士不会轻易撤退，而是会尽最大努力继续征服他们，如果不是通过剑，那么通过围攻饥饿。

To conserve supplies. Yes, because the Babylonians were preparing for a long siege. They understood the value of Babylon, a rich and flowering metropolis, a city of hanging gardens and gilded temples, and knew that Darius would not readily retreat but would do his utmost to continue their subjugation, if not by the sword, then by siege of hunger.

波斯国王一刻也没有浪费。叛乱的消息一传到他耳中，他就集结了全部军队，向他们进军。他一到达巴比伦就开始围攻这座城市，但居民们却丝毫不担心。他们常常爬上城墙的堡垒，在那里昂首阔步，嘲笑大流士和他的军队。有一次，他们中的一个人喊道：“波斯人，你们坐在那里做什么？你为什么不走开呢？只有当骡子开始生育时，巴比伦才会落入你的手中。”（正如我们所知，骡子通常是不育的。）

The king of the Persians did not waste a moment. As soon as news of the rebellion reached him, he mustered his army in full strength and marched against them. Once he reached Babylon he began to besiege the city, but the inhabitants were not in the slightest bit concerned. They used to climb up to the bastions of the city wall and strut about there, taunting Darius and his army. Once one of them called out, “What are you doing sitting there, men of Persia? Why don’t you just go away? Babylon will fall into your hands only when mules start bearing young.” (And mules, as we are meant to know, are usually infertile.)

他们嘲笑大流士和他的军队。

They jeered at Darius and his army.

让我们想象一下这个场景。世界上最强大的军队已经抵达巴比伦城门。它在城市周围扎营，周围是巨大的粘土砖墙。城墙高数米，宽达四匹马拉的马车，可以在城墙上行驶。有八个大门，整个建筑还受到一条深护城河的保护。面对这座巨大的防御工事，大流士的军队束手无策。火药在世界的这一地区出现还需要一千二百多年的时间。再过两千多年才发明火器。甚至没有任何攻城机器——波斯人没有攻城锤、投石车。因此，巴比伦人感到自己所向无敌，能够肆无忌惮地行事——他们不会有任何事情发生。因此，难怪他们站在城墙上嘲笑大流士和他的军队。嘲讽世界上最伟大的军队！

Let us imagine this scene. The world’s largest army has arrived at the gates of Babylon. It has made camp around the city, which is encircled by massive walls of clay brick. The city wall is several meters high and so wide that a wagon drawn by four horses all in a row can be driven along its top. There are eight great gates, and the whole thing is additionally protected by a deep moat. In the face of this monumental fortification, Darius’s army is helpless. It will be twelve hundred more years before gunpowder makes its appearance in this part of the world. Firearms won’t be invented for another two thousand years. There aren’t even any siege machines—the Persians do not possess battering rams, catapults. So the Babylonians feel invincible, able to behave with impunity—nothing can happen to them. It is no wonder, therefore, that standing atop their wall, they were taunting Darius and his army. Taunting the greatest army in the world!

双方距离很近，被围困者和被围困者可以交谈，前者咒骂后者的名字。如果大流士碰巧骑得靠近墙壁，他就能听到最恶毒的谩骂和辱骂。这是相当屈辱的，尤其是因为围困已经持续了很长时间：一年零七个月过去了，大流士和他的部下因无法战胜巴比伦人而感到沮丧。

The distance between both sides is so small that the besieged and the besiegers can converse, the former cursing the latter’s names. If Darius happens to ride close to the walls, he can hear the worst invectives and terms of abuse hurled his way. It is quite humiliating, all the more so because the siege has lasted so long already: A year and seven months passed, and Darius and his men were getting frustrated with their inability to overcome the Babylonians.

然后有些事情发生了变化。第二十个月，Zopyrus 发生了一件非凡的事情……：他的一只驮骡生下了孩子。

And then something changes. In the twentieth month a remarkable thing happened to Zopyrus … : one of his packmules gave birth.

年轻的佐皮洛斯是波斯贵族梅加比祖斯的儿子，属于波斯帝国的小精英。听到他的骡子产下后代的消息，他感到很兴奋。他从中看到了众神的迹象，他们的信号表明巴比伦确实可以被征服。他去找大流士，讲述了一切，并询问占领巴比伦对他来说有多重要。

Young Zopyrus is the son of the Persian noble Megabyzus and belongs to the small elite of the Persian empire. He is excited by the news that his mule has produced offspring. He sees a sign from the gods in this, their signal that Babylon can indeed be conquered. He goes to Darius, recounts everything, and asks how important the capture of Babylon is to him.

非常，达流斯回答道。但波斯人围攻巴比伦城已经快两年了，他们已经尝试了无数的手段、计谋和诡计，但都没有对巴比伦的城墙造成丝毫的削弱。大流士灰心丧气，不知道该怎么办：羞愧地撤军，而且还失去了帝国最重要的领地；到继续前进，征服这座城市的前景看起来微乎其微。

Very, Darius replies. But the Persians have been laying siege to the city for almost two years, they have already tried countless methods, stratagems, and subterfuges, none of which have made even the slightest dent in the walls of Babylon. Darius is discouraged and doesn’t know what to do: to withdraw covered in shame, and, moreover, losing the empire’s most important satrapy; to press on, the prospects for conquering the city looking impossibly slim.

怀疑、困境、犹豫。看到国王如此沮丧，佐皮洛斯试图找到一种方法，让他能够成为导致巴比伦陷落的人，作为自己的成就。想了想之后，他拿起一把铁刀或铜刀，割掉了自己的鼻子和耳朵，剃了光头（就像罪犯剃光头一样），然后对自己进行了鞭打。就这样，他被肢解了，受伤了，流着血，向大流士展示了自己。看到佐皮洛斯的伤势，大流士陷入了震惊。他大叫一声从王座上跳起来，质问到底是谁毁了他的容貌，为何而毁容。

Doubts, dilemmas, hesitations. Seeing the king so dejected, Zopyrus tried to find a way whereby he could be the one to bring about the fall of Babylon, as his own achievement. After giving it some thought, he takes up an iron or brass knife, cuts off his own nose and ears, shaves his head (as criminals are shorn), and has himself flogged. Thus mutilated, wounded, streaming with blood, he presents himself to Darius. At the sight of Zopyrus’s injuries, Darius goes into shock. He jumped up from his throne with a cry and asked who it was who had disfigured him and why.

他鼻子上的伤口，受损的骨头，一定非常疼痛，上唇、脸颊和脸的其他部分肯定肿胀得奇形怪状，他的眼睛流着血，但佐皮洛斯还是设法回答：

The wound where his nose had been, the damaged bone, must have been horribly painful, and the upper lip, cheeks, and the rest of his face were surely grotesquely swollen, his eyes bloodied, and yet Zopyrus manages to answer:

“没有人对我这么做，大人；毕竟，你是唯一可以做到的人。我对自己这么做，因为我认为亚述人嘲笑波斯人是可怕的。”

“No one did it to me, my lord; after all, you are the only person who could. I did it to myself, because I think it’s dreadful to have Assyrians mocking Persians.”

大流士对此回应：

To which Darius replies:

“不，那根本不行。声称你给自己造成这些永久性伤害是为了对我们围困的人采取一些行动，这是为了掩饰你行为的完全卑鄙。认为你的受伤可能会加速对手投降的想法是愚蠢的。你一定是脑子有病才会把自己毁成这样。”

“No, that won’t do at all. To claim that you have given yourself these permanent injuries as a way of doing something about the people we are besieging is to gloss over the utter vileness of your deed. It’s just stupid to think that your injuries might hasten our opponents’ surrender. You must be out of your mind to have disfigured yourself like this.”

在佐皮鲁斯之前的陈述中，希罗多德向我们展示了一种几千年前就在这种文化中表现出来的心态，即一个尊严受到损害、感到自己受到羞辱的人可以使自己摆脱耻辱和耻辱的灼烧感。只能通过自我毁灭的行为。我觉得我已经伤痕累累，伤痕累累，我无法生存。死亡比脸上烙着羞耻印记的生命更可取。 Zopyrus 想要将自己从正义中解放出来这样的感觉。他通过改变自己的脸来做到这一点，将巴比伦人嘲笑的可耻的波斯相貌变成了更可怕的版本。

In Zopyrus’s preceding statement Herodotus presents to us a mind-set that had manifested itself in this culture thousands of years before—namely, that a man whose dignity was undermined, who felt himself humiliated, could free himself from the burning sensation of shame and disgrace only by an act of self-destruction. I feel that I have been scarred, and being thus scarred, I cannot live. Death is preferable to a life with the mark of shame burning into my face. Zopyrus wants to liberate himself from just such a feeling. And he does this by altering his face, changing that ignominious Persian physiognomy which the Babylonians mocked into a more dreadful and terrifying version of itself.

值得注意的是，佐皮洛斯并不认为巴比伦人的侮辱是针对他个人的个人不公正行为。他没有说，他们侮辱了我；他说，他们侮辱了我们——我们所有波斯人。然而，他并不认为劝告所有波斯人参战是摆脱这种可耻困境的出路，而是选择了一种独特的、个人的自我毁灭（或自残）行为，这对他来说是一种解放。

It is noteworthy that Zopyrus does not consider the Babylonians’ affront as an individual act of injustice, directed against him alone. He does not say, They insulted me; he says, They insulted us—all us Persians. Yet he does not see exhorting all Persians to war as the way out of this degrading predicament and chooses instead a singular, individual act of self-destruction (or self-mutilation), which for him is a liberation.

确实，大流士谴责佐皮鲁斯的行为是不负责任和鲁莽的，但他很快就会利用这一点，将其作为最后的手段，拯救国家、帝国、君主权力的威严免遭耻辱。

It is true that Darius condemns Zopyrus’s action as irresponsible and reckless, but he will soon take advantage of it, seizing upon it as a last resort, a way to save the nation, the empire, the majesty of monarchical power from disgrace.

他接受了佐皮洛斯的计划，计划如下：佐皮洛斯将前往巴比伦人那里，假装他正在逃离大流士对他的迫害和折磨。还有什么比他的伤口更好的证明呢！他确信他会说服巴比伦人，赢得他们的信任，并让他指挥军队。然后他会让波斯人进入巴比伦。

He accepts Zopyrus’s plan, which is as follows: Zopyrus will go to the Babylonians, pretending that he is fleeing from persecutions and tortures inflicted upon him by Darius. And what better proof of this than his wounds! He is certain that he will convince the Babylonians, that he will gain their confidence, and that they will give him command of the army. And then he will let the Persians into Babylon.

有一天，巴比伦人在城墙上注意到一个衣衫褴褛、血迹斑斑的人影正拖着脚步走向他们的城市堡垒。这个可怜虫不断回头张望，确保自己没有被追赶。塔楼上的瞭望员发现了他，跑下来，打开一扇门，问他是谁，来这里做什么。他回答说他是佐皮洛斯，他正在投奔他们。这时，守门人把他带到了巴比伦议会，他站出来向他们抱怨他的痛苦。他指责大流士自残……他说，“……。他这样残害我，肯定不会逃脱惩罚。”

One day, from atop their wall, the Babylonians notice a bloodied human figure dressed in rags dragging itself toward their city-fortress. The wretch keeps looking over his shoulder, checking to be sure he is not being pursued. The look-outs posted on the towers spotted him, ran down, opened one of the gates a crack, and asked him who he was and what he had come for. He answered that he was Zopyrus and that he was deserting to their side. At this, the gatekeepers took him to the Babylonian council, where he stood forth and complained to them about his sufferings. He blamed Darius for his self-inflicted injuries … He said, “…. He will certainly not get away with mutilating me like this.”

议会相信了这些话，并给了他一支军队，以便他能够报仇。这正是 Zopyrus 所等待的。按照预先安排，在佐皮洛斯假装逃往巴比伦后的第十天，大流士派遣了一千名最弱的部队前往被围困的城市的一扇大门。巴比伦人从城门冲出，将波斯人杀得只剩最后一人。七天后，按照大流士和佐皮鲁斯的事先安排，波斯国王再次派出另一支低等士兵队伍前往巴比伦城门，这次有两千人，而巴比伦人在佐皮鲁斯的指挥下也消灭了这些士兵。佐皮洛斯在巴比伦人中的名声越来越大：他们认为他是英雄和救世主。二十天过去了，大流士按照计划又派出了四千名士兵。巴比伦人也消灭了他们，然后感激地任命佐皮洛斯为他们的总司令和城市的保卫者。

The council believes these words and gives him an army, so that he can exact his vengeance. That is precisely what Zopyrus was waiting for. As prearranged, on the tenth day after Zopyrus’s pretend flight to Babylon, Darius sends one thousand of his weakest troops toward one of the besieged city’s gates. The Babylonians burst out from the gate and cut down the Persians to the last man. Seven days later, once again as Darius and Zopyrus have prearranged, the Persian king sends another contingent of inferior soldiers to Babylon’s gate, two thousand of them this time, and the Babylonians, under Zopyrus’s command, decimate these as well. Zopyrus’s fame among the Babylonians grows: they consider him a hero and a savior. Twenty days pass and in accordance with the plan Darius sends out another four thousand soldiers. The Babylonians annihilate them, too, and then gratefully appoint Zopyrus their commander in chief and defender of the city.

佐皮洛斯现在拥有所有大门的钥匙。在指定的日子，大流士从四面八方袭击巴比伦，佐皮洛斯打开了城门。这座城市被征服了。现在巴比伦人已经落入他的手中，大流士拆毁了城墙，拆毁了所有的城门……他还把大约三千名最杰出的人物钉在木桩上。

Zopyrus is now in possession of the keys to all the gates. On the appointed day, Darius storms Babylon from all sides, and Zopyrus opens the gates. The city is conquered. Now that the Babylonians were in his power, Darius demolished the city wall and tore down all its gates … and he also had about three thousand of the most prominent men impaled on stakes.

希罗多德再次以最随意的方式对待这些灾难性事件。让我们跳过拆除墙壁的过程吧——尽管这一定是一项艰巨的任务。但要把三千人钉在火刑柱上？这是怎么做到的？当巴比伦人排成一排等待轮到他们时，是否已经立好了木桩？每个人都在看着面前的人被刺穿吗？他们一定要阻止他们逃跑吗？或者他们只是因为恐惧而瘫痪？巴比伦是世界学术中心，是世界杰出数学家和天文学家的城市。他们也被刺穿了吗？如果是这样，那么这种报应阻碍了多少代、甚至几个世纪以来人类知识的增长？

Once again Herodotus treats these catastrophic events in a most offhanded fashion. Let’s skip the demolishing of the walls—although this must have been a gargantuan undertaking. But impaling three thousand men on the stake? How was this done? Was one stake set, as the men of Babylon stood in a line, awaiting their turn? Did each look on as the man in front of him was impaled? Were they bound to prevent their escape? Or were they simply paralyzed with fear? Babylon was the center of world learning, a city of the world’s preeminent mathematicians and astronomers. Were they also impaled? If so, then for how many generations, centuries even, did this retribution stunt the growth of human knowledge?

但与此同时，大流士也在思考这座大都市及其居民的未来。他将这座城市归还给剩下的巴比伦人，让他们住在那里。正如前面所解释的，巴比伦人勒死他们的妻子，以确保她们有足够的食物。为了保证有足够的妇女生育后代，大流士命令所有附近的民族把妇女送到巴比伦，并给每个人一个配额，结果总共有五万妇女聚集在那里。今天的巴比伦人就是这些妇女的后裔。

But at the same time Darius was thinking about the future of that metropolis and its inhabitants. He returned the city to the remaining Babylonians and let them live there. As was explained earlier, the Babylonians had strangled their wives to ensure that they had enough to eat; so in order to make sure that they would have enough women to have offspring, Darius ordered all the nearby peoples to send women to Babylon, and gave each a quota, which resulted in a grand total of fifty thousand women congregating there. Today’s Babylonians are descended from these women.

作为奖励，他授予佐皮洛斯终身指挥巴比伦的权力。但据说大流士经常表示，他宁愿看到佐皮洛斯没有受伤，也不愿再获得二十个巴比伦。

As a reward, he gave Zopyrus command over Babylon for the rest of his life. But it is said that Darius often expressed the opinion that he would prefer to see Zopyrus without his injuries than gain twenty more Babylons.

野兔

THE HARE

其箭锐利

      ，其弓折

      马蹄，其轮如燧石，其轮如旋风。

Whose arrows are sharp

      and all their bows bent

      their horses’ hoofs shall be counted like flint and their wheels like a whirlwind.

——以赛亚书 5:28

—ISAIAH 5:28

时间波斯国王结束了一次征服，立即开始了另一次征服：占领巴比伦后，大流士亲自指挥的下一次军事远征是针对斯基泰人的。

The king of Persia concludes one conquest and immediately embarks on another: After the capture of Babylon, the next military expedition commanded by Darius in person was against the Scythians.

考虑一下巴比伦与斯基泰领土的关系。在希罗多德时代，为了从一处到达另一处，人们必须轻松穿越一半的已知世界。游行肯定持续了几个月。那时一支军队要一个月才能走五六百公里，我们这里建议多几倍的距离。

Consider where Babylon lay in relation to Scythian territories. To get from one to the other, one had to traverse easily half of the known world in Herodotus’s time. The march must have lasted months. It took a month in those days for an army to cover five or six hundred kilometers, and here we are proposing a distance several times greater.

即使对坚强的大流士来说，这项事业也肯定造成了损失。是的，他乘坐的是一辆皇家马车，但人们很容易想象，在那些日子里，即使是这样的马车也一定摇摇欲坠，嘎嘎作响。弹簧和悬架、轮胎甚至橡胶圈都是未知的。此外，在漫长的旅程中根本就没有道路。

This enterprise surely took its toll even on hardy Darius. Yes, he rode in a royal carriage, but one can easily imagine that in those days even such a conveyance must have lurched and rattled horribly. Springs and suspensions were unknown, as were tires or even rubber rings. Furthermore, over long stretches of the journey there would have been no roads at all.

因此，野心必须足够强大，足以克服所有不适、疲劳和身体疼痛的感觉。在大流士的在这种情况下，他是为了扩大他的帝国，并通过这样做来增加他对世界的影响力。思考那个时代的人们对“世界”的理解是很有趣的。仍然没有足够的地图、地图册或地球仪。托勒密要再过四个世纪才能诞生，墨卡托也要再过两千年才能诞生。鸟瞰我们的星球是不可能的（甚至可能有这样的概念吗？）。一个人通过了解不是自己人的邻居来获得地理知识，并且通过口头传播这一知识：

The ambition must therefore have been powerful enough to overrule all feelings of discomfort, fatigue, physical pain. In Darius’s case it was to expand his empire, and by doing so to increase his sway over the world. It is interesting to ponder what people in those times understood by “the world.” There were still no adequate maps, atlases, or globes. Ptolemy would not be born for another four centuries, Mercator not for another two millennia. It was impossible to gaze down on our planet taking a bird’s-eye view (could there even have been such a concept?). One acquired geographical knowledge by becoming aware of a neighbor not of one’s own people, and one passed on that knowledge orally:

我们被称为吉利加玛人。我们的邻居是阿斯比斯塔人。而你，Asbystae，你与谁有共同边界？我们吉利加玛？我们与 Auschisae 共享一个。还有 Auschisae 和 Nasamones。那你呢，纳萨莫内斯？南边是加拉曼特人，西边是马卡埃人。还有这些马卡埃——他们与谁相邻？马卡埃人毗邻金达内斯人。你呢？我们与食莲族有共同的边界。和他们？与奥塞斯。谁生活在更远的地方，真正遥远的地方？阿蒙人。而超越他们呢？亚特兰蒂斯人。那么亚特兰蒂斯之外呢？没有人知道，甚至没有人尝试去想象。

We are called the Giligamae. Our neighbors are the Asbystae. And you, Asbystae, whom do you share a border with? We Giligamae? We share one with the Auschisae. And the Auschisae with the Nasamones. And you, Nasamones? To the south, with the Garamantes, and to the west, with the Macae. And these Macae—whom do they adjoin? The Macae abut the Gindanes. And you? We share a border with the Lotus-eaters. And they? With the Ausees. And who lives beyond that, truly far, far away? The Ammonians. And beyond them? The Atlantes. And beyond the Atlantes? No one knows, and no one even attempts to imagine.

因此，没有人像学校（当时还不存在）所教授的那样，看一眼地图（毕竟还不存在）就可以确定俄罗斯与中国相邻。为了确定这一事实，人们必须询问数十个西伯利亚部落（首先选择向东行进），直到最终遇到那些与中国接壤的部落。但是，当大流士出发对抗斯基泰人时，他已经对他们有了一些了解，并且或多或少知道到哪里寻找他们。

So there was no glancing at a map (which didn’t exist, after all) to ascertain, as is taught in schools (which didn’t yet exist, either), that Russia neighbors China. To determine that fact, one had to question dozens of Siberian tribes (having first elected to travel in an easterly direction), until one finally encountered those that shared a border with the Chinese ones. But when Darius set out against the Scythians, he already possessed some knowledge about them and knew—more or less—where to look for them.

这位伟大的统治者专注于征服世界，他的行动有点像一位狂热但仍然有条不紊的收藏家。他对自己说：我已经有了爱奥尼亚人，我已经有了卡里亚人，吕底亚人。谁失踪了？我缺少特拉基尼亚人，我缺少盖塔人，我缺少斯基泰人。刹那间，他心中开始燃烧起想要占有那些仍无法掌控的东西的欲望。而他们，仍然自由和独立，却还不明白，通过吸引伟大统治者的注意力，他们已经导致了对自己的判决。剩下的事情也不只是时间问题。因为这样的判决很少是带着鲁莽和不负责任的冲动而执行的。通常在这种情况下，万王之王就像一个潜伏的掠食者，他已经将猎物纳入了他的视野，并耐心地等待着有利的时刻。

The Great Ruler, occupied with the conquest of the world, goes about it somewhat like an avid but still methodical collector. He says to himself: I already have the Ionians, I have the Carians and the Lydians. Who’s missing? I lack the Trachinians, I lack the Getae, I lack the Scythians. And instantly the desire to possess those still beyond his grasp begins to burn in his heart. Whereas they, still free and independent, do not yet comprehend that by attracting the attention of the Great Ruler, they have caused a sentence to be passed upon themselves. Nor that the rest is merely a matter of time. Because rarely is such a sentence carried out with reckless and irresponsible impetuosity. Usually in these situations, the King of Kings resembles a skulking predator, who already has his prey in his ken and waits patiently for the propitious moment.

诚然，在人事领域，也需要借口。重要的是赋予它普遍命令或神圣戒律的地位。选择的范围并不大：要么是我们必须保卫自己，要么是我们有义务帮助别人，要么是我们在履行天意。最佳借口将所有这三个动机联系起来。攻击者应该以受膏者的荣耀出现，以那些在他所选择的神的眼中得到青睐的人的角色出现。

In the realm of human affairs, admittedly, one also needs a pretext. It is important to give it the rank of a universal imperative or of a divine commandment. The range of choices is not great: either it is that we must defend ourselves, or that we have an obligation to help others, or that we are fulfilling heaven’s will. The optimal pretext would link all three of these motives. The attackers should appear in the glory of the anointed, in the role of those who have found favor in his chosen god’s eye.

大流士的借口？

Darius’s pretext?

几个世纪前，斯基泰人入侵了米底人（与波斯人一样的另一个伊朗民族）的领土，并统治了他们二十八年。达流斯决定为这一现已被遗忘的事件报仇。我们这里有希罗多德定律的另一个例子：开始做某事的人总是要负责的，并且因为他做了一些恶事，所以他必须受到惩罚，无论事后多少年。大流士发起了针对斯基泰人的战役。

Centuries ago, the Scythians invaded the territory of the Medes (another Iranian people, like the Persians) and ruled over them for twenty-eight years. Darius decides that he will avenge this now-forgotten episode. We have here another example of Herodotus’s law: the one who started something is always responsible, and because he did some evil, he must be punished, however many years after the fact. Darius launches a campaign against the Scythians.

很难定义斯基泰人。

It is difficult to define the Scythians.

它们似乎凭空出现，存在了一千年，然后消失了，留下了美丽的金属文物和埋葬死者的土堆。他们将自己组织成群体，后来甚至形成了居住在东欧和亚洲大草原的部落联盟。他们的精锐和先锋是皇家斯基泰人——这些骑马的战士，焦躁不安且贪婪，他们的大本营是黑海以北、多瑙河和伏尔加河之间的土地。

They appeared seemingly out of nowhere, existed for a thousand years, and then vanished, leaving behind beautiful metal artifacts and the mounds in which they buried their dead. They organized themselves into groups, later even a confederation of tribes that inhabited the expanses of eastern Europe and the Asiatic steppe. Their elite and vanguard were the Royal Scythians—mounted warriors, restless and rapacious, whose home base was the lands to the north of the Black Sea, between the Danube and the Volga.

斯基泰人也是一个可怕的神话。他们的名字是陌生而神秘的民族的代名词，他们野蛮而残忍，不知从何而来，袭击、掠夺、砍杀、绑架。

The Scythians were also a terrifying myth. Their name was synonymous with foreign and mysterious peoples, savage and cruel, who swoop down out of nowhere, attack, loot, slash, kidnap.

很难近距离看到斯基泰人的土地、他们的家园和牛群，因为一切都被雪白的面纱遮住了：根据斯基泰人的说法，在他们北方邻居的领土之外，有这样的羽毛堆，什么都看不见，也无法穿越土地。他们说，陆地和空中有太多羽毛，无法发挥视觉功能。希罗多德这样评论：根据斯基泰人的说法，空气中充满了羽毛，这些羽毛阻止了他们看到或旅行到更多的大陆？我的观点是，该地区北部一直在下雪（当然，夏天比冬天少），正是严酷的冬天使该大陆的北部地区无法居住。现在，雪看起来确实像羽毛，任何近距离看过厚厚的雪花的人都可以证实这一点；所以我认为斯基泰人和他们的邻居将雪比喻为羽毛。

It is difficult to see the Scythian lands, their homes and their herds, at close range, because all is obscured by a snow-white veil: Beyond the territory of their neighbours to the north there are such piles of feathers, according to the Scythians, that nothing can be seen and the land cannot be traversed either. They say that there are too many feathers filling the land and the air to enable sight to function. Which Herodotus comments upon this way: What about the feathers with which, according to the Scythians, the air is filled, and which stop them either seeing or travelling over more of the continent? My view is that it is constantly snowing north of the region in question (less in summer than in winter, of course), and that it is the harshness of the winter that makes the northern part of the continent uninhabitable. Now, snow does look like feathers, as anyone who has ever seen snow falling thickly from close up can confirm; so I think that the Scythians and their neighbours are describing the snow metaphorically as feathers.

正如二十四个世纪后拿破仑所做的那样，大流士现在出发前往这些土地。有人建议他反对：希斯塔斯皮斯的儿子阿尔塔班努斯要求他取消对斯基泰人的远征，并以难以接近他们为理由提出了这一要求。但大流士不听，在大规模准备之后，率领一支由所有部落和人民组成的大军出发……他统治了。希罗多德引用了当时的天文数字：人类总数—— 包括骑兵部队，不包括舰队——足有七十万，还有六百艘船只聚集在那里。

As Napoleon will do twenty-four centuries later, Darius now sets forth for these lands. He is counseled against it: Artabanus the son of Hystaspes asked him to cancel his expedition against the Scythians and cited the difficulty of getting at them as the reason for his request. But Darius does not listen, and after massive preparations departs at the head of a great army comprising all the tribes and peoples … he ruled. Herodotus cites what was then an astronomical figure: The total number of men— including cavalry contingents, but excluding the fleet—was 700,000, and then there were six hundred ships assembled there too.

他下令在博斯普鲁斯海峡上修建一座桥梁。坐在王座上，他观察着他的军队渡过它。他建造的下一座桥是在多瑙河上。当他的军队渡河后，他下令拆除这座桥。但他的一位指挥官，名叫科斯的人，恳求他不要这样做：

He orders a bridge built over the Bosporus. Sitting on this throne, he observes his army crossing it. The next bridge he constructs is over the Danube. Once his army has made its way across, he orders the bridge dismantled. But one of his commanders, a certain Coës, pleads with him not to do so:

“大人，”他说道，“您即将入侵一片农业完全未知、也没有定居点的土地。我建议你把这座桥留在原地……然后，如果我们找到斯基泰人并做我们来的目的，我们之后就有一条离开这个国家的路；或者，如果我们找不到他们，至少我们可以确保返回。我相信斯基泰人永远不会在战斗中击败我们，但我仍然担心，当我们四处游荡试图找到他们却失败时，我们会不会发生什么意外。”

“My lord,” he said, “you are about to invade a land where agriculture is completely unknown and there are no settlements. I would suggest that you leave this bridge in place … Then, if we find the Scythians and do what we came for, we have a way out of the country afterwards; alternatively, if we fail to locate them, our return, at least, is ensured. I’m confident that the Scythians will never defeat us in battle, but I still worry in case something untoward happens to us as we roam here and there trying and failing to locate them.”

这个科斯将会成为一位先知。

This Coës will turn out to be a prophet.

大流士同意保持桥梁完好无损并继续赶路。

Darius agrees to leave the bridge intact and continues on his way.

与此同时，斯基泰人得知一支大军正在向他们进攻，他们向邻国的国王发出了召开会议的消息，但他们发现会议已经在进行中。出席者中有布丁人的国王——这是一个人口众多的庞大部落，有着锐利的灰色眼睛和鲜红的头发。有一位阿加瑟尔人的国王，在他中间，任何女人都可以与任何男人发生性行为，以确保男人都是兄弟，并且他们彼此之间友好相处……有金牛座的国王——如果他们捕获敌人，每人砍下一个头颅，带回自己的房子，然后将其粘在一根长杆的末端，并将其竖立在自己房子上方的高处，通常是在烟囱上方。他们相信，这些头颅挂在那里可以保护整个家庭。

Meantime, the Scythians learn that a great army is advancing against them, and sending word to the kings of the neighboring nations for a meeting, they find a conference already in progress. Among those in attendance is the king of the Budinians—a large and populous tribe, with piercing grey eyes and bright red hair. There is the king of the Agathyrsians, among whom any woman is available to any man for sex, to ensure that the men are all brothers and that they are on amicable and good terms with one another … There is the king of the Taurians—if they capture their enemies, each of them cuts off a head and takes it back to his house, where he sticks it on the end of a long pole and sets it up to tower high above his house, usually over the chimney. It is their belief that these heads, hanging there, protect the whole household.

斯基泰代表向与会的各国国王发表讲话，向他们通报波斯雪崩即将来临，并呼吁：“你们绝对不能袖手旁观，眼睁睁看着我们被毁灭……”我们应该制定共同计划，共同抵抗入侵。”为了说服他们联合起来并进行合作，斯基泰人说波斯人的目标不仅是征服斯基泰人，而且是征服所有人民。[波斯人]一进入我们这片大陆，他就开始征服他路上的所有人。

The Scythian delegates address the various assembled kings and, informing them of the approaching Persian avalanche, make an appeal: “You absolutely must not stand idly by and watch us being destroyed. We should form a common plan and resist the invasion together.” And to persuade them to join forces and cooperate, the Scythians say that the Persians aim to conquer not only the Scythians, but all peoples. No sooner has [the Persian] entered this continent of ours than he sets about subjugating everyone in his path.

正如希罗多德所说，国王们听取了斯基泰人的论点，但意见分歧。有些人认为他们确实应该无条件地帮助斯基泰人，并在他们需要的时候支持他们，但其他人则宁愿暂时保持观望，并认为事实上波斯人只是想向斯基泰人报仇，会让其他人独自一人。

As Herodotus tells it, the kings listened to the Scythian argument but were divided in their views. Some believed that they should indeed help the Scythians unconditionally and support them in their hour of need, but others preferred to remain on the sidelines for the time being, and believed that in point of fact the Persians, wishing only to revenge themselves upon the Scythians, would leave the others alone.

面对这种不团结，斯基泰人知道他们的对手非常强大，决定不直接战斗和公开战争，而赞成撤退。计划是，当他们骑马撤退时，他们会填满他们经过的所有井和泉水，并摧毁他们发现的所有生长在地面上的植被。此外，他们会分成两组，始终与波斯人保持一天行军的距离，并且总是后退，不断改变方向，使他们迷失方向，同时将他们越来越深入到该国的内部。

In the face of this disunity, the Scythians, knowing their opponent to be very powerful, decided against straight fighting and open warfare, and in favour of retreat. The plan was that as they rode back in retreat they would fill in any wells and springs they passed, and destroy any vegetation they found growing in the ground. Furthermore, they would divide into two groups and, keeping themselves always at a distance of one day’s march from the Persians, always retreating, would disorient them with constant change of direction, all the while drawing them ever deeper into the interior of the country.

他们做出的决定现在就开始实施。

What they decided upon they now set about implementing.

但首先，他们所有的妇女和儿童所居住的马车都被送去，并接到命令继续向北行驶，他们所有的牲畜也都随着马车被送往……北方，那里的寒冷和雪可以为他们提供保护，免受炎热的人们的侵害。南方，波斯人。

But first the wagons in which all their women and children lived were sent off with orders to keep heading north, and all their livestock was sent with the wagons … North, where the cold and the snow would offer them protection from the people of the hot south, the Persians.

当大流士的军队最终进入时，斯基泰人并没有对其发动公开战争。他们的策略，他们的武器，就是欺骗、逃避、伏击。他们在哪里？它们像野兔一样狡猾、快速、难以捉摸，突然出现在草原上，又迅速消失。

When Darius’s army finally enters, the Scythians do not wage open warfare against it. Their tactic, their weapon, is deceit, evasion, ambush. Where are they? Cunning, fast, elusive as the hare, they appear suddenly on the steppe and vanish just as rapidly.

大流士在这里看到他们的骑兵，在那边看到他们的骑兵先锋队只是片刻之后就消失在地平线之外。他收到报告称他们在北部某处被观察到。他率领军队到达那里，但当军队到达时，所有人都意识到他们已经进入了一片荒地。这片长达七天的空旷土地，完全无人居住，位于布丁尼亚领土的北部，等等。希罗多德对此进行了详细的描述：斯基泰人为了迫使他们顽抗的邻居参战，编织了大约以这样的方式将大流士的追击部队推到拒绝参战的部落的土地上。现在，他们发现自己遭到了波斯人的入侵，他们必须与斯基泰人一起对抗大流士。

Darius sees their cavalry over here, over there, spots their swift vanguard only for it to disappear moments later beyond the horizon. He receives reports of their being observed somewhere in the north. He steers his army there, but when it arrives, everyone realizes that they have entered into a wasteland. This seven-day stretch of empty land, completely uninhabited by human beings, lies to the north of the Budinian territory, etc., etc. Herodotus writes at length about this: The Scythians, in order to force their recalcitrant neighbors into battle, weave about in such a way as to propel Darius’s pursuing troops onto the lands of the tribes that had refused to commit themselves to battle. Now, finding themselves invaded by the Persians, they must fight against Darius alongside the Scythians.

波斯国王感到越来越无助，最终派出一名使者去见斯基泰国王，要求他的军队要么坚持战斗，要么承认他对他们的统治。斯基泰人的国王回答说：他们没有逃跑，但既没有城市，也没有农田，他们没有什么可以保卫的。因此他认为没有理由战斗。但至于波斯人声称自己是他们的主人，并要求承认这一点——斯基泰人承诺大流士将付出惨重的代价。

The king of the Persians feels increasingly helpless, and finally sends a messenger to the Scythian king demanding that his troops either stand and fight or else recognize his dominion over them.
