To which the king of the Scythians replies: They are not in flight but having neither cities nor farmland they have nothing to defend. Therefore he sees no reason to fight. But as for the Persians claiming to be their master, and demanding acknowledgment of same—the Scythian promises Darius will pay dearly.

一提到奴隶制，斯基泰国王就勃然大怒。他们热爱自由。他们热爱草原。他们热爱无边无际的空间。他们对大流士对待他们的方式和他的羞辱感到愤怒，现在调整了策略。他们不仅决定摆动和编织，形成之字形和环形，而且还攻击波斯人在任何地方觅食以养活自己和他们的马匹。

The mention of slavery made the Scythian kings furious. They loved liberty. They loved the steppe. They loved boundless space. Outraged by how Darius was treating them, his shaming insult, they now adjust their tactics. They decide not only to bob and weave, making zigzags and loops, but also to attack the Persians wherever they forage to feed themselves and their horses.

大流士的军队发现自己陷入了越来越困难的困境。在这里，在大草原上，我们可以看到两种军事风格、两种结构的碰撞：正规军的紧密、僵化、单一的组织和松散、流动、不断变化的小型战术单元的配置。后者也是一支军队，但却是一支由阴影、幻影和稀薄空气组成的无定形军队。

Darius’s army finds itself in an increasingly difficult predicament. Here, on the great steppe, we can observe the collision of two military styles, two structures: the tight, rigid, monolithic organization of the regular army and the loose, mobile, ever-shifting configurations of small tactical cells. The latter is also an army, but an amorphous army of shadows, of phantoms, of thin air.

“展示一下自己吧！”大流士对着虚空喊道。但唯一的答案是一片陌生的、遥不可及的、不可估量的土地的寂静，他强大的军队就矗立在这片土地上——无用、无能、无能，因为只有对手才能实现这一点，而他不想出现。

“Show yourselves!” cries Darius into the emptiness. But the only answer is the silence of an alien, unattainable, immeasurable land, upon which his mighty army stands—useless, impotent, and feckless, for only an opponent could actualize it, and he does not wish to appear.

斯基泰人看到大流士了解他的困境，就派了一位使者给他带来礼物——一只鸟、一只老鼠、一只青蛙和五支箭。

The Scythians, seeing that Darius understands his predicament, send a herald with gifts for him—a bird, a mouse, a frog, and five arrows.

每个人都有自己特定的联想网络来识别和解释现实，而他通常本能地、不假思索地将其叠加到每组环境上。然而，这些外部环境常常不符合或不适合我们的网络结构，然后我们可能会误读不熟悉的现实，并错误地解释其要素。在这种情况下，我们在一个不真实的世界中行走，这是一个充满死胡同和误导性标志的景观。

Every human being has his own particular web of associations for identifying and interpreting reality, which, most often instinctively and unthinkingly, he superimposes upon every set of circumstances. Frequently, however, those external circumstances do not conform with, or fit, the structure of our webs, and then we can misread the unfamiliar reality, and interpret its elements incorrectly. On such occasions, we move about in an unreal world, a landscape of dead ends and misleading signs.

现在波斯人也是如此。

It is now thus for the Persians.

波斯人收到礼物后，互相讨论了此事。大流士认为斯基泰人正在给他土和水以及他们投降的象征。他的理由是：老鼠生于地，与人同食，青蛙生于水，鸟酷似马，他们以箭作为自己武力的象征。这是大流士表达的观点，但戈布里亚斯……挑战了大流士的这一观点，并提出了替代方案。他是这样解释礼物的信息的：“听着，波斯人：如果你们不变成鸟儿飞上天空，或者变成老鼠钻到地下，或者变成青蛙跳进湖里，那么你们”永远不会回家，因为你会被这些箭射倒。”因此波斯人试图弄清楚这些礼物的含义。

Having received the gifts, the Persians talked the matter over among themselves. Darius’ opinion was that the Scythians were giving him earth and water and tokens of their surrender. His reasoning was as follows: a mouse is born in the ground and eats the same food as human beings, a frog lives in water, a bird closely resembles a horse, and they gave arrows as symbols of their own military might. This was the view that Darius expressed, but Gobryas … challenged this view of Darius’ and came up with an alternative. This is how he explained the message of the gifts: “Listen, men of Persia: if you don’t become birds and fly up into the sky, or mice and burrow into the ground, or frogs and jump into the lakes, you’ll never return home, because you’ll be shot down by these arrows.” So the Persians were trying to work out what the gifts meant.

与此同时，斯基泰人……列队步兵和骑兵，准备攻击波斯人。他们一定是一个令人敬畏的景象。挖掘斯基泰人的墓葬——他们埋葬了衣冠楚楚的死者，还有他们的马匹、武器、工具和物品。珠宝——表明他们的衣服是用金和铜覆盖的，他们的马匹戴着镶嵌着雕刻金属的挽具，他们挥舞着剑和斧头，并携带精心雕刻和装饰华丽的弓和箭袋。

Meantime, the Scythians … drew up their infantry and cavalry and prepared to attack the Persians. They must have been an awesome sight. Excavations of Scythian burial mounds—and they interred their dead fully dressed, together with their horses, weapons, tools, and jewelry—indicate their clothing was covered in gold and copper, that their horses wore harnesses studded and clasped with sculpted metal, that they wielded swords and axes, and carried carefully chiseled and richly decorated bows and quivers.

两军对峙。第一个是波斯人，是世界上最大的，第二个是斯基泰人，体型较小，保卫着一片被白雪幕遮住的大流士内部的土地。

Two armies face each other. One the Persian, the largest in the world, and the second the Scythian, small, standing in defense of a land whose interior is hidden from Darius by a white curtain of snow.

我心里想，这一定是一个充满紧张的时刻——就在这时，一个男孩过来，说皮埃尔神父邀请我到庭院的另一端，那里的桌子上摆着一顿饭，桌子上有树荫。一棵蔓延的芒果树

It must be a moment brimming with tension, I think to myself—and just then a boy arrives to say that Abbé Pierre is inviting me to the other end of the courtyard, where a meal has been set out on a table in the shade of a spreading mango tree.

“一会儿！只要一秒钟！”我大声喊道。我擦了擦因兴奋而湿漉漉的额头，继续读着：

“In a moment! Just one second!” I call out. I wipe my forehead, damp from the excitement, and keep reading:

当一只野兔跑过双方之间的空地时，他们已经做好了准备，等待着，所有的斯基泰人都发现了它并纷纷追赶。大流士看到斯基泰人一片混乱，听到他们的呼喊声，他问为什么他的对手如此骚动。当他听说他们在追野兔时，他告诉他的心腹：“这些斯基泰人确实蔑视我们。我现在认为戈布里亚斯对他们礼物的解释是正确的，我们需要的是一个安全回家的好计划。”

They were ready and waiting in their ranks when a hare ran across the open space between the two sides, and one after another all the Scythians spotted it and gave chase. Seeing the Scythians in disarray and hearing their cries, Darius asked why his opponents were in such a state of commotion. When he heard that they were chasing a hare, he told his confidants, “These Scythians certainly hold us in contempt. I now think that Gobryas’ interpretation of their gifts was right, and what we need is a good plan for getting safely back home.”

野兔的历史作用？学者们一致认为，是斯基泰人阻止了大流士向欧洲进军。如果这件事没有发生，世界的命运可能会完全不同。决定大流士最后撤退的原因是，斯基泰人在波斯军队的众目睽睽之下轻松地追逐一只野兔，这表明他们忽视了它，对它嗤之以鼻，蔑视它。对于波斯国王来说，这种蔑视、这种羞辱，是比一场大战失败更可怕的打击。

The historic role of a hare? Scholars agree that it was the Scythians who stopped Darius’s march on Europe. If this hadn’t happened, the fate of the world might have been quite otherwise. And what decided Darius’s final retreat was that the Scythians, lightheartedly pursuing a hare in plain view of the Persian army, demonstrated that they were ignoring it, thumbing their nose at it, holding it in contempt. And this disdain, this humiliation, was a more dreadful blow for the king of the Persians than losing a great battle.

夜幕降临。

Night falls.

大流士命令点燃篝火，按照此时的惯例天。那些不再有力量行军的士兵——营地里的随从、流浪汉、病人——将留在火边。他命令把驴子拴起来，这样它们就会发出叫声，给人一种波斯营地里的生活照常进行的印象。而他本人则率领军队在夜色掩护下开始撤退。

Darius orders the campfires lit, as is customary at this time of day. The soldiers who no longer have the strength to march—the camp hangers-on, the vagabonds, the sick—are to remain by the fires. He commands that the donkeys be tethered, so that they will bray, creating the impression that life as usual continues in the Persian camp. And he himself, at the head of his army, begins the retreat under the cover of darkness.

死去的国王

和被遗忘的诸神之中

AMONG DEAD KINGS

AND FORGOTTEN GODS

瓦为了和大流士多呆一段时间，我打破了旅行的先后顺序，从 1960 年的刚果跳到 1979 年的伊朗——这个国家正经历着一场激烈的伊斯兰革命，而领导他的是一位白发苍苍、闷闷不乐、不屈不挠的老人，阿亚图拉。霍梅尼。

Wishing to stay awhile longer with Darius, I break the sequential order of my travels and leap now from Congo in 1960 to Iran in 1979—the country of a raging Islamic revolution at whose head stands a hoary, sullen, and unbowed old man, Ayatollah Khomeini.

作为时间无情进步的奴隶和受害者，我们时不时地发现从一个时代跳到另一个时代的诱惑，从而，哪怕只是片刻，哪怕只是虚幻，站在时间之上，随意并列，聚集或者分开，有不同的点、阶段、时期。

Slaves and victims as we are of time’s implacable progression, we find it tempting now and then to jump this way from epoch to epoch, and thereby, if only for a moment, if only illusorily, to stand above time and juxtapose at will, assemble or separate, its different points, stages, periods.

那么为什么大流士如此引人注目呢？阅读希罗多德对东方统治者的描述，我们可以看到，虽然他们都做出了残酷的事，但偶尔也有做得更多的人，而这个“更多”可能是有用的，甚至是好事。大流士就是这样。一方面，他是一个杀人犯。在这里，他正带着他的军队出发去对抗斯基泰人：此时，一个名叫奥奥巴祖斯的波斯人（他的三个儿子都在军队里）问大流士是否可以留下其中一个。大流士友好地回答，好像这个要求很合理，并表示他会留下三个人。俄奥巴祖斯对他的儿子们从兵役中被释放感到非常高兴，但大流士命令那些对此负责的人杀死他们三个。所以他确实把他们留在了苏萨——他们的喉咙被割断了。

So why is Darius so compelling? Reading what Herodotus writes on eastern rulers, we can see that although all of them perform cruel deeds, there are occasionally those capable of more, and that this “more” can be something useful and even good. It was thus with Darius. On the one hand, he was a murderer. Here he is setting off with his army against the Scythians: At this point a Persian called Oeobazus, all three of whose sons were in the army, asked Darius whether one of them could be left behind. Darius replied in a friendly fashion, as if the request were reasonable, and said that he would leave all three behind. Oeobazus was overjoyed at the prospect of his sons being released from military service, but Darius ordered those responsible for such things to kill all three of them. So he did leave them there in Susa—with their throats cut.

然而，另一方面，大流士是一位优秀的管理者，负责道路和邮件、铸造货币并支持贸易。首先也是最重要的，几乎从他戴上王冠的那一刻起，他就开始建造一座宏伟的城市波斯波利斯，我们可以将其重要性和光彩与麦加和耶路撒冷相媲美。

On the other hand, however, Darius was a good administrator, took care of the roads and the mail, minted money, and supported trade. And first and foremost, almost from the moment when he donned the royal diadem, he began to erect a magnificent city, Persepolis, whose importance and luster we would compare to that of Mecca and Jerusalem.

我在德黑兰见证了国王政权的最后几周。这座巨大的、甚至平时混乱的城市分散在一大片沙地上，现在却处于完全混乱的状态。每天无休止的示威活动导致交通瘫痪。男人们一律是黑发，女人们一律戴着头巾，排成一公里甚至几公里长的队伍，高喊口号，大喊大叫，有节奏地摇动举起的拳头。装甲车时不时驶入街道和广场，向示威者开火。他们真的开火了，当死者和伤者倒下时，惊慌失措的人群要么散去，要么躲到建筑物的入口处。

I am witness in Tehran to the last weeks of the shah’s regime. The gigantic, even normally chaotic city scattered over a large swath of sandy terrain is now in a state of total disarray. Traffic is paralyzed by endless daily demonstrations. Men, invariably black-haired, and women, invariably in hijabs, walk in kilometer-and even several-kilometer-long columns, chanting, shouting, rhythmically shaking their raised fists. Every now and then armored trucks drive into the streets and squares and fire at the demonstrators. They fire for real, and as the dead and wounded fall, the panicked crowds disperse, or hide in the entryways of buildings.

狙击手从屋顶开火。一个被子弹击中的人做出了一个好像绊倒并向前摔倒的姿势，但他立即被走在他旁边的人抓住，他们把他带到人行道边缘，而游行队伍继续前进，拳头有节奏地举起。有时，身穿白衣的女孩和男孩走在队伍的前面，额头上缠着白色的头巾。他们是烈士——准备好迎接死亡。他们的头带上是这么写的。有时，在队伍开始移动之前，我会走到他们面前，试图理解他们脸上的表情。没有任何东西——无论如何，没有任何东西是我不知道如何描述、我能找到合适的词语来形容的。

Snipers fire from the rooftops. Someone hit by a bullet makes a gesture as if he had tripped and was falling forward, but he is instantly caught by those walking beside him, who carry him to the edge of the sidewalk while the procession continues on, fists rising rhythmically. Sometimes, white-clad girls and boys march at the front of the columns, their foreheads encircled by white headbands. They are martyrs—ready to meet their deaths. It is so written on their headbands. On occasion, before the procession starts to move, I walk up to them, trying to understand what their faces express. Nothing—in any event, nothing that I would know how to describe, for which I could find the appropriate words.

下午示威停止了，商贩们开门营业，卖二手书的，其中有很多人。在这里，他们的收藏品散布在街道上。我从他们那里购买了两张关于波斯波利斯的专辑。国王为这座城市感到自豪。他在那里举办了盛大的仪式和节日，邀请世界各地的宾客参加。至于我，我想不惜一切代价去那里，因为是大流士开始建造它的。

In the afternoon the demonstrations ceased, merchants opened their shops, secondhand-book sellers, of whom there were many here, spread out their collections in the streets. I purchased two albums from them about Persepolis. The shah was proud of this city. He held great ceremonies and festivals there, to which he invited guests from around the world. As for me, I wanted to go there at all costs because it was Darius who had begun its construction.

幸运的是，斋月到来了，德黑兰变得平静。我找到了巴士总站，买了一张去设拉子的车票，设拉子靠近佩尔塞波利斯。我很顺利地拿到了车票，尽管后来巴士已经满员了。那是一辆装有空调的豪华奔驰车，无声地在高速公路上行驶。我们经过大片深米色的岩石沙漠，偶尔看到贫穷的泥房村庄，没有任何绿色的痕迹，一群群的孩子在玩耍，还有成群的山羊和绵羊。

Luckily Ramadan arrived, and Tehran grew calm. I located the bus terminal and bought a ticket to Shiraz, which is close to Per-sepolis. I had no trouble getting the ticket, although later the bus turned out to be full. It was a luxurious, air-conditioned Mercedes and it glided soundlessly over the excellent highway. We passed large stretches of dark beige, rocky desert, occasional poor, mud-house villages devoid of any trace of green, groups of children playing, herds of goats and sheep.

在休息站，人们总是得到同样的东西：一盘荞麦粉、一份热羊肉串、一杯水和一杯茶作为甜点。因为不懂波斯语，所以无法交谈，但气氛很愉快；男人们很友好，他们微笑。另一方面，女人们则凝视着另一个方向。我知道我不能看他们，这是被禁止的，然而当一个人在他们附近呆了一段时间时，他们中的一个人时不时地会调整她的罩袍，这样一会儿就可以看到一只眼睛在凝视。从下面露出来的——总是黑色、大而闪亮，周围有长长的睫毛。

At the rest stops one always gets the same thing: a plate of buckwheat grits, a hot lamb shish kebab, a glass of water, and, for dessert, a cup of tea. I can’t converse because I don’t know Farsi, but the atmosphere is pleasant; the men are friendly, they smile. The women, on the other hand, gaze the other way. I know that I must not look at them, that it is forbidden, and yet when one spends some time in their proximity, now and then one of them will adjust her chador in such a way that for a moment an eye can be seen peering out from under it—invariably black, large, shining, framed by long lashes.

我在公共汽车上有靠窗的座位，但几个小时后景色仍然一样，所以我从包里拿出希罗多德，阅读有关斯基泰人的文章。

I have a window seat on the bus, but after several hours the view is still the same, so I take Herodotus out of my bag and read about the Scythians.

这是他们在战争中的行为方式。当斯基泰人杀死他的第一个人时，他会喝一些他的血。他将在战斗中杀死的人的头颅献给国王，因为他这样做的报酬是他们在战斗中所获得的战利品的一部分，但没有头颅就意味着没有战利品。斯基泰人剥头的方式如下： 他在耳朵周围的头部周围做了一个圆形切口，然后把它捡起来，把头皮从头骨上抖下来。接下来，他用牛肋骨刮擦皮肤，然后，用手揉捏皮肤后，他得到了一种抹布，他自豪地把它系在他所骑的马的缰绳上。他之所以骄傲，是因为一个人拥有的破布越多，他就越勇敢。他们中的许多人通过将头皮缝成一件拼凑而成的皮革服装来制作外套，就像皮大衣一样……。人类的皮肤显然是厚厚的、闪亮的白色——事实上，比任何其他种类的皮肤都更有光泽。我不再继续读下去，因为窗外突然出现了棕榈树林、广阔的绿色田野、建筑物，以及更远的街道和路灯。屋顶之上，清真寺的圆顶闪闪发光。我们在设拉子，一座花园和地毯之城。

Here is how they conduct themselves in war. When a Scythian kills his first man, he drinks some of his blood. He presents the king with the heads of those he kills in battle, because his reward for doing so is a share of the spoils they have taken in the battle, but no head means no spoils. The way a Scythian skins a head is as follows: he makes a circular cut around the head at the level of the ears and then he picks it up and shakes the scalp off the skull; next he scrapes the skin with a cow’s rib, and then, having kneaded the skin with his hands, he has a kind of rag, which he proudly fastens to the bridle of the horse he is riding. The reason for his pride is that the more of these skin rags a man has, the braver he is counted. Many of them make coats to wear by sewing the scalps together into a patchwork leather garment like leather coats …. Human skin, apparently, is thick and shiny-white—shinier, in fact, than any other kind of skin. I read no further, because suddenly palm groves appear outside the window, broad green fields, buildings, and further on streets and streetlights. Above the rooftops glisten the cupolas of mosques. We are in Shiraz, city of gardens and carpets.

酒店接待处告诉我，前往波斯波利斯的唯一方法是乘坐出租车，而且最好在黎明前出发，因为这样人们就能看到太阳如何升起，并用第一束光线照亮皇家遗址。

I am informed at the hotel reception desk that the only way to get to Persepolis is by taxi and that it is best to set out before dawn, because that way one will see how the sun rises and illuminates the royal ruins with its first beams.

天还黑的时候，我发现司机在酒店门口等我，我们立刻出发了。月亮是满月，所以我可以看到我们所在的平原像干涸的湖底一样平坦。沿着空荡荡的道路行驶了半个小时后，贾法尔（司机的名字）停下来，从后备箱里取出一瓶水。这么早，天气很冷，水都结冰了，我自己都抖得厉害，他可怜我，给我盖上了毯子。

It was dark still when I found the driver waiting for me in front of the hotel, and we set off at once. The moon was full, so I could see that we were on a plain as flat as the bottom of a dried-up lake. After a half hour along the empty road, Jafar—that was the driver’s name—stopped and took a bottle of water out of the trunk. It was so cold at this early hour that the water was icy, and I myself was shaking so much that he took pity on me and covered me with a blanket.

我们仅通过手语进行交流。他告诉我我应该洗脸。我照做了，但当我想擦干时，他做了个手势不让我擦：脸不能擦，必须让太阳晒干。我明白这是一种仪式，于是耐心地站着等待。

We communicated solely via sign language. He showed me that I was supposed to wash my face. I did so, but when I wanted to dry it, he made a gesture forbidding it: one cannot wipe one’s face—the sun must dry off the moisture. I understood this to be a ritual, and stood patiently waiting.

沙漠里的日出总是明亮的，瞬间一个神秘的奇观，在傍晚时远离我们并消失在夜色中的世界突然回来了。天重现，地重现，人重现。这一切再次存在，我们可以再次看到这一切。如果附近有绿洲，我们就会看到；如果有一口井，我们也会看到它。在这个感人的时刻，穆斯林跪下并进行当天的第一个祈祷——salat as-subh。但他们的狂喜也传达给了非信徒。沙漠中的每个人都以同样的方式经历太阳回到地球的过程；它或许引发了唯一真正的普世情感。

Sunrise in the desert is invariably a luminous, and in moments a mystical spectacle, during which the world that sailed away from us in the evening and vanished into the night suddenly returns. The sky reappears, the earth and people reappear. It all exists once again, we can see it all again. Should there be an oasis somewhere close by, we will see it; if a well, we will see it, too. In this affecting moment, Muslims fall to their knees and say their first prayer of the day—the salat as-subh. But their rapture communicates itself also to unbelievers. Everyone in the desert experiences the return of the sun to the earth in the same way; it elicits perhaps the only truly ecumenical emotion.

明亮的日光降临，波斯波利斯展现出其所有的皇家荣耀。这是一座由寺庙和宫殿组成的巨大石头城市，坐落在一个巨大的平台上，平台是雕刻在山坡上的，这些山坡突然升起，没有任何中间阶段，就在我们现在站立的平原的尽头。太阳晒干了我的脸，这个仪式的要点如下：太阳就像人一样，需要水才能生存；太阳就像人一样，需要水才能生存；太阳需要水才能生存。如果它醒来后发现自己可以从一个人的脸上喝几滴，那么在它变得残酷的时刻——中午——它就会对他更加仁慈。它会通过为他提供阴凉来体现它的仁慈。它并不直接提供遮荫，而是通过其他各种东西——一棵树、一个屋顶、一个洞穴——来提供遮荫。我们非常清楚，如果没有阳光，这些东西本身就没有阴影。因此，太阳在袭击我们的同时，也为我们提供了防御盾。

Bright daylight arrives and then Persepolis reveals itself in all of its royal glory. It is a great stone city of temples and palaces situated on a vast, broad terrace carved into the slopes of mountains which rise abruptly, without any intermediate stages, in the place where the plain on which we are now standing ends. The sun dries my face, and the point of this ritual is as follows: The sun, much like man, needs water in order to live; if, upon awaking, it sees that it can drink a few drops from a man’s face, it will be kinder to him in the hour when it becomes cruel—at noon. And it will manifest its kindness by providing him with shade. It does not give shade directly, but by the agency of various other things—a tree, a roof, a cave. We know full well that without the sun, in and of themselves those things would have no shade. And so the sun, smiting us, also supplies us with a defensive shield.

那是一个黎明，与现在的黎明一模一样，大流士开始建造波斯波利斯两个世纪后，公元前330 年 1 月底，亚历山大大帝率领军队逼近这座城市。他还没有亲眼目睹这些建筑，但他听说过它们的宏伟，而且里面隐藏着无数的宝藏。在贾法尔和我所站的这片平原上，他遇到了一群奇怪的人：“他们遇到了第一个代表团就在河的另一边。但这些衣衫褴褛的人物与亚历山大迄今为止所接触的优雅的机会主义者和合作者有很大不同。他们的问候声，以及手中拿着的祈求者的树枝，都表明他们是希腊人：要么是中年人，要么是老年人，也许是前雇佣兵，曾与残酷的君主阿尔塔薛西斯·奥乔斯作战。他们的样子非常可怜，非常可怕，因为他们每个人都被严重毁容了。按照波斯人的典型做法，他们的耳朵和鼻子都被割掉了。有些人缺了手，有些人缺了脚。每个人的额头上都有一个毁容的烙印。 “这些人，”迪奥多说，“他们精通艺术和各种工艺，而且干得很好；他们的肢体被切断，只留下履行其职业所必需的部分。 ”

It was a dawn exactly like the one now when, two centuries after Darius began to build Persepolis, at the end of January of 330 B.C.E., Alexander the Great approaches the city at the head of his armies. He doesn’t yet see the buildings, but he has heard about their magnificence, and that they conceal countless treasures. On this very plain on which Jafar and I are standing, he encounters a strange group: “They encountered the first delegation immediately beyond the river. But these ragged figures differed greatly from the elegant opportunists and collaborators with whom Alexander hitherto had dealings. Their cries of greeting, as well as the branches of supplicants they carried in their hands, signified that they were Greeks: people either middle-aged or elderly, perhaps former mercenaries who had fought on the wrong side against the cruel monarch Artaxerxes Ochos. They were a pitiful, downright ghastly sight, because each of them was horribly disfigured. In accordance with the typical Persian method, they had all had their ears and noses cut off. Some were missing hands, others feet. All had a disfiguring brand on their foreheads. ‘These were people,’ says Diodor, ‘who were skilled in the arts and in various crafts, and did good work; they had their appendages cut off in such a way as to leave only those necessary for performing their profession.’ ”

尽管如此，这些不幸的人还是要求亚历山大不要命令他们返回希腊，而是把他们留在这里，留在他们正在建设的波斯波利斯：在希腊，从他们的外表来看，“他们每个人都会感到孤立，会成为被统治的对象”。可惜，是社会的弃儿。”

These unfortunates nevertheless ask Alexander that he not order them to return to Greece, but rather leave them here, in Persepolis, which they are building: in Greece, with their appearance, “each one of them would feel isolated, would be an object of pity, a social outcast.”

我们抵达波斯波利斯。一条又长又宽的楼梯通向城市，一侧有一座高大的浅浮雕，用深灰色、抛光良好的大理石雕刻而成，代表着封臣走向国王，向他致敬，表示忠诚和服从。每阶有一个诸侯，多的有数十阶。当你把脚踩在一个台阶上时，它指定的封臣会陪伴你，当你走上一步时，他会将你交给下一个封臣，而他本人则留在原地，守卫自己的台阶。令人惊讶的是，这些封臣的形象在外观、比例和形状等最细微的细节上都一模一样。他们他们都穿着华丽的及地长袍，头戴褶皱的头巾，双手举着长矛，肩上挂着装饰精美的箭袋。他们的表情严肃，尽管等待着他们的是奴性，但他们却笔直地走着，姿势充满了尊严。

We arrive in Persepolis. A long, wide staircase leads to the city, flanked on one side by a tall bas-relief carved in dark gray, well-polished marble and representing vassals walking to the king in order to pay him homage, proffer their loyalty and subservience. There is one vassal for every step, and there are several dozen steps. When you place your feet upon one step, you are accompanied by its appointed vassal, who, when you walk a step higher, will hand you over to the next vassal while he himself remains where he was, guarding his own step. It is astonishing that the figures of the vassals are identical to one another down to the most minute details of their appearance, proportions, and shape. They all have rich, floor-length gowns, creased head coverings, long spears which they hold before them with both hands, and decorated quivers slung over their shoulders. Their facial expression is serious, and despite the fact that an act of servility awaits them, they walk erect, their posture full of dignity.

伴随着你上楼梯的附庸的外观相同，创造了一种静止中运动的矛盾印象：你爬上楼梯，但因为你总是看到同一个附庸，所以你同时有站着不动的印象，就好像你被困住了一样在看不见的镜子里。当你到达山顶时，你转身回头看。景色非常壮丽：脚下是一片无边无际的平原，此时已经沐浴在耀眼的阳光下，只有一条路穿过——通往波斯波利斯的那条路。

The sameness of appearance of the vassals accompanying your ascent up the stairs creates a paradoxical impression of motion within immobility: you climb the stairs, but because you always see the same vassal, you simultaneously have the impression of standing still, as if you were trapped in invisible trick mirrors. When you have reached the top, you turn and look back. The view is magnificent: below you stretches a boundless plain, at this hour already bathed in blinding sunlight, and traversed by only one road—the one leading to Persepolis.

这种风景创造了两种完全不同、甚至相反的心理情境：

This scenery creates two entirely different, even opposite psychological situations:

——从国王的角度来看：国王站在楼梯顶上，俯视着平原。在它的另一端，即很远很远的地方，他看到出现了一些斑点，一些几乎看不见且难以识别的灰尘、颗粒、颗粒。国王看着，想知道这会是什么？过了一会儿，颗粒和谷粒越来越近，变大，并慢慢结晶。他认为，这些可能是封臣，但因为第一印象总是最重要的，在这种情况下，它是“微粒和谷粒”，所以国王对他的臣民的看法将永远如此。过了一段时间：他已经能看到人影、人的轮廓了。好吧，我没有弄错，国王对他周围的朝臣说，那些是封臣；我必须赶紧赶到觐见大厅，以便在他们到达之前我可以坐在王位上（除了坐在王位上之外，国王不会与下属说话）。

—From the king’s point of view: The king stands at the top of the stairs and looks down at the plain. At its other end, meaning very far, far away, he sees that some specks have appeared, some motes of dust, grains, particles barely visible and difficult to identify. The king watches, wonders—what could this be? After a while the motes and kernels of grain draw nearer, grow larger, and slowly crystallize. Those are probably vassals, he thinks, but because the first impression is always the most important, and in this case it was “motes and kernels of grain,” such will always remain the king’s view of his liege men. A certain amount of time passes: he can already see figures, the outlines of people. Well, I wasn’t mistaken, the king says to the courtiers surrounding him, those are vassals; I must hurry to the Audience Hall, so that I can sit down on the throne before they arrive (the king does not speak with subordinates other than while sitting on the throne).

——现在从相反的角度，封臣和其他人的角度：他们从对面出现在舞台上，面向波斯波利斯。他们看到了它神奇的、令人惊叹的建筑、镀金和玻璃。他们无言地跪下（尽管他们这样做了，但他们还不是穆斯林；伊斯兰教到达这里还需要一千年）。恢复镇静后，他们站起来抖掉衣服上的灰尘。这就是国王所认为的斑点和粒子的运动。当他们越来越接近波斯波利斯时，他们的狂喜与日俱增，但他们的谦卑感也随之增强，他们对自己的不幸、卑鄙、无价值的认识也随之增强。是的，我们什么都不是，国王可以随心所欲地对待我们；即使他判我们死刑，我们也会一言不发地接受判决。但如果他们成功地离开这里，他们将在族人中获得多么显赫的地位啊！其他人会说，他是拜访国王的人。后来——他是拜访国王的人的儿子、孙子、曾孙等等。这样一个人就可以确保自己的家族世世代代的地位。

—And now from the opposite point of view, that of the vassals and of everyone else: They appear on the stage from the opposite side, facing Persepolis. They see its magical, astounding constructions, its gilding and glazing. Speechless, they fall to their knees (although they do so, they are not yet Muslims; it will be another one thousand years before Islam arrives here). Having recovered their composure, they rise and shake the dust from their garments. That is what the king sees as being the movement of specks and particles. As they draw nearer to Persepolis, their rapture increases, but so does their humility, their awareness of their own wretchedness, baseness, worthlessness. Yes, we are nothing, the king can do with us as he pleases; even if he condemns us to death, we will accept the sentence without a word. But if they succeed in leaving here in one piece, what a distinguished rank they will acquire among their people! He is the one who visited the king, others will say. And later—he is the son of the one who visited the king, the grandson, the great-grandson, etc. One secures in this way one’s family’s standing for generations to come.

可以在波斯波利斯周围无限地步行。该建筑群荒凉而安静。没有导游、警卫、小贩、兜售者。贾法尔留在下面，而我独自一人在巨大的石头墓地上。石头被塑造成柱子和柱子，雕刻成浅浮雕和大门。这里没有一块石头具有自然形状，没有一块石头是像在地下或山坡上那样的。每一件都经过精心切割、安装和加工。多少年来，这一切付出了多少艰辛，多少千千万万的人辛勤劳作？有多少人死在搬运这些巨石的过程中？有多少人因疲惫和口渴而倒下？当我们看着毫无生气的寺庙、宫殿和城市时，我们无法不禁让人怀疑它们的建造者的命运。他们的痛苦，他们折断的背部，他们的眼睛被错误的石片挖出，他们的风湿病。关于他们不幸的生活，他们的苦难。但下一个总是出现的问题是：如果没有这种痛苦，这些奇迹是否会出现？没有监工的鞭子，没有奴隶的恐惧，没有统治者的虚荣心？总而言之，历代的不朽不正是人的消极、邪恶所创造的吗？然而，这种纪念性的存在难道不应该归功于某种信念，即人身上的消极和软弱只能被美所征服，只能通过他的创造物的努力和意志来克服吗？唯一不变的是美本身，以及我们内心对美的需求？

It is possible to walk endlessly around Persepolis. The complex is deserted and quiet. No guides, guards, hawkers, touts. Jafar stayed below, and I am alone on the great burial ground of stones. Stones shaped into columns and pillars, sculpted into bas-reliefs and portals. No stone here has a natural shape, none is as it would be in the ground, or as it would appear lying on a mountainside. Each is carefully cut, fitted, worked over. How much exacting labor went into all this over the years, how much toil and drudgery on the part of thousands upon thousands of people? How many of them died, hoisting these gigantic boulders? How many dropped from exhaustion and thirst? When we look at lifeless temples, palaces, and cities, we can’t help but wonder about the fate of their builders. Their pain, their broken backs, their eyes gouged out by errant splinters of stone, their rheumatism. About their unfortunate lives, their suffering. But the very next question that invariably arises is: Could these wonders have come into being without that suffering? Without the overseer’s whip, the slave’s fear, the ruler’s vanity? In short, was not the monumentality of past epochs created by that which is negative and evil in man? And yet, does not that monumentality owe its existence to some conviction that what is negative and weak in man can be vanquished only by beauty, only through the effort and will of his creation? And that the only thing that never changes is beauty itself, and the need for it that dwells within us?

我穿过宫殿，穿过百柱大厅，穿过大流士宫殿、薛西斯的后宫、财政部。天气非常热，我没有力气去参观阿尔塔薛西斯的宫殿、议会大厅，或者构成这座由死去的国王和被遗忘的诸神的城市的数十座其他建筑物和废墟。我走下巨大的楼梯，再次经过从石头中出现的封臣队伍，他们正在前往向国王致敬。

I walk through the propylaeum, through the Hall of the Hundred Columns, through Darius’s Palace, the Harem of Xerxes, the Treasury. It is terribly hot and I don’t have strength left for Artaxerxes’ Palace, or for the Council Hall, or for the dozens of other buildings and ruins making up this city of dead kings and forgotten gods. I descend the great staircase, passing once again the procession of vassals emerging from the stone, on their way to pay homage to the king.

贾法尔和我开车返回设拉子。

Jafar and I drive back to Shiraz.

我回头一看——波斯波利斯变得越来越小，汽车尾迹中升起的灰尘越来越模糊了后视，直到最后，当我们进入这座城市时，它在第一个转弯处完全消失了。

I look over my shoulder—Persepolis grows smaller and smaller, the dust rising in the car’s wake increasingly obscures the rear view, until finally, as we are entering the city, it disappears altogether behind the first turn.

我回到德黑兰。示威人群，口号和呼喊，轻武器的射击声和毒气的恶臭，狙击手和二手书卖家。

I return to Tehran. To demonstrating crowds, to chants and shouts, to the crack of small-arms fire and the stench of gases, to snipers and secondhand-book sellers.

我身边有希罗多德，他讲述了如何根据大流士的命令，他留在欧洲的一位指挥官梅加巴祖斯 (Megabazus)，征服色雷斯。希罗多德写道，色雷斯人中有一个部落，称为特劳斯人。特劳斯人的习俗与色雷斯其他地方的习俗基本相同，除了出生和死亡时的行为不同。每当一个婴儿出生时，他的亲人都会聚集在一起，为他出生后将要承受的苦难而悲伤，并讲述人生的所有苦难。然而，当有人去世时，他们会兴高采烈、欢欣鼓舞地埋葬他，因为他已经摆脱了许多疾病，现在处于一种完全幸福的状态。

I have with me Herodotus, who recounts how, on Darius’s order, one of the commanders he had left behind in Europe, Megabazus, conquers Thrace. There is among the Thracians, writes Herodotus, a tribe called the Trausians. Trausian customs are basically identical with those found elsewhere in Thrace, except for what they do at birth and death. Whenever a baby is born, its relatives gather around and grieve for the troubles it is going to have to endure now that it has been born, and they recount all the sufferings of human life. When anyone dies, however, they bury him in high spirits and with jubilation, on the grounds that he has been released from so many ills and is now in a perfectly happy state.



Histiaeus主席的荣誉

HONORS FOR THE HEAD

OF HISTIAEUS

我离开了波斯波利斯，现在我要离开德黑兰，回到二十年前，再次回到非洲。但一路走来，我必须在希罗多德的希腊-波斯世界中停下来——在我的脑海里，因为乌云开始笼罩在上面。

I left Persepolis and now I am leaving Tehran, going back twenty years and returning once more to Africa. But along the way I must stop—in my thoughts, that is—in the Greco-Persian world of Herodotus, for dark clouds are beginning to gather over it.

大流士并没有成功征服斯基泰人。他们把亚洲人挡在了欧洲的门口。他知道自己无法战胜他们。而且，他突然害怕他们现在会攻击并毁灭他。于是，在夜色的掩护下，他开始了他的逃亡之旅，只想要一件事：离开斯基泰，尽快返回波斯。当他的大军撤退时，斯基泰人立即出发追击。

Darius does not succeed in conquering the Scythians; they have stopped the Asian at the gates of Europe. He sees that he cannot prevail against them. Moreover, he is suddenly afraid that they will now attack and destroy him. And so under cover of night he begins his escape-retreat, desiring one thing only: to get out of Scythia and return as quickly as possible to Persia. As his enormous army withdraws, the Scythians immediately set off in pursuit.

大流士只有一条撤退之路：越过他在入侵之初亲手修建的多瑙河大桥。它由爱奥尼亚人（居住在小亚细亚的希腊人，在希罗多德时代处于波斯统治之下）为他看守。

Darius has only one path of retreat: over the bridge on the Danube which he himself built at the start of the invasion. It is being guarded for him by the Ionians (Greeks inhabiting Asia Minor, which in Herodotus’s time was under Persian rule).

世界的命运如何转变。斯基泰人知道最短的路线并骑着快马，在波斯军队之前到达桥梁并试图阻止他们撤退。他们呼吁爱奥尼亚人摧毁这座桥，这样斯基泰人就能消灭大流士，同样也能让爱奥尼亚人获得自由。

How the world’s fortunes turn. The Scythians, knowing the shortest routes and mounted on swift horses, reach the bridge ahead of the Persian forces and try to head off their retreat. They appeal to the Ionians to destroy the bridge, which would allow the Scythians to finish off Darius and by the same token would give the Ionians their freedom.

看来，从爱奥尼亚人的角度来看，这个提议确实是一个很好的提议，所以当他们召集议会讨论这个提议时，第一个发言的米尔蒂亚德斯说：太好了，让我们把桥拆掉吧！其他人似乎都同意（参加议会的不是爱奥尼亚人，而是暴君，他们是大流士事实上的中尉，被大流士安插在人民之上）。下一个发言的是米利都的希斯蒂埃乌斯：[他]采取了相反的路线；他认为，他们每一个人的暴君地位都归功于大流士，如果大流士倒台，他将无法统治米利都，他们也都不会继续掌权，因为没有一个人能够继续掌权。他们的社区不喜欢民主而不喜欢暴政。希斯蒂埃乌斯的论点立即赢得了会议上所有人的支持，尽管他们之前都赞成米尔蒂亚德的提议。

It would seem that from the Ionians’ perspective the proposal is indeed an excellent one, and so when they gather a council to discuss it, the first to speak, Miltiades, says: Great, let’s tear down the bridge! And everyone else seems to concur (it is not the Ionian people who participate in the council, but the tyrants, whom, as de facto lieutenants, Darius has installed over the population). The next one to speak is Histiaeus of Miletus: [He] took the opposite line; he argued that every one of them owed his position as tyrant of his community to Darius, and that if Darius were to fall, he would not be able to rule Miletus and none of them would remain in power either, because there was not one of their communities which would not prefer democracy to tyranny. Histiaeus’ argument immediately won everyone at the meeting over to his point of view, although they had previously been in favor of Miltiades’ proposal.

这种想法的改变当然是可以理解的：暴君们意识到，如果大流士失去了他的王位（可能还有他的头），明天他们也会失去他们的职位（可能还有他们的头）。尽管他们告诉斯基泰人他们将拆除这座桥，但实际上他们继续保护它并允许大流士安全返回波斯。

This change of mind is of course understandable: the tyrants realized that if Darius loses his throne (and probably his head), tomorrow they will lose their positions (and probably their heads) as well. Though they tell the Scythians that they will dismantle the bridge, in reality they continue to protect it and permit Darius safe passage back to Persia.

大流士很欣赏希斯蒂埃乌斯在这一决定性时刻所发挥的历史性作用，并根据后者的愿望给予了他丰厚的奖励。但他不允许他重返米利都暴君的宝座，而是将他带到波斯首都苏萨担任顾问。希斯蒂埃乌斯野心勃勃，愤世嫉俗，最好密切关注他的同类，尤其是现在他已经上升到帝国救世主的地位，在多瑙河大桥上，没有他的声音，可能已经不复存在了。

Darius, appreciating the historic role Histiaeus has played in this decisive moment, rewards him richly according to the latter’s desires. But he does not allow him to return to his seat as tyrant of Miletus, taking him instead to Susa, the Persian capital, as an adviser. Histiaeus is ambitious and cynical, and it is best to keep a close eye on his kind, all the more so now that he has risen to the status of savior of the empire, which without his voice, there by the bridge over the Danube, might have ceased to exist.

但希斯蒂埃乌斯并没有失去一切。爱奥尼亚的主要城市米利都的暴政现在由他忠实的女婿阿里斯塔戈拉斯接管。他也野心勃勃，渴望权力。所有这一切都发生在人们对社会不满甚至抵制的时代。波斯人对被征服的爱奥尼亚人的统治正在增强。父亲和女婿直觉地认为，利用当前情绪的时机已经成熟。

But all is not lost for Histiaeus. The tyranny of Miletus, the principal city of Ionia, is now assumed by his faithful son-in-law, Aristagoras. He, too, is ambitious and hungry for power. All this is happening at a time when discontent with, even resistance to, the Persian domination is growing among the subjugated Ionians. The father and the son-in-law intuit that the time is ripe to take advantage of the prevailing mood.

但他们如何沟通，如何就行动计划达成一致？一个信使要以轻快的速度走完苏萨（希斯蒂埃乌斯居住的地方）和米利都（阿里斯塔戈拉斯统治的地方）之间的距离，需要三个月的时间，而且沿途既有沙漠，也有山脉。希斯蒂埃乌斯没有选择的路线：事实上，碰巧的是，一个头上有纹身的男人从苏萨的希斯蒂埃乌斯来到这里，传达了告诉阿里斯塔戈拉斯反抗国王的信息。希斯蒂埃乌斯无法找到其他安全的方式向阿里斯塔戈拉斯传达他想要传达给他的信息，因为道路上有守卫，所以他剃掉了他最值得信赖的奴隶的头，将信息纹在了他的头皮上，然后等待他的到来。头发重新长出来。一旦完成，他就派他去米利都，只带了一个任务——告诉阿里斯塔戈拉斯，当他到达米利都时，剃掉他的头发并检查他的头皮。正如我已经说过的，纹身的信息是阿里斯塔戈拉斯应该反抗。希斯蒂埃乌斯之所以采取这一步，是因为他讨厌被关在苏萨。

But how are they to communicate, how to agree on a plan of action? It would take a messenger three months at a brisk pace to traverse the distance between Susa (where Histiaeus dwells) and Miletus (where Aristagoras rules)—and there are both deserts and mountains along the way. Histiaeus has no choice route: It … so happened, in fact, that a man with a tattooed head arrived from Histiaeus in Susa with a message telling Aristagoras to rebel against the king. Histiaeus could find no other safe way to communicate to Aristagoras the message he wanted to get through to him, because the roads were guarded, so he shaved the head of his most trustworthy slave, tattooed the message on his scalp, and then waited for his hair to grow back. As soon as it had, he sent him to Miletus with just the one task—to tell Aristagoras, when he got to Miletus, to shave his hair off and examine his scalp. And as I have already said, the tattooed message was that Aristagoras should revolt. The reason Histiaeus took this step was because he hated being kept in Susa.

阿里斯塔戈拉斯向他的支持者提出了希斯蒂埃乌斯的呼吁。他们倾听并一致投票支持起义。阿里斯塔戈拉斯现在出发出海寻找盟友，因为波斯比艾奥尼亚强大许多倍。首先，他航行到斯巴达。希罗多德指出，人们认为它的国王克里昂米尼并没有完全拥有他的才能——事实上，他几乎是疯了——但他设法表现出了极大的睿智和常识。当他听说眼前的事情是针对一位统治整个亚洲并居住在一个名叫苏萨的首都的国王的战争时，他明智地询问距离这个苏萨有多远。阿里斯塔戈拉斯此前一直很聪明，并成功地收留了克里昂米尼，但此时阿里斯塔戈拉斯却犯了一个错误。为了实现引诱斯巴达人前往亚洲的目的，他不应该说出真相，但他却说了：他告诉他内陆之旅需要三个月。他正要继续谈论更多关于这次旅程的事情，但克莱奥梅尼斯打断了他。 “先生，” 他说：“我命令你在日落之前离开斯巴达。如果你想让拉栖代梦人远离大海，你就没有说任何对他们有吸引力的话。”

Aristagoras presents Histiaeus’s appeal to his supporters. They listen and vote unanimously for the uprising. Aristagoras now sets off beyond the sea in search of allies, because Persia is many times stronger than Ionia. First, he sails to Sparta. Its king, Cleomenes, is thought, Herodotus notes, not to be in full possession of his faculties—practically insane, in fact—but he manages to display a great deal of sagacity and common sense. Upon hearing that the matter at hand was war against a king who ruled over all of Asia and resided in a capital city called Susa, he sensibly asked how far it was to this Susa. At that point Aristagoras, who up till then had been so clever and had been successfully taking Cleomenes in, made a mistake. In pursuit of his aim of seducing the Spartiates to Asia, he should not have told the truth, but he did: he told him that the journey inland would take three months. He was going on to say more about the journey, but Cleomenes interrupted him. “Sir,” he said, “I order you to leave Sparta before sunset. You are not saying anything attractive to the Lacedaemonians, if you want them to travel three months’ journey away from the sea.”

阿里斯塔戈拉斯被派遣前往希腊最强大的城市雅典。在这里，他改变了策略：他没有与统治者交谈，而是向群众讲话（根据希罗多德的另一条规则，愚弄群众似乎比愚弄一个人更容易），并直接呼吁雅典人帮助爱奥尼亚人。因此，现在他们已经被说服了，雅典人投票决定派出一支由二十艘船组成的舰队来帮助爱奥尼亚人……这二十艘船被证明是希腊人和非希腊人不幸的开始——也就是说，它们是希腊文明的萌芽。伟大的希波战争。

So dispatched, Aristagoras traveled to Athens—the most powerful city in Greece. Here he changed tactics: instead of speaking with the ruler, he addressed the crowd (in accordance with another of Herodotus’s rules, that it seems to be easier to fool a crowd than a single person) and appealed directly to the Athenians to help the Ionians. So now that they had been won over, the Athenians voted to send a fleet of twenty ships to help the Ionians … These twenty ships proved to be the beginning of misfortune for Greeks and non-Greeks alike—meaning, they were the germ of the great Greco-Persian war.

然而，在此之前，还有一些较小的事件。首先是爱奥尼亚人反抗波斯人的起义，这场起义将持续数年才被血腥镇压。几个场景：

Before it comes to that, however, there are some smaller incidents. To begin with, there is the Ionian uprising against the Persians, which will last several years before it is bloodily suppressed. Several scenes:

场景一：爱奥尼亚人在雅典人的支持下占领并烧毁了萨迪斯（仅次于苏萨的波斯第二大城市）。

Scene 1: The Ionians, supported by the Athenians, occupy and burn Sardis (the second largest Persian city after Susa).

场景二（著名的一幕）：过了一段时间，也就是两三个月后，这个消息传到了波斯国王大流士那里。然而，据说他听到这个消息的第一反应是贬低爱奥尼亚人，因为他有信心惩罚他们的叛乱，并询问雅典人是谁。据说，在听到回答后，他要求鞠躬。他抓住它，刻出一支箭，将它射向天空。当他把它射向空中时，他说：“宙斯大人，让我有机会惩罚雅典人。”然后，他命令他的一名侍从在每次上菜时对他重复三遍：“主人，记住雅典人。”

Scene 2 (a famous one): After a certain time, that is, after two or three months, news of this reaches the Persian king, Darius. It is said, however, that his first reaction to the news was to discount the Ionians, because he was confident of punishing them for their rebellion, and to ask who the Athenians were. On hearing the answer, he is said to have asked for his bow; he took hold of it, notched an arrow, and shot it up towards the sky. And as he fired it into the air, he said, “Lord Zeus, make it possible for me to punish the Athenians.” Then he ordered one of his attendants to repeat to him three times, every time a meal was being served, “Master, remember the Athenians.”

场景三：大流士召唤了希斯蒂埃乌斯，他开始怀疑他有什么事情，因为煽动爱奥尼亚起义的毕竟是他的女婿阿里斯塔戈拉斯。希斯蒂埃乌斯否认一切当着国王的面撒谎说：“陛下……我怎么可能参与策划任何会给您带来哪怕是最轻微痛苦的事情呢？我这样做可能有什么动机？”他指责国王把他带到苏萨，因为如果他希斯蒂埃乌斯在爱奥尼亚，就没有人会叛变反对大流士。所以现在就让我尽快前往艾奥尼亚，这样我就能从你们那里混乱的事务中恢复秩序，并将我留下来掌管米利都的人交到你们手中，他负责这一切。大流士被说服了，允许希斯蒂埃乌斯离开，并命令他在完成承诺的任务后返回苏萨。

Scene 3: Darius summons Histiaeus, whom he begins to suspect of something, because it was after all his son-in-law, Aristagoras, who fomented the Ionian uprising. Histiaeus denies everything and lies to the king’s face: “My lord … how could I be involved in planning anything that would cause you even the slightest amount of distress? What possible motive could I have for doing so?” And he blames the king for having brought him to Susa, because if he, Histiaeus, were in Ionia, no one would have mutinied against Darius. So now let me go as quickly as possible to Ionia, so that I can restore order out of all the chaos in your affairs there and deliver into your hands this man I left in charge of Miletus, who is responsible for all this. Darius lets himself be persuaded, allows Histiaeus to go, and commands him to return to Susa once he has accomplished the promised mission.

场景 4：与此同时，爱奥尼亚人和波斯人之间的战斗展开，结果各不相同且不确定。然而，随着时间的推移，数量更多、实力更强的波斯人逐渐占据了优势。希斯蒂埃乌斯的女婿阿里斯塔戈拉斯注意到了这一点，并决定退出起义，甚至离开爱奥尼亚。希罗多德轻蔑地描述了这一决定：米利都的阿里斯塔戈拉斯证明了自己是个胆小鬼。他在艾奥尼亚引起了轩然大波，惹出了很大的麻烦，但看到现在的情况，而且因为他现在对击败大流士国王感到绝望，他开始考虑逃跑。因此，他召开了一次支持者会议……声称他们应该有一个避难所，以防万一他们被赶出米利都……他……招募了一批志愿者，然后启航前往色雷斯。在那里，他控制了他出发的土地，并将其作为他的军事总部。然而……色雷斯人摧毁了他的军队，阿里斯塔戈拉斯本人也是伤亡者之一。

Scene 4: Meantime, battles between the Ionians and the Persians unfold with varying and inconclusive results. In time, however, the Persians, more numerous and powerful, gradually gain an edge. Histiaeus’s son-in-law, Aristagoras, notices this and decides to withdraw from the uprising, even to leave Ionia. Herodotus writes about this decision with contempt: Aristagoras of Miletus proved himself to be somewhat of a coward. He had caused all the commotion in Ionia and had stirred up a great deal of trouble, but seeing the current situation, and because he now despaired of ever defeating King Darius, he began to contemplate flight. He therefore convened a meeting of his supporters … claiming that they should have a bolt-hole available in case they were ever thrown out of Miletus … He … recruited a band of volunteers, and set sail for Thrace. There he gained control of the land he had set out for and made it his military headquarters. However … the Thracians destroyed his army, and Aristagoras himself was one of the casualties.

场景 5：被大流士释放的希斯蒂埃乌斯到达萨迪斯并拜访了大流士的侄子总督阿塔弗瑞尼斯。他们交谈。总督问他，你认为爱奥尼亚人为何叛乱？我不知道，希斯蒂埃乌斯假装不知道，回答道。但阿尔塔弗林内斯知道他所知道的：“我会告诉你到底发生了什么，希斯蒂埃乌斯：是你缝制了鞋子，而阿里斯塔戈拉斯只是穿上它。”

Scene 5: Histiaeus, released by Darius, reaches Sardis and calls on the satrap Artaphrenes, Darius’s nephew. They converse. What do you think, the satrap asks him, why did the Ionians revolt? I have no idea, Histiaeus replied, feigning ignorance. But Arta-phrenes knows what he knows: “I’ll tell you what actually happened, Histiaeus: it was you who stitched the shoe, while Aristagoras merely put it on.”

场景 6：希斯蒂埃乌斯意识到总督已经看穿了他向大流士寻求帮助是徒劳的：信使需要三个月的时间才能到达苏萨，而大流士在安全引导下的回程总共需要三到六个月，在此期间，阿塔弗涅斯可能会斩首他一百个头。次。因此，他趁夜逃离萨迪斯，向西奔向大海。到达海岸需要几天的时间，很容易想象希斯蒂埃乌斯心提到了嗓子眼，不断地向后看，寻找追赶的阿尔塔弗涅斯的侍从。他睡哪儿？他吃什么？我们不知道。有一点是确定的：他想在对抗大流士的战争中领导爱奥尼亚人。因此，希斯蒂埃乌斯两次被证明是一个叛徒：首先，他为了拯救大流士而背叛了爱奥尼亚人的事业；其次，他为了拯救大流士而背叛了伊奥尼亚人。现在他煽动爱奥尼亚人反对大流士，从而背叛了大流士。

Scene 6: Histiaeus realizes that the satrap has seen through him and that calling on Darius for help is futile: It would take three months for a messenger to get to Susa, and the return trip under safe-conduct from Darius another three to six months altogether, during which time Artaphrenes could have him beheaded a hundred times. Therefore he flees from Sardis by night heading west, toward the sea. It takes several days to reach the shore, and it is easy to imagine Histiaeus pushing ahead with his heart in his mouth, constantly looking behind him to look for Artaphrenes’ myrmidons in pursuit. Where does he sleep? What does he eat? We do not know. One thing is certain: he wants to assume leadership over the Ionians in the war against Darius. Histiaeus therefore proves a traitor twice over: first, he betrayed the cause of the Ionians in order to save Darius; and now he betrays Darius by inciting the Ionians against him.

场景 7：希斯蒂埃乌斯前往伊奥尼亚人居住的希俄斯岛。 （这是一个美丽的岛屿。我可以永远凝视着它的海湾和地平线上可见的海军蓝色山脉。总的来说，这部戏剧是在如此壮丽的风景中进行的。）但他刚刚登陆，伊奥尼亚人就逮捕了他并扔了他。他进监狱。他们怀疑他为大流士服务。希斯蒂埃乌斯发誓事实并非如此，他想指挥一场反波斯起义。他们最终相信了他并释放了他，但不愿意为他提供支持。他感到孤立无援，他对大流士发动大战的计划似乎越来越虚幻。但他的野心依然燃烧。尽管如此，他并没有失去希望，继续对权力的渴望；发号施令的欲望让他一刻也不得安宁。他向当地人寻求帮助，乘船返回大陆，前往米利都，他曾经在那里当过暴君。然而，米利都人很高兴摆脱了阿里斯塔戈拉，而且既然他们已经尝到了独立的滋味，他们并不急于欢迎另一个暴君进入他们的土地。事实上，当希斯蒂埃乌斯试图用武力恢复他的地位时 在黑暗的掩护下，他的大腿被一名米利都人打伤。他被驱逐出自己的家乡，回到了希俄斯。他试图说服中国人给他一支舰队，但他们拒绝了，所以他去了米蒂利尼，在那里他说服女同性恋者给他一些船只。伟大的希斯蒂埃乌斯，曾经是著名的米利都城的全权代表，最近才坐在万王之王大流士的身边，现在从一个岛屿到另一个岛屿漫游，寻找一个属于自己的地方，寻求同情的耳朵，寻求支持。但他总是要么必须逃跑，要么被扔进地牢，要么被推离城门，遭到殴打和受伤。

Scene 7: Histiaeus makes his way to the island of Chios, inhabited by Ionians. (This is a beautiful island. I could gaze forever at its bay and the navy blue mountains visible on the horizon. In general, the drama is set amidst such magnificent landscapes.) But he has barely made landfall when the Ionians arrest him and throw him into prison. They suspect him of serving Darius. Histiaeus swears that this is not so, that he wants to command an anti-Persian uprising. They believe him in the end and set him free, but are not inclined to offer him support. He feels isolated, and his plans for a great war against Darius appear increasingly delusional. Yet his ambition still burns. Despite everything, he does not lose hope, continues to lust for power; the desire to command leaves him not a moment’s peace. He asks the locals for help sailing back to the mainland, to Miletus, where he was once tyrant. The Milesians, however, were glad to have got rid of Aristagoras, and, now that they had tasted independence, they were in no great hurry to welcome another tyrant into their land. In fact, when Histiaeus tried to bring about his restoration by force and under cover of darkness, he was wounded in the thigh by one of the Milesians. Banished from his native city, he returned to Chios. He tried to persuade the Chians to give him a fleet, but they refused, so he went over to Mytilene, where he persuaded the Lesbians to give him some ships. Great Histiaeus, once the plenipotentiary of the famous city of Miletus, having sat so recently at the side of Darius, king of kings, now roams from island to island, searching for a place to call his own, for a sympathetic ear, for support. But invariably he either has to flee, or is thrown into dungeons, or is shoved away from the city gates, beaten and wounded.

场景8：但希斯蒂埃乌斯并没有放弃；但他仍然努力让自己的头露出水面。可能他继续幻想着权杖，被绝对力量的梦想所拜访。他设法给莱斯博斯岛的居民留下了足够好的印象，向他提供了八艘船。他率领这支舰队航行到拜占庭，在那里他们建立了一个基地，并开始扣押所有驶出黑海的船只，除非船员们承诺承认希斯蒂埃乌斯为他们的领导者。因此他的堕落仍在继续。渐渐地，他变成了一名海盗。

Scene 8: But Histiaeus does not give up; still he struggles to keep his head above water. It could be that he continues to fantasize about the scepter, visited by dreams of absolute might. He manages to make a good enough impression for the inhabitants of Lesbos to offer him eight ships. He sails at the head of this fleet to Byzantium, where they set up a base and proceeded to seize all ships sailing out of the Euxine Sea, unless the crews promised to recognize Histiaeus as their leader. Thus his degradation continues. Little by little, he turns into a pirate.

场景9：希斯蒂埃乌斯收到消息称，爱奥尼亚起义先锋米利都已被波斯人征服。在海军战胜爱奥尼亚人后，波斯人从陆地和海上封锁了米利都。他们使用了各种策略，例如破坏城墙，直到阿里斯塔戈拉斯叛乱后的第六年，这座城市、卫城等全部落入他们的手中。他们将这座城市沦为奴隶……

Scene 9: News reaches Histiaeus that Miletus, vanguard of the Ionian uprising, has been conquered by the Persians. After their naval victory over the Ionians, the Persians blockaded Miletus by land and sea. They used all kinds of stratagems, such as undermining the walls, until the city fell into their hands, acropolis and all, in the sixth year after Aristagoras’ revolt. They reduced the city to slavery …

（对于雅典人来说，米利都的失败是一次可怕的打击。当剧作家弗里尼库斯创作并制作了一部名为《米利都的陷落》的戏剧时，观众泪流满面。雅典当局对该剧的作者处以一千德拉克马的严厉罚款并禁止未来在他们的城市制作该剧。戏剧的目的是振奋精神，而不是重新揭开伤口。）

(For the Athenians, the defeat of Miletus was a terrible blow. When the playwright Phrynichus composed and produced a play called The Fall of Miletus, the audience burst into tears. The Athenian authorities imposed a draconian fine of a thousand drachmas on the play’s author and banned any future productions of it in their city. A play was meant to raise one’s spirits, not reopen wounds.)

听到米利都陷落的消息，希斯蒂埃乌斯的反应很奇怪。他停止掠夺船只，并与女同性恋者一起航行到希俄斯岛。他想离米利都更近一些吗？是为了逃得更远吗？但是哪里？目前，他在希俄斯岛组织了一场屠杀：岛上居民的驻军……拒绝让他通过；战斗打响了，许多中国人死了。然后，在女同性恋者的帮助下，他控制了岛上的其他地区……

At the news of Miletus’s fall, Histiaeus reacts bizarrely. He stops plundering ships and sails with the Lesbians to Chios. Does he want to be closer to Miletus? To run further away? But where? For the time being, he organizes a slaughter on Chios: a garrison of islanders … refused to let him pass; battle was joined and a great many of the Chians died. Then, with the help of his Lesbians, he gained control of the rest of the island …

但这场大屠杀并不能解决任何问题。这是一种绝望、愤怒、疯狂的姿态。他放弃了死气沉沉的土地，航行到萨索斯岛——色雷斯附近的一座金矿岛。他围攻萨索斯岛，但那里不受通缉，该岛也不屈服。他放弃了对黄金的希望，驶向莱斯博斯岛——他在那里受到了最好的接待。但现在莱斯博斯岛正面临饥饿，因为他有一支军队需要养活，所以他前往亚洲，前往密西亚的国家，他希望在那里收获一些庄稼，找到一些东西，任何东西可以吃。绞索正在收紧；他真的无处可去。他被困住了，他在谷底。人的渺小是没有极限的。一个小人沉浸在渺小之中，只会被它吞没，直到最后灭亡。

But this carnage does not solve anything. It is a gesture of despair, rage, madness. He abandons the lifeless land and sails to Thasos—an island of gold mines situated near Thrace. He lays siege to Thasos, but is not wanted there, and the island does not submit. Abandoning hope for gold, he sails for Lesbos—he had enjoyed the best reception there. But there is hunger on Lesbos now, and because he has an army to feed, he makes his way to Asia, to the country of Mysia, where he hopes to harvest some crops, find something, anything, to eat. The noose is tightening; he really has nowhere to go. He is trapped, he is at the bottom. There is no limit to man’s smallness. A small man immersing himself in smallness is only engulfed by it, until finally he perishes.

场景10：一个名叫哈帕格斯的波斯人，碰巧就在希斯蒂埃乌斯到达的那个地区，他指挥着一支规模庞大的军队。他在希斯蒂埃乌斯刚下船时就与他交战，活捉了他，并消灭了他的大部分部队。但在这一切发生之前，希斯蒂埃乌斯下船后试图逃跑：当他逃离战场时，一名波斯士兵追上了他，正要刺死他，这时希斯蒂埃乌斯用波斯语对他说话并让他知道。他是米利都的希斯蒂埃乌斯。

Scene 10: A Persian, Harpagus, happened to be in that part of the country which Histiaeus reached, with a sizeable army under his command. He engaged Histiaeus just as he disembarked, captured him alive, and wiped out most of his troops. But before this happened, Histiaeus, upon disembarking, tried to escape: As he was running away from the battlefield, a Persian soldier caught up with him and was just about to stab him to death when Histiaeus spoke in Persian to him and let him know that he was Histiaeus of Miletus.

场景 11：Histiaeus 被带到萨迪斯。在此，阿塔弗瑞尼斯和哈帕戈斯下令将他公开钉死。他们砍下他的头，进行防腐处理，然后将其送到苏萨的大流士国王那里。 （致苏萨！在路上三个月后，那个头，即使经过防腐处理，一定是什么样子！）

Scene 11: Histiaeus is brought to Sardis. Here Artaphrenes and Harpagos order that he be publicly impaled. They cut off his head, have it embalmed, and send it to King Darius in Susa. (To Susa! After three months on the road, what must that head, even embalmed, have looked like!)

场景 12：得知所发生的一切后，大流士斥责阿塔弗涅斯和哈帕戈斯没有让他活着。他下令将收到的废品清洗干净，穿戴整齐，并隆重埋葬。

Scene 12: On learning about all that has happened Darius rebukes Artaphrenes and Harpagos for not sending him Histiaeus alive. He orders the scrap he received washed, appropriately dressed, and buried with honors.

他希望，哪怕只是以这种方式，向这位元首致敬，几年前，在多瑙河大桥附近，这位元首提出了拯救波斯和亚洲、以及大流士王国和他的生命的想法。

He wants, if only in this way, to pay homage to the head in which, several years earlier, near the bridge over the Danube, arose the idea that saved Persia and Asia, as well as Darius’s kingdom and his life.

医生级别

AT DOCTOR RANKE’S

时间当我在刚果时，希罗多德所描述的事件让我如此着迷，以至于有时我对希腊人和波斯人之间即将到来的战争的恐惧比对当前刚果冲突事件的体验更加生动，我被指派报道这场冲突。 。当然，黑暗之心的国家也给我带来了损失，枪战频繁爆发，逮捕、殴打和死亡的持续危险，以及普遍存在的不确定、模糊和不可预测的气氛。最糟糕的情况随时可能发生在任何地方。没有政府，没有法治和秩序。殖民体系正在崩溃，比利时行政官员逃往欧洲，取而代之的是一个黑暗、疯狂的势力，它最常以醉酒的刚果军警为幌子。

The events described by Herodotus so absorbed me while I was in the Congo that at times I experienced the dread of the approaching war between the Greeks and the Persians more vividly than I did the events of the current Congolese conflict, which I was assigned to cover. And the country of The Heart of Darkness was also taking its toll on me, of course, what with the frequent eruption of gunfights, the constant danger of arrest, beatings, and death, and the pervasive climate of uncertainty, ambiguity, and unpredictability. The absolute worst could happen here at any moment and in any place. There was no government, no rule of law and order. The colonial system was collapsing, Belgian administrators were fleeing to Europe, and in their place was emerging a dark, deranged power, which most frequently assumed the guise of drunken Congolese military police.

人们可以清楚地看到，在缺乏等级制度和秩序的情况下，或者更确切地说，在缺乏道德的无政府状态下，自由是多么危险。在这种情况下，各种邪恶、野蛮、兽行的邪恶侵略势力立刻就占了上风。在这些宪兵统治下的刚果也是如此。与他们中的任何一个相遇都可能是致命的。

One could see clearly how dangerous freedom is in the absence of hierarchy and order—or, rather, anarchy in the absence of ethics. Under such circumstances, the forces of evil aggression—all manner of villainy, brutishness, and bestiality—instantly gain the upper hand. And so it was in the Congo, which fell under the rule of these gendarmes. An encounter with any one of them could be deadly.

我正走在利萨利小镇的街道上。

Here I am walking down the street in the small town of Lisali.

阳光明媚，空旷而安静。

It is sunny, empty, and quiet.

突然，我发现两个警察从相反的方向过来。我冻结。但逃跑是没有意义的——无处可逃，而且天气热得要命，我几乎无法拖着一只脚。宪兵们身穿迷彩服，深头盔遮住了半张脸，武器林立，每个人都拿着自动步枪、手榴弹、小刀、照明弹手枪、警棍和勺叉组合的金属工具——一个便携式武器库。为什么他们需要这一切？我想知道。还有更多。它们雄伟的轮廓还被各种腰带和可拆卸的衬里包围，上面缝有金属圈、别针、钩子和带扣的花环。

Suddenly, I spot two policemen approaching from the opposite direction. I freeze. But running away makes no sense—there is no place to run to, and, furthermore, it is dreadfully hot and I can barely drag one foot after the other. The gendarmes are in fatigues, with deep helmets which cover half their faces, and bristling with armaments, each carrying an automatic rifle, grenades, knife, flare pistol, truncheon, and a metal implement combining spoon and fork—a portable arsenal. Why do they need it all? I wonder. And there is more. Their imposing silhouettes are also encircled with all kinds of belts and detachable linings, to which are sewn garlands of metal circles, pins, hooks, buckles.

穿着短裤和衬衫，也许他们看起来是令人愉快的年轻人，是那种会礼貌地向你打招呼，并在有人询问时愉快地为你指路的人。但制服和武器改变了它们的性质和姿态，并且还发挥了另一个功能：使任何正常的人类接触变得困难，甚至不可能。向我走来的人不是随便遇到的普通人，而是非人性的生物，外星人。一个新物种。

Dressed in shorts and shirts, perhaps they would have seemed pleasant young men, the sort who would greet you politely and pleasantly offer directions if asked. But the uniform and the weaponry altered their nature and stance, and also performed yet another function: rendering difficult, even impossible, any normal human contact. The men walking toward me were not ordinary people to be casually encountered, but dehumanized creatures, extraterrestrials. A new species.

他们越来越近了，我浑身大汗，双腿沉重，每一秒都越来越重。整个情况的关键在于，他们和我一样清楚，无论他们判处什么刑罚，都不能上诉。没有更高的权威，没有法庭。他们想打我就打我；如果他们想杀我，他们就会杀了我。只有在这样的环境下，当我独自面对绝对的暴力力量时，我才会感到真正的孤独。世界变得空虚、寂静、人口稀少，最后退去。

They were drawing nearer and I was dripping with sweat, my legs leaden and getting heavier by the second. The key to the entire situation was that they knew as well as I did that to whatever sentence they might impose there was no appeal. No higher authority, no tribunal. If they wanted to beat me, they would beat me; if they wanted to kill me, they would kill me. I have only ever felt true loneliness in circumstances such as these—when I have stood alone face-to-face with absolute violent power. The world grows empty, silent, depopulated, and finally recedes.

而且，参与刚果小规模街景的不仅仅是两名宪兵和一名记者。镇。此外，还有大量的世界历史，许多世纪前我们就已经相互对立。我们之间站着一代又一代的奴隶贩子；利奥波德国王的仆人，他们砍掉了这些警察祖父的手和耳朵；棉花和甘蔗种植园的监工，手里拿着鞭子。这些折磨的记忆在部落故事中流传了很多年，而我即将遇到的人就是在这些故事中长大的，在以有一天报应的承诺结束的传说中长大的。今天就是那一天——他们和我都知道。

Furthermore, it is not merely two gendarmes and a reporter who are participating in this street scene in a small Congolese town. Also present is a huge swath of world history, which already set us against one another many centuries ago. Here between us stand generations of slave traders; the myrmidons of King Leopold, who cut off the hands and ears of the grandfathers of these policemen; the overseers of cotton and sugar plantations, whips in their hands. The memory of those torments was passed down for years in tribal stories, and the men whom I am about to encounter would have been reared on those tales, on legends ending with a promise of a day of retribution. And today is that day—both they and I know it.

会发生什么？我们已经很接近了，而且越来越接近。最后，他们停了下来。我也停下来。然后，从那堆积如山的齿轮和废铁下面，出现了一个我永远不会忘记的声音，它的语气谦虚，甚至是恳求：

What will happen? We are close already, and getting closer and closer. Finally, they stop. I too stop. And then, from under that mountain of gear and scrap iron, emerges a voice that I will never forget, its tone humble, even pleading:

“先生，你有一支香烟吗？你要抽烟吗？” ”

“Monsieur, avez-vous une cigarette, s’il vous plaît?”

那一定是多么令人兴奋、匆忙、彬彬有礼、甚至卑躬屈膝的景象，我把手伸进口袋去拿一包香烟，这是我的最后一包香烟，但这有什么关系，拿着吧，我亲爱的孩子们，把它们全部拿走，坐下来，立即抽整包烟，直到连一丝烟都没有留下！

What a sight it must have been, the zeal and the haste, the politeness, the servility even, with which I reached into my pocket for a pack of cigarettes, my last, but what does it matter, take it, my dear boys, take them all, sit and smoke the entire pack, right away, until not a puff of smoke is left!

奥托·兰克医生对我的好运感到​​很高兴。这些遭遇常常以非常糟糕的方式结束。宪兵已经杀死了很多人。白人和黑人都来见兰克医生，询问他们的伤势。受酷刑的人必须由其他人抬进来。警察不顾种族，屠杀自己的同胞，就像他们屠杀欧洲人一样，甚至比欧洲人更频繁。这样，他们就是自己国家的占领者，是不拘一格、不设界限的人。 “如果他们不碰我，”医生说，“那只是因为他们需要我。当他们喝醉了并且没有平民可以对付时他们愤怒，互相打斗，然后被带到这里，让我缝合他们的头，安上骨头。”兰克说，陀思妥耶夫斯基描述了毫无意义的残忍现象。他说，这些宪兵也是如此；他们毫无理由或没有必要地残忍。

Doctor Otto Ranke is pleased at my good fortune. These encounters often end very badly. The gendarmes have killed many people already. White and black both come to see Doctor Ranke about their injuries; the badly tortured must be carried in by others. The policemen spare no race, and massacre their own as readily as, even more frequently than, they do the Europeans. In this way they are occupiers of their own country, men who observe no moderation and no boundaries. “If they do not touch me,” says the doctor, “it’s only because they need me. When they are drunk and have no civilian handy upon whom to discharge their rage, they fight amongst themselves, and then they are brought here, for me to sew up their heads and set their bones.” Dostoevsky, Ranke says, described the phenomenon of pointless cruelty. So it is with these gendarmes, he says; they are cruel without reason or necessity.

兰克医生是奥地利人，自二战结束以来一直住在利萨利。年近八十的他身材瘦小，看上去很脆弱，但仍然充满活力和不知疲倦。他说，他的健康状况相对良好，是因为他每天早上，当阳光依然温和时，他都会走到绿意盎然、鲜花盛开的庭院里，坐在凳子上，让仆人用肥皂给他洗背。海绵和刷子的用力如此之大，以至于医生发出了真正的小呻吟，既满足又痛苦。聚集在医生周围观看按摩的孩子们的呻吟声、鼻息声和欣喜若狂的笑声唤醒了我，因为我房间的窗户就在附近。

Doctor Ranke is an Austrian and has been living in Lisali since the end of World War II. Slight, fragile-looking, yet still lively and indefatigable approaching his eightieth year. He owes his relative good health, he says, to his taking each day in the morning, when the sun is still gentle, a walk out into the green and flowering courtyard, where seated on a stool, he has a servant wash his back with a sponge and a brush so vigorously as to produce from the doctor actual little moans of both satisfaction and pain. These moans, snorts, and the laughter of overjoyed children who have gathered around the doctor to watch the rubdown, awaken me, because the windows of my room are nearby.

这位医生有一家小型私人医院——他居住的别墅附近有一座粉刷成白色的营房。他说，他没有和比利时人一起逃亡，因为他已经老了，在任何地方都没有家人。这里的人们认识他，他希望他们能够保护他。他说，他收留我是为了妥善保管。作为一名记者，我无事可做，因为与波兰没有任何通讯手段。这个地区没有一份报纸出版，没有正常运转的广播电台，也没有政府。我想离开这里——但是怎么办呢？最近的机场——斯坦利维尔——已经关闭，道路（现在正值雨季）被淹没，曾经往返于刚果河的船只也早已不再航行。我不知道我到底指望什么。我想，这需要一点运气，以及周围人的善意。最重要的是我希望世界会变得更好，这确实是一个空想的想法，但我必须相信一些东西。但这并不能阻止我四处走动时感到紧张和紧张。我感到愤怒和无助——在这一行工作中，我时而感到熟悉的心态，在这行工作中，我花了很多时间等待与自己的国家和世界沟通的方式，但毫无结果。

The doctor has a little private hospital—a white-painted barrack standing near the villa where he lives. He did not flee with the Belgians, he says, because he is old already and has no family anywhere. Here people know him and he hopes that they will protect him. He took me in, he says, for safekeeping. As a correspondent, I have nothing to do, because there are no means of communication with Poland. Not a single newspaper is being published in this part of the country, there is no functioning radio station, no government. I am trying to get out of here—but how? The closest airport—in Stanleyville—is closed, the roads (now in the rainy season) are swamped, the ship that once plied the river Congo has long ceased to do so. I do not know what it is exactly that I am counting on. A little bit of luck, I suppose, and the goodwill of the people around me. Most of all I am hoping that the world will change for the better, a chimerical idea to be sure, but I must believe in something. Still it does not keep me from walking around tense and nervous. I feel anger and helplessness—by turns familiar states of mind in this line of work, in which so much time is given over to fruitless waiting for a way to communicate with one’s country and with the world.

如果有人碰巧听说镇上没有宪兵，就可以冒险进入丛林探险。它无处不在，向四面八方挺立，遮蔽了整个世界。只能沿着穿过森林的红土路才能进入森林。这座坚不可摧的堡垒没有入口，只有一大片绿色的树枝、藤蔓和树叶。双腿陷入黏糊糊、散发着恶臭的沼泽中，各种蜘蛛、甲虫和蠕虫开始如雨点般落在人们的头上。没有经验的人无论如何都不敢跳入原始森林，而闯入其中的想法对当地人来说是不可想象的。丛林就像海洋或连绵的高山一样，是一个封闭的、离散的、独立的实体，不能随意进入。它总是让我充满恐惧——掠食者会突然从灌木丛中猛扑过来，毒蛇会以无形的速度袭击我，或者我会太迟听到接近的箭的嗖嗖声。

If one happens to hear that there are no gendarmes in town, one can venture an expedition into the jungle. It is all around, rearing up in every direction, screening out the world. One can enter the forest only along the laterite road that has been cut through it. There is no entrance to this otherwise impregnable fortress, a green mass of branches, vines, and leaves; legs sink into slimy, foul-smelling bogs, as all sorts of spiders, beetles, and worms begin to rain down upon one’s head. The inexperienced in any event do not dare plunge into the virgin forest, and the idea of hacking one’s way through it is unthinkable to the locals. The jungle no less than the ocean, or a range of high mountains, is a closed, discrete, independent entity, not to be idly entered into. It always fills me with fear—that from its thickets a predator will suddenly pounce, that a poisonous snake will with invisible speed strike me, or that I will hear too late the swish of an approaching arrow.

通常当我向绿色巨人出发时，一群孩子会追上我——他们想陪我。一开始他们兴高采烈，有说有笑，嬉闹。但当路进入森林时，他们就变得沉默、严肃。也许他们想象在丛林的黑暗中的某个地方，潜伏着绑架不听话的孩子的幽灵、幽灵和女巫。留意孩子们也能理解的内容，保持安静并集中注意力，这是很好的做法。

Usually a group of children catches up with me just as I’m setting out toward the green colossus—they want to accompany me. At the outset they are in high spirits, laughing and frolicking about. But when the road enters the forest, they grow silent, serious. Perhaps they imagine that somewhere, in the darkness of the jungle, lurk phantoms, wraiths, and witches that kidnap disobedient children. It is well to be mindful of what even children understand, to be quiet and pay attention.

有时我们会沿着路停下来，就在丛林边缘附近。这里仿若黄昏，空气中弥漫着浓浓的香气。路上没有动物，但能听到鸟叫声。还有水滴落在树叶上的声音。无法解释的沙沙声。孩子们喜欢来这里，他们有宾至如归的感觉，什么都知道。哪种植物可以采摘并咬住，哪种植物则不能触摸。哪些水果可以吃，哪些有毒。他们知道蜘蛛非常危险，而蜥蜴则完全不危险。他们知道必须抬头看树枝，因为那里可能潜伏着一条蛇。女孩们比男孩们更认真、更小心，所以我观察她们的举动并命令男孩们也跟着学。我们所有人都会有同样的感觉，这让我想起进入一座伟大而崇高的大教堂，在里面，一个人感到自己的渺小，并意识到其他一切都比他自己大得多。

Sometimes we stop along the road, right near the edge of the jungle. It resembles twilight here, and the air is thick with aromas. There are no animals on the road, but you can hear the birds. And the sound of drops falling on leaves. Unaccountable rustlings. The children like to come here, they feel at home and know everything. Which plant one can pick and bite into, and which one cannot so much as touch. Which fruit is comestible, and which poisonous. They know that spiders are very dangerous, and lizards not at all. And they know that one must look up at the branches, because a snake might be lurking there. The girls are more serious and more careful than the boys, and so I observe their actions and order the boys to follow suit. All of us are subject to the same sensation, that which reminds me of entering a great, lofty cathedral, in which a human being feels minuscule and conscious of how much larger than himself everything else is.

兰克医生的别墅坐落在一条宽阔的道路旁，这条道路穿过刚果北部，靠近赤道，穿过班吉到达几内亚湾的杜阿拉，终点大约是费尔南多波的高度。但那距离这里很远，有两千多公里。这条路的一部分已经铺好了，但今天只剩下不成形的沥青碎片。当我不得不在一个无月的夜晚步行到这里时（热带的黑暗是厚重的、难以穿透的），我缓慢地前进，拖着我的脚沿着地面，尽我所能去感觉、测试道路——舒舒尔，舒舒尔——警惕地、小心地，因为有太多看不见的洞、凹坑、凹陷。夜间，当成群结队的逃犯经过这条路时，有时会突然听到一声叫喊——有人掉进了深洞，很可能摔断了一条腿。

Doctor Ranke’s villa stands beside a wide road that cuts through northern Congo and, running close to the equator, leads through Bangui to Douala on the Gulf of Guinea, where it ends roughly at the height of Fernando Po. But that is far from here, more than two thousand kilometers away. A portion of this road had been paved, but only shapeless scraps of asphalt remain today. When I have to walk here on a moonless night (and tropical darkness is thick, impenetrable), I advance slowly, dragging my feet along the ground, to feel, testing the way as best I can—shur-shur, shur-shur—vigilantly, carefully, because there are so many invisible holes, pits, depressions. When columns of fugitives pass this way at night, one sometimes hears a sudden cry—of someone having fallen into a deep hole and probably broken a leg.

逃犯。一时之间，所有人都成了逃亡者。 1960 年夏天，刚果独立，部落冲突爆发，最终爆发战争，从那时起，道路上就充满了逃亡者。宪兵，士兵和临时部落民兵参与实际战斗，而平民（通常指妇女和儿童）则逃离。这些航班的航线通常很难重新创建。一般来说，目标是尽可能远离战斗，但又不至于迷失方向而无法返回。另一个重要的考虑因素是人们是否能够沿着特定的逃生路线找到吃的东西。这些都是穷人，他们只有很少的财产：女人，一件密布连衣裙；男士们，一件衬衫和一条裤子。除此之外，或许还有一块晚上遮盖的布，一个锅，一个杯子，一个塑料盘，一个可以装所有东西的盆。

Fugitives. Suddenly, everyone has become a fugitive. The Congo’s independence in the summer of 1960 was accompanied by the eruption of tribal strife, and eventually warfare, and ever since the roads have been filled with fugitives. Gendarmes, soldiers, and ad hoc tribal militias engage in the actual fighting, whereas civilians, which usually means women and children, flee. The routes of these flights are often difficult to re-create. Generally, the goal is to get as far away as possible from the battle, though not so far as to lose one’s way and later be unable to return. Another important consideration is whether or not one will be able to find something to eat along a particular escape route. These are poor people, and they have but a few belongings: the women, a percale dress; the men, a shirt and a pair of pants. Other than that, perhaps a piece of cloth for cover at night, a pot, a cup, a plastic plate, and a basin in which to carry everything.

但选择路线时最重要的一个因素是部落关系：一条特定的道路是穿过友方领土，还是直接通向敌方领土（上帝禁止）。居住在路边村庄和丛林空地上的各个氏族和部落之间的关系构成了一个困难而复杂的知识体系，每个人都从童年开始吸收这些知识。它使人们能够生活在相对安全的环境中，避免可避免的冲突。我现在所在的地区就居住着数十个部落。他们组成了各种协会和联合会，其习俗和法规只有他们自己知道。作为一个外国人，我无法在精神上导航、组织、破译、理解这些知识，哪怕是一小部分。对于姆瓦卡和潘达之间，或者班达和巴亚之间的关系状况，我能有什么可能的指导呢？

But the single most important factor in choosing a route is tribal relationships: whether a given road goes through friendly territory or, God forbid, leads straight into enemy lands. The relations among the various clans and tribes inhabiting the roadside villages and jungle clearings constitute a difficult and complex body of knowledge, which everyone begins to absorb in childhood. It is what enables people to live in relative safety, avoiding avoidable conflicts. Dozens of tribes inhabit just the one region where I happen to be right now. They are grouped in a variety of associations and confederations whose customs and regulations are known only to themselves. As a foreigner, I have no way of mentally navigating, organizing, deciphering, understanding even a fraction of this knowledge. What possible guide could I have to the state of relations between Mwaka and Panda, or between Banda and Baya?

但当地人对这个州很了解；他们的生命依赖于此。

But the locals know the state well; their lives depend upon it.

他们知道谁把有毒的荆棘放在哪条路上，哪里埋着斧头。

They know who places poisoned thorns on which path, where a hatchet lies buried.

为什么有这么多部落？就在一百五十年前，非洲还有一万人。即使在今天，你所拥有的一切要做的就是沿着路走一段路：在一个村庄，图拉马部落；下一个是阿鲁西（Arusi）；穆尔勒河的一侧；在对岸，托波塔河。山顶上住着一个部落；在它的脚下，是一个完全不同的地方。

Why so many tribes? Just one hundred and fifty years ago there were still ten thousand of them in Africa. Even today, all you have to do is walk down the road a ways: in one village, the Tulama tribe; in the very next one, the Arusi; on one side of the river, the Murle; on the other bank, the Topota. On the summit of the mountain lives one tribe; at its foot, an entirely different one.

每个人都有自己的语言、自己的仪式、自己的神。

Each has its own language, its own rituals, its own gods.

这是怎么变成这样的？这种奇妙的多样性、这种不可思议的丰富变化从何而来？这一切是如何开始的？什么时候？在什么地方？人类学家告诉我们，它始于一小群人。也许有几个。每个人的数量大约有三十到五十。如果更小，人们就无法保卫自己；如果更小，人们就无法自卫；如果更小，人们就无法保护自己。较大的人找不到足够的食物。即使在我那个时代，我也曾在东非遇到过两个人数不超过一百人的部落。

How did it come to this? Whence this fantastic diversity, this improbable richness of variation? How did it all begin? When? In what place? Anthropologists tell us it started with a small group. Perhaps with several. Each had to number approximately thirty to fifty. If smaller, men could not defend themselves; bigger ones could not find enough to eat. Even in my time I managed to encounter in East Africa two tribes neither of which numbered more than one hundred people.

好吧，那么——比方说，三十到五十个人。这就是部落的核心。但为什么这样的核心必然需要自己的语言呢？人类的思维怎么可能发明出如此一系列令人惊讶的言语形式，每种形式都有自己的词汇、语法、语调变化等等？一个拥有百万人口的伟大国家通过长期的共同努力为自己创造了一种人们可以掌握的语言。但在非洲丛林中，这是一个小部落勉强维持生计的问题，他们赤脚行走，永远处于饥饿之中。然而他们有意愿和能力为自己设计语言——独特的、专有的、专属于他们的语言。

All right, then—let’s say, thirty to fifty individuals. Such is the nucleus of a tribe. But why does such a nucleus necessarily come to need its own language? How could the human mind even invent such an astonishing array of forms of speech, each one with its own vocabulary, grammar, inflections, and so on? A great, million-strong nation creating a language for itself through a prolonged communal effort—that one can grasp. But here in the African bush it is a matter of small tribes barely eking out a living, walking barefoot and eternally hungry; and yet they had the will and the capacity to devise languages for themselves—distinct, proprietary, theirs alone.

不仅仅是语言。因为从他们诞生之日起，他们就同时创造了神。每个部落都有自己独特的神灵。为什么他们不应该一开始就崇拜一位神，而是立即崇拜几位神呢？为什么人类经历了数千年才发展出单一神的观念？理性可能表明这样的概念首先会出现。

And not just languages. Because simultaneously, since their very origins, they invent gods. Each tribe has its own unique deities. And why should they not have started with one god, but right away with several? Why does humanity endure for thousands and thousands of years before developing the idea of a single deity? Reason might suggest such a concept would first arise.

言归正传，科学已经确定，一开始只有一个群体——无论如何也不会超过几个群体。随着时间的推移，其他人也随之发展起来。奇怪的是，一个新的小组到达现场时，不会首先勘察地形，估量局势，听取流行的说法。不——它以自己的语言、自己的万神殿、自己的传统宇宙出现。它以相对直接的方式明显地强调了自己的差异性。

And so to resume, science has determined that in the beginning there was only one group—in any event not more than a few. With time, others developed. Curious, that a new group, arriving on the scene, as it were, would not first survey the terrain, size up the situation, listen to the prevailing parlance. No—it emerges with its own language, its own pantheon of gods, its own universe of traditions. With relative immediacy it demonstratively underscores its own otherness.

多年来，几个世纪以来，这些部落核心越来越多。在这片由许多人、多种语言和许多神灵组成的大陆上，它开始变得拥挤起来。

Over years, over centuries, there are more and more of these tribal nuclei. It starts to get crowded on this continent of many people, many languages, and many gods.

希罗多德无论身在何处，总是试图记下部落的名称、他们的位置和习俗。有人居住的地方。他的邻居是谁。这是因为，关于世界的知识——无论是当时的利比亚和斯基泰，还是今天的刚果北部——不是垂直积累的，而是水平积累的，是从鸟瞰角度综合而来的。我认识我最近的邻居，仅此而已；他们知道自己的；这些人仍然认识其他人。这样我们就会到达天涯海角。谁来收集所有这些碎片并安排它们？

Herodotus, wherever he was, always tried to note the names of tribes, their location and customs. Where someone lives. Who are his neighbors. This because knowledge of the world—whether back then in Libya and Scythia or today here in the northern Congo—accrues not vertically but horizontally, synthetically from a bird’s-eye view. I know my nearest neighbors, and that is all; they know theirs; and those know others still. In this way we will arrive at the ends of the earth. And who is to gather up all these bits and arrange them?

没有人。

No one.

他们无法被安排。

They cannot be arranged.

当人们阅读希罗多德的著作中长达一页的部落及其习俗清单时，似乎邻居们是根据差异来选择彼此的。这就是为什么他们之间有如此多的敌意，如此多的争斗。兰克医生的医院里也是如此。日夜守在病人床边的家人、各个氏族和部落都占据不同的房间。目标是让每个人都有宾至如归的感觉，并防止一方对另一方施咒。

When one reads in Herodotus those lists of tribes and their customs, which stretch for pages on end, it seems as if neighbors select one another based on differences. That is why there is so much enmity between them, so much fighting. It is thus in Doctor Ranke’s hospital as well. Night and day families at the patients’ bedside, individual clans and tribes, occupy separate rooms. The goal is to have everyone feel at home, and to prevent one side from casting spells on the other.

我小心翼翼地尝试推断差异。我在小医院里走来走去，看看房间里的情况——这并不是一件难事，因为在这种炎热潮湿的气候下，一切都是敞开的。但人们看起来都很相似，无一例外都是贫穷而无精打采的，只有仔细听才能发现他们说着不同的语言。如果有人对他们微笑，他们也会以同样的方式回应，但像他们这样的微笑需要很长时间才能形成，并且只会在脸上停留片刻。

Discreetly, I try to infer the differences. I walk around the little hospital, look into the rooms—not a difficult thing to do, because in this hot and humid climate everything is wide open. But the people all seem alike, invariably poor and listless, and only if one listens carefully does one notice that they speak different languages. If one smiles at them, they will respond in kind, but a smile such as theirs will take a long time forming and will remain upon the face for only a moment.

希腊人的技术

THE GREEK’S TECHNIQUE

我我要离开利萨利，因为我已经搭便车了。搭便车——这就是如今人们在这里出行的方式。突然，一辆汽车出现在荒芜多日的道路上。一看到我们的心跳就开始加快。当它临近时，我们将其标记下来。“您好，先生，”我们讨好地对司机说，然后满怀希望地说道，“您在这个地方吗？ ” 他当然不知道——车里总是满载的。但里面的每个人都已经挤在一起了，现在，本能地，没有刺激或说服，挤得更紧了，不知何故，我们所有人都挤成最向后弯曲的位置，现在出发了。只有当汽车再次上路时，我们才会询问坐在我们最近的人是否知道我们要去哪里。这个问题确实没有明确的答案，因为没有人真正知道我们的目的地。我们只是尽可能地开车。

I’m leaving Lisali because I have managed to hitch a ride. Hitching a ride—that is how one travels here these days. All of a sudden, a car appears on a road that has been deserted for days. At the mere sight our hearts start to beat faster. We flag it down as it draws near. “Bonjour, monsieur,” we say ingratiatingly to the driver, and then, hopefully, “avez-vous une place, s’il vous plaît?” Of course he doesn’t—the cars are always full. But everyone inside, already squeezed together, now, instinctively and without prodding or persuasion, squeezes together even more, and somehow, all of us jammed into the most back-bending positions, now set off. It is only when the car is once again on its way that we ask those sitting closest to us if by any chance they know where we are going. There really isn’t a clear answer to this question, because no one actually knows our destination. We are simply driving where we can.

人们很快就会得到这样的印象：每个人都想走得尽可能远。战争让刚果这个幅员辽阔、没有公共交通系统的最偏远角落的人们感到惊讶，现在那些远离家乡的人，要么是找工作，要么是探望家人，想要返回，但没有办法。这样做。他们唯一的希望就是搭便车，或多或少地朝着想要的方向行驶——只是开车。

One quickly gets the impression that everyone would like to journey as far as possible. The war surprised people in the farthest corners of the Congo—an enormous country with no public transportation system—and now those who were far away from their homes, either looking for work or visiting their families, would like to return but have no means of doing so. Their only hope is to hitch rides going in more or less the desired direction—simply to drive.

有些人已经在路上呆了几周、几个月了。他们没有地图，但即使他们出现在地图上，他们也很难在地图上找到他们想要返回的村庄或城镇的名称。即使它在那里，对他们来说也没什么用——他们大部分都是文盲。这些流浪者的惊人之处在于他们对所遇到的一切都默许。如果有机会搭便车，他们就会抓住。如果没有，他们就蹲在路边的石头上等待。我最着迷的是那些失去了方向感、无法将所遇到的地点的名称与他们熟悉的地方联系起来的人，最终他们在任何意义上都远离了家乡。怎么办？他们究竟会如何重新定位自己？现在他们发现，家乡的名字对任何人来说都毫无意义。

There are people who have already been on the road for weeks, months. They have no maps, but even if they should happen on one, it is doubtful that they would find upon it the name of the village or town to which they wish to return. Even if it were there, it would be of little use to them—they are largely illiterate. What is astounding about these wanderers is their acquiescence to everything they encounter. If there is an opportunity to get a ride, they take it. If there isn’t, they squat down on a roadside rock and wait. I was most fascinated by those who, having lost any sense of direction and unable to associate the names of locales they came across with any familiar to them, ended up someplace far from home in every sense. Now what? How exactly would they reorient themselves? Where they now found themselves, the names of their home villages meant nothing to anyone.

当以这种方式漂流和迷失时，最好呆在一起，在更大的部落群体中旅行。当然，人们不能指望搭车，而必须步行数天、数周。就走走。人们经常会遇到这样的流浪氏族和部落。有时，他们排成长长的、交错的队列，头上顶着所有的财产——包袱、盆子、水桶。双手始终保持空闲，这是保持平衡所必需的，也有助于赶走苍蝇和蚊子以及擦掉脸上的汗水。

When drifting and straying in this way, it is best to stay together, to travel in a larger, tribal group. Of course, one cannot then count on getting a ride, and one has to walk—for days, weeks. Just walk. One encounters such wandering clans and tribes often. Sometimes they form long, staggered columns, carrying all their possessions on their heads—in bundles, basins, buckets. The hands are always kept free, which is necessary for maintaining one’s balance and useful for chasing away flies and mosquitoes as well as wiping the sweat off one’s face.

人们可以在路边停下来与这些行人交谈。如果他们知道如何回答，他们就会乐意回答别人的问题。问道：“你要去哪里？”他们回应金杜、康戈洛、卢桑博。问道：“那是哪里？”他们很尴尬；如何向陌生人解释金杜在哪里？但有时他们中的一些人用手指示方向——向南。问：“远吗？”他们更加尴尬，因为说实话，他们不知道。问“你是谁？”他们回答说他们叫 Yeke、Tabwa 或 Lunda。 “你们人数很多吗？”这又是他们不知道的。如果有人询问年轻人，他们会建议你去和老年人交谈。如果一问老人家，他们就会互相争论。

One can stop at the edge of the road and strike up a conversation with these wayfarers. If they know how to reply, they answer one’s questions willingly. Asked “Where are you going?” they respond, to Kindu, to Kongolo, to Lusambo. Asked “Where is that?” they are embarrassed; how does one explain to a stranger where Kindu is? But on occasion some of them indicate a direction with their hand—to the south. Asked “Is it far?” they are even more embarrassed, because, if truth be told, they don’t know. Asked “Who are you?” they answer that they are called Yeke, or Tabwa, or Lunda. “Are there many of you?” This again they do not know. If one queries the young, they will suggest that one go and speak to the elderly. If one asks the elderly, they will begin to argue among themselves.

从我携带的地图（“非洲。Carte Generale”，Kummerly + Frey 在伯尔尼出版，未注明日期）来看，我似乎位于斯坦利维尔和伊鲁努之间的某个地方。我正试图前往当时仍处于和平状态的乌干达的坎帕拉，我希望能够从那里与伦敦取得联系，并在他们的协助下开始向华沙发送急件。在这个职业中，旅行的乐趣和对所见所闻的迷恋不可避免地服从于与总部保持联系并向他们传达当前和重要信息的必要性。这就是我们被派到这个世界的原因——除此之外没有其他的自我辩护理由。我心想，如果我能到达坎帕拉，那么我就能到达内罗毕，从那里到达累斯萨拉姆和卢萨卡，然后再到达布拉柴维尔、班吉、拉米堡等地。计划、意图、梦想，坐在一栋迷人别墅的宽阔阳台上，用手指在地图上绘制，别墅被九重葛、鼠尾草和攀缘天竺葵淹没，这座别墅已被比利时人遗弃，他是一家现已关闭的锯木厂的老板。别墅周围的孩子们静静地注视着白人。世界上正在发生奇怪的事情——不久前，大人们还告诉他们白人已经走了，现在他们似乎又回来了。

From the map which I’m carrying (“Afrique. Carte Generale,” published in Bern by Kummerly + Frey, undated), it appears that I am somewhere between Stanleyville and Irunu. I am trying to get to Kampala, in then still peaceful Uganda, from where I hope to be able to communicate with London and with their assistance to start sending dispatches to Warsaw. In this profession, the pleasure of traveling and the fascination with what one sees is inevitably subordinate to the imperative of maintaining one’s ties with headquarters and of transmitting to them what is current and important. That is why we are sent out into the world—and there are no other self-justifications. If I can just reach Kampala, I think to myself, then I’ll be able to get to Nairobi, from there to Dar es-Salaam and Lusaka, then on to Brazzaville, Bangui, Fort Lamy, and beyond. Plans, intentions, dreams, drawn with a finger on a map while sitting on the wide verandah of a charming villa drowning in bougainvillea, sage, and climbing geraniums, which has been abandoned by a Belgian, the owner of a now-shuttered sawmill. Children standing around the villa observe the white man attentively, in silence. Strange things are happening in the world—not long ago, adults were telling them that the whites had gone, and now it seems that they are back again.

非洲之旅一直在继续，随着时间的流逝，地点和日期变得纠缠在一起——这里的一切都如此之多：事件的数量之多——以至于我对这片大陆的印象无法控制地膨胀。我一直在旅行和写作感觉我周围正在发生重要而独特的事情，我也必须见证这些事情，无论多么零散。

The African journey goes on and on, and with the passage of time places and dates become tangled—there is so much of everything here: the sheer number of events—that my impressions of the continent swell uncontrollably. I travel and write, the whole while feeling that all around me important and unique things are occurring to which I must also bear witness, however fragmentarily.
