尽管如此，如果我有足够的力量，我仍然会在空闲时间尝试阅读：英国妇女玛丽·金斯利（Mary Kingsley）1899年的《西非研究》，她的观察很敏锐，指南针也很勇敢；《班图哲学》，牧师普拉西德·坦普尔斯 (Placide Tempels) 于 1945 年出版；或者是法国人类学家乔治·巴兰迪埃（Georges Balandier）深刻而深思熟虑的非洲模糊性（巴黎，1957）。当然，此外还有希罗多德。

Despite this, however, I still try, in my spare moments and if I have strength enough, to read: the 1899 West African Studies, by the Englishwoman Mary Kingsley, who was penetrating in her observations and courageous in her compass; Bantu Philosophy, by the priest Placide Tempels, published in 1945; or the profound, thoughtful Afrique ambiguë, by French anthropologist Georges Balandier (Paris, 1957). And in addition, of course, Herodotus.

但在这段时间里，我暂时放弃了他所写的人民的命运和战争，转而专注于他的技巧。他是如何工作的，即他对什么感兴趣，他如何接触他的消息来源，他问了他们什么，他们回答了什么？我非常有意识地尝试学习报告文学的艺术，希罗多德给我留下了深刻的印象，他是一位有价值的老师。我对他的遭遇很感兴趣，正是因为我们所写的大部分内容都源于我们与其他人的关系——我-他，我-他们。可以说，这种关系的质量和温度对最终文本有直接影响。我们依赖他人；报告文学也许是最依赖集体的写作形式。

But during this period, I abandoned momentarily the fortunes of the people and wars he wrote about and concentrated instead on his technique. How did he work, i.e., what interested him, how did he approach his sources, what did he ask them, what did they say in reply? I was quite consciously trying to learn the art of reportage and Herodotus struck me as a valuable teacher. I was intrigued by his encounters, precisely because so much of what we write about derives from our relation to other people—I-he, I-they. That relation’s quality and temperature, as it were, have their direct bearing on the final text. We depend on others; reportage is perhaps the form of writing most reliant on the collective.

我在阅读有关希罗多德的书籍时还注意到，没有任何权威机构只关心希腊文本本身及其准确性和可靠性，通常不关心他如何收集原材料，然后用它编织出巨大而丰富的挂毯。在我看来，这正是值得深入研究的方面。

I noticed also from reading books about Herodotus that no authorities concerned themselves exclusively with the Greek’s text itself, with its accuracy and reliability, generally paying no heed to how he gathered his raw material and then wove from it his immense and rich tapestry. It is precisely that aspect that seemed to me worth delving into.

我的订婚还有更多的特殊之处。随着时间的推移，我不断地回到《历史》，我开始对希罗多德产生一种类似于温暖，甚至是友谊的感觉。事实上，我对这本书的兴趣并不是那么浓厚，而是它的声音和作者的性格。一种复杂的感觉，我无法完整描述。这是一种与一个我不认识的人的密切关系，但他与他人的关系方式、他的存在方式以及无论他出现在哪里，都立即成为核心或砂浆的方式让我着迷。 ，人类社会，将其组合在一起，使其形成。

And there was more to the idiosyncrasy of my engagement. As time went by and I kept returning to The Histories, I began to feel something akin to warmth, even friendship, toward Herodotus. I actually became attached not so much to the book, as to its voice, the persona of its author. A complicated feeling, which I couldn’t describe fully. It was an affinity with a human being whom I did not know personally, yet who charmed me by the manner of his relationships with others, by his way of being, by how, wherever he appeared, he instantly became the nucleus, or the mortar, of human community, putting it together, bringing it into being.

希罗多德是他的文化和气候的产物——对人类如此有利——它是在其中发展起来的。这是一种热情好客的长桌文化，在温暖的夜晚，人们围坐在一起吃奶酪和橄榄，喝清凉的葡萄酒，交谈。不受围墙限制的开放空间，无论是在海边还是在山腰，解放了人类的想象力。欢乐的氛围为闲聊者提供了大放异彩的机会，他们可以参加自发的比赛，在比赛中，那些讲述最迷人的故事、讲述最非凡事件的人将获得尊重。事实与幻想在这里混合在一起，时间和地点被错误地描述，传说诞生，神话出现。

Herodotus was a child of his culture and of the climate—so favorable to humans—within which it developed. It was a culture of long and hospitable tables, at which one sat in large groups of a warm evening to eat cheese and olives, drink cool wine, converse. Open space unrestricted by walls, either at the seashore or on a mountainside, liberated the human imagination. The conviviality afforded confabulators the chance to shine, to engage in spontaneous tournaments during which those who recounted the most beguiling tale, retold the most extraordinary events, would reap regard. Facts mixed here with fantasy, times and places were misstated, legends were born, myths arose.

阅读希罗多德，我们的印象是他作为一个专注而专注的倾听者，热切地参加了这样的宴会。他一定有惊人的记忆力。我们现代人被科技的力量宠坏了，在记忆方面是残废的，每当我们手边没有书或电脑时就会惊慌失措。然而，即使在今天，仍有一些社会证明了人类的记忆力有多么惊人。希罗多德正是生活在这样一个看似完全回忆的世界中。这本书非常罕见，石头和墙壁上的铭文更是罕见。

Reading Herodotus, we have the impression that he eagerly participated in such banquets as an attentive and self-applied listener. He must have had a phenomenal memory. We modern folk, spoiled by the power of technology, are cripples when it comes to recollection, panicking whenever we do not have a book or computer at hand. Yet even today there are societies to be found that demonstrate how prodigiously capacious human memory can be. And it is precisely in such a world of seemingly total recall that Herodotus lived. The book was a great rarity, inscriptions on stones and walls an even greater one.

社区的内容由两个基本要素组成：第一，个人；第二，他们通过直接的个人接触相互传播的内容。人，为了存在，必须沟通，而为了沟通，必须感觉到他身边有另一个人的存在，必须看到他并听到他——没有其他形式的交流，因此没有其他生活方式。口耳相传的文化拉近了他们的距离。人们不仅将自己的同伴视为会帮助他们收集食物和防御敌人的人，而且还将其视为一个独特且不可替代的人，一个能够解释世界并指导他的同伴度过这个世界的人。

The stuff of community was made up of two essential elements: first, individuals, and second, that which they transmitted to one another through immediate, personal contact. Man, in order to exist, had to communicate, and in order to communicate, had to feel beside him the presence of another, had to see him and hear him—there was no other form of communication, and so no other way of life. The culture of oral transmission drew them closer; one knew one’s fellow not only as one who would help them gather food and defend against the enemy, but also as someone unique and irreplaceable, one who could interpret the world and guide his fellows through it.

这种直接接触和苏格拉底式的互惠互利的原始、古老的语言是多么丰富啊！因为其中重要的不仅仅是文字。重要且常常是最重要的是通过面部表情、手势、身体动作无言地传达的信息。希罗多德明白这一点，并且像每一位​​记者或民族学家一样，他试图与对话者进行最直接的接触，不仅听他们说的话，而且观察他们如何说，以及他们说话时的行为。

And how much richer is this primeval, antique language of direct contact and Socratic give-and-take! Because it is not only words that matter in it. What is important, and frequently paramount, is what is communicated wordlessly, by facial expression, hand gesture, body movement. Herodotus understands this, and like every reporter or ethnologist he tries to be in the most direct contact with his interlocutors, not only listening to what they say, but also watching how they say it, how they act as they speak.

他的任务很复杂：一方面，他知道最宝贵、几乎唯一的知识来源是他所遇到的人的记忆；另一方面，他知道最宝贵的、几乎唯一的知识来源是他所遇到的人的记忆。另一方面，他意识到这种记忆是一种脆弱的东西，易变且转瞬即逝——记忆有一个消失点。所以他才着急——人们忘记了，或者搬到某个地方就再也找不到他们了，最后他们就死了。希罗多德致力于收集尽可能多的合理可信的事实。

His task is complex: on the one hand, he knows that the most precious and almost the only source of knowledge is the memory of those he meets; on the other hand, he is aware that this memory is a fragile thing, volatile and evanescent—that memory has a vanishing point. That is why he is in a hurry—people forget, or else move away somewhere and one cannot find them again, and eventually they die. And Herodotus is out to collect as many reasonably credible facts as possible.

知道自己处于如此不值得信任和不稳定的境地，他在自己的叙述中非常小心，不断发出警告，强调他与他所提供的材料保持距离：

Knowing that he is on such untrustworthy and unstable ground, he is very careful in his accounts, constantly issuing warnings, emphasizing his distance from the material he presents:

  • 据我们所知，吉吉斯是第一位在德尔福奉献奉献的非希腊人……

  • As far as we know, Gyges was the first non-Greek to dedicate offerings at Delphi …

  • 他们说，他希望到达伊萨卡……

  • He wished, so they say, to reach Ithaca …

  • 据我所知，波斯人有以下习俗……

  • As far as I know, the following customs exist among the Persians …

  • 因此，我认为，从已知的未知事物中得出结论……

  • And thus, so I assume, drawing conclusions from the known about the unknown …

  • 当我从他们的言论中了解到……

  • And as I learned from what they say …

  • 这是我对最遥远国家的描述……

  • This is my account of what is said about the most distant countries …

  • 如果这是真的，我不知道。我只写那些所说的……

  • If this is true, I don’t know. I write only that which is said …

  • 我无法准确地说出这场战斗中哪些爱奥尼亚人是懦夫，哪些人是勇敢的，因为他们都互相指责……

  • I cannot accurately state which of the Ionians proved to be cowards in this battle, and which courageous, because they all accuse one another …

希罗多德明白自己身处一个事物不确定、知识不完善的世界，这就是为什么他经常为自己的缺点找借口，解释并为自己辩护：

Herodotus understands that he is in a world of uncertain things and imperfect knowledge, which is why he frequently makes excuses for his shortcomings, explains and justifies himself:

  • 不可能反驳谈论海洋的人，因为这个故事是基于一些晦涩而可疑的东西。我不知道任何河流海洋的存在，我认为荷马或过去时代的其他诗人之一发明了这个名字并将其引入他的诗歌中。

  • It is impossible to argue against the person who spoke about the Ocean, because the tale is based on something which is obscure and dubious. I do not know of the existence of any River Ocean, and I think that Homer or one of the other poets from past times invented the name and introduced it into his poetry.

  • 显然没有人了解欧洲，无论是东部还是北部的部分，以及它是否被大海包围......

  • Clearly no one knows about Europe, neither about the parts lying to the East nor to the North, and whether it is surrounded by the sea …

  • 这片土地之外还有什么……没有人确切知道；我无法从任何可以说他亲眼所见的人那里找到任何信息……

  • What lies beyond this land … no one knows precisely; I can find out nothing from anyone who can say that he saw it with his own eyes …

  • 我无法准确确定斯基泰人的数量，并且听到了相当矛盾的事情……

  • I was unable to determine precisely how numerous were the Scythians, and heard quite contradictory things about this …

但只要有可能做到这一点——考虑到这个时代，这意味着巨大的努力投入和巨大的个人决心——他试图检查一切，找到来源，确定事实：

But to the extent that it is possible to do so—and, given the epoch, this speaks to a tremendous expenditure of effort and to great personal determination—he tries to check everything, to get to the sources, to establish the facts:

  • 尽管我很努力，但我无法从任何目击者那里得知欧洲北部是否存在海洋……

  • Although I tried very hard, I was unable to learn from any eyewitness if a sea exists north of Europe …

  • 我通过研究发现，这座神庙是所有阿芙罗狄蒂神庙中最古老的......

  • This temple, as I discovered through research, is the oldest of all the temples to Aphrodite …

  • 为了从能够传授给我的人那里获得可靠的信息，我什至航行到腓尼基的提尔，因为我听说那里有一座赫拉克勒斯神庙……我与这位神的祭司进行了交谈并问他们……但我看到他们的答案与希腊人所说的不一致……

  • Wanting to obtain reliable information from people who would be able to impart it to me, I even sailed to Tyre in Phoenicia, because I had heard that there was a temple to Heracles there … and I engaged in a conversation with the priests of the god and asked them … But I saw that their answer does not agree with what the Greeks say …

  • 在阿拉伯的一个地方，我亲自去收集有关有翼蛇的信息。到达那里后，我看到了数量无法描述的蛇骨和脊椎……

  • There is a place in Arabia where I went myself to gather information about winged snakes. Upon arrival there, I saw the snakes’ bones and backbones in quantities impossible to describe …

  • （关于凯米斯岛）……埃及人说这是一个浮岛。我自己从未见过它漂浮或移动，而且……

  • (about the island Chemmis)… it is said by the Egyptians to be a floating island. I myself never saw it floating or moving, and …

  • 但这些故事在我看来都是无稽之谈……因为我看到自己……

  • But these stories are in my opinion nonsense … because I saw myself that …

如果他知道某事，他是怎么知道的？因为他听到了，他看到了：

And if he knows something, how does he know it? Because he heard, he saw:

  • 我只说利比亚人自己叙述的事情……

  • I say only that which the Libyans themselves recount …

  • 这是否属实，我不知道。我只写被告知的内容……

  • Whether this is true or not, I do not know. I write only that which is told …

  • 根据特拉奇斯河的故事，伊斯特河左岸栖息着蜜蜂……

  • According to the stories of the Trachis, the left bank of the Ister is populated by bees …

  • 到目前为止，我的叙述都是由我自己的观察、判断和调查驱动的：然而，从现在开始，我打算根据我所听到的来谈论埃及的历史；这也会时不时地伴随着我自己所看到的……

  • Until now my accounts were driven by my own observations, judgments, and investigations: from now on, however, I intend to speak of Egyptian history according to what I have heard about it; this will also be accompanied from time to time by what I myself saw …

  • 任何认为这些事情可信的人都可以按照自己的意愿来解读这些埃及故事。在整个叙述中，我的工作只是记录每个消息来源告诉我的一切。

  • Anyone who finds such things credible can make of these Egyptian stories what he wishes. My job, throughout this account, is simply to record whatever I am told by each of my sources.

  • 当我询问祭司们希腊人讲述的故事是否纯粹是胡言乱语时，他们声称他们是从对墨涅拉俄斯本人的采访中了解到这一点的……

  • When I queried the priests as to whether the story told by the Greeks is sheer blather or not, they declared that they know about it from an interview conducted with Menelaus himself…

  • （关于科尔奇人）我首先意识到自己，然后听到

  • (about the Colchians) I first came to realize myself, and then heard from

  • 后来其他人认为科尔奇人显然是埃及人……我自己就猜到了他们的埃及血统，不仅因为科尔基人皮肤黝黑，头发卷曲……更重要的是因为科尔基人、埃及人和埃塞俄比亚人是世界上唯一实行割礼的民族，而且一直这样做。

  • others later, that the Colchians are obviously Egyptian…. I myself had guessed their Egyptian origin not only because the Colchians are darkskinned and curly-haired … but more importantly because Colchians, Egyptians, and Ethiopians are the only peoples in the world who practice circumcision and have always done so.

  • 我将像一些波斯人一样写作，他们不想美化居鲁士的历史，而是想呈现真实的真相，他们说……

  • And I will write as some Persians, who do not want to beautify the history of Cyrus but to present the actual truth, say …

希罗多德对事物时而感到惊讶，时而震惊，时而欣喜，时而恐惧。对于某些人来说，他根本不相信，因为他知道人们是多么容易被幻想所迷惑：

Herodotus is by turns surprised, astounded, delighted, terrified by things. To some he simply gives no credence, knowing how easily people can be carried away by fantasy:

  • 这些牧师说，神亲自来到教堂……这对我来说似乎不可信。

  • These same priests say, which does not seem credible to me, that the god himself comes to the chapel…

  • [埃及国王拉姆普西尼图斯]……他所做的——故事是这样的，但我觉得难以置信——就是把他的女儿安置在一个房间里，并指示不分青红皂白地接受所有男人……

  • [The Egyptian king Rhampsinitus]… what he did—so the story goes, but I find it unbelievable—was install his daughter in a room with instructions to accept all men indiscriminately …

  • 光头[??]人说，这让我觉得不太可能，山上居住着一种山羊足种族，当人们经过他们时，你会发现其他人，他们一次睡六个月。我根本不敢相信……

  • The bald-headed [??] people say, which strikes me as improbable, that the mountains are inhabited by a goat-footed race, and when one passes them, one will find others, who sleep for six months at a time. I cannot believe that at all …

  • （关于神经人将自己变成狼的能力）：我个人不相信这一点，但他们尽管难以置信，却还是提出了这一说法，甚至发誓他们说的是实话。

  • (about the Neurians’ ability to turn themselves into wolves): Personally I do not believe this, but they make the claim despite its implausibility, and even swear that they are telling the truth.

  • （关于在人们面前跪下的雕像）：这件事对我来说似乎不可信，但也许对其他人来说——是的……

  • (about statues that fell to their knees before people): This thing does not seem credible to me, but perhaps to someone else—yes …

历史上第一位全球主义者对同时代人的无知嗤之以鼻：当我看到所有绘制世界地图的人中没有一个人明智地将其绘制出来时，我感到好笑。他们把海洋描绘成一条绕地球外部流动的河流，就像用圆规画出的圆形一样，把亚洲和欧洲画成同样大小。我现在要 简要解释每个大陆有多大以及每个大陆在地图上的样子。

History’s first globalist sneers and scoffs at the ignorance of his contemporaries: I am amused when I see that not one of all the people who have drawn maps of the world has set it out sensibly. They show Ocean as a river flowing around the outside of the earth, which is as circular as if it had been drawn with a pair of compasses, and they make Asia and Europe the same size. I shall now briefly explain how big each of these continents is and what each of them should look like on the map.

在描绘了亚洲、欧洲和非洲之后，他用这句话结束了他对世界的描述：我不知道为什么地球——毕竟是单一的——有三个不同的名字（每个名字都是一个女士） …

And after delineating Asia, Europe, and Africa, he ends his description of the world with the sentence: I have no idea why the earth—which is, after all, single—has three separate names (each of which is the name of a woman)…



在他被狗和鸟撕碎之前

BEFORE HE IS TORN APART

BY DOGS AND BIRDS

时间与我一起在埃塞俄比亚旅行最频繁的司机名叫内古西（Negusi），我是通过一条有些迂回的路线，穿过乌干达、坦桑尼亚和肯尼亚到达埃塞俄比亚的。他身材瘦小，瘦削的脖子上青筋暴起，脖子上有一个不成比例的大而匀称的脑袋。他的眼睛非常引人注目——巨大、黑暗，被一层闪亮的薄膜遮住，就像一个梦幻女孩的眼睛。内古西非常爱干净：每到一站，他都会用随身携带的小刷子小心地清除衣服上的灰尘。在这个国家，这并非完全没有道理，在旱季，没有一个地方没有灰尘和沙子。

The driver with whom I traveled about most frequently in Ethiopia—which I had reached by a somewhat circuitous route, through Uganda, Tanzania, and Kenya—was called Negusi. He was a slight, thin man, on whose skinny neck swollen with veins rested a disproportionately large yet shapely head. His eyes were remarkable—enormous, dark, obscured by a shiny film, like the eyes of a dreamy girl. Negusi was compulsively neat: at each stop he carefully removed the dust from his clothes with a little brush, which he always carried with him. This was not wholly unjustified in this country, where in the dry season, there was no place free of dust and sand.

我和内古西的旅行——我们在困难和危险的条件下一起行驶了数千公里——再次让我明白了人类有多么丰富的标志和信号。一个人所要做的就是努力注意并解释它们。我们倾向于认为另一个人仅通过口头或书面文字与我们交流，因此我们不会停下来考虑还有许多其他对话方式。一切都会说话：面部和眼睛的表情、手的姿势和身体的动作、身体发出的振动、他的衣服和样子磨损；数十个其他发射器、放大器和消音器，它们共同构成了个体的存在，以及——用英语世界的自负来说——他的个人化学反应。

My travels with Negusi—and we drove thousands of kilometers together under difficult and hazardous conditions—were yet another lesson in what an abundance of signs and signals any human being is. All one has to do is make an effort to notice and interpret them. Predisposed to thinking that another person communicates with us solely by means of the spoken or written word, we do not stop to consider that there are many other methods of conversation. Everything speaks: the expression of the face and eyes, the gestures of the hand and the movements of the body, the vibrations which the latter sends out, his clothing and the way it is worn; dozens of other transmitters, amplifiers, and mufflers, which together make up the individual being and—to use the conceit of the Anglophone world—his personal chemistry.

技术将人类的交流简化为电子信号，使这种多种多样的非语言语言变得贫乏和静音，当我们在一起、近距离接触时，我们会不断地、无意识地进行交流。而且，这种不言而喻的语言，即面部表情和微小手势的语言，比口头或书面的语言更加真诚和真诚。不言而喻地撒谎、掩盖谎言和虚伪要困难得多。为了使一个人能够真正地掩饰自己的思想，因为思想的泄露可能会带来危险，中国文化完善了冰冷的面孔、神秘的面具和空洞的目光的艺术：只有在这样的屏障后面才能真正隐藏一个人。

Technology, which reduces human exchange to an electronic signal, impoverishes and mutes this multifarious nonverbal language with which, when we are together, in close proximity, we continually and unconsciously communicate. This unspoken language, moreover, the language of facial expression and minute gesture, is infinitely more sincere and genuine than the spoken or written one; it is far more difficult to tell lies without words, to conceal falsehood and hypocrisy. So that a man could truly camouflage his thoughts, the disclosure of which could prove dangerous, Chinese culture perfected the art of the frozen face, of the inscrutable mask and the vacant gaze: only behind such a screen could someone truly hide.

内古西只知道两种英语表达方式：“问题”和“没问题”。

Negusi knew only two expressions in English: “problem” and “no problem.”

但在最令人担忧的情况下，我们却用这种胡言乱语进行了有效的沟通。结合每个人特有的无言信号，只要我们仔细观察他，就可以表达出丰富的信息——可以说，把他吸进去——两个词足以让我们感觉不到我们之间的鸿沟，使一起旅行成为可能。

But using this gibberish we communicated ably in the most fraught circumstances. In conjunction with the wordless signals particular to each human being and which can speak volumes if only we would observe him carefully—drink him in, as it were—two words sufficed for us to feel no chasm between us and made traveling together possible.

一支军事巡逻队在戈巴山脉拦住了我们。这些地区的士兵肆意掠夺而不受惩罚，他们被宠坏了，贪婪，而且经常喝醉。四周山峦叠嶂，荒凉空旷，没有一个生灵。内古西参与谈判。我看得出来他正在解释什么；他把手放在心口上。其他人也在说话。他们调整自动步枪，将头盔拉低到额头上，这只会让他们看起来更具威胁性。 “内古西，”我问道，“问题？”答案可以是双重的。他可以轻蔑地回答：“没问题！”然后继续前行，看上去很满意。或者他可以用严肃甚至恐惧的声音说：“问题！”这意味着我必须掏出十美元，他将把这些钱交给士兵，以便他们让我们通过。

A military patrol stops us in the Goba mountains. Soldiers in these parts maraud with impunity, are spoiled, greedy, and frequently drunk. All around are craggy mountains, a desolate emptiness without a single living soul. Negusi gets into a negotiation. I can see that he is explaining something; he puts his hand to his heart. The others are also speaking. They adjust their automatic rifles, pull their helmets lower down on their foreheads, which only makes them look more menacing. “Negusi,” I ask, “problem?” The answer can be twofold. He can reply, dismissively: “No problem!” and drive on, looking satisfied. Or he can say in a serious, even fearful voice, “Problem!,” which signals that I must pull out ten dollars, which he will hand over to the soldiers so that they might let us pass.

突然之间，出于我无法理解的原因，当我们开车穿过一个无人居住、毫无生气的地区时，内古西在路上什么也没有看到，他变得焦虑、蠕动并环顾四周。 “内古西，”我问道，“有什么问题吗？”他没有回答，继续向各个方向张望，显然很紧张。车内的气氛变得紧张起来。他的恐惧开始影响我：谁知道等待我们的是什么？一个小时就这样过去了，拐过一个弯后，内古西放松下来，随着阿姆哈拉歌曲的节奏愉快地拍打方向盘。 “Negusi，”我问，“没问题吗？” “没问题！”他高兴地喊道。后来我在附近的一个城镇发现，我们行驶的一段道路因武装团伙经常袭击、抢劫甚至杀害路人而臭名昭著。

All of a sudden, for reasons I cannot fathom, seeing nothing on the road as we drive through an unpopulated, lifeless area, Negusi becomes anxious, squirming and looking all around. “Negusi,” I ask, “problem?” He doesn’t answer and continues to peer in all directions, visibly nervous. The atmosphere in the car grows tense. His fear starts to rub off on me: who knows what lies in wait for us? An hour passes in this way, and then, after a turn in the road, Negusi relaxes and happily slaps the steering wheel to the rhythm of some Amharic song. “Negusi,” I ask, “no problem?” “No problem!” he exclaims joyfully. I discover later in a nearby town that we had been driving a stretch of road notorious for armed bands that attack, rob, and even kill passersby on a regular basis.

这里的人们对外面的世界知之甚少，对非洲不了解，甚至不了解自己的国家，但在自己的小故乡、自己的部落的疆土上，他们却熟悉每条小路、每一棵树、每一块石头。这些地方对他们来说并不神秘，因为他们从小就熟悉它们，无数次在夜色中行走，用手触摸路边的巨石和树木，用赤脚感受看不见的小路。 。

People here have little awareness of the greater world, do not know Africa well, or even their own country, but on the circumscribed territory of their small homeland, of their own tribe, they are familiar with every path, every tree and stone. Such places hold no mystery for them, because they have come to know them from childhood, walking countless times at night in the darkness, touching with their hands the boulders and trees standing by the roadside, feeling with their bare feet where the invisible paths run.

在我与内古西一起穿越阿姆哈拉土地时，情况就是如此。他是一个穷人，但在他内心的某个小角落里，他对这片广阔的地区感到自豪，这片地区的真正边界只有他才能真正划定。

It is thus during my travels over Amharic lands with Negusi. He is a poor man, but in some little corner of his heart he feels pride in this vast region, whose real boundaries only he can truly delineate.

我口渴了，内古西在一条小溪边停下来，鼓励我喝一口冰凉、晶莹的水。

I am thirsty, so Negusi stops by a stream and encourages me to sip its cold, crystalline water.

“没问题！”他看到我在犹豫这水是否干净，便叫道，于是他把大头埋进了水里。

“No problem!” he calls, seeing that I am hesitating about whether this water is clean, and he submerges his large head in it.

后来，我想坐在附近的一些岩石上，但内古西禁止：

Later, I want to sit down on some nearby rocks, but Negusi forbids it:

“问题！”他发出警告，并用手做了一个锯齿状的动作，表示那里可能有蛇。

“Problem!” he warns, and indicates with a zigzagging motion of his hand that there might be snakes there.

每一次深入埃塞俄比亚的探险都是一种奢侈。平时，我的日子都花在收集信息、写电报和去邮局上，这样值班的电报员就可以将我的电报转发到伦敦的波兰通讯社办事处（事实证明，这比直接寄到华沙的成本要低） ）。信息收集是一项耗时、困难且危险的工作，就像一次狩猎探险，很少能捕获猎物。这里只出版一份报纸：名为《埃塞俄比亚先驱报》的四版报纸。 （我在乡村多次目睹一辆公共汽车从亚的斯亚贝巴驶来，不仅载着乘客，还载着一本该出版物。人们聚集在市场上，市长或当地教师大声朗读阿姆哈拉语的文章，并总结了这些文章用英文写的。每个人都全神贯注地听着，气氛几乎是喜庆的：一份报纸从首都寄来了！）

Every expedition into the depths of Ethiopia is a luxury. Ordinarily, my days are spent gathering information, writing telegrams, and going to the post office, so the telegrapher on duty can forward my dispatches to the Polish Press Agency offices in London (this turns out to be less costly than sending them directly to Warsaw). The collecting of information is a time-consuming, difficult, and dodgy business—a hunting expedition that rarely results in capturing one’s quarry. Only one newspaper is published here: four pages called the Ethiopian Herald. (I witnessed several times in the countryside a bus arriving from Addis Ababa, bringing not only passengers but a single copy of this publication as well. People gathered in the marketplace and the mayor or a local teacher read aloud the articles in Amharic and summarized those written in English. Everyone listened raptly and the atmosphere was almost festive: a newspaper had arrived from the capital!)

此时埃塞俄比亚有一位皇帝统治；没有政党、工会或议会反对派。有厄立特里亚游击队，但在遥远的北部，大多是难以穿越的山区。索马里反对派运动在同样难以进入的地区——欧加登沙漠开展活动。是的，我可以以某种方式到达这两个地方，但这需要几个月的时间，而且我是波兰在整个非洲的唯一通讯员。我不能突然变得沉默，消失在大陆无人居住的荒原中。

An emperor rules Ethiopia at this time; there are no political parties, trade unions, or parliamentary opposition. There are Eritrean guerrillas, but far away in the north, in mostly impenetrable mountains. A Somali opposition movement operates out in a region of equally difficult access, the desert of the Ogaden. Yes, I could somehow make my way to both places, but it would take months, and I am Poland’s only correspondent in all of Africa. I cannot just suddenly go silent, disappear into the continent’s uninhabited wastelands.

那么我该如何收集我的材料呢？我来自富裕新闻机构（路透社、美联社或法新社）的同事聘请翻译，但我缺乏这方面的资金。此外，他们的办公室还配备了一台功能强大的收音机：美国的 Zenith 收音机、Trans Oceanic 收音机，人们可以通过它们收听全世界的广播。但这要花很多钱，我只能幻想。所以我会走路、询问、倾听、哄骗、搜集信息，并将事实、观点和故事串联起来。我不抱怨，因为这种方法使我能够结识很多人，了解媒体或广播中没有报道的事情。

So how am I to gather my material? My colleagues from the wealthy news agencies—Reuters, AP, or AFP—hire translators, but I lack the funds for this. Furthermore, their offices are equipped with a powerful radio: an American Zenith, a Trans Oceanic, from which one can tune in the entire world. But it costs a fortune, and I can only fantasize about it. So I walk, ask, listen, cajole, scrape, and string together facts, opinions, stories. I don’t complain, because this method enables me to meet many people and find out about things not covered in the press or on the radio.

当休息的时候，我会和内古西安排去野外。一个人不能冒险太远，因为在广阔的地方很容易被困一连几天，甚至几周。我想到的距离是一百或两百公里，距离大山的起点还有一段距离。此外，圣诞节即将来临，整个非洲，甚至穆斯林部分，都明显变得更加安静，更不用说十六个世纪以来一直信奉基督教的埃塞俄比亚了。 “去阿尔巴·明奇！”给那些知情人士提供建议，他们说得如此坚定，以至于这个名字开始对我产生神奇的共鸣。

When there’s a lull, I make arrangements with Negusi to go out into the field. One cannot venture too far, because out there in the vastness it is easy to get stuck for days on end, weeks even. I have in mind a distance of one hundred or two hundred kilometers, before the great mountains begin. Furthermore, Christmas is approaching, and all of Africa, even the Muslim part, is growing noticeably quieter, to say nothing of Ethiopia, which has been Christian for sixteen centuries. “Go to Arba Minch!” advise those in the know, and they say it with such conviction that the name begins to acquire a magical resonance for me.

事实上，这确实是一个非凡的地方。在一片平坦空旷的平原上，阿巴亚湖和查莫湖之间的低地峡上，矗立着一座漆成白色的木制营房——伯克勒莫尔酒店。每个房间都有一个开放式阳台，一直延伸到湖边——人们可以从甲板上直接跳进翠绿的湖水中（根据太阳光线的角度，湖水可以变成天蓝色、绿色、几乎紫色，晚上则为海军蓝和黑色）。

Indeed, it turns out to be a truly extraordinary place. On a flat and empty plain, on a low isthmus between the lakes of Abaya and Chamo, stands a wooden, white-painted barracks—the Berkele Mole Hotel. Each room gives onto an open verandah which extends right to the edge of a lake—one can jump from the deck straight into the emerald water (which, depending on the angle of the sun’s rays, can turn azure blue, green, almost purple, and, in the evenings, navy blue and black).

早上，一位身着白袍的农妇在阳台上放了一张木制扶手椅和一张巨大的木雕桌子。寂静、水、几棵金合欢树，远处的背景是巨大的深绿色阿马罗山脉。在这里，人们感觉自己就像是世界之王。

In the morning, a peasant woman in a white robe sets up on the verandah a wooden armchair, as well as a massive sculpted wooden table. Silence, water, several acacia trees, and in the far distance, in the background, the gigantic, dark green Amaro mountains. One feels like the king of the world here.

•••

•   •   •

我随身带着一捆关于非洲的期刊，但我时不时也会拿起那本与我形影不离的大部头，它已成为我惯常的避难所，是逃离世界紧张局势和紧张情绪的避难所。追求新奇，进入一个充满阳光和安静的和平境界，这种境界源于已经发生的事件、现在已经离开的人，有时甚至从未在那里，只是想象、虚构和阴影的产物。但这一次我逃跑的希望落空了。我看到我的希腊世界正在发生严重而危险的事情，我感觉到一场历史性的风暴正在酝酿，一场险恶的飓风正在逼近。

I’ve brought with me a bundle of periodicals with articles about Africa, but from time to time I also reach for the tome from which I am inseparable, which has become my accustomed refuge, a retreat from the tensions of the world and the nervous pursuit of novelty into a peaceful realm of sunshine and quiet that emanates from events that have already occurred, people now gone and sometimes who were never there, having been only contrivances of the imagination, fictions, shadows. But this time my hopes for escape come to naught. I can see that serious and dangerous things are happening in my Greek’s world, I sense a historic storm brewing, a sinister hurricane approaching.

到目前为止，我已经和希罗多德一起漫游了很远很远的地方，到了他宇宙的边缘——埃及人和马萨格泰人，斯基泰人和埃塞俄比亚人。但现在是停止这些遥远的游历的时候了，因为事件正在从这些遥远的边境转移到地中海东部，转移到波斯和希腊，或者更一般地说，亚洲和欧洲的交汇处——简而言之，转移到地中海东部。非常世界上的脐。

Until now I had wandered far and wide with Herodotus, to the edges of his universe—to the Egyptians and the Massagetae, to the Scythians and the Ethiopians. But it is time to cease these farflung peregrinations, for events are now shifting from these distant borderlands to the eastern part of the Mediterranean Sea, to where Persia and Greece, or more generally speaking, Asia and Europe, meet—in short, to the very omphalos of the world.

就好像希罗多德在他的全部作品的第一部分中建造了一个巨大的露天圆形剧场，在其中放置了来自亚洲、欧洲和非洲的数十个甚至数百个国家和部落——他所知道的全人类——并说对他们说：现在仔细观察，因为在你们眼前将上演世界上最伟大的戏剧！他希望大家仔细观看。而舞台上的动作确实发生了戏剧性的突然转变。

It is as if in the first part of his oeuvre Herodotus constructed a massive open-air amphitheater in which he placed dozens, even hundreds of nations and tribes from Asia, Europe, and Africa—from all of humanity as he knew it—and said to them: Now observe carefully, because here before your very eyes will unfold the world’s greatest drama! He wants everyone to watch carefully. And the action on the stage does indeed take an abrupt dramatic turn.

波斯国王老大流士正在准备一场针对希腊的大战，为他在萨迪斯和马拉松的失败报仇（希罗多德的法则之一：不要羞辱人民，因为他们将在复仇的梦想中生存）。他把他的整个帝国、整个亚洲都征召入伍，投入到准备工作中。但在这一切之中这发生在公元前485 年。 （顺便说一句，这是希罗多德出生的推测年份），他在统治三十六年后去世。经过各种斗争和阴谋，他的小儿子薛西斯继承了王位，这是大流士的妻子阿托莎（现已寡妇）心爱的孩子，希罗多德认为阿托莎在帝国中发挥了巨大的影响力。

Old Darius, king of the Persians, is preparing a great war against Greece to avenge his defeats in Sardis and at Marathon (one of Herodotus’s laws: Do not humiliate people, because they will thereafter subsist on dreams of revenge). He conscripts his entire empire, all of Asia, into the preparations. But in the midst of all this, in 485 B.C.E. (the presumed year, incidentally, of Herodotus’s birth), he dies, having ruled for thirty-six years. After various struggles and intrigues, his young son Xerxes assumes the throne—the beloved child of Darius’s wife, now widow, Atossa, whom Herodotus credits with having exercised great influence in the empire.

薛西斯继承了他父亲的计划——准备对希腊人发动战争——但他一心想首先进攻埃及：埃及人已经叛变，反对波斯占领他们的土地并要求独立。薛西斯认为，平息埃及的起义是更紧迫的事情，而对希腊人的远征可以等待。但他的年长且非常有影响力的表弟、已故大流士姐姐的儿子马多尼斯却持不同意见。谁关心埃及人？他说。我们必须向希腊人发起进攻！ （希罗多德怀疑马尔多尼乌斯可能是为了成为被征服的希腊的最终总督而急于获得权力）：“大师，雅典人对波斯造成的所有伤害却逍遥法外，这是错误的。”

Xerxes inherits his father’s project—preparing for war against the Greeks—but he is of a mind to strike against Egypt first: the Egyptians have mutinied against the Persian occupation of their land and want independence. Xerxes believes that quelling the Egyptian uprising is a more pressing matter, and that the expedition against the Greeks can wait. But his older and very influential cousin, Mardonius, son of deceased Darius’s sister, is of another opinion. Who cares about the Egyptians? he says. We must move against the Greeks! (Herodotus suspects Mardonius of being in a hurry to attain power probably as eventual satrap of a conquered Greece): “Master, it’s wrong for the Athenians to go unpunished for all the harm they’ve done Persia.”

希罗多德告诉我们，随着时间的推移，马尔多尼乌斯确实让薛西斯相信希腊战争的必要性。尽管如此，波斯国王还是先出征埃及，镇压了叛乱，再次征服了该国，然后才将注意力转向希腊人。尽管如此，他还是非常清楚眼前行动的严重性，这就是为什么他召集了波斯领导人的精选来开会，因为他想听听他们要说什么。他与他们分享了他征服世界的计划：“重述居鲁士、冈比西斯和我父亲大流士赢得的所有胜利以及他们吞并的所有人民是没有意义的，因为你们已经很清楚他们的成就了。但我呢？当我成为波斯国王时，我开始思考如何避免被那些在我之前享有这一荣誉地位的人抛在后面，并且 我怎样才能像他们一样扩张波斯帝国……那么，我 召开这次会议，就是为了告诉你们我的计划。

Herodotus tells us that with time Mardonius does indeed convince Xerxes of the necessity of the Greek war. The Persian king nonetheless sets off first against Egypt, suppresses the rebellion, subjugates the country once again, and only then turns his attention to the Greeks. Still he is quite cognizant of the seriousness of the action at hand, which is why he summoned the pick of the leading Persians to a meeting, because he wanted to hear what they had to say. He shares with them his plans for world conquest: “There would be no point in recounting all the victories Cyrus, Cambyses, and my father Darius won, and all the peoples they annexed, because you are already well aware of their achievements. But what about me? When I became the king of Persia, I began to wonder how to avoid being left behind by those who preceded me in this position of honour, and how I might increase the Persian empire just as much as they did …. The reason I have convened this meeting, then, is to tell you my plans.

我打算架起赫勒斯滂海峡，派军队穿越欧洲，进攻希腊，这样我就能让雅典人为他们对波斯和我父亲所做的一切付出代价……。在占领雅典并将其付之一炬之前我不会休息。

I intend to bridge the Hellespont and march an army through Europe and against Greece, so that I can make the Athenians pay for all that they have done to Persia and to my father …. I will not rest until I have captured Athens and put it to the torch.

“......如果我们征服了他们和他们的邻居......我们将使波斯领土仅限于天空，宙斯的领域，这样太阳就不会照射到我们边界之外的任何土地......。如果我的信息准确的话，一旦我们消灭了我提到的那些人，就没有人——没有城镇或人民——能够向我们提供武装抵抗。因此，无辜者将与那些冤枉我们的人一起承受奴隶制的枷锁。”

“… If we conquer them and their neighbours … we will make Persian territory end only at the sky, the domain of Zeus, so that the sun will not shine on any land beyond our borders …. If my information is accurate, once we have eliminated those I have mentioned, there will be nobody left—no town or people—capable of offering us armed resistance. And so the innocent will bear the yoke of slavery along with those who have wronged us.”

接下来发言的是马尔多纽斯。为了赢得薛西斯的芳心，他以阿谀奉承开始：“主人，您是有史以来最伟大的波斯人，将来也没有人能与您相比。”在这种仪式性的介绍之后，他试图向薛西斯保证征服希腊人不会有任何困难。 '没问题！”马多纽斯似乎在兴奋地说道。然后他声称“希腊人通常以极其愚蠢的方式发动战争，因为他们无知且无能……”那么，陛下，谁会反对您呢？当你率领一支庞大的军队和整个舰队从亚洲而来时，谁会用战争威胁你呢？我相信希腊人不会那么鲁莽。”

Next to speak is Mardonius. To win over Xerxes, he begins with flattery: “Master, you are the greatest Persian there has ever been, nor will there ever be anyone to equal you in the future either.” After this ritualistic introduction, he tries to assure Xerxes that there will be no difficulties in conquering the Greeks. ‘No problem!” the excited Mardonius seems to be saying. He then claims that “the Greeks usually wage war in an extremely stupid fashion, because they’re ignorant and incompetent …. So, my lord, who is going to oppose you? Who is going to threaten you with war when you come from Asia at the head of a massive army and with your whole fleet? I am sure that the Greeks are not so foolhardy.”

聚集的波斯人陷入了沉默：没有其他人有话要说——当然，没有人敢发表与他们面前的相反的意见……。

Silence falls among the assembled Persians: No one else had anything to say—certainly, no one dared to voice an opinion contrary to the one before them ….

这是非常可以理解的。想象一下这种情况：我们在波斯帝国的首都苏萨。在皇宫里一个通风良好、阴影笼罩的大厅里，年轻的薛西斯坐在王座上，周围的石凳上坐着波斯领袖的精选人选。议会正在商议世界的终极之战：如果取得胜利，整个世界将属于波斯人的国王。

It is quite understandable. Imagine the situation: We are in Susa, the capital of the Persian empire. In an airy, shadow-filled hall in the royal palace, young Xerxes sits on a throne, and all around on stone benches sits the pick of the leading Persians. The council is deliberating the world’s ultimate battle: if victory is achieved, the whole world will belong to the king of the Persians.

而且，预期的战场距离苏萨很远——敏捷的信使需要三个多月的时间才能穿越波斯首都和雅典之间的距离。在这样的距离进行的操作有些不真实。但这并不是被召唤的波斯人不敢表达相反观点的原因。尽管他们很重要，很有影响力，尽管是精英中的精英，但他们却知道自己生活在一个独裁专制的国家，只要薛西斯一点头，他们中的任何一个人都会失去理智。于是他们坐在那里，害怕地擦着额头。他们不敢说话。气氛肯定与斯大林领导下的政治局类似：在这两种情况下，不仅一个人的职业生涯受到威胁，而且生活本身也受到威胁。

Furthermore, the prospective field of battle is far away from Susa—agile messengers need more than three months to cross the distance between the Persian capital and Athens. There is something unreal about an operation taking place at such a remove. But that is not why the summoned Persians dare not express contrary views. Despite being important and influential, despite constituting the elite of the elites, they nevertheless know that they live in an authoritarian and despotic state, and that it takes only a nod from Xerxes for any one of them to lose his head. So there they sit, frightened, mopping their foreheads. They dare not speak. The atmosphere must have resembled that of the Politburo under Stalin: at stake in both instances not just one’s career, but life itself.

然而，有人可以毫无畏惧地大声说话。这是老阿达班努斯，大流士的兄弟，薛西斯的叔叔。即便如此，他还是谨慎地开始，确保为敢于发表意见提供理由：“大人，除非听到反对的意见，否则不可能在各种计划中挑选并决定哪一个最好。”他还提醒薛西斯，他警告他的父亲和他自己的兄弟大流士不要对斯基泰人进行远征，因为他认为那次探险也会有糟糕的结局。事情确实如此。现在希腊人？！“大人，您正在计划的这场战役是针对比斯基泰人优越得多的人；他们在陆地和海上都因勇敢而享有最高声誉。”

There is someone, however, who can speak up without fear. It is old Artabanus, Darius’s brother and Xerxes’ uncle. Even so, he begins cautiously, making certain to offer justification for daring to voice his opinions: “My lord, unless opposing views are heard, it is impossible to pick and choose between various plans and decide which one is best.” He also reminds Xerxes that he cautioned his father, and his own brother, Darius, not to undertake the expedition against the Scythians, because that one too, he had felt, would end badly. And so it did. And now the Greeks?! “This campaign you’re planning, my lord, is against men who are vastly superior to the Scythians; they have the highest reputation for bravery on both land and sea.”

他建议谨慎行事并进行长期反思。他攻击马尔多尼斯鼓励国王发动战争，并向他建议：“让我们每个人都用我们孩子的生命来赌这个结果。如果事情的发展如你所说的对国王有利，就让我的孩子们被处死吧，我也会加入他们的行列；但如果事情如我所预测的那样，让你的孩子遭受这种命运，如果你能回家的话，你也一样。如果你不准备冒险，但仍决心将军队带到海外希腊，我可以告诉你，马多尼乌斯的什么消息将传到留在这里的人的耳朵里：他们会被告知，马多尼乌斯 给波斯带来了一场巨大的灾难，然后你在雅典境内的某个地方被狗和鸟撕碎了……”

He counsels prudence and long reflection. He attacks Mardonius for encouraging the king to go to war, and proposes to him: “Let each of us gamble the lives of our children on the outcome. If matters turn out as you say they will for the king, let my children be put to death, and I will join them; but if things turn out as I am predicting, let your children suffer that fate, and you too, if you make it back home. If you aren’t prepared to run the risk, but are still determined to take the army overseas to Greece, I can tell you what news of Mardonius will reach the ears of those who stay behind here: they will be told that Mardonius was the cause of a great disaster for Persia, and that you were then torn apart by dogs and birds somewhere in Athenian territory …”

当每个人都在思考所提出的赌注时，紧张气氛加剧。薛西斯勃然大怒，称阿尔达班努斯为胆小鬼，并禁止他随行参战作为惩罚。他解释说：“现在任何一方都不可能退出，唯一的问题是我们是否积极主动。最终要么整个波斯落入希腊人手中，要么整个希腊落入波斯人手中；这场战争没有中间立场。”

Tensions rise as everyone reflects on the wager proposed. Xerxes becomes angry, calls Artabanus a coward, and as punishment forbids him to go with him to war. He explains: “It is impossible for either side to withdraw now, the only question at stake is whether or not we actively take the initiative. And in the end either all Persia will be in Greek hands, or all Greece will be in Persian hands; there is no middle ground in this war.”

他解散了委员会。后来，到了晚上，薛西斯仍然对阿达班努斯所表达的观点感到担忧。晚上他想了想，他非常确信进军希腊并不符合他的最佳利益。在这次改变主意之后，他睡着了，在夜间他做了以下的梦，至少波斯人是这么说的。薛西斯梦见一个高大英俊的男人站在他身边，说道：“波斯人，你改变主意了吗？你决定不去希腊探险吗……？……不，继续你白天决定的行动方针。”薛西斯的梦中，说完这句话，那人就飞走了。

And he dissolves the council. Later, during the night, Xerxes was still worried by the view Artabanus had expressed. He thought it over during the night and he became quite convinced that it was not in his best interests for him to march on Greece. After this change of heart, he fell asleep, and during the night he had the following dream, or so the Persians say. Xerxes dreamt that a tall, handsome man stood over him and said, “Are you changing your mind, Persian? Are you deciding against taking an expedition to Greece …?… No, keep the course of action you decided on during the daytime.” In Xerxes’ dream, after delivering this speech, the man flew away.

天亮时，薛西斯再次召开会议。他无视这个梦，宣布他改变了主意，不会有战争。波斯人听了薛西斯的话很高兴，在他面前跪拜。

Come daylight, Xerxes once again convenes a council. Ignoring the dream, he announces that he has changed his mind and that there will be no war. The Persians were delighted with what Xerxes said, and prostrated themselves before him.

然而那天晚上，当薛西斯睡着的时候，同样的人影又出现在他的梦中，说道：“……如果你不立即出去征战，就会发生这样的事情。你已经迅速上升到了显赫而重要的地位，但你也会同样迅速地再次被贬低。”

That night, however, when Xerxes was asleep, the same figure appeared to him again in a dream and said, “… if you do not go out on this campaign immediately, this is what will happen. You have risen rapidly to a position of prominence and importance, but you will be laid low again just as swiftly.”

薛西斯被这个幽灵吓坏了，他从床上跳起来，派了一个信使去找阿达巴努斯。他向他承认，自从他决定回忆起对希腊人的远征那一刻起，噩梦就一直困扰着他：“……自从我退缩并改变主意后，我就一直被一个不同意的梦想人物所困扰。我正在做的事情。事实上他刚才威胁了我，然后就消失了。如果这个梦是神送来的，那么他只有在战役结束时才会满意 如果发生了针对希腊的事情，同样的梦想也会飞向你，并给你和我一样的指示。”

Terrified by this apparition, Xerxes jumps out of bed and sends a messenger for Artabanus. He confesses to him the nightmares that have plagued him from the moment he decided to recall the expedition against the Greeks: “… ever since I’ve backed down and changed my mind, I’ve been haunted by a dream figure who does not approve of what I’m doing at all. In fact he threatened me just now, and then disappeared. If this dream is being sent by a god and he will be satisfied only when the campaign against Greece takes place, the same dream should wing its way to you as well, and give you the same instructions as it did me.”

阿达班努斯试图安抚薛西斯：“事实上，……梦想并非来自众神，我的孩子……。我们在梦中出现的景象往往是我们白天所关心的事情。而且，你看，我们这些天一直忙于这次探险。”

Artabanus tries to calm Xerxes: “In actual fact, … dreams don’t come from the gods, my son …. The visions that occur to us in dreams are, more often than not, the things we have been concerned about during the day. And, you see, we have been extremely occupied with this expedition for some days now.”

但薛西斯无法平静：幽灵继续拜访他，劝告他参战。他建议，既然阿尔达班不相信他，他就应该穿上王袍，坐在王位上，然后在晚上躺在王床上。阿尔达班这样做了，当他睡着的时候，梦中的人物出现在他面前，就像薛西斯出现的那样。那人站在阿达巴努斯身边说道：“这么说，你就是那个一直试图阻止薛西斯进攻希腊的人，对吗？ ……好吧，无论现在还是将来，你都无法逃脱因试图扭转不可避免的后果而受到的惩罚。” ……

But Xerxes cannot calm down: the phantom continues to visit him, exhorting him to go to war. He proposes that since Artabanus does not believe him, he should put on the royal robes, sit on the royal throne, and then, at night, lie down on the royal bed. Artabanus does this and while he was asleep the same dream figure came to him as had appeared to Xerxes. The figure stood over Artabanus and said, “So you’re the one who has been trying to discourage Xerxes from attacking Greece, are you? … Well, you will not escape punishment, either now or in the future, for trying to deflect the inevitable.”…

阿达班努斯梦见，幽灵除了发出这些威胁之外，还准备用烧红的烤串烧穿他的眼睛。他大叫一声，从床上跳起来，坐在薛西斯身边。他首先描述了他在梦中所见，然后说道：“既然你的冲动是天赐的，既然希腊人遭受的毁灭显然是天赐之物，那么现在轮到我退缩并改变主意了。” ……

Artabanus dreamt that as well as making these threats the phantom was about to burn his eyes out with red-hot skewers. He uttered a loud cry, jumped out of bed, and sat himself down next to Xerxes. First he described what he had seen in his dream, and then he said, … “Since your impetuousness is god-given, and since the destruction overtaking the Greeks is apparently heavensent, it is my turn to back down and change my mind.”…

后来，当薛西斯全神贯注于他的征战时，有一天晚上，他做了第三个梦，梦中他看到自己戴着一个用橄榄树的小枝制成的花环，这棵橄榄树的树枝遮盖了整个世界，但随后花环从他的头上消失了。他向东方贤士描述了这个梦，他们将其解释为整个世界，意味着他将获得对整个人类的统治。

Later, with Xerxes all intent on his campaign, he had a third dream one night, in which he saw himself wearing a garland made out of sprigs of an olive-tree whose branches overshadowed the whole world, but then the garland disappeared from his head. He described the dream to the Magi and they interpreted its reference to the whole world as meaning that he would gain dominion over the whole human race.

“内古西，”我早上说，然后开始收拾行李，“我们要回亚的斯亚贝巴了。”

“Negusi,” I said in the morning and started to pack, “we’re going back to Addis Ababa.”

“没问题！”他高兴地回答，微笑着，露出洁白得惊人的牙齿。

“No problem!” he answered cheerfully and smiled, showing his fantastically white teeth.

薛西斯

XERXES



  从一开始，结局就不明显。

The end is not apparent

  From the very outset.

——希罗多德

—Herodotus

中号就像希罗多德笔下的幽灵一样，在我们回到亚的斯亚贝巴后很长一段时间里，这个场景仍然困扰着我。它传达的信息是悲观的、宿命论的：人没有自由意志。他的命运就像他的遗传密码一样——他必须去命运注定的地方，做命运注定的事。宿命是至高无上的存在，是一种无所不在、包罗万象的宇宙因果力量。没有人能凌驾于它之上——万王之王不能，甚至诸神本身也不能。这就是为什么拜访薛西斯的幽灵没有神的形状。人可以与神谈判，也可以违背神，甚至试图愚弄他；就命运而言，这是不可能的。它是匿名的、无定形的，没有名字或明显的特征，它所做的只是警告、命令或威胁。它什么时候这样做？

Much like the phantom from Herodotus’s account, this scene continued to haunt me long after our return to Addis Ababa. Its message is pessimistic, fatalistic: man has no free will. He carries his fate within him like his genetic code—he must go where, and do what, destiny has ordained. Predestination is the Supreme Being, an omnipresent and all-encompassing Cosmic Causal Force. No one is above it—not the King of Kings, not even the gods themselves. Which is why the apparition that visits Xerxes does not have the shape of a god. One could negotiate with a god, one could disobey and even try to fool him; with destiny, that is impossible. It is anonymous and amorphous, lacking a name or distinct features, and all it does is warn, command, or threaten. When does it do this?

人的命运已被一成不变地铭刻在心，他只需阅读剧本，并忠实地、一点一点地执行它。如果他错误地解释了它，或者试图改变它，那么命运的幽灵就会出现，首先向他摇动手指，如果失败了，使不幸和惩罚降临在吹牛者的头上。

With his fate immutably inscribed, man has but to read the script and enact it faithfully, point by point. If he interprets it erroneously, or else attempts to alter it, then the phantom of fate will appear, at first to shake its finger at him, and failing that to bring misfortune and punishment down upon the braggart’s head.

因此，生存的条件是对自己的命运保持谦卑。薛西斯一开始接受了他的使命，即向希腊人报复，因为他们普遍侮辱了波斯人，特别是他的父亲。他向他们宣战，并发誓不征服雅典并放火焚烧它就决不罢休。但后来，听了理性的声音，他改变了主意。他压抑战争的念头，搁置入侵计划，撤退。就在这时，幻影出现在他的梦中：“疯子”，似乎在说：“别犹豫！打击希腊人就是你的命运！”

The condition for survival, therefore, is humility vis-à-vis one’s destiny. Xerxes at first accepts his mission, which is to exact vengeance on the Greeks for their having insulted the Persians generally and his father in particular. He declares war against them and vows not to rest until he conquers Athens and sets it on fire. Later, however, listening to the voices of reason, he changes his mind. He suppresses thoughts of war, sets aside the invasion plans, pulls back. It is then that the phantom appears in his dreams: “Madman,” it seems to be saying, “do not hesitate! It is your destiny to strike against the Greeks!”

最初，薛西斯试图忽视夜间来访者，将其视为一种幻觉，并以某种方式超越它。但这样做只会进一步激怒幽灵，幽灵再次出现在他的王座和床边，这次受到了严重冒犯，充满威胁。薛西斯四处寻找救援，想知道也许他的责任重担没有让他发疯——毕竟，他必须做出一个决定，这将决定世界的命运，而且最终会变得清楚，为下一个世界做出决定。一千年。他召唤了他的叔叔阿塔巴努斯。 “帮助！”他恳求道。后者首先建议薛西斯忽略这个梦：我们梦到的是白天让我们全神贯注的事情，仅此而已。阿塔班努斯说，这个梦想只是一个幻想。

Initially, Xerxes tries to ignore the nighttime visitant, to treat it as an illusion, to rise above it somehow. But by doing so he only further irritates and angers the phantom, which appears once more by his throne and bedside, this time seriously offended, menacing. Xerxes looks around for succor, wondering if perhaps the weight of his responsibility has not driven him mad—he must make a decision, after all, that will determine the fate of the world, and as will eventually become clear, determine it for the next thousand years. He summons his uncle, Artabanus. “Help!” he pleads. The latter at first counsels Xerxes to ignore the dream: we dream about what has preoccupied us during the day, that is all. The dream, Artabanus says, is but a chimera.

国王不相信，因为幻影不肯屈服；它变得比以往任何时候都更加纠缠不休、更加顽固。最后，即使是阿尔达班努斯——一个明智而明智的人，一个理性主义者和一个怀疑论者——也向幽灵的存在低头，不仅放弃了他之前的立场，而且从怀疑者变成了狂热的信徒，幽灵命运法令的执行者：希腊人？我们走吧——立刻！”人类同时受到尘世和精神世界的束缚，在这一集中我们可以看到精神的力量大于物质现实的力量。

The king is not convinced, because the phantom does not relent; it becomes more importunate and implacable than ever. Finally even Artabanus—a sensible and a wise man, a rationalist and a skeptic—bows to the ghostly presence, not only abandoning his earlier position, but changing from doubter into ardent believer, an executor of the phantom-fate’s decree: “Move against the Greek? Let’s go—at once!” Man is in thrall to both the earthly and the spiritual world, and in this episode we can see that the power of spirits is greater than the power of material reality.

听到薛西斯的这些噩梦，普通的波斯人或希腊人可能会嘀咕：“诸神啊，如果如此强大的人，万王之王，世界的统治者，不过是命运手中的一颗棋子，那么我呢？一个普通人，一无所有，一粒尘埃！”这种想法有理由让人感到安慰、宽慰——甚至是乐观。

Hearing about these nightmares of Xerxes, the average Persian or Greek might murmur, “Gods, if such a mighty person, the King of Kings, the ruler of the world, is but a pawn in the hands of destiny, then what about me, an ordinary man, a nothing, a mote of dust!” There is cause for comfort in this thought, relief—even optimism.

薛西斯是个奇怪的人物。尽管他统治世界相当长一段时间（事实上，几乎整个已知世界，除了雅典和斯巴达这两个城市，受到他的不懈折磨），我们对他知之甚少。他三十二岁时即位。他被对绝对权力的渴望所吞噬——对一切、所有人的权力。我想起了我曾经注意到的一篇报纸报道的标题，不幸的是我不记得作者了：“母亲，有一天我们会拥有一切吗？”这正是薛西斯一世的动力所在：他想要拥有一切。没有人反对他，因为这样做是要付出人头代价的。但在这样默许的气氛下，只要有一个反对的声音，统治者就会感到焦虑、犹豫。这遭到了阿尔达班的反对。薛西斯听完叔叔的话后失去了勇气，变得非常不确定，最终决定放弃征服计划。但这些都是人的冲动、冲突和怀疑；一位更高的力量，即决定者，现在登上了尘世的舞台。从现在开始，一切都将遵循它的法令。缘分一定要实现；你无法改变或避免它，即使它会导致深渊。

Xerxes is an odd figure. Although he ruled the world quite some time (almost the entire known world, in fact, with the exception of two cities, Athens and Sparta, to his unremitting torment), we know little about him. He assumed the throne at age thirty-two. He was consumed by desire for absolute power—power over everything and everyone. I am reminded of the title of a newspaper story I once noticed, whose author I unfortunately do not recall: “Mother, will we have everything someday?” That is precisely what animated Xerxes: he wanted to have everything. No one opposed him, because one would have had to pay with one’s head for doing so. But in such an atmosphere of acquiescence, it takes only one dissenting voice for the ruler to feel anxiety, to hesitate. It was thus with Artabanus’s objection. Xerxes lost his nerve, grew so uncertain having heard his uncle that he decided to abandon his plans of conquest. But these are human impulses, conflicts, and doubts; a Higher Power, the Deciding One, now steps onto the earthly stage. And from here on all will follow its decree. Fate must be fulfilled; you cannot alter or avoid it, even if it leads into an abyss.

因此，薛西斯按照命运之声的命令，发动战争。他认识到自己最大的优点是东方的力量，亚洲的力量——数量，不可估量的人口数量，并相信其力量和势头将压垮和粉碎敌人。 （我想起了第一次世界大战的场景：在波兰的马祖里湖区，俄罗斯将军们派出整个团突袭德军阵地，其中只有一部分士兵拥有步枪——还有一些缺乏弹药。）

Therefore Xerxes, in accordance with the Voice of Destiny’s commands, goes to war. He recognizes his greatest strength, the East’s strength, Asia’s strength—numbers, the immeasurable human mass, and trusts that its weight and momentum will crush and pulverize the enemy. (Scenes from World War I come to mind: In Poland’s lake district of Mazury, Russian generals storming German positions sent forth entire regiments in which only a portion of the soldiers had rifles—and those lacking ammunition.)

首先，薛西斯花了四年时间组建了他的军队——一个世界范围的军事联盟，帝国的所有民族、部落和氏族都将被招募到这个联盟中。光是说出它们的名字就需要希罗多德好几页纸。他计算出这支军队——步兵、骑兵和海军——人数约为五百万。当然，他夸大了。即便如此，这也是一支庞大的战力。怎么喂养它呢？如何为其提供充足的饮水？这些人和动物会沿途吸收整条河流，留下空床。幸运的是，有人观察到薛西斯每天只吃一顿饭。如果国王和他的整个军队每天吃两次饭，他们就会把整个色雷斯、马其顿和希腊变成沙漠，当地居民就会饿死。

First, Xerxes spends four years creating his army—a worldwide military coalition into whose ranks will be recruited all the peoples, tribes, and clans of the empire. Just naming them all takes Herodotus several pages. He calculates that this army—infantry, cavalry, and naval crews—numbered some five million men. He exaggerates, of course. Even so, it was a gigantic fighting force. How to feed it? How to supply it with sufficient drinking water? These men and animals would imbibe entire rivers along the way, leaving empty beds behind them. Someone observes that, luckily, Xerxes ate only once a day. If the king, and with him the entire army, ate twice daily, they would have turned all of Thrace, Macedonia, and Greece into a desert, and the local populations would have died of hunger.

希罗多德着迷于这支军队的前进，着迷于令人眼花缭乱的人员、野兽、装备、制服和盔甲的浩瀚河流。由于每个民族都有自己的服饰，这群人的多彩多姿难以描述。游行队伍的中心是两辆战车：宙斯的神圣战车，由八匹白马牵引。跟着他们步行（因为这辆战车的座位上不允许任何人）的是驾驭者，手里拿着缰绳。

Herodotus is fascinated by this army’s advance, by the vertiginous mighty river of men, beasts, and equipment, of uniforms and armor. Because each ethnic group has its own attire, the colorful diversity of this throng is difficult to describe. Two chariots form the center of the procession: the sacred chariot of Zeus, which was drawn by eight white horses. Following them on foot (because no human being is allowed to mount the seat of this chariot) came the charioteer, with the reins in his hands.

在他身后是薛西斯本人，坐在由尼萨马牵引的战车上……。

Behind him came Xerxes himself, seated on a chariot drawn by Nesaean horses….

跟随他的是 1,000 名最勇敢、最高贵的波斯矛兵，……然后 另外1000名精锐的波斯骑兵，紧随其后的是剩下的10000名最优秀的波斯步行士兵……。最后剩下的军队都在最后，不分青红皂白地聚集在一起。

He was followed by the 1,000 bravest and noblest Persian spearmen,… and then another 1,000 élite Persian horsemen, and after them came the 10,000 best remaining Persian soldiers on foot …. And finally the rest of the army brought up the rear, all massed together indiscriminately.

但我们不要被这支开赴战争的军队的装饰主义所误导。这不是狂欢节，也不是节日派对。相反。希罗多德指出，军队在沉默中艰难地行走，时不时地不得不用鞭子驱赶。

But let us not be misled by the ornamentalism of this army marching off to war. This is no carnival, no holiday party. On the contrary. Herodotus notes that the troops, walking with difficulty and in silence, had to be driven on now and then by whips.

他详细描述了波斯国王的行为。薛西斯性格不平衡，不可预测，是一个令人惊讶的矛盾体（在这一点上他很像斯塔夫罗金）。

He describes in detail the behavior of the Persian king. Xerxes is unbalanced, unpredictable, an astonishing bundle of contradictions (in this he resembles Stavrogin).

他正在和他的军队前往萨迪斯。他在路边发现一棵梧桐树，非常美丽，便给它贴上了金色的装饰，并任命一位仙人作为守护者来照顾它。

Here he is, making his way to Sardis with his army. He spots a plane-tree along the road which was so beautiful that he presented it with golden decorations and appointed one of the Immortals as guardian to look after it.

当他仍然被这棵树的魅力所迷惑时，他收到消息称赫勒斯滂海峡的一场大风暴摧毁了他下令建造的桥梁，以便他的军队能够从亚洲穿越欧洲进入希腊。薛西斯一听这话，勃然大怒。他命令手下对赫勒斯滂海峡进行三百次鞭打，并将一对脚镣沉入海中。我曾经听说他还派人去烙印赫勒斯滂海峡。不管怎样，他确实告诉人们，他已经用希腊人永远不会听到的语言来痛斥大海。 “苦水，”他们说，“这是你冤枉你的主人而他并没有亏待你的惩罚。无论你是否同意，薛西斯国王都会与你作对。人们不应该向像你这样浑浊的咸水溪流献祭，这是对的！ ”于是，大海按照他的命令受到了惩罚，他把赫勒斯滂海峡的桥梁监督者斩首了。

He is still under the spell of the tree’s charms when news reaches him that a great storm in the straits of the Hellespont has destroyed the bridges which he had ordered built so that his army could cross from Asia into Europe in its advance on Greece. Upon hearing this, Xerxes flew into a rage. He ordered his men to give the Hellespont three hundred lashes and to sink a pair of shackles into the sea. I once heard that he also dispatched men to brand the Hellespont as well. Be that as it may, he did tell the men he had thrashing the sea to revile it in terms you would never hear from a Greek. “Bitter water,” they said, “this is your punishment for wronging your master when he did no wrong to you. King Xerxes will cross you, with or without your consent. People are right not to sacrifice to a muddy, brackish stream like you!” So the sea was punished at his orders, and he had the supervisors of the bridging of the Hellespont beheaded.

我们不知道有多少个头被砍掉了。我们不知道那些被定罪的建筑工人是否温顺地献出了自己的脖子，或者是否跪下求饶。屠杀一定是可怕的，因为这样的桥梁是由数千人建造的落在成千上万的人身上。无论如何，这些惩罚让薛西斯满意，帮助他恢复了心理平衡。他的人民在赫勒斯滂海峡上修建了新的桥梁，贤士们宣布所有预兆都是吉祥的。

We do not know how many of these heads were cut off. We do not know if the condemned builders meekly offered up their necks, or if they fell on their knees and begged for mercy. The carnage must have been horrific, because such bridges were built by thousands upon thousands of people. In any event, the punishments satisfy Xerxes, help him regain his mental equilibrium. His people build new bridges across the Hellespont, and the Magi announce that all omens are auspicious.

国王听到这个消息欣喜若狂，决定继续前进，这时他认识的吕底亚人皮修斯来到他面前请求帮助：“主人，我有五个儿子，他们都必须与您一起征战希腊。请大人怜悯我年老的时候，将我的一个儿子，也就是长子，从兵役中释放出来，让他可以照顾我，管理我的财产。但请带上其他四个人——祝您满载而归！ ”

The king, overjoyed at this news, decides to press on when a Lydian he knew, Pythius, comes to him and begs for a favor: “Master, I have five sons, all of whom have to march with you against Greece. Please, my lord, take pity on me in my old age and release one of my sons, the eldest one, from military service, so that he can look after me and manage my property as well. But take the other four with you—and may you return home with all your objectives attained!”

薛西斯听了这句话，再次勃然大怒：“可恶！ “薛西斯对老人喊道。 “……你怎么敢提到你的儿子，你只不过是我的奴隶，应该带着你的全家、妻子和所有人跟随我？……”薛西斯一给皮蒂乌斯这个答案，他就命令那些人他手下负责这些事情的人找到了皮蒂乌斯的长子并将他砍成两半。然后把一半放在路的右边，一半放在路的左边，让军队从中间通过。

At these words Xerxes once again falls into a fury: “Damn you!” Xerxes shouts at the old man. “… How dare you mention a son of yours, when you are no more than my slave, and should follow in my train with your whole household, wife and all?…” As soon as Xerxes had given Pythius this answer, he ordered those of his men who were responsible for such matters to find the eldest of Pythius’ sons and to cut him in half. Then they were to place one half on the right of the road and the other half on the left, so that the army would pass between them.

这就是发生的事情。

And that is what happened.

源源不断的军队沿着道路鱼贯而行，在鞭子的噼啪声的驱使下，所有的士兵都看到了躺在他们两侧的皮蒂乌斯长子的血淋淋的遗骸。皮修斯当时在哪里？他站在尸体旁边吗？是通过哪一部分来实现的？当薛西斯驾着战车接近时，他表现如何？他脸上是什么表情？这是不可知的，因为作为奴隶，他必须脸朝地跪下。

The unending river of troops filed down the road, urged along by the crack of the whips, and all the soldiers saw lying on either side of them the bloody remains of Pythius’s eldest son. Where was Pythius at that moment? Did he stand by the corpse? By which part of it? How did he behave when Xerxes approached in his chariot? With what expression on his face? This is unknowable, because, being a slave, he had to kneel with his face to the ground.

一种不确定性困扰着薛西斯。它啃咬着他。他用一种傲慢和骄傲来隐藏它。为了让内心感觉更强大他对自己的权力充满信心，组织了一次检阅他的陆军和舰队。这巨大的规模不能不令人印象深刻，不能不令人屏息。所有弓箭同时射出的箭矢数量如此之多，以至于遮蔽了太阳。船只数量如此之多，以至于人们无法看到海湾的水域：当他们在阿比杜斯时，薛西斯决定要调查他的整个军队。已经专门为他建造了一座白色石头讲台……并建立在那里的一座小山上。从这个有利位置，他可以俯瞰海岸，看到陆军和舰队。当他看着他们时，他产生了观看船只比赛的愿望……薛西斯在比赛中，甚至在整个军队中，都感到非常高兴。

An uncertainty afflicts Xerxes. It gnaws at him. He hides it with a show of haughtiness and pride. In order to feel stronger, internally shored up, assured of his power, he organizes a review of his army and fleet. The immensity of this mass cannot but impress, cannot but take one’s breath away. So great is the number of arrows released simultaneously from all the bows that it obscures the sun. The ships are so many that one cannot see the waters of the bay: While they were in Abydus, Xerxes decided that he would like to survey his whole army. A dais of white stone had already been made especially for him … and set up on a hill there. From this vantage-point he could look down on to the seashore and see both the land army and the fleet. As he watched them he conceived the desire to see the ships race … Xerxes took great pleasure in the race, and indeed in the whole army.

赫勒斯滂海峡被他的船只完全覆盖，阿比杜斯的海岸和平原完全被人类占领，薛西斯一开始感到深深的自我满足，但后来他开始哭泣。

The sight of the Hellespont completely covered by his ships and the coast and plains of Abydus totally overrun by men first gave Xerxes a feeling of deep selfsatisfaction, but later he began to weep.

国王哭了？

The king crying?

他的叔叔阿达班努斯看到薛西斯的眼泪，对他这样说道：“大人，不久前您还对自己的处境感到高兴，现在您却在哭泣。心情彻底改变了！ ”

His uncle, Artabanus, seeing Xerxes’ tears, spoke to him thus: “My lord, a short while ago you were feeling happy with your situation and now you are weeping. What a total change of mood!”

“是的，”薛西斯回答道。 “我在反思时，突然意识到人的生命是多么短暂，这让我产生了悲悯之心。看看所有这些人——但一百年后他们中没有一个人还活着。”

“Yes,” Xerxes answered. “I was reflecting on things and it occurred to me how short the sum total of human life is, which made me feel compassion. Look at all these people—but not one of them will still be alive in a hundred years’ time.”

他们就这样关于生与死的话题讨论了很长一段时间，之后国王把他的老叔叔送回苏萨，等待黎明，下令穿越赫勒斯滂海峡到对岸——欧洲：日出时，薛西斯将金杯中的奠酒倒入大海，面向太阳，请求太阳神避免发生任何可能阻止他到达欧洲外部边界并征服整个大陆的事故。

They converse thus about life and death for a long while still, after which the king sends his old uncle back to Susa and, having waited for the dawn, orders the crossing of the straits of the Hellespont to the other side—to Europe: At sunrise, Xerxes poured a libation from a golden cup into the sea and, facing the sun, asked the sun-god to avert any accidents which might stop him from reaching the outer limits of Europe and conquering the whole continent.

薛西斯的军队喝干河流的水，吃掉所遇到的一切食物，并坚守爱琴海北岸，穿越色雷斯、马其顿和色萨利，到达塞莫皮莱。

Xerxes’ army, drinking the rivers dry, consuming whatever food it comes across, and keeping to the northern shores of the Aegean Sea, crosses Thrace, Macedonia, and Thessaly, and reaches Thermopylae.

•••

•   •   •

每所学校的课程都包括温泉关；将整个班级投入其中的情况并不常见。

Every school curriculum includes Thermopylae; it is not usual to devote an entire class to it.

塞莫皮莱是一条狭窄的地峡，是大海和位于今天希腊首都西北部的一座高山之间的通道。占领了这条通道，就等于打开了通向雅典的道路。波斯人明白这一点，当然希腊人也明白这一点。正因如此，双方才会在这里展开一场激烈的战斗。希腊的战斗人员将全部灭亡，但波斯的损失也将是巨大的。

Thermopylae is a narrow isthmus, a passage between the sea and a high mountain lying to the northwest of today’s Greek capital. To seize this passage is to have an open road to Athens. The Persians understand this, as do the Greeks, of course. Which is why both will wage a fierce battle here. The Greek combatants will all perish, but Persian losses will also be immense.

最初，薛西斯指望少数保卫塞莫皮莱的希腊人一看到庞大的波斯军队就会逃跑，所以平静地等待着这种情况的发生。但希腊人在列奥尼达的指挥下并没有撤退。薛西斯不耐烦地派出一名侦察兵骑在马背上执行侦察任务。侦察兵接近希腊人的营地，对他们进行严密监视……他观察他们的各种活动，比如赤身裸体地锻炼、梳理头发；这让他感到惊讶，但他仔细记录了他们的人数，然后返回薛西斯，没有遇到任何反对。没有人追赶他，事实上，他遭到了完全的冷漠。当他回来后，他向薛西斯详细报告了他所看到的情况。

Initially, Xerxes counted on the handful of Greeks defending Thermopylae simply to flee at the sight of the gigantic Persian army, and so calmly waited for that to occur. But the Greeks, under the command of Leonidas, do not retreat. Impatient, Xerxes sends out a scout on horseback on a mission of reconnaissance. The scout approached the Greeks’ camp and kept them under close surveillance … He watched them in a variety of occupations, such as exercising naked and combing their hair; this surprised him, but he took careful note of their numbers and then made his way back to Xerxes, without meeting any opposition. No one set out after him, and in fact he met with total indifference. When he got back he gave Xerxes a thorough report on what he had seen.

薛西斯听着斥候的话，但他无法理解，事实上，希腊人正在竭尽全力地杀戮或被杀。

Xerxes listened to what the scout had to say, but he could not understand that in actual fact the Greeks were getting themselves ready to kill or be killed to the best of their ability.

战斗持续了数天，只有一个叛徒才打破了平衡，他为波斯人指明了穿越山脉的道路。他们包围了希腊人，希腊人全部被杀。战斗结束后，薛西斯走过遍地尸体的战场，寻找列奥尼达的尸体。当他来到列奥尼达斯的尸体前时……他让手下砍下他的头颅，并将其挂在一根柱子上。

The battle lasts several days, and the balance is tipped only by a traitor who shows the Persians the path through the mountains. They surround the Greeks, all of whom are killed. After the battle, Xerxes walks over the battlefield strewn with corpses, looking for the body of Leonidas. When he came to Leonidas’ corpse, … he told his men to cut off his head and stick it on a pole.

薛西斯随后的所有战斗都失败了。当薛西斯意识到灾难的严重程度时，他感到害怕。如果希腊人得到了怎么办？ 航行到赫勒斯滂海峡并拆除他的桥梁的想法？那样的话，他就会被困在欧洲，很可能就会被消灭。于是薛西斯的思绪转向了逃跑。

Xerxes lost all of his subsequent battles. When Xerxes realized the extent of the disaster that had taken place, he became afraid. What if the Greeks got the idea … of sailing to the Hellespont and demolishing his bridges? In that case, he would be trapped in Europe, and would probably be wiped out. And so Xerxes’ thoughts turned to flight.

他确实逃跑了，在战争结束前放弃了战场。他回到苏萨。他三十多岁了。他将继续担任波斯国王十五年，在此期间他将致力于扩建他在波斯波利斯的宫殿。也许他感到内心疲惫不堪？也许他患有抑郁症？无论如何，就世界而言，他消失了。权力、统治一切和所有人的梦想逐渐消失。据说他只对女人感兴趣；他为他们建造了一座巨大而宏伟的后宫，我已经看到了它的废墟。

And flee he does, abandoning the theater of war before the war’s end. He returns to Susa. He is thirty-something years old. He will be king of the Persians for another fifteen years, during which time he will occupy himself with expanding his palace in Persepolis. Perhaps he felt internally spent? Perhaps he suffered from depression? In any event, insofar as the world was concerned, he disappeared. The dreams of might, of ruling over everything and everyone, faded away. It is said that he was interested only in women; he constructed for them an immense, imposing harem, whose ruins I have seen.

公元前465 年，他被保安队长阿达班努斯谋杀，当时他五十六岁。这位阿尔达班努斯推举薛西斯的弟弟阿尔塔薛西斯为王。阿尔塔薛西斯后来在宫殿里爆发的一场战斗中谋杀了阿尔达班努斯。阿尔塔薛西斯的儿子薛西斯二世于425年被他的兄弟粟特人谋杀，后来又被大流士二世谋杀，等等。

He was fifty-six years old when, in 465 B.C.E., he was murdered by Artabanus, the commander of his security guard. This Artabanus put up Xerxes’ younger brother, Artaxerxes, to be king. Artaxerxes in turn later murdered Artabanus, during a fight that broke out in the palace. The son of Artaxerxes, Xerxes II, was murdered in 425 by his brother Sogdians, who was later murdered by Darius II, etc., etc.

雅典誓言

THE OATH OF ATHENS

乙在被击败的薛西斯撤出欧洲并带着他的瘦弱、病弱和饥饿的军队返回苏萨之前（无论他们走到哪里，遇到什么人，他们都会偷走并吃掉他们的庄稼。如果没有庄稼，他们就吃草他们在地里找到生长的草药，从野树和栽培树上剥下或拔下树皮和叶子。他们太饿了，什么都不尝试。此外，他们受到疾病的蹂躏，到处都有人死于痢疾。薛西斯还留下患病的部队来照顾和维持他们在行军的特定地点到达的任何社区……在这一切发生之前，许多其他事情将会发生，并且将会流淌大量的鲜血。

Before the defeated Xerxes pulls out of Europe and returns to Susa with his emaciated, sick, and starving forces (wherever they went and whatever people they encountered, they stole and ate their crops. If there were no crops to be had, they ate grass and herbs they found growing in the ground, and bark and leaves they peeled or pulled off both wild trees and cultivated ones. They were so hungry that they left nothing untried. Moreover, they were ravaged by disease, and men were dying of dysentery throughout the journey. Xerxes also left sick troops in the care and maintenance of whichever community they had reached at that particular point of the march …, before all this happens, many other things will come to pass and much blood will be spilled.

毕竟，一场战争正在进行，波斯将征服希腊，这意味着亚洲将占领欧洲，专制主义将摧毁民主，奴隶制将战胜自由。

There is a war going on, after all, one in which Persia is to conquer Greece—meaning, Asia is to seize Europe, despotism is to destroy democracy, and slavery is to prevail against freedom.

起初，一切都表明这确实会发生，事情会是这样。波斯军队向欧洲进军数百公里，没有遇到任何抵抗。更有什者，几个希腊小国担心如此大军必胜，不战而降，加入了波斯一方。因此，薛西斯的军队随着前进而变得更加庞大和强大。占领了塞莫皮莱屏障后，薛西斯到达了雅典。他占领并烧毁了这座城市。然而，尽管雅典撒谎在废墟中，希腊仍然存在——并且它将被地米斯托克利的天才所拯救。

At first, everything suggests that this will in fact occur, that it will be thus. The Persian army marches hundreds of kilometers into Europe without meeting any resistance. What is more, several small Greek states, fearing that such a great army’s victory is inevitable, surrender without a fight and join the Persian side. So Xerxes’ army grows even larger and more powerful as it advances. Having seized the barrier that was Thermopylae, Xerxes reaches Athens. He occupies and burns down the city. Yet while Athens lies in ruins, Greece still exists—and it will be saved by the genius of Themistocles.

地米斯托克利刚刚被选为雅典领导人。这是在一个困难时期、一个非常紧张的时刻发生的，因为众所周知，薛西斯正在准备入侵。恰巧此时，雅典收到了劳里姆银矿产生的大量资金。民粹主义者和煽动者立即乘风破浪，喊出口号：平等分配给所有人！最后，每个人都会拥有一些东西，每个人都会感到坚强和安全。

Themistocles has just been chosen leader of Athens. This takes place at a difficult time, during a moment of great tension, because it is known that Xerxes is preparing an invasion. It so happens that just at that moment, Athens receives a large influx of funds generated by its silver mines in Laurium. The populists and the demagogues instantly feel the wind in their sails and come out with a slogan: Distribute it to all equally! Finally, everyone will have something, everyone will feel strong and secure.

但地米斯托克利明智而勇敢地行事：他呼吁雅典人醒悟过来！毁灭的危险笼罩在我们头上。我们唯一的救赎不是散布这笔钱，而是用它建造一支能够抵抗波斯军队的强大舰队！

But Themistocles acts sensibly and courageously: Athenians, he calls, come to your senses! The danger of annihilation hangs over our heads. Our only salvation, instead of spreading that money about, is to build with it a strong fleet capable of resisting the Persian force!

希罗多德用对比的方式描绘了这场古代伟大战争的图景：一方面，从东方涌来一股巨大而强大的压路机，这是一支受制于王主、王神专制意志的盲目力量。 。另一边则是分散的、内部争吵不断的希腊世界，充满了争端和对抗，这是一个由部落和独立城市组成的世界，没有一个共同的政府来约束它们。雅典和斯巴达这两个城市中心占据了这个不连贯的混合物的顶端，它们的关系和安排加在一起将决定古希腊历史的主轴。

Herodotus paints the picture of this great war of antiquity by means of contrasts: On the one side, from the East, comes surging an immense, powerful steamroller, a blind force subject to the despotic will of a king-master, a king-god. On the other side sprawls the scattered, internally quarrelsome Greek world, rife with disputes and antagonisms, a world of tribes and independent cities without a common government to bind them. Two urban centers, Athens and Sparta, rise to the top of this incoherent amalgam, and taken together, their relations and arrangements will determine the principal axis of ancient Greek history.

两个人也在这场战争中面对面。年轻的薛西斯一世有着无限权力的强烈意识，而年长的地米斯托克利则坚信自己的事业是正义的，在思想和行动上都勇敢无畏。他们的处境没有可比性：薛西斯绝对统治，随意发布命令；地米斯托克利在发布命令之前，首先必须征得那些只是名义上对他负责的军事指挥官的同意，而且还必须赢得民众的认可。他们的角色也各有不同：一个是率领大军如雪崩般前进，急于取得决定性的胜利；另一个只是一个首席，他花时间与不断召集争论的希腊人说服、辩论和讨论。

Two individuals also face each other in this war. The young Xerxes, with a strong sense of boundless power, and Themistocles, older, convinced his cause is just, courageous in thought and in deed. Their situations are not comparable: Xerxes rules absolutely, issuing orders at will; before Themistocles can issue an order, he must first secure the consent of military commanders who only nominally answer to him, and he must also win the approval of the populace. Their roles, too, are different: one rides at the head of an army advancing like an avalanche, in a hurry to attain a decisive victory; the other is merely a primus inter pares, and spends his time convincing, debating, and discussing with the continually convening disputatious Greeks.

波斯人没有面临任何困境——他们的唯一目标就是取悦他们的国王。他们就像亚当·密茨凯维奇诗《奥登的堡垒》中的俄罗斯士兵。

The Persians face no dilemmas—their single goal is to please their king. They are like Russian soldiers from the poem “Ordon’s Redoubt” by Adam Mickiewicz.

士兵们如何倒下，他们的上帝和信仰是沙皇。

How the soldiers fall, whose God and faith is the Czar.

沙皇生气了：让我们死吧，让沙皇高兴。

The Czar is angry: let us die, and make the Czar happy.

相比之下，希腊人本质上是分裂的。一方面，他们依恋自己的小家园、城邦，每个国家都有自己独特的利益和野心；另一方面，他们通过共同的语言和共同的神以及一种更大的希腊爱国主义的模糊感觉（尽管有时会产生强烈共鸣）而团结在一起。

The Greeks by contrast are by nature divided. On the one hand, they are attached to their small homelands, their city-states, each with its distinct interests and separate ambitions; on the other hand, they are united by a common language and common gods, as well as by a vague feeling—which nevertheless resonates forcefully at times—of a greater Greek patriotism.

战争在两条战线上进行：陆地和海上。波斯人占领温泉关后，很长一段时间没有遇到抵抗。然而，他们的舰队却不断遭受戏剧性的挫折。首先，它因暴风雨和大风而遭受巨大损失。突然的狂风将波斯船只推向海岸岩石，它们像火柴盒一样破碎，船员被淹死。

The war is taking place on two fronts: on land and at sea. After seizing Thermopylae, the Persians encounter no resistance for a long time. Their fleet, however, keeps suffering dramatic setbacks. To begin, it sustains large losses as a result of storms and gales. Sudden violent winds propel Persian ships onto coastal rocks, where they shatter like matchboxes and their crews drown.

最初，希腊舰队的危险性比风暴要小。波斯人拥有数倍以上的船只和这种数量优势抑制希腊士气；他们一次又一次陷入恐慌，灰心丧气，想着逃跑。他们远非天生的杀手。他们不喜欢当兵。如果有机会避免冲突，他们就会急切地抓住它。有时他们会竭尽全力避免发生小冲突。当然，除非对手是另一个希腊人——在这种情况下，他们会与他凶猛地摔跤。

Initially, the Greek fleet is a lesser danger than the storms. The Persians have many times more ships and this numerical superiority depresses Greek morale; time and again they fall into a panic, lose heart, think of escaping. They are far from being born killers. They do not have a taste for soldiering. If there is an opportunity to avoid a clash, they eagerly seize it. Sometimes they will go to great lengths just to avoid a skirmish. Unless the opponent is another Greek, of course—in which case they will wrestle with him ferociously.

现在，在波斯的压力下，希腊舰队也不断撤退。它的指挥官地米斯托克利竭尽全力阻止它。坚持住，他劝告船员们，尽量坚守阵地！有时他们会听他的话，但并非总是如此。撤退仍在继续，直到希腊船只最后在雅典附近的萨拉米斯湾找到了港口。希腊船长在这里感到安全。海湾的入口非常狭窄，波斯国王在带着庞大的舰队驶入海湾之前会三思而后行。

Now too, under Persian pressure, the Greek fleet keeps retreating. Its commander, Themistocles, tries as far as he can to restrain it. Hold on, he exhorts the crews of the ships, try to maintain your positions! Sometimes they listen to him, but not always. The withdrawal continues, until at last the Greek ships find harbor in the bay of Salamis, near Athens. The Greek captains feel safe here. The entrance to the bay is so narrow that the Persian king will think twice before sailing in with his gigantic fleet.

薛西斯和地米斯托克利现在都在思考自己的处境。薛西斯：进去还是不进去？地米斯托克利：如果我能把薛西斯吸引到这个小海湾里，那么海湾的面积太小了，他的人数将成为劣势。薛西斯：我会赢，因为我将坐在海边的王位上，波斯人看到他们的国王在注视着，就会像狮子一样战斗！地米斯托克利还不知道薛西斯在想什么，为了确保波斯人进入海湾，他使出了一个诡计：他……向他的一个手下（他的一个家奴，准确地说是他孩子们的侍从）通报了情况。 ——他的名字叫西辛努斯（Sicinnus）），并把他送到波斯营地乘船……。西辛努斯航行过来，对波斯指挥官说：“我正在为雅典指挥官执行一项秘密任务，他实际上同情薛西斯的事业，并且希望你们在战争中占据上风，而不是希腊人。其他希腊人都不知道我在这里。从我主人那里得到的消息是，希腊人正处于恐慌之中，正计划撤退。除非你袖手旁观，让他们逃走，否则你就有机会在这里取得辉煌的胜利。他们不团结，不 给你带来阻力的位置；事实上，你会看到他们用自己的船只互相攻击，那些站在你一边的人与那些不站在你这边的人作战。”送完这条消息后，西辛努斯就离开了。

Both Xerxes and Themistocles now ponder their situations. Xerxes: To go in or not to go in? Themistocles: If I can draw Xerxes into the little bay, its surface is so small that his numbers will prove a disadvantage. Xerxes: I will win, because I will sit on the throne at the edge of the sea, and the Persians, seeing that their king is watching, will fight like lions! Themistocles doesn’t yet know what Xerxes is thinking, and to make certain that the Persians enter the bay, he resorts to a trick: He … briefed one of his men (a house-slave of his—his children’s attendant, to be precise—whose name was Sicinnus), and sent him over to the Persian camp in a boat …. Sicinnus sailed over and said to the Persian commanders, “I am on a secret mission for the Athenian commander, who is in fact sympathetic to Xerxes’ cause and would prefer you to gain the upper hand in the war rather than the Greeks. None of the other Greeks know that I am here. The message from my master is that the Greeks are in a state of panic and are planning to retreat. Unless you just stand by and let them escape, you have an opportunity here to achieve a glorious victory. They are disunited, in no position to offer you resistance; in fact you’ll see them pitting their ships against one another, those who are on your side fighting those who are not.” After delivering this message, Sicinnus left.

地米斯托克利后来被证明是一位优秀的心理学家。他知道薛西斯和所有统治者一样，是一个虚荣的人，而虚荣使人盲目，削弱人理性思考的能力。这次也是如此。受到有关希腊争吵的虚假信息的鼓舞，他没有避开小海湾总是为大舰队带来的陷阱，而是下令驶入萨拉米斯并封锁希腊人的逃生路线。波斯人在黑暗的掩护下执行了这一行动。

Themistocles turned out to be a good psychologist. He knew that Xerxes, like every ruler, was a vain man, and that vanity makes one blind, impairs one’s ability to think rationally. And so it was this time. Encouraged by the disinformation about Greek squabbling, instead of steering clear of the trap that a small bay always poses for a large fleet, he gives the order to sail into Salamis and block the Greeks’ escape route. The Persians execute this maneuver, under the cover of darkness.

当天晚上，就在波斯人秘密而悄悄地接近海湾时，希腊人之间爆发了另一场争端，他们没有意识到正在发生什么：因此萨拉米斯的指挥官们激烈地互相争吵。他们仍然没有意识到自己已经被波斯舰队包围了，并继续认为敌人还留在他们白天驻扎的地方。

That same evening, even as the Persians are secretively and quietly approaching the bay, another dispute flares up among the Greeks, who do not realize what is transpiring: So the commanders at Salamis were furiously hurling arguments at one another. They were still unaware that they had been surrounded by the Persian fleet, and continued to assume that the enemy had remained where they had seen them stationed during the day.

当他们听说波斯人逼近时，一开始并不相信这个消息，但最终接受了这个消息，并在地米斯托克利的激励下做好了战斗准备。

When they hear of the Persians’ approach, they initially give the news no credence, but finally accept the information and, spurred on by Themistocles, prepare themselves for combat.

战斗在黎明时开始，因此坐在萨拉米斯对面被称为埃加利奥斯山脚下的王座上的薛西斯可以观察到这场战斗。每当他一方进展顺利时，他就会询问这艘船的船长是谁，然后他的抄写员写下这个人的名字、他的父亲以及他来自的城镇。薛西斯对胜利充满信心，希望以后能够奖励他的英雄。

The battle begins at dawn, so that Xerxes, sitting on a throne at the foot of the mountains which lie opposite Salamis and are called the Aegaleos, can observe it. Whenever anything went well for his side, he asked who the captain of the ship in question was, and his scribes wrote down the name of the man, his father, and the town he came from. Xerxes, confident of victory, wants to be able to reward his heroes later.

每个时代的文学中都可以找到对战争的忠实描述，它们有一个共同点：它们描绘了一幅巨大的混乱、巨大的混乱和壮观的混乱的画面。即使是最精心策划的参与正面碰撞的瞬间，陷入了血腥、颤抖的漩涡，让人难以辨认，更不用说控制了。有些人一心要杀人，有些人则想方设法溜走，或者至少要躲避打击，一切都被喊叫、呻吟和尖叫声覆盖，一片混乱和烟雾。

Faithful descriptions of battles which can be found in the literature of every epoch have one thing in common: they paint a picture of tremendous chaos, monstrous confusion, spectacular disorder. Even the most carefully orchestrated engagement in the moment of frontal collision descends into a bloody, quivering vortex, in which it is difficult to get one’s bearings let alone to gain control. There are those hell-bent on killing others, and those looking for a means to slip away, or at the very least to duck the blows, and everything is overlaid with shouts, moans, and yelps, amidst turmoil and smoke.

萨拉米斯的情况也是如此。在两个人之间的战斗中，人们可能会看出敏捷，甚至优雅，但两支由木船组成、由数千只桨推动的舰队在狭小空间内的碰撞，一定就像一个大桶，有人将数百个缓慢爬行、笨拙的物体扔进了这个大桶。攀爬和混乱缠绕的螃蟹。一艘船撞上了另一艘船，一艘侧翻，另一艘与全体船员一起沉入海底，另一艘船试图撤退，在其他地方，几艘船互相搏斗，似乎永远锁在一起，在其他地方，一艘船正试图转向周围，​​另一个正试图溜出海湾，在普遍的混乱中，希腊人攻击希腊人，波斯人攻击波斯人，直到最后，经过几个小时的海上地狱后，波斯人放弃了，而那些剩下的人——那些人没有被淹死或以其他方式杀死——逃脱。

So it was at Salamis. Whereas in the combat between two individuals one may discern agility, even grace, the collision in tight quarters of two fleets consisting of wooden ships and propelled by thousands of oars must have resembled a great bucket into which someone has thrown hundreds of sluggishly creeping, clumsily clambering, and chaotically entangled crabs. One ship rammed another, one listed on its side, another sank to the bottom with its entire crew, yet another attempted retreat, somewhere else several struggled against one another, permanently locked together it would have seemed, somewhere else a ship was trying to turn around, another was attempting to slip out of the bay, and in the general confusion Greeks fell upon Greeks, Persians upon Persians, until at last, after hours of this maritime hell, the Persians gave up and those of them who were left—those not drowned or otherwise killed—escaped.

薛西斯对失败的第一反应是恐惧。他要做的第一件事就是送他的孩子们去以弗所（他的一些私生子也参加了这次探险）。他指定出生于佩达萨的高级宫廷太监赫尔莫蒂莫斯为他们的监护人。

Xerxes’ first reaction to the defeat is fear. The first thing he undertakes is to send his children to Ephesus (some illegitimate children of his had come along on the expedition). He gives them as a guardian Hermotimus, a high-ranking court eunuch who was born in Pedasa.

希罗多德对这个人的命运非常感兴趣，并详细地描述了这一点：据我们所知，没有人比赫莫提姆斯对他所犯的错误施加更彻底的报应。他在一场战争中被俘，被出售后被希俄斯的一个名叫帕尼纽斯的人买下。现在，帕尼纽斯以可以想象到的最残暴的方式谋生。他过去所做的就是获取 那些长得好看的男孩，阉割了他们，然后把他们带到撒狄和以弗所，在那里他会以很高的价格出售他们。在国外，太监的价格比完整的男人高，因为他们完全可靠。帕尼纽斯的受害者之一——众多受害者之一，因为这是他谋生的方式——是赫莫蒂姆斯。然而事实上，赫莫提莫斯的运气并不全是坏事：他作为众多礼物之一从萨迪斯被送到薛西斯的宫廷，并最终成为国王最有价值的太监。

Herodotus is very interested in this man’s fate and writes about it in detail: No one we know of has ever exacted a more total retribution for a wrong done to him than Hermotimus. He was taken prisoner in a war, put up for sale, and bought by a man from Chios called Panionius. Now, Panionius made a living in the most atrocious way imaginable. What he used to do was acquire good-looking boys, castrate them, and take them to Sardis and Ephesus, where he would offer them for sale at very high prices; in foreign countries eunuchs command higher prices than whole men on account of their complete reliability. One of Panionius’ victims—one among a great many, because this was the way he made a living—was Hermotimus. In fact, however, Hermotimus’ luck was not all bad: he was sent from Sardis to Xerxes’ court as one of a number of gifts, and eventually became the king’s most valued eunuch.

现在，当薛西斯在萨迪斯，在率领军队进攻雅典的途中，赫莫提姆斯因公事来到了密西亚的阿塔纽斯地区，那里是希俄斯人居住的地方，他在那里遇见了帕尼尼乌斯。他与帕尼纽斯进行了一次长时间、友好的交谈，首先列出了帕尼纽斯给他带来的所有好处，然后提出愿意为他做同样多的好事作为回报。他说，他所要做的就是将家人搬到阿塔纽斯并住在那里。帕尼纽斯很高兴地接受了赫莫蒂姆斯的提议，并将他的妻子和孩子搬到了那里。因此，当赫莫提姆斯将帕尼纽斯和他的全家带到他想要的地方时，他说：“帕尼纽斯，世界上没有人像你一样以肮脏的方式谋生。我或我的家人对你或你们的家人做了什么伤害？为什么你把我变成一个无足轻重的人而不是一个男人？你以为诸神不会注意到你在那些日子里所做的事情，但他们遵循的法律是正义的，并且因为你的罪行，他们将你交付到我手中。因此，你应该没有理由抱怨我将从你那里收取的款项。”当他责备完毕后，他把帕尼尼乌斯的儿子们带进房间，并开始强迫他阉割他们四个人。这件事是在强迫下完成的，后来赫莫提姆斯强迫儿子们阉割了他们的父亲。这就是复仇和赫莫提姆斯如何追上帕尼纽斯的。

Now, when Xerxes was in Sardis, in the course of setting out with his army against Athens, Hermotimus went down on some business or other to the part of Mysia called Atarneus, where people from Chios live, and he met Panionius there. He entered into a long, friendly conversation with him, first listing all the benefits that had come his way thanks to Panionius, and then offering to do as much good to him in return; all he had to do, he said, was move his family to Atarneus and live there. Panionius gladly accepted Hermotimus’ offer and moved his wife and children there. So when Hermotimus had Panionius and his whole family where he wanted, he said, “Panionius, there is no one in the world who makes a living in as foul a way as you do. What harm did I or any of my family do to you or any of yours? Why did you make me a nothing instead of a man? You expected the gods not to notice what you used to do in those days, but the law they follow is one of justice, and for your crimes they have delivered you into my hands.
