As a result, then, you should have no grounds for complaint about the payment I am going to extract from you.” When he had finished this rebuke, he had Panionius’ sons brought into the room and proceeded to force him to castrate all four of them. The deed was done, under compulsion, and afterwards Hermotimus forced the sons to castrate their father. And that is how vengeance and Hermotimus caught up with Panionius.

犯罪与惩罚、不公正与报复——迟早总会有一个人追随另一个人。人与人之间如此，国家之间也是如此。在希罗多德看来，无论谁首先发动战争，因此犯下了罪行，无论是立即还是经过一段时间后，都会受到报复和惩罚。这关系，这种势不可挡的配对，就是命运的本质，就是不可逆转的命运的意义。

Crime and punishment, injustice and revenge—one always follows the other, sooner or later. As it is in relations between individuals, so it is between nations. Whoever first starts a war, and therefore, in Herodotus’s opinion, commits a crime, will be revenged upon and punished, be it immediately or after the passage of time. This relation, this inexorable pairing, is the very essence of fate, the meaning of irreversible destiny.

帕尼纽斯经历过，现在轮到薛西斯了。就万王之王而言，事情就更复杂了，因为他也是国家和帝国的象征。苏萨的波斯人得知萨拉米斯舰队被歼灭后，并没有撕破衣服；相反，他们为国王的命运而颤抖，并希望他不会发生任何意外。这就是为什么当他最终回到波斯时，他的归来是一个盛大而壮丽的时刻——人们感到高兴、松了一口气。谁关心成千上万的死者和溺水者，关心破碎的船只——最重要的是国王还活着，他再次与我们在一起！

Panionius experienced it, and now it was Xerxes’ turn. In the case of the King of Kings, the matter is more complicated, because he is also the symbol of the nation and of the empire. The Persians in Susa, having learned about the annihilation of the fleet at Salamis, do not rend their garments; rather, they tremble for the fate of their king and hope that nothing untoward happens to him. Which is why, when he finally does return to Persia, his homecoming is a grand and magnificent occasion—the people are happy, relieved. Who cares about the thousands of dead and drowned, about the shattered ships—what matters most is that the king is alive and that he is once again with us!

薛西斯逃离希腊，但将部分军队留在那里。他任命他的表弟、大流士的侄子马尔多尼乌斯为指挥官。

Xerxes escapes from Greece, but leaves part of his army there. As its commander he designates his cousin, Darius’s nephew—Mardonius.

马多纽斯小心翼翼地开始说道。首先，他不慌不忙地在色萨利平静地过冬。然后他派遣一名特殊的使者前往各个神谕处，了解他们的预言。在这些指导下，他派了一位马其顿人亚历山大……带着消息前往雅典。他选择亚历山大执行此任务的原因之一是因为亚历山大与波斯有家族联系……他认为亚历山大是赢得雅典人的最佳选择，他之所以想这样做，是因为他显然听说他们是一个人口众多且好战的种族，而且他他知道波斯人在海上遭遇的失败主要是他们造成的。有雅典人站在他一边，马多尼乌斯确信——这是完全合理的信心——他将毫无困难地控制海洋，而据他所知，他在陆地上已经拥有相当大的优势。这就是他战胜希腊人的计划。

Mardonius begins cautiously. First, without hurrying, he calmly winters in Thessaly. Then he sends a special messenger to the various oracles, to learn their prophecies. Guided by these, he sent a Macedonian, Alexander …, off to Athens with a message. One reason he chose Alexander for this mission was because Alexander had family ties with Persia … He considered Alexander his best bet for winning over the Athenians, which he wanted to do because he had apparently heard that they were a populous and warlike race, and he was aware that the defeat the Persians had met at sea was due mainly to them. With the Athenians on his side, Mardonius was sure—an entirely justified confidence—that he would have no difficulty in gaining control of the sea, while he already had a considerable advantage on land, as far as he could see. So this was his plan for overcoming the Greeks.

亚历山大抵达雅典并试图说服其居民，他们不应该对波斯人发动战争，但相反，应该与他们达成协议，否则他们就会灭亡，因为国王拥有令人难以置信的权力和深远的影响力。

Alexander arrives in Athens and tries to convince its inhabitants that they should not wage war against the Persians but instead should enter into an agreement with them, for otherwise they will perish, seeing as how the king has incredible power at his command and a very long reach.

雅典人却回答道：“事实上，我们已经意识到我们所拥有的资源与薛西斯的巨大力量之间的差距，所以不需要你的尖锐提醒。尽管如此，我们如此专注于自由，我们将尽我们所能地为之奋斗……。去把雅典人的信息传达给马多尼乌斯：只要太阳保持目前的运行方向，我们就永远不会与薛西斯达成协议。相反，我们将走上战场与他作战，我们相信众神和英雄的支持，他对他们感到极度蔑视，以至于烧毁了他们的房屋和雕像。”

The Athenians nevertheless reply as follows: “In actual fact, we were already aware of the disparity between the resources at our disposal and Xerxes’ enormous power, so there was no need for your pointed reminder. Nevertheless, we are so focused on freedom that we will fight for it however we can…. Go and take this message from the Athenians to Mardonius: as long as the sun keeps to its present course, we will never come to terms with Xerxes. On the contrary, we will take to the field and fight against him, confident of the support of the gods and heroes for whom he felt such utter contempt that he burnt their homes and statues.”

对于到达雅典的斯巴达人来说，他们担心这座城市会与波斯人达成协议，他们这样说：“你们非常了解雅典人的气质。你应该知道，地球上没有足够的黄金，也没有任何如此美丽和肥沃的土地，我们会接受它以换取与敌人合作并奴役希腊......。因此，如果您之前不知道的话，我们可以向您保证，只要有一个雅典人还活着，我们就永远不会与薛西斯达成协议。”

And to the Spartans, who had arrived in Athens fearing that the city would come to terms with the Persians, they said this: “You are perfectly well aware of the Athenian temperament. You should have known that there isn’t enough gold on earth, or any land of such outstanding beauty and fertility, that we would accept it in return for collaborating with the enemy and enslaving Greece…. So if you didn’t know it before, we can assure you that so long as even a single Athenian remains alive, we will never come to terms with Xerxes.”

听完这句话，亚历山大和斯巴达人离开了雅典。

After hearing these words, Alexander and the Spartans departed from Athens.

时间消失

TIME VANISHES

时间他不再是亚的斯亚贝巴，而是达累斯萨拉姆，一座海湾上的城市，被雕刻成如此完美的半圆形，看起来就像数百个温柔的希腊海湾之一——这个海湾不知何故被运送到了这里，到了非洲东海岸。大海总是风平浪静；缓慢的小波浪，产生安静、有节奏的水花，无影无踪地沉入岸边温暖的沙子里。

This was no longer Addis Ababa but Dar es-Salaam, a city on a bay that had been sculpted into such a perfect semicircle that it seemed like one of hundreds of gentle Greek coves—this one somehow transported here, to the eastern shore of Africa. The sea was always calm; slow little waves, creating a quiet, rhythmic splash, sank without a trace into the warm sand of the shore.

尽管这座城市的人口不超过二十万，但现在似乎有一半的世界都聚集在它身上，并称之为家园。仅它的名字“达累斯萨拉姆”在阿拉伯语中的意思是“和平之家”，就谈到了它与中东的联系（当然，这是臭名昭著的联系，因为阿拉伯人通过这里运送非洲奴隶）。城镇中心主要由印度人和巴基斯坦人占据，他们的文明产生了各种语言和信仰：其中有锡克教徒、阿迦汗的追随者、穆斯林和来自果阿的天主教徒。这里有来自印度洋岛屿——塞舌尔群岛、科摩罗群岛、马达加斯加和毛里求斯——的移民殖民地——这是一个有吸引力的、甚至是美丽的群体，是由南方不同民族的融合而形成的。还有最近抵达的中国人，他们来这里修建坦赞铁路。

Although the city numbered no more than two hundred thousand, it seemed now as if half the world had converged upon it and was calling it home. Its name alone, Dar es-Salaam, which in Arabic means “House of Peace,” spoke of its ties to the Middle East (infamous ties, to be sure, because Arabs shipped out African slaves through here). The center of town was occupied largely by Indians and Pakistanis, with all the permutations of language and faith their civilization has produced: Among them were Sikhs, followers of the Aga Khan, Muslims, and Catholics from Goa. There were colonies of immigrants from the Indian Ocean islands—from the Seychelles and the Comoros, Madagascar and Mauritius—an attractive, even beautiful group that came into being from the mingling of various peoples of the South. And there were the more recently arrived Chinese, who had come here to build the Tanzania-Zambia railroad.

第一次遇到如此多元化的民族和与 20 世纪 60 年代的达累斯萨拉姆所体现的文化不同，欧洲人并没有因为意识到欧洲边界之外存在着各种各样的其他世界而感到震惊——他已经意识到这一点，至少在理论上，很长一段时间了。 ——但最重要的是，这些世界在没有欧洲调解的情况下相遇、混合和共存，在某种程度上，也没有欧洲的知情和同意。许多世纪以来，欧洲确实是世界的中心，以至于欧洲人现在很难认识到，没有他，也没有他，其他民族和文明仍然继续着各自的传统和独特的问题。而且他是这里的新来者、外国人，他的宇宙不过是一个遥远而抽象的现实。

Encountering for the first time such a diversity of peoples and cultures as were in evidence in Dar es-Salaam of the 1960s, the European was struck not so much by the realization that all manner of other worlds existed beyond Europe’s boundaries—he had been aware of this, at least theoretically, for quite some time—but above all by the fact that these worlds met, mixed, and coexisted without the mediation and, to some degree, without the knowledge and consent of Europe. For many centuries, Europe was the center of the world in such a literal and obvious way that it now dawned upon the European with difficulty that, without him and beyond him, other peoples and civilizations carried on with their respective traditions and their distinct problems. It was moreover he who was the newcomer here, the foreigner, and his universe but a distant and abstract reality.

第一个认识到世界的本质多样性的是希罗多德。 “我们并不孤单，”他在作品中告诉希腊人，为了证明这一点，他踏上了前往天涯海角的旅程。 “我们有邻居，他们也有他们的邻居，我们共同生活在一个星球上。”

The first to realize the world’s essential multiplicity was Herodotus. “We are not alone,” he tells Greeks in his opus, and to prove this he undertakes his journeys to the ends of the earth. “We have neighbors, they in turn have their neighbors, and all together we populate a single planet.”

对于一个在此之前还生活在一个小家园的人来说，他可以轻松地步行穿越其领土，这次史无前例的行星调查是一次觉醒，它改变了他的理解，赋予了他迄今为止未知的维度，一个全新的维度。价值尺度。

For a human being who until then had lived within the confines of a small homeland whose territory he could easily cover on foot, this unprecedented, planetary survey was an awakening, one that transformed his understanding, imparting to it hitherto unknown dimensions, an entirely new scale of values.

希罗多德在旅行和遇到不同的部落和民族时，观察并记录了他们每个人都有自己的历史，这些历史独立于其他历史而平行展开——换句话说，人类历史远不是一个故事，从整体上看，人类历史就像一部伟大的历史。在其永远沸腾的表面上，无数粒子不断碰撞，每个粒子都在自己的轨道上运动，沿着在无数点相交的轨迹。

Traveling and encountering various tribes and peoples, Herodotus observes and records that each of them has its own history, which unfolds independently from yet parallel to other histories—in other words, that far from being one story, human history in its aggregate resembles a great cauldron whose perpetually simmering surface sees incessant collisions of innumerable particles, each moving in their own orbits, along trajectories that intersect at an infinite number of points.

希罗多德还发现了其他东西，即时间的多样性，或者更准确地说，测量时间的方法的多样性。因为在过去，农民以一年中的季节来计算时间，城市居民以世代来计算时间，古代国家的编年史家以统治王朝的长度来计算时间。如何比较这些测量结果，如何找到一种转换方法或共同点？希罗多德不断地思考这个问题，寻找解决方案。习惯了精确的机械测量的我们，并没有意识到时间的计算曾经是一个多么大的问题，其中潜伏着多少困难，有多少谜语和奥秘。

Herodotus discovers something else as well, namely, the multiformity of time, or, more precisely, the multiplicity of methods of measuring it. For in the old days, peasants calculated time by the seasons of the year, city dwellers by generations, the chroniclers of ancient states by the length of the ruling dynasties. How does one compare these measurements, how does one find a means of conversion or a common denominator? Herodotus wrestles with this issue constantly, searches for solutions. Accustomed to an exacting mechanical measurement, we do not realize what a problem the computation of time once presented, how much difficulty lurked therein, how many riddles and mysteries.

有时，下午或晚上有空，我会开着那辆破旧的绿色路虎去海景酒店，坐在阳台上，点一杯啤酒或一杯茶，听听大海的叹息。大海，或者从黄昏开始，伴随着蝉鸣声。这是达累斯萨拉姆最受欢迎的聚会场所之一，其他机构或出版物的同事经常会过来。白天，我们都在城里闲逛，试图找出答案。这个偏远的省会城市没有发生太多事情，为了收集任何信息，我们必须合作，而不是竞争。我们中的一个人比其他人有更好的耳朵，一个人有更好的眼睛，另一个人更有新闻运气。时不时地——在街上、在有空调的咖啡馆里、或者在海景酒店——交换战利品的事情时不时地发生。有人听说发生了一场针对蒙博托的政变；其他人则认为这是流言蜚语，但如何才能验证这一点呢？根据这些谣言、耳语、猜想以及事实，我们拼凑出我们的报告并将其寄回国内。

At times, when I had a free afternoon or evening, I would drive in my beat-up green Land Rover to the Sea View Hotel, where one could sit on the verandah, order a beer or some tea, listen to the sigh of the sea or, beginning at dusk, to the chirping of the cicadas. It was one of Dar es-Salaam’s most popular meeting places, and colleagues from other agencies or publications would often drop by. During the day, we all cruised about town, trying to find things out. There was not much happening in this remote, provincial city, and to collect any information at all we had to cooperate, not compete. One of us had a better ear than others, one a better eye, another more journalistic luck. Every now and then—in the street, in one of the air-conditioned cafés, or here at the Sea View Hotel—the exchange of loot took place. Someone had heard that there had been a coup against Mobutu; others dismissed this as gossip—and how could one verify it, anyway? From such rumors, whispers, conjectures—and facts, too—we cobbled together our reports and sent them back home.

有时阳台上没人出现，如果碰巧有希罗多德在身边，我就会随意翻开这本书。这 历史充满了故事、离题、观察、道听途说。当然，色雷斯的人口是世界上最多的，仅次于印第安人。在我看来，如果他们由一个人统治或有一个共同的目标，他们将是无敌的，并且将是迄今为止世界上最强大的国家。然而，这对他们来说是完全不可能的——这根本不可能发生——这就是他们软弱的原因……。他们有卖孩子出口国外的做法。他们不限制年轻女性的行为，而是让她们与任何她们想要的男性发生性关系；然而，他们对妻子的监视非常严格。他们花重金从女方父母那里买来妻子。在他们看来，有纹身是出身高贵的标志，而没有纹身则是出身低贱的标志。他们认为最好不要工作，而在土地上工作被认为是最不光彩的职业。在他们看来，最好的谋生方式就是靠战利品谋生。这是他们最显着的习俗。

Sometimes no one appeared on the verandah, and if I happened to have Herodotus with me, I would open the book at random. The Histories is full of stories, digressions, observations, hearsay. The population of Thrace is the largest in the world, after the Indians, of course. If they were ruled by a single person or had a common purpose, they would be invincible and would be by far the most powerful nation in the world, in my opinion. This is completely impossible for them, however—there is no way that it will ever happen—and that is why they are weak …. They have the practice of selling their children for export abroad. They do not restrict the behaviour of their young women, but let them have sex with any men they want; however, they keep a very strict eye on their wives. They buy their wives from the woman’s parents for a great deal of money. Being tattooed is taken by them to be a sign of high birth, while it is a sign of low birth to be without tattoos. They consider it best not to work, and working the land is regarded as the most dishonourable profession. The best way to make a living, in their judgement, is off the spoils of war. These are their most remarkable customs.

我抬起头，注意到在色彩缤纷的花园里，一位身穿白衣的服务员——一个名叫阿尼尔的印度教徒——正在给挂在芒果树树枝上的一只驯服的猴子喂香蕉。动物做出滑稽的表情，阿尼尔哈哈大笑。侍者、傍晚、温暖、蝉声、香蕉、茶，都让我想起了印度，想起了那些迷恋又迷茫的日子；无论在这里还是那里，热带都以同样的强度渗透到人的每一根纤维中。我什至觉得这里有印度的气味，但这只是阿尼尔的香气，还有他的槟榔、茴香和佛手柑。从某种意义上说，印度无处不在——人们不断看到印度教寺庙、餐馆、剑麻和棉花种植园。

I raise my eyes and notice that in the colorfully illuminated garden a white-clad waiter—a Hindu called Anil—is feeding a banana to a tame monkey hanging from the branches of a mango tree. The animal is making comical faces, and Anil is roaring with laughter. The waiter, the evening, the warmth, the cicadas, the banana, and the tea, all remind me of India, of my days of fascination and confusion; both here and there, the tropics penetrate with the same intensity every fiber of one’s being. It even seems to me that the scents of India are reaching me here, but it’s just the aroma of Anil, with his betel, anise, and bergamot. And India, in a sense, is all around—one keeps seeing Hindu temples, restaurants, sisal and cotton plantations.

我回到希罗多德。

I return to Herodotus.

经常阅读他的作品，甚至对它产生某种亲密感——一种熟悉，一种习惯，一种依赖，甚至——开始对我产生一种奇怪的、难以形容的影响。可以肯定的是，我不再意识到时间的障碍，不再意识到与希腊人所描述的事件相隔了两年半——这是一个名副其实的深渊，罗马和中世纪、古代文明的诞生和发展都位于其中。伟大的宗教、美洲的发现、文艺复兴和启蒙运动、蒸汽机和电力、电报和飞机、数百场战争，包括两次世界大战、抗生素的发现、人口爆炸、成千上万的事情当我们阅读希罗多德时，这些事件就消失得无影无踪，就好像它们不存在一样，或者至少退到了背景中，从前面移到了阴影中。

The frequent reading of his work and even a certain kind of intimacy with it—a familiarity, a habit, a dependence, even—started to exert an odd indefinable influence on me. What is certain is that I was no longer conscious of a barrier of time, of being separated from the events the Greek describes by two and a half thousand years—a veritable abyss in which lie Rome and the Middle Ages, the birth and development of the Great Religions, the discovery of America, the Renaissance and the Enlightenment, the steam engine and electricity, the telegraph and the airplane, hundreds of wars, including two world wars, the discovery of antibiotics, the population explosion, thousands upon thousands of things and events which, when we read Herodotus, vanish as if they didn’t exist, or at least recede into the background, moving from the fore into the shadows.

在这个时间鸿沟的另一边出生、生活和工作的希罗多德会因此感到贫困吗？我们无法做出合理的推断，他确实这么做了，而且他也没有给出任何迹象。相反：他生活得很充实，探索了整个世界，遇见了无数的人，听了数百个故事；他是一个活跃、精力充沛、不知疲倦的人，不断地寻找、不断地忙于某事。他想了解更多的事情、问题、奥秘，解决更多的谜语，找到一长串问题的答案，但他根本没有足够的时间——足够的时间或力量。他根本无法管理这一切，就像我们无法管理这一切一样——人的一生是如此短暂！他是否为还没有高铁、飞机，甚至自行车而烦恼？人们有理由怀疑这一点。如果他有火车或飞机可供使用，他会收集并留下更多信息吗？人们也应该怀疑这一点。

Could Herodotus, who was born, lived, and worked on the other side of this chronological chasm, feel impoverished as a result? We can make no reasoned inference that he did, and he gives no indication. On the contrary: he lives fully, explores the whole world, meets numerous people, listens to hundreds of tales; he is an active, energetic, and tireless man, constantly searching, constantly busy with something. He would like to learn about many more things, issues, mysteries, solve many more riddles, find the answers to a long litany of questions, but he simply does not have time enough—enough time or strength. He simply cannot manage it all, just as we cannot manage it all—man’s life is so brief! Is he bothered that there are no high-speed trains or planes yet, or even bicycles? One can reasonably doubt that. If he had a train or plane at his disposal, would he have gathered and left us even more information? One should doubt that as well.

我的印象是希罗多德的问题完全不同：他决定，可能在生命的最后一刻，写一本书，因为他意识到自己已经积累了如此巨大的财富。大量的故事和事实，除非他保存下来，否则它们就会消失。他的书是人类与时间、记忆的脆弱性、记忆的短暂性以及记忆自我抹去和消失的永恒趋势的斗争的另一种表达。这本书的概念，任何一本书，都源于这场战斗。文字具有持久性，甚至可以说是“永恒”。人知道，随着岁月的流逝，他会越来越清楚地认识到这一点，也越来越敏锐地感觉到，记忆是脆弱而转瞬即逝的，如果他不写下他所学到的和经历过的东西，他内心所承载的东西就会消失。当他这样做的时候，他就会灭亡。这就是为什么似乎每个人都想写一本书。歌手和足球运动员、政治家和百万富翁。如果他们自己不知道如何做，或者没有时间，他们就会委托别人来为他们做这件事。事情就是这样，而且永远都是这样。造成这种现实的印象是写作是一种简单而简单的追求，尽管那些赞同这种观点的人可能最好思考一下托马斯·曼的观察：“作家是一个写作比其他人更困难的人。”

I have the impression that Herodotus’s problem was altogether different: He decides, probably toward the end of his life, to write a book because he realizes that he has amassed such an enormous trove of stories and facts that unless he preserves them, they will simply vanish. His book is yet another expression of man’s struggle against time, against the fragility of memory, its ephemerality, its perpetual tendency to erase itself and disappear. The concept of the book, any book, arose from just this battle. The written word has a durability, one would even like to say “eternality.” Man knows, and in the course of years he comes to know it increasingly well, feeling it ever more acutely, that memory is weak and fleeting, and if he doesn’t write down what he has learned and experienced, that which he carries within him will perish when he does. This is why it seems everyone wants to write a book. Singers and football players, politicians and millionaires. And if they themselves do not know how, or else lack the time, they commission someone else to do it for them. That is how it is and always will be. Engendering this reality is the impression of writing as an easy and simple pursuit, though those who subscribe to that view might do well to ponder Thomas Mann’s observation that “a writer is a man for whom writing is more difficult than it is for others.”

由于希罗多德希望尽可能多地为他人保留他所发现和经历过的事情，因此他的书并不是简单地记录王朝、国王和宫廷阴谋的历史——尽管他确实写了很多在讲述统治者和权力的同时，他也讲述了普通人的生活，讲述了他们的宗教信仰和农业实践，讲述了疾病和自然灾害，讲述了山川河流、植物和动物。例如，关于猫：如果房子着火，猫的遭遇就非常不寻常。埃及人懒得去扑灭大火，而是在房子周围每隔一段距离就站好，留意猫的踪迹。然而，猫们却从他们中间溜走，甚至跳过他们，冲进火里。这让埃及人陷入深深的悲痛之中。在猫自然死亡的家庭中 死后，住在那里的人都剃掉眉毛——仅此而已。在狗死了的家庭里，他们会把狗的全身、头和全身的毛都剃光。

As a result of Herodotus’s desire to preserve for others as much as possible of what he has found out and lived through, his book is not a simple recording of the histories of dynasties, kings, and palace intrigues—though he does write a great deal about rulers and power, he tells us also about the life of simple people, about their religious beliefs and agricultural practices, about illnesses and natural disasters, about mountains and rivers, plants and animals. For example—about cats: If a house catches fire, what happens to the cats is quite extraordinary. The Egyptians do not bother to try to put the fire out, but position themselves at intervals around the house and look out for the cats. The cats slip between them, however, and even jump over them, and dash into the fire. This plunges the Egyptians into deep grief. In households where a cat dies a natural death, all the people living there shave off their eyebrows—nothing more. In households where a dog dies, they shave their whole bodies, head and all.

或者关于鳄鱼：

Or about crocodiles:

这就是鳄鱼的样子。他们在冬季的四个月里什么也不吃。它们是四足的，并且是两栖的……据我们所知，没有任何凡人生物能够从如此微小的开始成长到如此之大。它产下的蛋并不比鹅蛋大多少，新生的鳄鱼的大小与其蛋的大小相当，但成年的鳄鱼至少可以有十七肘长，而且

This is what crocodiles are like. They eat nothing during the four winter months. They are four-footed, and amphibious … As far as is known, there is no mortal creature which grows so big from such small beginnings. The eggs it lays are not much bigger than a goose’s eggs, and the size of a new-born crocodile corresponds to that of its egg, but a fully grown adult can be at least seventeen cubits long, and

也许更多。它有猪一样大的眼睛，但牙齿和獠牙却很大……。随着

maybe more. It has eyes the size of a pig’s, but huge teeth and tusks …. With the

除了鹬以外，其他鸟类和动物都躲避它。然而鹬却和它关系很好，因为它对鳄鱼有用。当鳄鱼爬出水面并爬上陆地时，它会大大地打哈欠（通常是面朝西方时），然后鹬就会溜进它的嘴里吞掉水蛭。这对鳄鱼有好处，给它带来快乐，因此不会伤害鹬。

exception of the sandpiper, all other birds and animals run away from it The sandpiper, however, is on good terms with it, because it is of use to the crocodile. When the crocodile climbs out of the water and on to land, it yawns widely (usually when facing west), and then the sandpiper slips into its mouth and swallows the leeches. This does the crocodile good and gives it pleasure, so it does not harm the sandpiper.

起初我没有注意到这些猫和鳄鱼。只有在连续阅读后，它们才会出现在我面前，令我惊恐的是，我突然注意到，那些疯狂的猫跳进了火里，当我坐在尼罗河边时，我发现附近有张开的火堆。鳄鱼的嘴和一只无所畏惧的小鸟在里面翻找。这是很自然的：我们必须反复阅读希罗多德的书以及每一本伟大的著作；每次阅读都会揭示另一层内容，即以前被忽视的主题、图像和含义。因为每一本伟大的书里都有其他几本。

I did not notice these cats and crocodiles at first. They appeared to me only upon successive readings, when I suddenly noticed, to my horror, the crazed cats leaping into the fire, and when it seemed to me, as I was sitting at the edge of the Nile, that I spotted nearby the opened jaws of a crocodile and a small, fearless bird rummaging around inside them. This is natural: one must read Herodotus’s book—and every great book—repeatedly; with each reading it will reveal another layer, previously overlooked themes, images, and meanings. For within every great book there are several others.

希罗多德生活得很充实；他不为没有电话或飞机而烦恼，也不担心没有自行车。这些机器只会在数千年后出现——那又怎样呢？他没有想到这些东西对他可能有用，也许是因为没有它们他也能表现出色。他的世界，他的人生都有自己的力量，自己的拥有不减且自给自足的能量。他感觉到了它，它给了他翅膀。他一定是一个开朗、轻松、善良的人，因为只有对这样的人，陌生人才会透露他们的秘密。他们不会向孤僻、阴郁的人敞开心扉；悲观的性格会唤醒其他人离开的欲望、对距离的需求，甚至会引起恐惧。如果希罗多德就是这样的性格，他就无法完成他所做的事情，我们也不会有他的书。

Herodotus lives fully; he is not bothered by the lack of the telephone or the airplane, nor does he worry about not having a bicycle. These machines will appear only thousands of years later—and so what? It doesn’t occur to him that such things might have been useful to him, perhaps because he manages excellently without them. His world, his life have their own strength, their own undiminishing and self-sufficient energy. He senses it, and it gives him wings. He must have been a cheerful, relaxed, kind man, because it is only to such people that strangers reveal their secrets. They do not open up to someone withdrawn and gloomy; pessimistic dispositions awaken in others the desire to move away, the need for distance, and can even elicit fear. If such had been Herodotus’s personality, he would have been unable to accomplish what he did, and we wouldn’t have his book.

我经常想到这一点，同时感觉到，不无惊讶，甚至有一定程度的焦虑，随着我越来越沉浸在希罗多德的书中，我在情感上和认知上越来越认同他所回忆的世界和事件。 。我对雅典的毁灭比苏丹最近的军事政变感触更深，对我来说，波斯舰队的沉没比刚果的另一场军队叛变更加悲惨。我所经历的世界不仅是非洲的世界（我应该以一家新闻机构的记者的身份来写这个世界），而且还包括那个远离这里、数百年前就消失了的世界。

I thought about this frequently, sensing at the same time, not without surprise and even a measure of anxiety, that as I immersed myself increasingly in Herodotus’s book, I identified more and more, emotionally and cognitively, with the world and events that he recalls. I felt more deeply about the destruction of Athens than about the latest military coup in the Sudan, and the sinking of the Persian fleet struck me as more tragic than yet another mutiny of troops in Congo. The world that I was experiencing was not only the African one, about which I was supposed to be writing as the correspondent of a press agency, but also that one far from here which vanished hundreds of years ago.

因此，在一个闷热的热带夜晚，坐在达累斯萨拉姆海景酒店的阳台上，我想到了马尔多尼斯军队中冰冻的士兵，他们在一个寒冷的夜晚——因为它当时欧洲正值冬天——他们试图在火边温暖麻木的双手。

And so there was nothing strange about the fact that, sitting of a steamy tropical evening on the verandah of the Sea View Hotel in Dar es-Salaam, I was thinking about the freezing soldiers of Mardonius’s army, who on a frosty night—for it was winter in Europe then—tried to warm their numb hands by the fire.

沙漠和海洋

THE DESERT AND THE SEA

我暂时搁置希腊-波斯战争，以及野蛮军队无休无止的游行和好争吵的希腊人的争论——谁是最重要的，谁的领导权应该得到承认——因为阿尔及利亚大使朱迪刚刚打电话给我们。 ，并表示“见面是值得的”。 “值得见面”这个表达有一个潜台词，通常意味着某种承诺、充满希望的最终结果、值得感兴趣和密切关注的事情；就好像有人说：“来见我吧，我有东西给你——你不会后悔的。”

I set aside for a while the Greco-Persian war, with its endless processions of barbarian armies and the arguing of the quarrelsome Greeks—over who among them is most important, whose leadership should be recognized—because the Algerian ambassador, Judi, has just called, saying that “it would be worthwhile to meet.” The expression “it would be worthwhile to meet” has a subtext, and usually implies a promise of some sort, a hopeful eventuality, something worthy of interest and closer attention; it is as if someone said, “Come meet me, I have something for you—you won’t regret it.”

朱迪有一座宏伟的住所——一座白色、通风的别墅，具有宏伟的毛里塔尼亚古老风格，其建造方式可以在各处创造阴影，甚至在那些按理说应该受到阳光直射的地方也是如此。我们坐在花园里，高墙后面传来大海的声音。当时正值涨潮，巨大的波浪从水深处的某个地方，从地平线之外的远处向我们袭来，并在附近拍打，因为别墅就坐落在水面上，在一个低矮的岩石海岸上。

Judi had a magnificent residence—a white, airy villa, in the grand old Mauritanian style, constructed in such a way as to create shade everywhere, even in those places which, logically, should be in direct sunlight. We sat in the garden, and from behind the high wall the sounds of the ocean washed over us. It was high tide, and from somewhere in the watery depths, from far beyond the horizon, enormous waves moved toward us and crashed nearby, for the villa stood right on the water, on a low, rocky shore.

我们聊了一切，但没有聊什么重要的事情，正当我开始奇怪他为什么邀请我来这里时，他突然说：

We spoke about everything, but about nothing important, and just as I was beginning to wonder why he had invited me here, he suddenly said:

“我认为去阿尔及尔值得你花时间。现在那里可能很有趣。如果你愿意的话，我可以给你签证。”

“I think that it would be worth your while to go to Algiers. It might be interesting there now. If you want, I’ll give you a visa.”

我吃了一惊。那是 1965 年，阿尔及利亚没有发生什么值得注意的事情。它已经成为一个独立国家三年了，并且有一位聪明、受欢迎的年轻领导人：艾哈迈德·本·贝拉。

I was taken aback. It was 1965, and there was nothing of note happening in Algeria. It had been an independent country for three years now, and had an intelligent, popular young leader: Ahmed Ben Bella.

朱迪不再多说什么，因为穆斯林晚祷的时间即将到来，他已经开始拨弄他的翡翠念珠，我意识到是时候该走了。我面临着两难的境地。如果我向上级请求允许进行这次旅行，他们就会开始问我问题。我不知道为什么我应该去那里。与此同时，无缘无故地穿越半个非洲将是一种严重的不服从行为——更不用说财务风险了，特别是考虑到我的雇主是一家新闻机构，资金如此短缺，以至于必须详细证明最小的支出是合理的。

Judi would say no more, and because the hour of Muslim evening prayers was approaching and he had begun to finger his emerald prayer beads, I realized it was time for me to go. I faced a dilemma. If I were to approach my superiors for permission to take this trip, they would start asking me questions. I had no idea why I should be going there. At the same time, traveling halfway across Africa for no good reason would be a grave insubordination—not to mention a financial risk, especially given that my employer was a press agency so short of funds one had to justify at length the smallest expenditure.

但朱迪的态度和鼓励的语气中，有一种令人信服、甚至坚持的东西，让我决定抓住这个机会。我从达累斯萨拉姆飞经班吉、拉米堡、阿加德兹，这些航线上的飞机又小又慢，甚至航迹上限也很低，可以很好地看到撒哈拉上空的航线，它充满了迷人的图像——要么是欢乐的色彩，要么是单调的荒凉，尽管即使在后者中，在月球死气沉沉的地方，也会突然出现一片明亮而拥挤的绿洲。

But there was something so convincing, insistent even, in Judi’s manner, in the encouraging tone of his voice, that I decided to take the chance. I flew from Dar es-Salaam through Bangui, Fort Lamy, and Agadez, and on these routes the planes are small and slow and even the upper limit of their flight paths are low, affording one an excellent view of the route over the Sahara, which is full of captivating images—either joyfully colorful or monotonously bleak, though even in the latter, there will suddenly appear, amidst a lunar lifelessness, a bright and crowded oasis.

事实上，阿尔及尔的机场空无一人，甚至已经关闭。我们的飞机被允许降落，因为它属于国内航空公司。穿着灰绿色迷彩服的士兵立即包围了它，并护送我们几名乘客到一座玻璃建筑前。护照检查并不繁琐，士兵们虽然沉默寡言，但彬彬有礼。他们只会说夜间发生了政变，“暴君已被推翻”，并且这项权力已被总参谋部掌握。 “暴君？”我想问，什么暴君？两年前我在亚的斯亚贝巴见过本·贝拉。他看起来是一个很有礼貌，甚至令人愉快的人。

The airport in Algiers was empty, closed, in fact. Our plane was allowed to land because it belonged to the domestic carrier. Soldiers in gray-green camouflage jackets immediately surrounded it and escorted us—several passengers—to a glass building. The passport control was not onerous and the soldiers were courteous, although reticent. They would say only that there had been a coup d’état during the night, that “the tyrant had been removed,” and that power had been seized by the general staff. “Tyrant?” I wanted to ask, “what tyrant?” I had seen Ben Bella two years earlier in Addis Ababa. He seemed a polite, even pleasant man.

这座城市很大，阳光明媚，广阔地分布在海湾周围，呈圆形剧场。人必须不断地爬上坡或下坡。这里有时尚的法国街道，也有熙熙攘攘的阿拉伯街道。一切都是地中海建筑风格、服装和习俗的混合体。一切都闪闪发光、散发着气味、令人陶醉、疲惫不堪。一切都会引起好奇、吸引人、着迷，但也会让人焦虑。如果您累了，您可以在数百家阿拉伯或法国咖啡馆之一坐下来。您可以在数百家酒吧或餐馆之一就餐。由于靠近大海，菜单上有大量的鱼类，以及数不清的美味佳肴——甲壳类动物、蛤蜊、头足类动物、章鱼、牡蛎。

The city is large, sunny, spread out broadly, amphitheatrically, around the bay. One must constantly climb uphill or down. There are stylish French streets and bustling Arab ones. All about is a Mediterranean mixture of architectural styles, clothing, and customs. Everything sparkles, smells, intoxicates, exhausts. Everything arouses curiosity, draws one in, fascinates—but also makes one anxious. If you are tired, you can sit down in one of the hundreds of Arab or French cafés. You can eat in one of the hundreds of bars or restaurants. Because the sea is close by, there are plenty of fish on the menus, and untold delicacies of frutti di mare—crustaceans, clams, cephalopods, octopi, oysters.

但阿尔及尔首先是基督教和阿拉伯两种文化相遇并共存的地方。这种共存的历史就是这座城市的历史（当然，它还有其他更古老的历史章节——腓尼基、希腊、罗马）。不可避免地在教堂或清真寺的阴影下移动，阿尔及尔居民不断地意识到两个领域之间蜿蜒的边界线。

But Algiers is first and foremost a place where two cultures meet and coexist—the Christian and the Arab. The history of this coexistence is the history of this city (although, of course, it also has other, much older historical chapters—Phoenician, Greek, Roman). Moving inevitably in the shadow of either a church or a mosque, the Algiers resident is continually made aware of the winding borderline between the two realms.

走市中心。它的阿拉伯部分被称为卡斯巴。你沿着宽阔的石阶上山进入它——有几十级。但楼梯不是问题；问题在于。困难在于，当我们冒险深入卡斯巴的深处时，我们会感受到一种被侵入的感觉。但我们是否真的去探究、尝试去渗透那些隐藏的角落呢？或者我们是否应该快点走，一心要把自己从一种不舒服、有点尴尬的境地中解脱出来——因为我们注意到有几十双一动不动的眼睛，焦急地注视着我们走路？也许只是我们的想象？难道是我们太敏感了吗？为什么在法国街头有人盯着我们看时我们无动于衷？为什么它不会打扰我们，或者让我们感到不适，而在卡斯巴却会呢？毕竟，眼睛和观察行为是相似的，但我们对这两种情况的反应却截然不同。

Take downtown. Its Arab section is called the Casbah. You enter it walking uphill, along wide stone steps—dozens of them. But the stairs are not the problem; the difficulty is the sense of intrusion we feel as we venture deeper into the Casbah’s recesses. But do we really look into, try to penetrate those hidden corners? Or do we instead hurry along, intent on extricating ourselves from an uncomfortable, somewhat awkward situation—for we have noticed dozens of pairs of motionless eyes, importunately attentive, watching us as we walk? Are we perhaps only imagining it? Could it be that we are oversensitive? Why are we indifferent when someone stares at us on a French street? Why does it not bother us then, or cause us discomfort, whereas here in the Casbah it does? The eyes are similar, after all, likewise the act of observing, and yet we react to the two situations in such dissimilar ways.

当我们终于走出卡斯巴，发现自己再次来到法国社区时，我们可能不会松一口气，但我们确实会感到更轻松、更自在、更自然。为什么我们无法控制这些潜在的、甚至潜意识的态度和情绪？几千年来，全世界——什么都没有？

When we finally emerge from the Casbah and find ourselves once more in a French neighborhood, we may not breathe an audible sigh of relief, but we certainly feel lighter, more at ease, more natural. Why can we not control these latent, even subconscious attitudes and emotions? For thousands of years, all over the world—nothing?

一个可能和我同一天抵达阿尔及尔的外国人不会意识到前一天晚上发生了像政变这样重要的事情，国际上受欢迎的本·贝拉被一个不知名的人驱逐了，很快人们就会发现，性格内向、沉默寡言的军队指挥官胡阿里·布迈丁(Houari Boumedienne)。整个生意都是在晚上进行的，远离市中心，在一个叫伊兹拉的高级别墅区，而且那部分地区被政府和将军占领，普通行人无法进入。

A foreigner who might have arrived in Algiers on the same day as I did would not have realized that something as important as a coup d’état had taken place the previous night, that the internationally popular Ben Bella had been ousted by an unknown who, as would soon become apparent, was the introverted, taciturn commander of the army, Houari Boumedienne. The entire business was carried out at night, far from the center of town, in an exclusive villa neighborhood called Hydra, and in that part of it, moreover, that is occupied by the government and the generals and thus inaccessible to ordinary pedestrians.

人们听不到城市本身的枪声或爆炸声；街上没有坦克，也没有行进的军队。早上，人们像往常一样开车或步行去上班，店主开门营业，小贩们摆起摊位，调酒师邀请人们来喝早间咖啡。管理人员在人行道上洒水，让这座城市在正午炎热之前得到一丝湿润的清新。公共汽车在艰难地爬上陡峭的街道时发出可怕的轰鸣声。

One could not hear the shots or explosions in the city itself; there were no tanks in the streets, no marching troops. In the morning, people drove or walked to work as usual, shopkeepers opened their shops, vendors set up their stalls, and bartenders invited one in for morning coffee. Superintendents doused the sidewalks to give the city a bit of moist freshness in advance of the noontime heat. Buses roared terrifyingly as they struggled to scale steep streets.

我四处走动，心情沮丧，并对朱迪感到愤怒。他为什么鼓励我进行这次旅行？我来这里是为了什么？什么我会从这里开始写吗？我如何证明支出的合理性？沮丧之余，我突然发现穆罕默德五世大道上聚集了一群人。不幸的是，他们只是被十字路口相撞的两名司机的争吵所吸引的围观者。在街道的另一端，我看到另一个小型聚会。我跑到那里。但他们只是耐心等待邮局开门的人们。我的笔记本是空的，我没有目睹任何值得描述的事件。

I walked around crushed—and furious at Judi. Why did he encourage me to make this trip? What did I come here for? What would I write about from here? How would I justify the expenditure? Dejected, I suddenly noticed a crowd gathering on the avenue Mohammed V. Unfortunately, they were merely gawkers drawn by the quarrel of two drivers who had collided at the intersection. At the other end of the street I saw another small gathering. I ran there. But they were merely people patiently waiting for the post office to open. My notebook was empty, and I had not witnessed a single event worth describing.

但正是在阿尔及尔，我开始担任记者几年后，我才慢慢开始意识到，我当时已经走上了一条错误的道路。在那次觉醒之前，我一直在寻找壮观的意象，在幻觉中苦苦挣扎，认为它是令人信服的、可观察到的画面，以某种方式证明了我的存在是合理的，免除了我理解眼前事件的责任。这是一种谬论，认为人们只能通过世界在痉挛的时刻所选择向我们展示的东西来解释世界，当世界被枪声和爆炸所摇晃，被火焰和烟雾吞没，被灰尘和燃烧的恶臭所窒息。当一切都倒塌成废墟时，人们对亲人的遗骸感到绝望。

But it was here in Algiers, several years after I had begun working as a reporter, that it slowly began to dawn on me that I had set myself on an erroneous path back then. Until that awakening I had been searching for spectacular imagery, laboring under the illusion that it was compelling, observable tableaux that somehow justified my presence, absolving me of responsibility to understand the events at hand. It was the fallacy that one can interpret the world only by means of what it chooses to show us in the hours of its convulsions, when it is rocked by shots and explosions, engulfed in flames and smoke, choked in dust and the stench of burning, when everything collapses into rubble on which sit people despairing over the remains of their loved ones.

这一奇观是如何产生的呢？这些充满呐喊和血腥的破坏场面想表达什么？是什么力量——皮下的、看不见的，但却强大且不可抑制的力量——带来了它们？这些场景是某些事情的结束还是开始，是紧张和冲突即将到来的预兆吗？谁来监视他们？我们，记者、记者？不，死者几乎不会被埋葬，被烧毁的汽车残骸将刚刚被清理干净，街道上的碎玻璃也将被清扫干净，我们将已经收拾好行李继续前进，前往其他人正在焚烧汽车、粉碎汽车的地方商店橱窗，为阵亡者挖掘坟墓。

How did this spectacle come about? What do these scenes of destruction, replete with shouts and blood, mean to express? What forces—subcutaneous and invisible, yet powerful and unrestrainable—brought them about? Are these scenes the end or beginning of something, portents of tensions and conflicts still to come? And who will monitor them? We, the correspondents and reporters? No. The dead will barely have been buried, the wrecks of incinerated cars will have just been cleared away and the streets swept of the broken glass, and we will have already packed our bags and moved on, to where others are burning cars, shattering shop windows, and digging graves for the fallen.

但也许无法打破那种壮观的刻板印象，超越图像，尝试更深入？看来只有尝试才可行。我无法写下坦克、被烧毁的汽车和被抢劫的商店——因为我什么也没看到——并且想为我未经授权的旅行辩护，我开始寻找阿尔及利亚政变的背景和源泉，试图确定背后的原因。它以及它的含义；交谈、观察人和地​​方、阅读——简而言之，就是尝试理解。

But might it not be possible to pierce that spectacular stereotype, to move beyond imagery, attempt to reach deeper? It seemed only practical to try. Unable to write about tanks, burned cars, and looted stores—having seen nothing of the sort—and wanting to justify my unauthorized journey, I went in search of the background and the wellspring of the Algerian coup, to try to determine what lay behind it and what it signified; to talk, to observe people and places, and to read—in short, to try to understand.

直到那时，我才开始将阿尔及尔视为地球上最迷人、最引人注目的地方之一。在这座美丽但拥挤的城市的狭小空间里，当代世界的两大冲突交织在一起。第一个是基督教和伊斯兰教之间的冲突（这里表现为殖民法国和殖民阿尔及利亚之间的冲突）。第二个问题在法国独立和离开后立即获得了人们的关注，它是伊斯兰教核心的冲突，是其开放的、辩证的——我什至敢说“地中海的”——当前与其他之间的冲突，内向的人，源于对当代世界的不确定性和困惑感，受到原教旨主义者的指导，他们利用现代技术和组织原则，但同时认为捍卫信仰和习俗以对抗现代性是最重要的他们自己存在的条件，他们唯一的身份。

It was only then that I began to see Algiers as one of the most fascinating and dramatic places on earth. In the small space of this beautiful but congested city intersected two great conflicts of the contemporary world. The first was the one between Christianity and Islam (expressed here in the clash between colonizing France and colonized Algeria). The second, which acquired a sharpness of focus immediately after the independence and departure of the French, was a conflict at the very heart of Islam, between its open, dialectical—I would even venture to say “Mediterranean”—current and its other, inward-looking one, born of a sense of uncertainty and confusion vis-à-vis the contemporary world, guided by fundamentalists who take advantage of modern technology and organizational principles yet at the same time deem the defense of faith and custom against modernity as the condition of their own existence, their sole identity.

阿尔及尔在希罗多德时代最初是一个渔村，后来成为腓尼基和希腊船只的港口，面向大海。但就在城市的后面，在它的另一边，有一个广阔的沙漠省份，这里被称为“布莱德”，这是一个声称效忠古老、严格内向的伊斯兰教法律的人民声称拥有的领土。在阿尔及尔，人们简单地说存在两种伊斯兰教：一种被称为沙漠伊斯兰教，另一种被定义为河流（或海洋）伊斯兰教。第一个是好战的游牧部落所信奉的宗教，他们在世界上最恶劣的环境之一撒哈拉沙漠中挣扎求生。第二个伊斯兰教是商人、流动小贩、路上和集市上的人的信仰，对他们来说，开放、妥协和交换不仅有利于贸易，而且对生活本身也是必要的。

Algiers, which at its beginnings, in Herodotus’s time, was a fishing village, and later a port for Phoenician and Greek ships, faces the sea. But right behind the city, on its other side, lies a vast desert province that is called “the bled” here, a territory claimed by peoples professing allegiance to the laws of an old, rigidly introverted Islam. In Algiers one speaks simply of the existence of two varieties of Islam—one, which is called the Islam of the desert, and a second, which is defined as the Islam of the river (or of the sea). The first is the religion practiced by warlike nomadic tribes struggling to survive in one of the world’s most hostile environments, the Sahara. The second Islam is the faith of merchants, itinerant peddlers, people of the road and of the bazaar, for whom openness, compromise, and exchange are not only beneficial to trade, but necessary to life itself.

在殖民主义统治下，这两种伊斯兰教派因共同的敌人而联合起来。但后来他们相撞了。

Under colonialism, both these strains of Islam were united by a common enemy; but later they collided.

本·贝拉是一位地中海人，受过法国文化教育，生性开放、善于和解。当地法国人在谈话中称他为河流和海洋的穆斯林。相反，布迈丁是一支军队的指挥官，这支军队多年来一直在沙漠中作战，在那里设有基地和营地，从那里招募新兵，并利用游牧民族和阿拉伯人提供的支持和帮助。绿洲和沙漠山脉。

Ben Bella was a Mediterranean man, educated in French culture, open-minded and conciliatory by nature. Local Frenchmen referred to him in conversation as a Muslim of the river and of the sea. Boumedienne, on the contrary, was the commander of an army which for years had fought in the desert, had its bases and camps there, drew its recruits from there, and took advantage of the support and help proffered by the nomads, people of the oases and of desert mountains.

两个人连外貌都不同。本·贝拉总是衣着整洁、优雅、精致、彬彬有礼、笑容可亲。当布迈丁在政变后首次公开露面时，他看上去就像一位刚刚从被撒哈拉沙漠覆盖的运输车中走出来的坦克指挥官。他确实尝试微笑，但没有多大成功——这根本不是他的风格。

The two men differed even in their appearance. Ben Bella was always well-groomed, elegant, refined, courteous, smiling amiably. When Boumedienne appeared for the first time in public after the coup, he looked like a tank commander who had just stepped out of his conveyance covered in the sands of the Sahara. He did try to smile, but without much success—it was simply not his style.

在阿尔及尔，我第一次看到地中海。我近距离地看到了它——我可以把手伸进去，感受它的触感。我不需要问路；我知道只要往下走，再往下走，我最终就会到达那里。它无处不在，远远可见，在各种建筑物后面闪闪发光，出现在陡峭街道的底部。

In Algiers I saw the Mediterranean Sea for the first time. I saw it up close—I could dip my hand into it, feel its touch. I didn’t have to ask for directions; I knew that just by walking downward, then down some more, I would eventually reach it. It was everywhere, visible from afar, glimmering from behind various buildings, appearing at the bottoms of steep streets.

最底层是港口区，一排排简陋的木吧台，弥漫着鱼香、酒香、咖啡香。但那是海水的酸味最为明显，每一阵风都带着一种温和、平静的清凉。

At the very bottom was the port district, with simple wooden bars all in a row, smelling of fish, wine, and coffee. But it was the tart scent of the sea that was most noticeable, a gentle, calming refreshment carried on each gust of the wind.

我从来没有去过一个自然对人类如此仁慈的城市。因为它同时提供了一切——阳光、凉爽的微风、明亮的空气、银色的大海。大海对我来说似乎很熟悉，也许是因为我读过很多关于它的书。平静的波浪象征着美好的天气、和平，就像是旅行和体验的邀请。让人有一种想加入那边刚刚起航的两个渔夫的冲动。

I had never been in a city where nature is so kind to man. For it offered everything all at once—the sun, a cooling breeze, the brightness of the air, the silver of the sea. The sea seemed familiar to me, perhaps because I had read so much about it. Its smooth waves signified fine weather, peace, something like an invitation to travel and experience. One had the urge to join those two fishermen over there, who were just setting sail.

我回到达累斯萨拉姆，但朱迪已经离开了。有人告诉我，他已被召回阿尔及尔，并且因为他是胜利阴谋的参与者，所以我认为此举意味着升职。无论如何，他没有回到这里。事实上，我再也没有见过他，所以无法感谢他鼓励我去旅行。阿尔及利亚的军事政变是整个系列、一整套类似叛乱的开始，这些叛乱将在接下来的四分之一个世纪里摧毁非洲大陆年轻的后殖民国家。这些国家从一开始就很弱，而且其中许多国家至今仍然如此。

I returned to Dar es-Salaam, but Judi had left. I was told that he had been recalled to Algiers and assumed, because he was a participant in the victorious conspiracy, that the move meant a promotion. In any event, he did not return here. I never met him again, in fact, and so was unable to thank him for having incited me to take the trip. The military coup in Algeria was the start of an entire series, a whole chain of similar revolts, which for the next quarter century would decimate the continent’s young postcolonial states. Those states turned out to be weak from the start, and many of them have remained so to this day.

尤其重要的是，由于这次旅程，我第一次站在地中海岸边。我觉得从那一刻起，我比以前更了解希罗多德了。他的想法，他的好奇心，他如何看待这个世界。

Not least, thanks to this journey, I stood for the first time on the shore of the Mediterranean Sea. It seems to me that from that moment on I understood Herodotus even better than before. His thoughts, his curiosity, how he saw the world.

锚

THE ANCHOR

瓦我们仍在地中海，希罗多德的海，只是现在在它的东端，欧洲与亚洲毗邻，两大洲通过一系列轮廓平缓、阳光明媚的岛屿相交，其安静、平静的海湾鼓励水手们前来参观和停留一段时间。

We are still by the Mediterranean, Herodotus’s sea, only now on its eastern end, where Europe adjoins Asia and where the two continents meet across a chain of gently contoured, sunny isles, whose quiet, calm bays encourage sailors to visit and to stay awhile.

波斯人的指挥官马尔多尼乌斯离开了色萨利的冬季巢穴，率领军队南下进攻雅典。然而，当他到达这座城市时，他发现它无人居住。雅典被摧毁并被遗弃。人们已经搬走，到萨拉米斯避难。他派了一位特使前往那里，名叫穆里希德斯，他的任务是再次向雅典人建议他们不战而降，并承认薛西斯国王为他们的统治者。

The commander of the Persians, Mardonius, leaves his winter lair in Thessaly and heads south at the head of his army to attack Athens. When he reaches the city, however, he finds it uninhabited. Athens is destroyed and deserted. The population has moved away, taking shelter in Salamis. He sends an envoy there, a certain Murichides, whose mission is to once again propose to the Athenians that they surrender without a fight and recognize King Xerxes as their ruler.

穆里希德斯向雅典最高权力机构五百人议会提出了这一提议，一群雅典人聆听了议会的审议。委员会的一位成员利西德斯认为，最好接受马尔多尼乌斯的和解提议，并与波斯人达成某种协议。雅典观众闻言勃然大怒，将演讲者团团围住，并当场用石头砸死了他。

Murichides presents this proposal to the highest Athenian authority, the Council of Five Hundred, and a crowd of Athenians listens in on the assembly’s deliberations. One member of the council, Lycides, argues that it would be best to accept Mardonius’s conciliatory offer and come to some kind of an agreement with the Persians. Hearing this, the Athenian audience erupts in anger, surrounds the speaker, and stones him to death on the spot.

让我们在这个场景上暂停一下。

Let us pause a moment at this scene.

我们身处民主的希腊，为其言论自由感到自豪和思想。一名公民公开表达了他的观点——结果会怎样呢？顿时一片哗然。利西德斯只是忘记了战争正在进行，而在战时，包括言论自由在内的所有民主自由通常都被束之高阁。战争产生了它自己的、独特的法律，通常复杂的统治原则准则被简化为一个基本的命令：不惜一切代价取得胜利！

We are in democratic Greece, proud of its freedom of speech and of thought. One of its citizens publicly expresses his views—and what happens? There is an instant outcry. Lycides simply forgot that there was a war going on, and that in wartime all democratic freedoms, including the freedom of speech, are typically put on the shelf. War engenders its own, distinct laws, and the normally complex code of governing principles is reduced to a single fundamental imperative: victory at any cost!

利西德斯的演讲刚结束就被处决了。人们可以想象听他演讲的人群是多么的慌乱、激动和近乎歇斯底里。波斯军队紧追不舍，他们已经失去了半个国家，失去了城市。在议会审议和围观者聚集的地方不难找到石头。希腊是一个石头之国；他们无处不在。每个人都走在上面。你只需弯下腰。这正是所发生的情况。人们伸手去拿最近的一块石头，就是手边最近的一块，然后把它扔向利西德斯。起初，他可能会惊恐地大喊；后来，他已经浑身是血，痛苦地呻吟、畏缩、喘息、乞求怜悯。徒然。众人愤怒了，陷入了疯狂的状态，不再听，不再想，也无法阻止自己。只有当最后一块石头消灭了利西德斯的生命，把他变成肉浆，让他永远沉默之后，它才会醒悟过来。

Lycides has barely finished his speech when he is put to death. One can imagine how rattled, agitated, and near hysteria the crowd listening to him must have been. The Persian army is hot on their heels, they have already lost half their country, they have lost their city. It is not difficult to find stones in the spot where the council deliberated and the onlookers gathered. Greece is a country of stones; they are everywhere. Everyone walks on them. You need only bend down. And that is exactly what happens. People reach for the nearest stone, the one closest at hand, and hurl it at Lycides. At first, he probably shouts in terror; later, already dripping with blood, he moans from pain, cowers, wheezes, begs for mercy. In vain. The throng, furious, in a state of mad frenzy, no longer hears, no longer thinks, and is incapable of stopping itself. It will come to its senses only after the last stone has extinguished the life of Lycides, turned him to pulp, silenced him forever.

但这还没有结束！

But that is not the end of it!

希罗多德写道，萨拉米斯因利西德斯而发生的骚乱提醒了雅典妇女注意正在发生的事情。随着每个女人的觉醒并争取邻居的支持，她们自发地涌向利西德斯的家，用石头砸死了他的妻子和孩子。

Herodotus writes that the uproar in Salamis over Lycides alerted the Athenian women to what was happening. With every woman arousing and enlisting the support of her neighbor, they spontaneously flocked to Lycides’ house, where they stoned his wife and his children to death.

他的妻子和孩子！雅典的孩子们对他们的父亲主张与波斯人妥协有什么罪责呢？他们是否了解这些波斯人，更不用说仅仅建议与他们交谈就会被判处死刑？

His wife and children! How were the Athenian tots guilty of their daddy’s advocacy of a compromise with the Persians? Did they even know anything about these Persians, much less that the mere suggestion of talking to them was punishable by death?

他们中最小的一个能想象死亡是什么样子吗？有多可怕？他们什么时候意识到突然在自家门前看到的祖母和阿姨给他们带来的不是糖果和葡萄，而是石头，他们现在开始用石头敲开他们的头？

Were the youngest among them even able to imagine what death looks like? How terrible it is? At what point did they realize that the grandmothers and aunts whom they suddenly saw in front of their house were not bringing them sweets and grapes, but rather stones, with which they would now start to crack open their heads?

利西德斯的命运表明希腊人即使考虑与入侵者合作也深感痛苦，这引起了多么巨大的焦虑。一个人应该做什么？一个人应该如何行事？一个人应该做出怎样的选择？合作还是反抗？进行谈判还是抵制？是做出安排并努力生存，还是选择英雄主义并光荣出走？困难、令人烦恼的问题、折磨人的困境。

Lycides’ fate demonstrates the Greeks’ deeply felt pain of even contemplating collaboration with the invader, what great angst it aroused. What should one do? How should one behave? What choice should one make? Cooperate or resist? Enter into talks or boycott? Come to an arrangement and try to survive, or opt for heroism and go out in a blaze of glory? Difficult, rankling questions, tormenting dilemmas.

希腊人对这些替代方案存在分歧，他们的分歧并不局限于讨论和口头争论。他们在战场上用武器互相争斗——雅典人与底比斯人，福基亚人与色萨利人；他们互相掐断对方的喉咙，挖出对方的眼睛，砍下对方的头。没有哪个波斯人会像另一个希腊人那样激起希腊人如此大的仇恨——只要他来自对立的阵营或来自与自己不和的部落。也许各种情结都会导致这种情况，例如内疚感、不忠诚、背叛？隐藏的恐惧，一想到神圣诅咒就感到恐惧？

The Greeks are divided over these alternatives, and their disagreements are not confined to discussions and verbal sparring. They fight one another with weapons, on battlefields—the Athenians with the Thebans, the Phocians with the Thessalians; they go for one another’s throats, gouge out one another’s eyes, cut off one another’s heads. No Persian provokes so much hatred in a Greek as another Greek does—just so long as he is from an opposing camp or from a tribe that is at odds with his. Perhaps various complexes contribute to this, feelings of guilt, disloyalties, treacheries? Hidden fears, terror at the thought of a divine curse?

这场战争的最后两场战斗即将在普拉提亚和米卡勒发生新的对抗。

A fresh confrontation is about to take place, in the last two battles of this war, which will be fought at Plataea and Mycale.

首先是普拉提亚。在马尔多尼乌斯认定雅典人和斯巴达人不会屈服于他之后，他夷平了雅典，并向北撤退到底比斯人的领土，底比斯人正在与波斯人合作，那里平坦、平坦的土地非常适合到重骑兵，标志性的波斯军事编队。追击的雅典人和斯巴达人现在也到达了普拉提亚附近的这片平原。两军面对面摆好阵势，排成一排——等待。所有人都感觉到一个伟大的时刻即将到来，一个决定性的、致命的时刻。日子一天天过去，双方都保持着令人不安和虚弱的一动不动，向诸神（双方各自的）询问开始战斗的时间是否合适。但答案是否定的。

First, Plataea. After Mardonius determined that the Athenians and the Spartans would not bend to come to terms with him, he leveled Athens and withdrew to the north, to the territories of the Thebans, who were collaborating with the Persians and whose flat, even lands were well suited to heavy cavalry, the signature Persian military formation. The pursuing Athenians and Spartans now also arrived at this plain in the vicinity of Plataea. Both armies took up positions facing each other, formed into lines—and waited. All sensed that a great moment was approaching, a decisive and deadly one. Days passed, and both sides remained in a disconcerting and enervating motionlessness, asking the gods—each side its own—if the time was right to begin the battle. But the answers were no and no.

在那些日子里，一位底比斯人，希腊合作者阿塔吉努斯，为马尔多尼乌斯组织了一场宴会，他邀请了五十位最杰出的波斯人和尽可能多的最杰出的底比斯人，每对波斯人和底比斯人都坐在一张单独的沙发上。其中一张沙发上坐着瑟桑德尔（Thersander），一个希腊人，一个波斯人，希罗多德没有透露他的名字。他们一起吃喝，在某个时刻，波斯人显然带着沉思的心情，对瑟桑德尔说：“看看宴会上的这些波斯人，再想想我们留在河边扎营的军队。”他一直被预感所折磨，从他接下来的话中可以清楚地看出：“用不了多久，你就会看到他们中很少有人还活着。”波斯人一边说一边哭着。瑟桑德仍然清醒，他试图结束他沮丧且醉酒的室友的抽泣，他非常明智地说道：“你不应该把这件事告诉马多尼斯和下一个最高级别的波斯人吗？ ” 波斯人以一种听起来悲惨的智慧回答道：“我的朋友，神所注定的事件是人无法避免的，因为即使是那些说真话的人，也没有人愿意相信。很多波斯人都清楚我刚才所说的话，但我们跟随我们的领导人，因为我们别无选择。一个人所能承受的最可怕的痛苦莫过于看得清清楚楚却无能为力。”

During one of those days, a Theban, the Greek collaborator Attaginus, organizes a banquet for Mardonius to which he invites fifty of the most eminent Persians and as many of the most distinguished Thebans, seating each Persian-Theban pair on a separate couch. One of the couches is occupied by Thersander, a Greek, and a Persian whose name Herodotus does not provide. They eat and drink together, and at a certain moment the Persian, clearly in a reflective mood, says to Thersander, “Look at these Persians here at the banquet, and consider also the army which we have left encamped on the river.” He has been tormented by premonitions, as is clear from what he says next: “Before much time has passed you’ll see few of them left alive.” The Persian was weeping as he spoke. Thersander, still sober, tries to end the sobbing of his dejected and drunk couchmate by saying, quite sensibly: “Shouldn’t you be telling this to Mardonius and the next highest-ranking Persians?” To which the Persian replies with a tragic-sounding wisdom: “My friend, an event which has been decreed by the god cannot be averted by man, for no one is willing to believe even those who tell the truth. A great many Persians are well aware of what I’ve just said, but we follow our leaders because we have no choice. There’s no more terrible pain a man can endure than to see clearly and be able to do nothing.”

普拉提亚大战将以波斯人的失败而告终，并将确立欧洲的长期霸权在亚洲之前，波斯骑兵袭击了防御的希腊人，发生了一些小规模的冲突。其中一次，波斯军队的实际指挥官马西斯提乌斯（Masistius）身亡。马西提乌斯的马走在前面，被箭击中侧面，伤口的疼痛使它直立起来，把他摔倒了。马西提乌斯一落地，雅典人就扑了上来，抓住马西提乌斯，杀死了马西提乌斯，但他奋起反抗。事实上，一开始他们并没有杀死他：他贴身穿着金鳞制成的胸甲，上面穿着红色外衣，因此雅典人的打击不断击中胸甲，却毫无效果。然而，最终其中一人意识到发生了什么事，并击中了马西斯蒂乌斯的眼睛。直到这时，他才倒地身亡。

The great battle of Plataea, which will end with the defeat of the Persians and will establish Europe’s long-lasting hegemony over Asia, is preceded by minor skirmishes in which the Persian cavalry attacks the defending Greeks. In one of them, the de facto commanding officer of the Persian army, Masistius, perishes. Masistius’ horse, which was out in front of the rest, was hit in the side by an arrow, and the pain of the wound made it rear up and unseat him. As soon as Masistius landed on the ground, the Athenians sprang forward, seized the horse and killed Masistius, although he fought back. At first, in fact, they failed to kill him: next to his skin he was wearing a breastplate made of gold scales, with a red tunic on top, so the Athenians’ blows kept hitting the breastplate and achieving nothing. Eventually, however, one of them realized what was happening and struck Masistius in the eye. Only then did he fall to the ground and die.

随后，身体上爆发出激烈的挣扎。领袖的尸体是神圣的东西。逃亡的波斯人在撤退时为争夺该地的所有权而战。但他们徒劳无功，空手而归，返回营地。当骑兵返回波斯营地时，马尔多尼乌斯和他的全军得知马西斯提乌斯的死讯后深感悲痛。他们不仅剃掉了自己的毛发，还剃掉了马匹和牲畜的毛发，并陷入无尽的悲痛之中。整个维奥蒂亚都回荡着哀悼的声音，因为在马尔多尼乌斯之后，波斯没有人比他更受波斯人，特别是国王的尊敬。

A fierce struggle then erupted over the body. The corpse of a leader is a sacred thing. The fleeing Persians fought for its possession as they retreated. But they fought in vain, returning in empty-handed defeat to their camp. When the cavalry got back to the Persian encampment, Mardonius and the whole of his army were deeply upset to hear of Masistius’ death. They shaved off not only their own hair, but also that of their horses and their yoke-animals, and gave themselves over to unending lamentation. The whole of Boeotia echoed with the sound of mourning, since, after Mardonius, there was no one in Persia who was more highly respected by the Persians in general and the king in particular.

而希腊人在设法抓住马西斯提乌斯的尸体后所做的第一件事就是将尸体装上一辆马车，然后将其游行到他们的防线上。马西提乌斯身材高大，相貌英俊（这实际上就是他们对尸体这样做的原因），人们打破队列去看他。

Whereas the first thing the Greeks did, having managed to hold on to Masistius’s body, was to load the corpse on to a cart and parade it past their lines. Masistius had been remarkably tall and good-looking (which is in fact why they did this with the body), and the men broke ranks to go and see him.

所有这一切都发生在这场伟大而决定性的战斗之前几天，双方都不敢发动这场战斗，因为预兆仍然不利。在波斯方面，算命师是赫格斯特拉图斯（Hegesistratus），他是伯罗奔尼撒半岛的希腊人，但却是斯巴达人和雅典人的敌人。赫吉西斯特拉图斯曾因可怕的罪行而被斯巴达人逮捕并监禁，等待处决。 他们在他手中遭受了可怕的待遇。在这种绝望的情况下，因为他的生命处于危险之中，他宁愿忍受可怕的痛苦也不愿死去，所以他做了一件难以形容的事情。他被关在木铁制成的枷锁里，并不知何故拿到了一把偷运进监狱的刀片。他随后立即着手做的事情一定比我们听说过的任何事情都需要更大的勇气。他发现，如果他砍掉脚的大部分，那么他的其余脚就会摆脱木桩，所以他开始这样做。然后，由于受到看守，他在墙上挖了一个洞，逃到了特该亚，晚上行进，白天在林地的掩护下休息。尽管拉栖代梦人全力寻找他，但他在逃脱后两晚才抵达泰该亚。当拉栖代梦人发现他的半只脚躺在那里时，他们对他的勇气感到惊讶，但他们找不到他。

All this takes place several days before the great and conclusive battle, which neither side dares initiate because the omens continue to be unfavorable. On the Persian side, the fortune-teller is a certain Hegesistratus, a Greek from the Peloponnese but an enemy of the Spartans and the Athenians. Hegesistratus had once been arrested and imprisoned by the Spartiates to await execution for the terrible and horrific treatment they had suffered at his hands. In this desperate situation, because his life was in danger and he was prepared to suffer gruesome agonies rather than die, he did something that defies description. He was being kept in stocks made of wood bound with iron, and somehow got hold of a blade which had been smuggled into the prison. What he then immediately set about doing must have taken more courage than anything else we have ever heard of. He worked out that the rest of his foot would get free of the stocks if he cut off the bulk of his foot, so he proceeded to do so. Then since he was under guard, he dug a hole through the wall and ran away to Tegea, travelling by night and resting by day under the cover of woodland. Although the Lacedaemonians were out looking for him in full force, he managed to reach Tegea two nights after escaping. The Lacedaemonians were amazed by his courage when they found half of his foot lying there, but they could not find him.

他是如何做到的？

How did he do that?

砍掉自己的脚确实是一件很辛苦的事。

Cutting off one’s own foot is hard work indeed.

仅仅切断肌肉是不够的。还得锯断筋骨。自残在我们这个时代也时有发生：目击者声称，在古拉格集中营，人们偶尔会砍掉自己的手，或者用刀刺穿自己的腹部。甚至还描述了一名囚犯将其成员钉在木板上的事件。我们的目标始终是不惜一切代价将自己从繁重的劳动中解放出来，去医院，在那里躺一会儿，休息一下。但是砍掉自己的脚然后立刻逃跑？

It is not enough to sever the muscles. One also has to saw through the tendons and bones. Self-mutilations have occurred in our times as well: witnesses claim that in the gulags people occasionally cut off their own hands or pierced their stomachs with knives. An incident is even described in which a prisoner nailed his member to a wooden board. The goal was always to free oneself at any cost from the backbreaking labor, to go to the hospital, and there to be able to lie down awhile, to rest. But to cut off one’s foot and then run off immediately?

逃离？

To escape?

快点？

To hurry?

这怎么可能？最有可能的是用手和另一条腿爬行。但那条残缺不全的腿一定伤得很厉害，而且流了很多血。他是怎么止住血的？他不是在飞行途中累得晕倒了吗？因口渴？来自痛苦？难道他不觉得自己已经到了疯狂的边缘吗？他会吗不是见过鬼吗？难道他没有被幻觉所困扰吗？幻影？吸血鬼？而且伤口没有感染吗？毕竟，他必须把那根树桩刮过地面，穿过灰尘和污垢——否则他怎么能拖着它呢？那条腿难道就没有开始肿胀吗？充满脓液？变蓝色？

How was this even possible? Most likely by crawling on one’s hands and the other leg. But that mutilated leg must have hurt fiendishly and bled profusely. How did he stanch the blood? Did he not faint from exhaustion during the flight? From thirst? From pain? Did he not feel himself on the verge of madness? Would he not have seen ghosts? Was he not plagued by hallucinations? Apparitions? Vampires? And did the wound not get infected? After all, he had to scrape that stump over the ground, through dust and dirt—for how else could he drag it along? Did that leg therefore not start to swell? Fill with pus? Turn blue?

尽管如此，他还是逃脱了斯巴达人的追捕，康复了，给自己削了一个木制假肢，甚至成为了波斯人指挥官马尔多尼乌斯的占卜者。

And yet despite all this he escapes the Spartans, recovers, whittles himself a wooden prosthesis, and even becomes the soothsayer of Mardonius, commander of the Persians.

普拉蒂亚附近的紧张局势加剧。经过十几天向众神的供奉毫无结果后，迹象变得稍微有利，马尔多尼乌斯决定开始敌对行动。这是普通人的弱点：他急于击溃敌人，想尽快成为雅典和全希腊的总督。现在，希腊军队的每一支部队都在波斯骑兵的标枪和箭矢的攻击下伤亡惨重。当箭袋耗尽时，两军便展开了可怕的肉搏战。数十万人互相摔跤，用致命的拥抱互相掐住，用致命的拥抱互相窒息。任何手头有东西的人都会用它猛击对手的头部，或者将刀插在他的肋骨之间，或者踢他的小腿。人们几乎可以听到集体的喘息和呻吟，呻吟和喘息，咒骂和哭泣！

Tensions mount near Plataea. After a dozen days of fruitless offerings to the gods, the signs become slightly more favorable and Mardonius decides to commence hostilities. It is an ordinary human weakness: he is in a hurry to rout the enemy, wants to become the satrap of Athens and of all of Greece as quickly as possible. Now every unit of the Greek army took casualties from the javelins and arrows of the Persian cavalry as they bore down on them. And when the quivers are emptied, the two armies resort to terrifying hand-to-hand combat. Several hundred thousand men wrestle with one another, grip one another in murderous holds, choke one another in deadly embraces. Whoever has something at hand pounds his opponent over the head with it, or sticks a knife between his ribs, or kicks him in the shins. One can almost hear the collective panting and groaning, the moans and wheezes, the curses and cries!

根据希罗多德的说法，在这场血腥骚乱中，最勇敢的人是斯巴达阿里斯托达姆斯。他是列奥尼达军团三百名士兵中的最后一个，该军团在保卫温泉关时牺牲了。没有人真正知道阿里斯托达姆斯是如何幸存下来的，但为了那点运气，他遭受了羞辱和蔑视。根据斯巴达法典，一个人不可能光荣地从温泉关幸存下来：无论是谁在那里，真正为保卫祖国而战的人，肯定会死。因此，列奥尼达军团的集体墓碑上刻着这样的铭文：“路人，告诉斯巴达，我们死在这里的人都忠实于它的法律。”

In this bloody tumult, the most courageous man, according to Herodotus, turned out to be the Spartan Aristodamus. He had been the last of the three hundred soldiers of Leonidas’s regiment, which perished defending Thermopylae. Aristodamus, no one really knows how, survived, but for that piece of luck he suffered shame and contempt. According to the code of Sparta, one could not have honorably survived Thermopylae: whoever was there, and truly fought in the defense of his homeland, would surely have died. Hence the inscription on the collective tombstone of Leonidas’s regiment: “Passerby, tell Sparta that we who perished here were faithful to its laws.”

显然，斯巴达严格的法律并没有设想失败方有不同类别的战斗人员。谁上战场，只有胜利，才能生存；谁上战场，谁就能生存。败了，就得死。这是阿里斯托达姆斯，唯一的幸存者。这种区别使他陷入耻辱和耻辱。没有人愿意和他说话，所有人都轻蔑地转身走开。他奇迹般的得救很快就开始让他烦恼、窒息、燃烧。它压在他身上，变得越来越难以承受。他寻找解决办法，寻求一些解脱。随之而来的是一个机会，可以消除耻辱的烙印，或者更确切地说，英勇地结束如此烙印的生活：普拉提亚之战。阿里斯托达姆斯创造了勇敢的奇迹：他显然因为自己的名字受到诽谤而一心求死，因此鲁莽地打破了军衔，并取得了如此英雄的业绩。

Evidently, Sparta’s strict laws did not envisage different categories of combatants on the losing side. Whoever went into battle could survive only if he were victorious; defeated, one had to die. And here was Aristodamus, sole survivor. This distinction plunges him into infamy and ignominy. No one wants to speak to him, everyone turns away with disdain. His miraculous salvation soon starts to rankle, smother him, burn. It weighs upon him, becoming increasingly difficult to bear. He searches for a solution, for some relief. And along comes a chance to remove the humiliating brand, or, rather, to end heroically the life so branded: the battle of Plataea. Aristodamus accomplishes miracles of bravery: he had clearly wanted to die, because of the slur against his name, and so had recklessly broken rank and achieved such heroic exploits.

徒然。斯巴达的法律是无情的。他们身上没有怜悯，没有人情。过失一旦犯下，便是永远的过错，凡是玷污了自己的人，就永远无法洗净。因此，阿里斯托达姆斯的名字并不在希腊人认可的这场战役的英雄之中——在这场战役的阵亡者中，除了阿里斯托达姆斯之外，我提到的所有人物都获得了特殊的荣誉。阿里斯托达姆斯没有这么做，原因已经提到过——他想死。

In vain. The laws of Sparta are implacable. There is no pity in them, no human feeling. A fault once committed remains a fault forever, and whoever tainted himself can never be cleansed. And so Aristodamus’s name is not among the heroes of this battle recognized by the Greeks—among those who fell in this battle, all the men I mentioned, apart from Aristodamus, received special honours. Aristodamus did not, for the reason already mentioned—that he wanted to die.

普拉提亚战役的结果由波斯指挥官马尔多尼乌斯的死亡决定。在那个时代，指挥官们并不是躲在战线后面的伪装掩体中，而是率领军队投入战斗。然而，当指挥官死亡时，他的部队就会分散并逃离战场。因此，指挥官必须从远处可见（最常见的是，他坐在马上），因为他的士兵的行为取决于他自己的所作所为。普拉提亚的情况也是如此——马尔多尼乌斯骑着白马投入战斗……但在他被杀，他的营里的人——波斯方面最有效率的部队——被砍倒之后，所有其他人都转身逃跑了。拉栖代梦人。

The outcome of the battle of Plataea was decided by the death of the Persian commander, Mardonius. In those times, commanders did not hide behind the lines in camouflaged bunkers, but went into battle at the head of their armies. When a commander died, however, his troops would disperse and flee the field. The commander therefore had to be visible from afar (most frequently, he sat on a horse), because the conduct of his soldiers depended on what he himself was doing. And so it was at Plataea—Mardonius rode into battle on his white horse… But after he had been killed and the men of his battalion, the most effective troops on the Persian side, had been cut down, all the others turned and fled before the Lacedaemonians.

希罗多德指出，希腊方面有一个人因其顽固不化而脱颖而出。索法尼斯，他是一个雅典人：他常常带着一个铁锚，用一根青铜链系在他的胸甲腰带上，每当他到达靠近敌人的地方时，他就会抛锚，这样当敌人从他身边冲过来时，他就会抛锚。他们的队伍无法让他移动；但如果他们转身逃跑，他的计划就是起锚追击。

Herodotus notes that one man on the Greek side stood out because of his exemplary inflexibility. He was an Athenian, Sophanes: he used to carry an iron anchor, attached with a bronze chain to the belt of his breastplate, and whenever he reached a spot near the enemy he would drop anchor, so that as the enemy charged at him from their ranks they could not make him move; if they turned and fled, however, it was his plan to pick up the anchor and go after them.

多么伟大的比喻啊！与其说是一条让我们被动地漂浮在水面上的生命线，不如说它更能把我们束缚在我们的劳动中。

What a great metaphor! Rather than a lifeline, which allows us to float passively upon the surface, how much greater that which can chain us to our labors.

黑色很美丽

BLACK IS BEAUTIFUL

我当地渡轮从达喀尔码头航行到戈雷岛只需不到半小时。站在船尾，人们可以看到这座城市，它似乎在螺旋桨产生的波浪的波峰上起伏，越来越小，最后变成了沿着地平线延伸的明亮的岩石岸。就在那一刻，渡轮将船尾转向岛屿，在引擎隆隆声和铁器撞击声中，渡轮的侧面刮擦着码头的混凝土边缘。

It takes less than half an hour for the local ferry to sail from the dock of Dakar to the island of Goree. Standing at its stern, one can see the city, which seems to bob on the crests of the waves created by the propeller as it grows smaller and smaller, and finally is transformed into a bright, rocky bank stretching along the horizon. At just that moment, the ferry turns its stern toward the island and, amidst the din of rumbling engines and rattling iron, scrapes its side on the concrete edge of the marina.

我先沿着木码头走，然后是沙滩，然后是一条蜿蜒狭窄的小街，直到到达家庭旅馆，看守人阿卜杜和寄宿公寓里安静但总是忙碌的女房东玛丽姆在那里等着我。阿卜杜和玛丽姆已经结婚，从玛丽姆的身材来看，他们很快就会有一个孩子。虽然他们都还很年轻，但这已经是他们的第四次了。阿卜杜满意地看着妻子明显突出的腹部：这证明他们家里一切都很好。阿卜杜说，如果一个女人走路时肚子平坦，玛丽姆点头表示同意，那就意味着有什么不顺利的事情发生了，有违背自然规律的事情发生。焦虑的家人和朋友开始提出问题、打听、编造各种可怕的、有时甚至是恶意的故事。一切都应该采取根据世界自然节律的位置，这意味着女性应该每年一次明显地证明她慷慨且不知疲倦的生育能力。

I walk first along a wooden pier, then a sandy beach, then a twisting, narrow little street until I reach the pension de famille, where I am awaited by Abdou, the watchman, and Mariem, the boardinghouse’s quiet, always busy landlady. Abdou and Mariem are married, and, judging by Mariem’s figure, will shortly have a child. Although they are both still very young, this will be their fourth. Abdou looks with satisfaction at his wife’s clearly protruding abdomen: it is proof that all is well in their home. If a woman walks about with a flat stomach, says Abdou—and Mariem nods in assent—it means that something untoward is happening, something contrary to the order of nature. Anxious family and friends start asking questions, prying, spinning various frightening and sometimes also malicious tales. Everything should take place in accordance with the world’s natural rhythms—and this means that a woman should once a year give visible proof of her generous and indefatigable fertility.

阿卜杜和玛丽姆都属于塞内加尔最大的民族珀尔族。珀尔说沃洛夫语，皮肤比其他西非人更苍白——这就是为什么一种理论认为，很久以前，当撒哈拉沙漠还被绿色覆盖时，他们从尼罗河畔、埃及来到了这片大陆。人们可以安全地在今天的沙漠中漫步。

Abdou and Mariem both belong to the Peul community, which is the largest ethnic group in Senegal. Peul speak the Wolof language and have a paler skin than other West Africans—which is why one theory has it that they arrived in this part of the continent from the banks of the Nile, from Egypt, long ago when the Sahara was awash in green and one could wander safely over what today is desert.

由此衍生出塞内加尔历史学家和语言学家谢赫·安塔·迪奥普 (Cheih Anta Diop) 在 20 世纪 50 年代提出的一个更普遍的理论，该理论关于希腊文明的非洲裔埃及根源，同样，欧洲和西方文明也有非洲裔埃及根源。他认为，正如人类起源于非洲一样，欧洲文化也可以追溯到这片大陆。安塔·迪奥普（Anta Diop）创建了一本大型埃及语和沃洛夫语比较词典，对他来说，最大的权威是希罗多德，他在书中指出，希腊文化的许多元素是从埃及和利比亚收集和吸收的——换句话说，欧洲文化的许多元素是从埃及和利比亚收集和吸收的。文化，尤其是其地中海文化表现形式，具有非洲血统。

From this stems a more general theory, developed in the 1950s by the Senegalese historian and linguist Cheih Anta Diop, about the Afro-Egyptian roots of Greek civilization and, by the same token, of European and Western civilization. Just as humankind arose physically in Africa, so European culture, too, he maintained, could trace its beginnings to this continent. For Anta Diop, who created a large comparative dictionary of the Egyptian and Wolof languages, the great authority was Herodotus, who had argued in his book that many elements of Greek culture were gathered and assimilated from Egypt and Libya—in other words, that European culture, especially its Mediterranean manifestations, had an African ancestry.

安塔·迪奥普 (Anta Diop) 的论文与 20 世纪 30 年代末在巴黎发展起来的流行的黑人运动 (Négritude) 相吻合。它的作者是两位年轻诗人，塞内加尔人利奥波德·桑戈尔和马提尼克岛奴隶后裔艾梅·塞泽尔。在他们的诗歌和宣言中，他们宣扬黑人的自豪感——对自己种族的自豪感，几个世纪以来，这个种族一直受到白人的羞辱——并赞扬黑人的成就和价值观以及他们对世界文化的贡献。

Anta Diop’s thesis dovetailed with the popular movement of Négritude, developed in Paris at the end of the 1930s. Its authors were two young poets, the Senegalese Léopold Senghor, and Aimé Césaire, a descendant of slaves from Martinique. In their poetry and in their manifestos, they promulgated black pride—pride in their race, which for centuries had been humiliated by the white man—and praised the accomplishments and values of black people and their contributions to world culture.

•••

•   •   •

所有这一切或多或少都发生在二十世纪中叶，当时非欧洲的意识正在觉醒，非洲和所谓的第三世界人民正在寻找自己的身份，而非洲的居民也正在寻找自己的身份。尤其是希望摆脱奴役情结。安塔·迪奥普的论文以及桑戈尔和塞泽尔对黑人性的倡导——在萨特、加缪和戴维森的著作中可以找到呼应——使欧洲人认识到，我们这个被欧洲统治了几个世纪的星球正在进入一个新的、多元文化的时代，并且非欧洲社区和文化也有自己在人类家庭中获得尊严和尊重的野心。

All this occurred more or less in the middle of the twentieth century, when a non-European consciousness was awakening, when the people of Africa and of the so-called Third World in general were searching for their own identity, and the inhabitants of Africa in particular wished to rid themselves of the complex of slavery. Anta Diop’s thesis and Senghor and Césaire’s advocacy of Négritude—echoes of which can be found in the writings of Sartre, Camus, and Davidson—contributed to a European realization that our planet, dominated for centuries by Europe, was entering a new, multicultural epoch, and that non-European communities and cultures would have their own ambitions for dignity and respect in the family of man.

这就是他者的他者性问题出现的背景。直到现在，当我们思考我们与他者的关系时，他者总是与我们来自同一文化。然而现在，他者完全属于一种外来文化，是一个由其独特的习俗和价值观形成并拥护其独特习俗和价值观的个体。

This is the context in which the problem arises of the Otherness of the Other. Until now, when we pondered our relation with the Other, the Other was always from the same culture as us. Now, however, the Other belongs to an altogether foreign culture, an individual formed by and espousing its distinct customs and values.

1960年塞内加尔获得独立。上述诗人利奥波德·桑戈尔 (Léopold Senghor) 成为巴黎拉丁区俱乐部和咖啡馆的常客，成为该协会的主席。多年来，这一直是他和他的来自非洲、加勒比海地区和美洲的朋友们所怀有的理论、计划和梦想——回到象征性根源、失去的源头、回到他们所来自的世界的梦想。曾经被成群的奴隶贩子残酷地撕裂，并让整整几代人陷入陌生、卑鄙和敌对的囚禁——现在第一次可以付诸实践，转化为雄心勃勃的项目、大胆而深远的倡议。

In 1960 Senegal gains its independence. The aforementioned poet, Léopold Senghor, a habitué of the clubs and cafés of the Latin Quarter in Paris, becomes its president. That which for years had been a theory, a plan, a dream harbored by him and by his friends from Africa, the Caribbean, and both Americas—the dream of a return to symbolic roots, to lost sources, to a world from which they had been brutally torn by hordes of slave traders and cast for entire generations into an alien, debasing, and hostile captivity—now for the first time can be put into practice, translated into ambitious projects, bold and far-reaching initiatives.

从担任总统的第一天起，桑戈尔就开始筹备首届世界黑人艺术节（Premier Festival Mondial des Arts Negres）。正是——世界节日：因为这里的目标是所有黑人的艺术，而不仅仅是非洲人的艺术；我们的目标是展示艺术的广阔性、伟大性、普遍性、多样性和活力。是的，非洲是它的源头，但它遍及全球。

From the first days of his presidency, Senghor starts preparing the first-ever world festival of black art (Premier Festival Mondial des Arts Negres). Exactly—world festival: because the goal here is the art of all black people, not just Africans; the ambition is to show that art’s immensity, greatness, universality, diversity, and vitality. Yes, Africa was its source, but it spans the globe.

1963 年，桑戈尔在达喀尔为音乐节揭幕；它会持续几个月。因为我迟到了开幕式，而且镇上所有的酒店都已经住满了，所以我在岛上订了一个房间，在玛丽姆和阿卜杜经营的家庭旅馆里，他们是来自珀尔的塞内加尔人，也许是一些埃及人的后裔，或者——谁知道呢？——即使是其中一位法老。

Senghor inaugurates the festival in 1963 in Dakar; it is to last several months. Because I am late to the opening ceremonies and all the hotels in town are already full, I secure a room on the island, in the pension de famille run by Mariem and Abdou, Senegalese from Peul, perhaps the descendants of some Egyptian fellahin or—who knows?—even of one of the pharaohs.

早上，玛丽姆在我面前放了一块多汁的木瓜、一杯甜咖啡、半根法棍面包和一罐蜜饯。虽然她最喜欢安静，但按照惯例，她会在早上例行询问：我睡得怎么样，休息得好吗，天气对我来说是否太热，我有没有被蚊子咬，我有没有做梦？如果我没有梦想怎么办？我问。玛丽姆说，这是不可能的。她总是有梦想。她梦想着她的孩子，梦想着美好的时光，梦想着去乡下看望她的父母。非常美好而愉快的梦。

In the morning Mariem sets down before me a piece of juicy papaya, a cup of very sweet coffee, half a baguette, and a jar of preserves. Although she likes silence best, custom dictates serving up a ritual morning portion of inquiries: how did I sleep, am I rested, was it too hot for me, was I not bitten by mosquitoes, did I have dreams? What if I had no dreams? I ask. That, says Mariem, is impossible. She always has dreams. She dreams about her children, about good times, about visiting her parents in the countryside. Very good and pleasant dreams.

我感谢她的早餐，然后前往港口。渡轮带我去达喀尔。这座城市以节日为生。展览、讲座、音乐会、戏剧。东部和西部非洲以及南部和中部非洲都有代表；还有巴西和哥伦比亚，以及整个加勒比地区，其中牙买加和波多黎各位居前列；有阿拉巴马州和乔治亚州，以及大西洋和印度洋的岛屿。

I thank her for breakfast and go to the harbor. The ferry takes me to Dakar. The city lives and breathes the festival. Exhibitions, lectures, concerts, plays. Eastern and western Africa are represented here, as well as southern and central; there are also Brazil and Colombia, and all of the Caribbean, with Jamaica and Puerto Rico at the fore; there are Alabama and Georgia, and the islands of the Atlantic and of the Indian Ocean.

街道和广场上都有戏剧表演。非洲戏剧不像欧洲戏剧那样形式主义。一群人可以即兴聚集在某个地方，表演一场即兴表演。没有文字；一切都是产品的产物瞬间，瞬间的心情，自发的想象。主题可以是任何东西：警察抓捕一伙盗贼，商人为阻止城市夺走他们的市场而奋斗，妻子们为了争夺爱上另一个女人的丈夫而相互竞争。主题必须简单，语言必须为所有人所理解。

Theatrical performances abound in the streets and squares. African theater is not as formalistic as the European. A group of people can gather someplace extemporaneously and perform an impromptu play. There is no text; everything is the product of the moment, of the passing mood, of spontaneous imagination. The subject can be anything: the police catching a gang of thieves, merchants fighting to keep the city from taking away their marketplace, wives competing among themselves for their husband, who is in love with some other woman. The subject matter must be simple, the language comprehensible to all.

有人有想法并自愿当导演。他分配角色，戏剧开始。如果这是一条街道、一个广场、一个庭院，很快就会聚集一群路人。人们在表演期间大笑、评论、鼓掌。如果情节以有趣的方式展开，观众就会不顾烈日全神贯注地站在那里；如果戏剧没有凝结，并且临时剧团无法有效地沟通和推动行动，演出很快就会结束，观众和演员就会散去，为其他运气更好的人让路。

Someone has an idea and volunteers to be director. He assigns roles and the play begins. If this is a street, a square, or a courtyard, a crowd of passersby soon gathers. People laugh during the performance, offer running commentary, applaud. If the action unfolds in an interesting way, the audience will stand there attentively despite the punishing sun; if the play does not jell, and the ad hoc troupe proves unable to communicate and move the action forward effectively, the performance is soon over and the spectators and actors disperse, making way for others who may have better luck.

有时我看到演员们打断对话，开始一场仪式性的舞蹈，所有观众都加入进来。这可以是欢快、欢乐的舞蹈，也可以相反——舞者们认真、专注、集体参与共同的节奏，显然是一种舞蹈。对他们来说是深刻的经历，是一些触动他们内心的有意义的事情。但舞蹈结束后，演员们又回到了自己的台词上，观众们一时间仿佛出神了一样，再次大笑起来，开心而有趣。

Sometimes I see the actors interrupt their dialogue and begin a ritual dance, with the entire audience joining in. It can be a cheerful and joyous dance, or the opposite—with the dancers serious, focused, and collective participation in the common rhythm an evidently profound experience for them, something meaningful touching their core. But then the dancing ends, the actors return to their spoken parts, and the spectators, for a moment still as if entranced, laugh once again, happy and amused.

街头戏剧不仅仅包括舞蹈。它的另一个重要的、甚至是不可分割的元素是面具。演员们有时戴着面具表演，或者，因为在这么热的天气里很难长时间戴着面具，所以就干脆把面具拿在手里、腋下，甚至绑在背上。面具是一种符号，一种充满情感和共鸣的结构，它讲述着另一个宇宙的存在，它是另一个宇宙的标志、印记或存在。它沟通对我们有某事，警告我们有关某人的事情；它看似没有生命、一动不动，却试图通过它的出现来唤起我们的感情，让我们着迷。

Street theater includes not just dance. Its other important, even inseparable element is the mask. The actors sometimes perform in masks, or, because it is difficult in this heat to wear one for long, simply hold them near—in their hands, under their arms, even strapped to their backs. The mask is a symbol, a construct full of emotion and resonance that speaks of the existence of some other universe, whose sign, stamp, or presence it is. It communicates something to us, warns us about someone; seemingly lifeless and motionless, it attempts by means of its appearance to arouse our feelings, put us under its spell.

桑戈尔从各个博物馆借来了成千上万个这样的面具。当整体来看时，它们唤起了一个独立的、神秘的世界。浏览该系列是一种独特的体验。人们开始理解面具如何对人们产生如此大的影响力，它们如何能够催眠、压倒或引导人们进入狂喜状态。人们很清楚为什么面具——以及对其神奇功效的信仰——将整个社会团结起来，使他们能够跨越大陆和海洋进行交流，赋予他们一种社区和认同感，构成一种传统和集体记忆的形式。

Borrowing from various museums, Senghor collected thousands upon thousands of such masks. When seen in the aggregate, they evoke a separate, mysterious world. Walking through that collection was a singular experience. One began to understand how masks acquired such power over people, how they could hypnotize, overwhelm, or lead people into ecstasy. It became clear why the mask—and faith in its magical efficacy—united entire societies, enabled them to communicate across continents and oceans, gave them a sense of community and identity, constituted a form of tradition and collective memory.

从一场戏剧表演走到另一场表演，从一场面具和雕塑展览走向另一场表演，我有一种见证了一种伟大文化的重生，见证了它的独特性、重要性和自豪感的觉醒，以及它的意识的觉醒。其通用范围。这里不仅有来自莫桑比克和刚果的面具，还有来自里约热内卢的用于马昆巴仪式的灯笼、海地伏都教守护神的盾牌以及埃及法老石棺的复制品。

Walking from one theatrical performance to another, from one exhibit of masks and sculptures to others, I had the sense of being witness to the rebirth of a great culture, to the awakening of its sense of distinctness, importance, and pride, the consciousness of its universal range. Here were not only masks from Mozambique and Congo, but also lanterns for macumba rituals from Rio de Janeiro, the escutcheons of the guardian deities of Haitian voodoo, and copies of the sarcophagi of Egyptian pharaohs.

但伴随着全球社区复兴的喜悦也伴随着失望和幻灭。示例：在达喀尔，我读到了理查德·赖特 (Richard Wright) 最近出版的感人至深的著作《黑人权力》 (Black Power)。 20世纪50年代初，来自哈林区的非裔美国人赖特怀着回到非洲这片祖先的土地的愿望，前往加纳旅行。当时加纳正在争取独立，集会、示威、抗议不断。赖特参加了这些，了解了城市的日常生活，参观了阿克拉和塔科拉迪的市场，与商人和种植园主交谈，并得出结论，尽管他与他们有着同样的黑皮肤，但他们，非洲人，和他，美国人，彼此完全陌生，没有共同语言，对他们来说重要的事情对他来说却完全无关紧要。在非洲之旅的过程中，赖特感受到的疏离感变得越来越难以忍受，成为一种诅咒和一场噩梦。

But this joy at the renaissance of a worldwide community was accompanied also by a sense of disappointment and disillusion. Example: It is in Dakar that I read Black Power, the affecting and then recently published book by Richard Wright. At the start of the 1950s, Wright, an African American from Harlem, moved by the desire to return to Africa, the land of his ancestors, went on a trip to Ghana. Ghana was fighting for its independence just then, and there were constant meetings, demonstrations, protests. Wright took part in these, got to know the daily life in cities, visited the marketplaces in Accra and Takoradi, conversed with merchants and planters—and concluded that despite his sharing with them the same black skin, they, the Africans, and he, the American, were total strangers to each other, had no common language, and what was important to them was of utter indifference to him. In the course of the African journey the alienation Wright felt became for him increasingly difficult to bear, a curse and a nightmare.

我在家庭旅馆二楼有一个房间。它很大，全部用石头凿成，在窗户的地方有两个开口，还有一个大的开口，比例像一扇入口门，门就在那里。我还有一个宽阔的露台，从那里可以看到一直延伸到地平线的大西洋。海洋，还有更多的海洋。凉爽的微风不断地吹过房间，我有一种住在船上的感觉。岛屿是静止的，从某种意义上说，持续平静的大海也是静止的，而颜色却一直在变化——水的颜色、天空的颜色、白天和黑夜的颜色。一切，真的——墙壁和屋顶，邻近的村庄，渔船的帆，海滩上的沙子，棕榈树和芒果树，总是在这里盘旋的海鸥和燕鸥的翅膀。这个昏昏欲睡、甚至毫无生气的地方会让任何对色彩敏感的人感到头晕、着迷、震惊，一段时间后会让他麻木和疲惫。

In the pension de famille I have a room on the second floor. It is immense, all hewn out of stone, with two openings in the place of windows and a single large one, proportioned like an entrance gate, where the door would be. I also have a wide terrace, from which one can see the Atlantic stretching to the horizon. Ocean and more ocean. A cool breeze wafts constantly through the room, and I have the sensation of living on a ship. The island is motionless and in a sense the continuously calm sea is also motionless, whereas the colors are always changing—the colors of the water, of the sky, of day and night. Of everything, really—of walls and rooftops, of the neighboring village, of the sails of fishermen’s boats, of the sand on the beach, of the palm and mango trees, of the wings of the seagulls and terns that always circle here. This sleepy, even lifeless place can render anyone sensitive to color dizzy, can enthrall, stun, and after a time numb and exhaust him.

离我的寄宿处不远，在巨大的海滨巨石和茂密的植被之间，人们可以看到被时间和盐破坏的钙化墙的残余物。这些城墙——事实上，整个戈雷——都是臭名昭著的。两百年来，甚至更长的时间里，这个岛一直是一座监狱、一座集中营，也是非洲奴隶被送往另一个半球——北美、南美和加勒比地区——的登船港口。根据据各种估计，有数百万人、一千二百万、甚至两千万青年男女被驱逐出戈雷。在当时，这些数字是惊人的。大规模绑架和驱逐导致非洲大陆人口锐减。

Not far from my boardinghouse, between large waterfront boulders and thick vegetation, one can see remnants of calcified walls ruined by time and salt. These walls—and all of Goree, in fact—are infamous.
